1801 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re R.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Richmond County


115 Misc. 2d 937; 454 N.Y.S.2d 820; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3794



October 7, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner father filed a person in need of supervision (PINS) action against respondent son.


OVERVIEW: The father alleged that the son was a person in need of supervision in that he was beyond the lawful control of his parents. Moreover, the father contended that his son threatened him with a knife and damaged household property. Thereafter, the son had been "voluntarily" placed in a residential treatment center, at which time he ran away and returned home. The father then amended his petition to add an additional allegation that the son truanted from school for over two years and truanted while in placement. The court held that a general allegation that the son was beyond the lawful control of his parents could not be utilized as a predicate to subject the son's life to parental attack. Furthermore, the court held that the son's liberty interest suffered substantial infringement by his placement at the residential treatment center against his will. Additionally, the court held that the father failed to establish beyond a reasonable doubt that the son was sufficiently truant from school prior to the placement in order to sustain a finding that he was incorrigible, ungovernable or habitually disobedient and beyond the lawful control of his parents. Thus, the petition was denied.


OUTCOME: The court denied the father's PINS petition.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Infants -- Person in Need of Supervision -- Due Process Rights

1. The due process rights accorded to a respondent in a juvenile delinquency proceeding apply with equal force to a respondent in a person in need of supervision (PINS) proceeding since a PINS proceeding is quasi-criminal in nature, involving the potential for significant governmental interference in the liberty of the child (Family Ct Act, §§ 754, 756) and, therefore, a general allegation in a PINS petition that a respondent is beyond the lawful control of his parents does not afford a PINS respondent adequate notice of the charges (Family Ct Act, § 732, subd [a]) and may not be utilized as a predicate to subject the child's life to parental attack; the parents' voluntary placement of the 13-year-old respondent in foster care (Social Services Law, § 358-a) against his will at a residential facility housing voluntarily placed children, PINS and juvenile delinquents creates a sufficient deprivation of liberty so as to require review by a neutral fact finder and since the failure to hold such review for over seven months violated respondent's constitutional rights to due process of law, respondent was legally [***2] justified in absconding from the placement and his refusal to return to the facility cannot constitute behavior sufficient to sustain the PINS proceeding; accordingly, the PINS petition is dismissed and a final order of protection issued to the child directing petitioner father to cease and desist in his efforts to have respondent return to the facility.

Infants -- Person in Need of Supervision -- Truancy

2. The failure of the 13-year-old respondent to attend school at a foster care residential facility where he was placed against his will by his parents in violation of his constitutional due process rights due to the lack of a review by a neutral fact finder for over seven months cannot be grounds to sustain a person in need of supervision (PINS) proceeding since respondent lacked the requisite intent to be truant from school or to disobey the mandates of article 65 of the Education Law inasmuch as his truancy was a manifestation of his deep-seated desire not to be at the facility, a desire that was never evaluated in a manner consistent with constitutional due process; respondent's sporadic absences from school prior to his placement are insufficient to support a PINS [***3] finding since, in order to sustain a PINS finding on the basis of truancy, there must be a substantial and intentional failure to attend school and such has not been established.

Infants -- Person in Need of Supervision

3. Where a child is placed at a foster care residential treatment center pursuant to a voluntary placement (Social Services Law, § 358-a), it is inappropriate for that facility to suggest the filing of a person in need of supervision petition (Family Ct Act, § 732, subd [a]) because of behavior that presumably led to the child's placement there in the first place since the function of such facilities is to treat such behavior and not complain about it.

 

1802 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. De Shields


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, New York County


115 Misc. 2d 1038; 455 N.Y.S.2d 469; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3811



September 29, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion to dismiss the petition designating him a predicate felon is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss a petition designating him as a predicate felon.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that his conviction for robbery, when he was 18 years old and without any prior felony convictions, was unconstitutional. Specifically, he argued that the sentencing court failed to enter an express finding as to his possible youthful offender status. The state supreme court held that this did not constitute a "constitutional infirmity" for purposes of using such conviction in calculating his subsequent predicate felony status. Defendant further argued that his defense counsel failed to request youthful offender treatment. The court found that his counsel's actions did not violate his Sixth Amendment right to effective assistance where the conviction resulted from an indictment including both robbery in first-degree count and robbery in second-degree count. The court also noted that, at such time, defendant already had a prior record of eight arrests and five misdemeanor convictions. Moreover, the alleged robbery involved the use of a gun, and defendant, under a plea arrangement obtained by counsel, was permitted to plead to a single D felony to cover the indictment and received only a five-year probationary sentence.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Second Felony Offender -- Validity of Predicate Offense

The failure of the sentencing court, in 1974, to enter an express finding regarding defendant's eligibility for youthful offender status does not constitute such constitutional infirmity as would nullify his conviction for purposes of enhancing the penalty upon a subsequent felony conviction ( CPL 400.21, subd 7); furthermore, although defendant had a constitutional right to reasonably competent legal representation, his Sixth Amendment right to counsel was not violated on the predicate charge, since his attorney's failure to request youthful offender status for defendant during plea bargaining negotiations was knowing and strategic, resulting in a lenient sentence under circumstances where youthful offender status was unlikely in light of defendant's prior record.

 

1803 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Burrows v. Board of Assessors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Columbia County


116 Misc. 2d 133; 455 N.Y.S.2d 196; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3847



September 29, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petitions will be granted to the extent stated in this decision, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In separate proceedings under N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law art. 7, petitioners, taxpaying veterans, appealed an assessment of their real property by respondent assessors on the ground it was overvalued by reason of the operation of N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 458(5) as interpreted by the N.Y. Board of Equalization and Assessment. The veterans also sought a declaratory judgment that such statute, as interpreted, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The veterans all qualified for the veterans' exemption under N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 458. Section 458(5)(a) provided an automatic proportionate tax exemption to veterans residing in a tax district where the change to full value assessment was court ordered while § 458(5)(b) gave the same exemption to veterans living in tax districts where the change was voluntary only if the tax district so provided by local law. The Board issued an opinion that § 458(5)(b) operated prospectively and that the only tax districts that could pass local laws giving veterans the same increase in exemption provided for in § 458(5)(a) were those tax districts that changed to full value assessment after a certain date. The court entered judgment in favor of the veterans, declaring unconstitutional as violative of the Equal Protection Clause that portion of § 458(5) that required the passage of a local law in order for some veterans to gain the exemption and directed the assessors to change the veterans' assessments. Applying a rational basis test, the court could not conceive of any justification for distinguishing between veterans based solely upon geographical location.


OUTCOME: The court entered a judgment in favor of the veterans.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection -- Validity of Statute -- Inconsistent Application of Veterans' Real Property Tax Exemption

While section 458 (subd 5, par [a]) of the Real Property Tax Law grants an automatic proportionate tax exemption to veterans residing in a tax district where the change to full value property assessment was court ordered, section 458 (subd 5, par [b]) makes the same exemption for veterans residing in tax districts where the change was voluntary contingent upon the tax district so providing by local law and, thus, is unconstitutional as violative of the equal protection clause since there is no rational basis for such inconsistent application of the exemption to similarly situated veterans and the court cannot conceive of any state of facts that would justify distinguishing between veterans solely upon the basis of whether the tax districts in which they reside went to full value assessment by court order or voluntarily.

 

1804 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Manton v. New York City Bd. of Standards & Appeals


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


117 Misc. 2d 255; 457 N.Y.S.2d 675; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4050



September 24, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The determination of the board of standards and appeals is upheld, and the petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner interested parties appealed a decision by respondent New York City Board of Standards and Appeals (board), which refused to revoke a certificate of occupancy issued by the buildings department to respondent non-profit company, which ran drug rehabilitation facilities. Petitioners claimed that the certificate was contrary to the Zoning Resolution of the City of New York (resolution) and that the resolution was void for vagueness.


OVERVIEW: The buildings department concluded, and the board agreed, that under the broad language of the resolution the premises involved could be operated as a drug-abusers' rehabilitation center as a matter of right, without any need for a special permit from any agency. Petitioners' case was essentially that the resolution was so broad and all-encompassing that it was unconstitutionally void for vagueness and overbreadth, and hence rendered any action taken under it as void. The court affirmed the decision that upheld the resolution and the decision to issue the certificate of occupancy. Petitioners did not have standing to challenge the zoning resolution under a void-for-vagueness argument because they did not claim that they were performing any act regulated by the resolution, and they had no standing to claim that it was void as to third parties by reason of vagueness. Even if they had standing, the resolution was not vague or ambiguous; although the resolution was broad, it was not vague, as those were two separate concepts. Although the statute swept a wide path, it swept it very neatly.


OUTCOME: The determination of the board was upheld, and the petition was dismissed, in petitioners' challenge to the issuance of a certificate of occupancy to a non-profit company that operated drug-abusers' rehabilitation facilities.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Standing to Challenge Statute as Void for Vagueness

1. In a CPLR article 78 proceeding, petitioners lack standing to seek an annulment of the certificate of occupancy granted to respondents for premises to be operated as a drug-abusers' rehabilitation facility on the ground that the language of the Zoning Resolution of the City of New York under which the certificate was granted is so broad as to make it unconstitutionally void for vagueness since such an argument is only available to one who acknowledges that his own conduct falls within the ambit of the statute he attacks, either on its face or as it is interpreted by those charged with its enforcement, and petitioners do not claim that they are performing any act regulated by the statute in question.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Statute Not Void for Vagueness

2. A provision of the Zoning Resolution of the City of New York which prescribes, as a matter of right, the granting of a certificate of occupancy for premises in a specific zoning district if [***2] used as a "philanthropic or non-profit institution * * * with sleeping accommodations" is not unconstitutionally void for vagueness since, although the statute is broad, it is not vague or ambiguous and petitioners have shown no examples where the applicability of the statute was questionable or a matter of fair argument and, in addition, the statute does not vest respondent New York City Board of Standards and Appeals with an overabundance of discretion in granting the certificates of occupancy as an unconstitutionally vague statute would typically do.

 

1805 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Stampler


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Trial Term, Nassau County


115 Misc. 2d 547; 454 N.Y.S.2d 411; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3726



September 13, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] There being no error nor any ground for resentence, the motion is, therefore, denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to set aside his sentence pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.20 as a result of the amendment of N.Y. Penal Law § 70.00.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had been convicted of grand larceny in the third degree and was sentenced over a year later. He was sentenced to four years with no minimum term specified. After his conviction but prior to the sentence, the Legislature amended N.Y. Penal Law § 70.00 to require courts to set minimum sentences instead of the Parole Board, which had occurred in defendant's case. Defendant sought to have his sentence set aside. The court found that, in some cases, defendants had been allowed to have their sentences set aside when the statutory minimum would have been less than that imposed by the Parole Board. However, in defendant's case, the statutory minimum would have been one month longer than the minimum imposed by the Parole Board. Therefore, the court found that it would have been in violation of the ex post facto clause to grant defendant's request. Further, it would not be in defendant's best interest to grant the request.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to set aside his sentence.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sentence -- Minimum Term of Imprisonment

Defendant was properly sentenced to serve an indeterminate term of imprisonment not to exceed four years, with no minimum term specified, although section 70.00 (subd 3, par [b]) of the Penal Law was amended, following his conviction of a class E felony but prior to sentencing, to provide for a minimum term of imprisonment of one year but not more than one third of the maximum imposed, since the statute is silent as to retroactive application and should only be applied retroactively where it ameliorates the sentence; in the instant case, fixing the minimum sentence at one third of the maximum, or 16 months, is a greater punishment than a sentence fixing no minimum, since the Parole Board fixed a minimum of 15 months. In any event, the sentence was reviewed by the Appellate Division after the effective date of the statutory amendment and unanimously affirmed.

 

1806 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


R. v. R.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


115 Misc. 2d 674; 454 N.Y.S.2d 779; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3755



September 10, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff wife sued defendant husband, alleging cruel and inhuman treatment and abandonment. Husband filed a motion to preclude evidence going to the cruelty claim on equitable distribution issues. The wife sought to withdraw her abandonment action so that only the issue of cruelty remained. In addressing the parties' motions, the court focused on the issue of whether to consider marital fault under N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 236(B)(5)(d), cl. 10.


OVERVIEW: In reviewing the parties' equitable distribution, the court held that equitable distribution was premised on a concept of partnership. Because a partner in a business enterprise still received his or her share on dissolution of a partnership, so should a partner in marriage. The court found that the dissolution of the marriage itself acted as relief for the "innocent" partner. Accordingly, the court held that fault was not a consideration under § 236(B)(5)(d), cl. 10, and evidence of fault was inadmissible in the determination of equitable distribution. In so holding, the court concluded that (1) any additional consideration of fault went beyond the reasonable interpretation of the language of § 236(B)(5)(d) cl. 10; (2) consideration of fault caused lengthy, burdensome, excessive, and ultimately inconclusive litigation; and (3) assigning fault resulted in impermissible punishment for protected private activity, or resulted in invidious gender discrimination of a sort the legislature specifically excluded.


OUTCOME: The court granted the husband's motion for summary judgment and his motion to preclude evidence, and denied the wife's motion to withdraw her cause of action for abandonment. The court held that fault could not be considered under the state's equitable distribution law when determining division of the parties' marital property.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Husband and Wife -- Equitable Distribution -- Divorce on Fault Grounds

The "fault" of one spouse may not be considered with respect to equitable distribution of marital property in a matrimonial action under section 236 (part B, subd 5, par d, cl [10]) of the Domestic Relations Law; equitable distribution is premised on the concept of partnership and, as would the partner in a business enterprise who commits adultery, who drinks excessively or, as here, is allegedly homosexual, the "guilty" spouse still receives his or her share of the assets on dissolution of the partnership, with the termination of the marriage itself comprising the relief for the "innocent" spouse. Accordingly, on defendant husband's motion, plaintiff wife is granted a judgment of divorce upon the uncontested ground of abandonment ( CPLR 3212, subd [b]) and plaintiff's motion at trial to withdraw the abandonment cause of action is denied ( CPLR 3217, subd [b]); although plaintiff might have sustained her burden of proof on a second cause of action for cruelty, she would ultimately be entitled to no more than a judgment of divorce and would have gained no further rights or benefits by pursuing said claim,  [***2] since evidence of cruelty would not be admissible upon the only remaining issue, equitable distribution of the parties' marital property.

 

1807 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Eisenberg v. Citation-Langley Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


115 Misc. 2d 650; 454 N.Y.S.2d 627; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3752



September 7, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action to recover moneys claimed due and owing on a promissory note executed by defendant corporation and a guarantee executed by defendant guarantor, plaintiff promisee moved to confirm an order of attachment. The guarantor cross-moved to dismiss the order of attachment.


OVERVIEW: The promisee brought an action to recover moneys claimed due and owing on a promissory note executed by the corporation and a guarantee executed by the guarantor. The issue raised in the case was whether the five-day period to confirm an attachment order pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 6211(b) ran from the date the first levy occurred regardless of whether the garnishee was in possession of the property or debt sought to be attached or if it ran from the date the order was served on a garnishee who was actually in possession of property belonging to the guarantor. The court concluded Rule 6211(b) was to be interpreted to require the promisee to move to confirm the order of attachment within five days from the date the order was served on the first garnishee regardless of whether the garnishee was then holding property belonging to the guarantor. Upon finding that the promisee did not move to confirm the order within five days after the order was first served, his motion to confirm was denied and the guarantor's motion to vacate the order of attachment was granted.


OUTCOME: The court denied the promisee's motion to confirm an order of attachment and granted the guarantor's motion to vacate the order of attachment. The guarantor was also granted the costs and disbursements of the motion.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Attachment -- Ex Parte Order -- Confirmation

The failure of a plaintiff to make a motion on notice to confirm an ex parte order of attachment within five days of levy as required by CPLR 6211 (subd [b]) is fatal and invalidates the order and, furthermore, where an ex parte order of attachment is levied at different times on several garnishees, the five-day period to confirm the order runs from the date of the first levy, regardless of whether the garnishee first served is in possession of the property or debt of the defendant sought to be attached for jurisdictional or security purposes; if the five-day period to confirm did not begin to run until the order was served on a garnishee presently holding property belonging to the debtor defendant, an unknowing debtor defendant could have his assets placed in jeopardy of immediate attachment for an extended period of time ( CPLR 6214, subd [b]) and, knowing that any assets received from the defendant might have to be transferred to the Sheriff, it is unlikely that a garnishee not holding property belonging to defendant would continue to transact business with him once served with the order of attachment, thus depriving the defendant [***2] of his ability to transfer or alienate his property without notice or an opportunity for an immediate hearing, which would undermine the purpose of CPLR 6211 (subd [b]) and jeopardize the constitutional validity of the statute as applied.

 

1808 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


2961 Realty Corp. v. Temporary Com. of Investigation


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


115 Misc. 2d 768; 454 N.Y.S.2d 632; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3769



September 7, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a corporate owner of a building and its vice president (owners), brought a motion to quash subpoenas served by respondent Temporary Commission of Investigation of the State of New York (Commission), or for a declaratory judgment holding (1) 1958 N.Y. Laws, ch. 989 unconstitutional as applied to the owners and (2) that the subpoenas violated their constitutional rights. The commission brought a cross-motion to compel compliance.


OVERVIEW: The commission issued the subpoenas on the owners in connection with an investigation into arson for profit, demanding production of records and the appearance of the vice president. The commission asserted that the fire destroying the corporate property was determined to be arson, and that the ownership interest and mortgage had been omitted from an insurance claim filed by the corporation. The court denied the owner's motion and granted the commission's motion, with the exception of certain documents. The court held that the commission was empowered to conduct the investigation under 1958 N.Y. Laws ch. 989 and N.Y. Const. art. V, § 3. The court found that the owner's due process rights were satisfied by the commission's compliance with N.Y. Civ. Rights Law § 73. The court found that the records sought bore a reasonable relation with the matter under investigation and the public purpose served. The court found that the subpoenas sought relevant information relative to the ownership interest, value and insurance claims, but not relative to corporate minutes, tax returns and financial statements, thus, limited the requested order to compel compliance.


OUTCOME: The court denied the owner's application to quash the subpoenas and granted the commission's cross-motion to compel compliance, limited to certain records.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Witnesses -- Subpoena Duces Tecum -- Temporary State Commission of Investigation

Subpoenas duces tecum were properly served by respondent Temporary State Commission of Investigation on petitioners in connection with an investigation into arson for profit through insurance fraud, requiring attendance at a hearing by the individual petitioner, an officer of petitioner insured corporation, which reported a fire loss alleged to have been caused by arson and inaccurately represented in the sworn proof of loss statement that it was the owner in fee and that no other person had any interest in or incumbrance on the premises, and directed the production of records by petitioner corporation and its predecessor in interest in the premises; the commission acted within its powers (L 1958, ch 989, § 2, subds 1, 11) and petitioners' due process rights were satisfied since the commission complied with section 73 of the Civil Rights Law by providing a "general statement of the subject of the investigation" in its subpoenas. Accordingly, petitioners' application to quash the subpoenas in their entirety is denied; however, to the extent that the subpoenas request production of books and records [***2] such as corporate minutes, tax returns and financial statements that are not presently relevant to the investigation, they are too far-reaching and those items are deleted and need not be furnished by petitioners.

 

1809 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Shore Terrace Realty Associates v. Smosna


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Queens County


115 Misc. 2d 581; 454 N.Y.S.2d 507; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3736



September 3, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord filed suit against respondent, a rent-controlled tenant, when the tenant did not remit the increased monthly rental amount after the landlord's building had been moderately rehabilitated and for which an increased initial rent was set by the New York City Department of Housing Preservation and Development (D.H.P.D.) pursuant to N.Y. Priv. Hous. Fin. Law § 608.


OVERVIEW: The landlord owned a multiple dwelling building. The rent-controlled tenant executed a renewal lease for three years. During the lease, the landlord entered into a modest rehabilitation contract with D.H.P.D. The tenant remained in his apartment during the rehabilitation project. Under the newly-enacted § 608 and after the rehabilitation project was completed, D.H.P.D. set the initial rent that the landlord could charge. The landlord filed a petition against the tenant when the tenant remitted the rental amount specified under his lease. The initial rent increase was three times the amount of the tenant's rent. The court noted that there was no caselaw interpreting the application of a rent increase to a pre-existing lease. It held that the initial rent increase granted by D.H.P.D. to the landlord did not affect existing leases. It found the tenant's rent remained the same amount found in the unexpired lease agreement. The increase was to be held in abeyance until the lease expired, at which time the landlord could offer the tenant a new one or two-year lease with the new rental amount specified in it.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Rent Increase Following Moderate Rehabilitation of Rent-Stabilized Apartments

 Section 608 of the Private Housing Finance Law, which provides limited authority to the New York City Department of Housing Preservation and Development (D.H.P.D.) to establish an "initial rent" upon completion of the moderate rehabilitation of housing units in multiple dwellings pursuant to certain Federal housing assistance programs, does not authorize D.H.P.D. to increase the rents of rent-stabilized tenants in possession pursuant to an unexpired lease.

 

1810 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Franza v. Carey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, New York County


115 Misc. 2d 882; 454 N.Y.S.2d 1002; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3787



August 24, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state officials filed a motion to dismiss the action filed by plaintiff novelty store owners, who sought a preliminary injunction to prevent the enforcement of the Head Shop Act, N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law art. 39. The state officials alleged that the complaint was barred by res judicata and failed to state a cause of action.


OVERVIEW: The store owners sold smoking accessories that could be considered drug paraphernalia. Although the store owners had filed an action challenging the constitutionality of art. 39 in federal court, the parties vacated the federal court order and dismissed the action by stipulation. Therefore, the court held the state action was not barred by res judicata. The store owners also stopped selling single-use items after a state court decision that those items were validly prohibited under an ordinance with provisions similar to art. 39. The only issue remaining was the application of art. 39 to multi-use items. The court held that art. 39 was constitutional under both the federal and New York Constitutions, because it could be applied validly to the sale and display of single-use items and, under the court's construction, it mandated post-seizure hearing and notice. The court granted the store owners' request for a preliminary injunction regarding the sale and display of multi-use items and ordered an expeditious trial of the factual issues involved.


OUTCOME: The court denied the state officials' motion to dismiss and granted the store owners' application for a preliminary injunction on the condition that the store owners furnished an undertaking and filed a note of issue.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- "Head Shop Act"

Article 39 of the General Business Law, the so-called "Head Shop Act" which prohibits the possession and sale of drug-related paraphernalia, authorizes the seizure and forfeiture of such goods and the revocation of retail licenses and permits and provides injunctive and civil penalties for violations thereof, is constitutional under both State and Federal law, since it can be validly applied to the sale and display of items used solely for drug-related purposes and sold with the knowledge and intent that they will be so used, and since the seizure provisions must be construed to mandate postseizure hearing and notice. However, an application by plaintiff boutique and novelty shop owners, who sell and display only multiuse items, for a preliminary injunction barring enforcement of the statute pending a trial on the issue of the constitutionality of the statute as applied to the sale of items suitable for legal or illegal purposes, is granted; plaintiffs are not barred herein from litigating claims which were raised or which could have been raised in a prior Federal action, since said action terminated in a stipulation [***2] of dismissal and was not a final judgment against plaintiffs on the merits.

 

1811 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lew


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Greene County


115 Misc. 2d 421; 454 N.Y.S.2d 225; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3703



August 19, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendant's conviction is set aside and the charge dismissed because of the constitutional infirmity of subdivision 30 of section 375 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction in the Town Court of the Town of Catskill (New York) for a violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 375(30), which prohibited the obstruction of the view through a windshield, on the ground that the statute was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for a violation of § 375(30) based upon the presence of a radar detector on his front windshield. The radar detector clearly obstructed only the driver's view of an area of the auto's hood. The State asserted that § 375(30) forbade any obstruction of the windshield. At trial, the arresting officer testified that he had interpreted the law as having been violated if the object was of sufficient size to obstruct vision through the windshield and the road ahead. Objects such as Kleenex boxes, dice from the mirror, cigarettes, sun glasses, gum, candy, pencils, and toll receipts did not violate the law. The court held that the statute was unconstitutional. Although it specified that the window obstruction had to have prevented a full and clear view of the road and condition of the traffic behind the vehicle, it contained no such standard for the more important view to the front. The arresting officer had added his own interpretation of the statute with an intent to arrest defendant for having a radar detector. Because of the lack of a valid standard, the statute was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court set aside defendant's conviction and dismissed the charge against him.


HEADNOTES

Motor Vehicles -- Obstruction of Driver's View -- Radar Detector

A driver of a car equipped with a radar detector may not be convicted under subdivision 30 of section 375 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law for obstructing the view through the vehicle's windshield since no standard exists under the statute for determination of what constitutes an obstruction and the statute is therefore unconstitutional.

 

1812 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Barone v. Carey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


113 Misc. 2d 711; 453 N.Y.S.2d 596; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3367



August 17, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a county political party chairman and a candidate, filed an action against defendants, the Governor of the State of New York, the New York State Board of Elections, and another, seeking a declaratory judgment that N.Y. Elec. Law § 8-100(2) was unconstitutional and requesting an order directing the Board to promulgate rules and regulations establishing polling hours throughout the State of New York. The court rendered a decision.


OVERVIEW: Section 8-100(2) provided, in part, that the polls would be open for voting in State primary elections between noon and 9:00 p.m. in all counties except in the City of New York and selected urban counties, where the polls would be open between 6:00 a.m. and 9:00 p.m. Plaintiffs contended, among other things, that § 8-100(2) denied them the equal protection of the laws guaranteed by the Fourteenth Amendment and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11 and requested an order directing the Board to promulgate rules and regulations establishing the polling hours in all election districts throughout the State from 6:00 a.m. to 9:00 p.m. The court ruled that the portion of § 8-100(2) relating to primary polling hours could not pass constitutional muster even under the least exacting standard of constitutional scrutiny because defendants failed to demonstrate any rational basis for differentiating generally between polling hours for primary and general elections or specifically between expanding primary polling hours for some counties but not others. The scheme of § 8-100(2) was arbitrary, not uniform, and not supported by any rational basis.


OUTCOME: The court granted the relief requested in the complaint to the extent that it sought a declaration that the provision of the New York Election Law setting the times for primary elections was unconstitutional. The court denied the requested relief in all other respects.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Elections -- Primary Elections -- Polling Hours

Subdivision 2 of section 8-100 of the Election Law, which provides that polls shall be open for voting in State primary elections between noon and 9:00 p.m. in all counties except in the City of New York and the Counties of Nassau, Suffolk, Westchester, Rockland and Erie where the polls shall be open between 6:00 a.m. and 9:00 p.m., and which also provides that for general, special, and other elections, the polls shall be open between 6:00 a.m. and 9:00 p.m., is unconstitutional insofar as it gives preference to primary election voters in selected urban counties over those in rural counties without any rational basis for doing so. Accordingly, in view of the unconstitutionality of the statute regarding the times for polling hours in primary elections, such hours shall be consistent with the remaining portion of the statute which provides that in a general, special and "every other election" polling hours shall be from 6:00 a.m. to 9:00 p.m.

 

1813 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


St. Luke's German Evangelical Lutheran Church v. Rochester


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


115 Misc. 2d 199; 453 N.Y.S.2d 1012; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3660



August 17, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] There being no factual issues requiring a trial, the motion of defendants for summary judgment is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, city and corporation, filed a motion for summary judgment in connection with plaintiff church's action to declare an ordinance abandoning a public street unlawful and to enjoin its implementation.


OVERVIEW: The city abandoned a portion of a public street in connection with a plan to promote the expansion of the corporation's business. The church, whose parking lot abutted the abandoned street, claimed that the ordinance was invalid. The court granted summary judgment to the city and corporation, holding that the city provided the appropriate notice for amending its official map under N.Y. Gen. City Law § 29 and that there was no requirement that the church be directly notified. Further, § 29 authorized the city to close existing streets whenever and as often as it might deem it for the public interest, and there was evidence supporting the city's conclusion that closing the street would promote the expansion of business and job opportunities, increase the city's tax base, and lead to an improvement of traffic conditions. The abandonment did not deprive church members of suitable access to the church because its parking lot could still be entered from another street. The fact that access was made more circuitous by an additional four tenths of a mile, upon paved city streets, did not render such access unsuitable.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion of the city and the corporation for summary judgment on the church's action to declare an ordinance abandoning a public street unlawful and to enjoin its implementation.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Law of the Case

1. A decision denying plaintiff's motion for a preliminary injunction prohibiting implementation of a challenged city ordinance is not binding under the law of the case doctrine when the merits of the action are subsequently reached.

Municipal Corporations -- Planning -- Amendment of City Map

2. A city ordinance abandoning a portion of a public street to the private use of defendant commercial enterprise is not invalid by reason of the city's failure to notify plaintiff neighboring property owner of the procedural steps taken to approve defendant's application, since the city complied with the requirement of section 29 of the General City Law and the city code that notice of a public hearing regarding a change in the city map be published once, five days in advance of the hearing; furthermore, although it may be the practice of the city planning commission to notify affected landowners of such proposed changes in the official map when the commission meets to report to the council, that practice is not required by law and the commission's [***2] failure to notify plaintiff, whether inadvertent or not, does not affect the city's action.

Municipal Corporations -- Planning -- Abandonment of Public Street to Private Use

3. An action by plaintiff church to declare unlawful a city ordinance abandoning a portion of a public street to the private use of defendant neighboring property owner, a commercial enterprise, thus eliminating or reducing one avenue of access to plaintiff's parking lot while allowing for enlargement of defendant's facility, is dismissed; the city is authorized to close streets for public or municipal purposes, including "the promotion, creation, development or expansion of business, commerce, industry or job opportunities" (General City Law, §§ 21, 29) and, although access may now be somewhat more circuitous, the members of plaintiff's congregation have not been deprived of suitable access to the property.

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Religion

4. The constitutional right to freely practice one's religion is not violated when a general law, secular in purpose, incidentally burdens one's religious practices. Accordingly, a city ordinance abandoning a portion of a public street to the [***3] private use of defendant commercial enterprise, thus allowing defendant to enlarge its facility and reducing or eliminating one of the two means of access to the parking lot of plaintiff church, a neighboring landowner, does not unconstitutionally inhibit the rights of plaintiff's congregants to freely practice their religion.

 

1814 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Lai Chun Chan Jin v. Board of Estimate


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


115 Misc. 2d 774; 454 N.Y.S.2d 601; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3770



August 4, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the reasons set forth above, the actions of the Board of Estimate in amending the New York City Zoning Resolution and in amending the zoning map are declared to be illegal, contrary to the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution, and void.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners brought an action under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 against respondents, the Board of Estimate of the City of New York (New York), a corporation, and another party, seeking judicial review of the Board's actions in amending the New York City Zoning Resolution and in amending the zoning map. The court rendered a decision.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners sought review of the Board's actions creating a special zoning district and amending the zoning map to delineate the district's boundaries. They argued that the form of notice given to the public by the Board violated the due process requirements of the Fourteenth Amendment. The court stated that a merely formal notice, not calculated to reach the members of the community affected, did not fulfill the legislative intent behind New York City, N.Y., Charter § 197-c. Publication in obscure publications much more likely to be read by some lawyers, professional planners, and real estate developers than by the man or woman in the street, completely failed to provide adequate notice to the community. If residents of a local community were to be put on notice, the notice ought to have been placed in a newspaper of general circulation within the community. Because of inadequate notice, the Board's actions in amending the zoning map and in granting a special permit could not stand. It was constitutionally impermissible to grant by law a right to be heard at a public hearing on the one hand, and on the other to adopt a notice procedure that rendered that right utterly meaningless.


OUTCOME: The court declared the Board's actions in amending the New York City Zoning Resolution and in amending the zoning map to be illegal, contrary to the Fourteenth Amendment, and void.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Planning -- Concurrent Judicial and Administrative Jurisdiction

1. A motion to adjourn an article 78 proceeding to review the New York City Board of Estimate's approval of a special permit allowing respondent to proceed with a proposed project pending a final determination by the New York City Planning Commission as to whether the permit should be rescinded is denied since serious issues of notice, due process and abuse of discretion would not be rendered moot despite administrative rescission of the permit; furthermore, although petitioners challenge the constitutionality of certain legislative acts in the context of an article 78 proceeding, the court can treat those portions of the petition as requests for declaratory relief ( CPLR 103, subd [c]).

Municipal Corporations -- Planning -- Notice of Hearing

2. The New York City Planning Commission's notice of hearing regulations,  [***2] which provide that notice to the public of land use proposals or applications before the Planning Commission shall consist of 10 days' publication in the City Record and publication in the Comprehensive City Planning Calendar, violate the due process clause of the United States Constitution and frustrate the public hearing requirements of section 197-c of the New York City Charter since the notice was not calculated to reach the members of the community affected; accordingly, where the affected community is Chinatown, due process requires that notice be given in both an English and Chinese-language newspaper of general circulation in the community; furthermore, although section 200 of the New York City Charter requires only 10 days' publication in the City Record prior to the Planning Commission's adoption of a resolution, in order to preserve the constitutionality of this enactment, publication in the City Record must be interpreted as being additional to the notice reasonably calculated to inform the community.

 

1815 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Corning v. Board of Elections


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


113 Misc. 2d 707; 453 N.Y.S.2d 364; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3366



August 2, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner voters brought an action seeking to have N.Y. Elec. Law § 8-100(2) declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Section 8-100(2) contained provisions that allowed for the extension of polling hours in some areas but not in others. The voters brought an action challenging the statute as violative of equal protection, and the court declared the provisions extending the polling hours to be unconstitutional. The court held that the provisions struck at the very heart of a person's voting rights because every person should have been entitled to the same opportunity to vote. The state failed to meet its burden of showing a justification for the extensions, which presented the danger of discouraging voter turnout. However, because only the extension provisions were unconstitutional, the proper remedy was to strike only those provisions from § 8-100(2).


OUTCOME: The court granted the petition only with respect to provisions that extended extra voting privileges to citizens in certain geographic areas while withholding it in others.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Elections -- Primary Elections -- Polling Hours

The extension of extra polling hours set forth in subdivision 2 of section 8-100 of the Election Law (as amd by L 1982, ch 97), wherein it is provided that polling places shall be open in a primary election from 12 noon until 9 o'clock in the evening, except for the City of New York and the Counties of Nassau, Suffolk, Westchester, Rockland and Erie, where polling places are required to be open from 6:00 a.m. until 9:00 p.m., is unconstitutional, since no rational basis or compelling State interest exists to justify the different polling times from one county to another. Fixing hours for voting is a legislative task and, as such, the court cannot extend the voting hours in the three counties represented by petitioners; the only alternative is to excise the unconstitutional exception in the statute, thereby leaving all polling places open from noon until 9:00 p.m.

 

1816 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Citibank, N.A. v. Gonzalez


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Bronx County


114 Misc. 2d 1007; 452 N.Y.S.2d 1012; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3602



July 14, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant borrower filed a motion, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 8501(2), to require plaintiff national bank to post security for litigation costs on the grounds that it was a nonresident corporation. The bank sought to recover for monies advanced to the borrower for credit card purchases made by the borrower and/or his designees.


OVERVIEW: The borrower contended that the bank was a nonresident corporation within the meaning of § 8501(a). The bank asserted that it was exempt from the requirements of § 8501(a) and that the application of § 8501(a) against it was unconstitutional. The court noted that the bank removed itself from the State to South Dakota for the purpose of charging higher interest rates to its customers than was permitted by New York law. Although Congress had the authority to regulate all national banks, it had not preempted the application of State law to national banks to exempt them from § 8501(a). The National Bank Act, 12 U.S.C.S § 24, empowered the bank to sue in the New York but did not mandate the bank's exemption from N.Y. C.P.L.R. § 8501(a). There was no federal or State legislative intention to exempt the bank from § 8501. The filing of security costs under § 8501(a) did not expressly conflict with federal law, frustrate the bank's purpose, or impair the efficiency of the bank's discharge of its federal duties. The bank had voluntarily chosen nonresident status and was not permitted to circumvent the constitutional provisions of § 8501(a).


OUTCOME: The court granted the borrower's motion to require the bank to post security for litigation costs. The bank was ordered to post an amount for security.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Banks and Banking -- National Banks -- State Regulation

Plaintiff, a national banking association based in South Dakota which is not licensed to do business in New York, must post security for costs as a nonresident plaintiff pursuant to CPLR 8501 (subd [a]) upon defendant's motion, since national banks are subject to State law unless the State law expressly conflicts with Federal law or infringes upon national banking laws or imposes an undue burden upon the performance of the national banks' functions, factors which are not presented herein; specifically, that provision of the National Bank Act which empowers a national bank to sue in the State courts as fully as a natural person could (US Code, tit 12, § 24), does not exempt plaintiff from the requirement that it post security for costs under CPLR 8501 (subd [a]), and the mandatory provisions of said State statute do not constitute an undue restraint on interstate commerce.

 

1817 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ingham


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


CITY COURT OF NEW YORK, ROCHESTER


115 Misc. 2d 64; 453 N.Y.S.2d 325; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3630



July 14, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The court sentences the defendant to a conditional discharge of one year.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The matter was before the court for sentencing pursuant to defendant's plea of guilty to a violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1192(3), operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of alcohol. The court was required to impose sentence under § 1192(5), as amended by 1981 N.Y. Laws 910.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of alcohol. Section 1192(5), as amended by 1981 N.Y. Laws 910, required the court to either impose a jail sentence or to fine defendant a minimum of $ 350. The court sentenced defendant to a conditional discharge of one year, finding that the statutory provision was unconstitutional as applied to this particular defendant only. The court held that the facts of the case did not justify a term of imprisonment, and the minimum fine was excessive due to defendant's indigence. The court held that one of the meanings of excessiveness was a relative comparison between the amount of the fine and the wealth of the defendant.


OUTCOME: The court sentenced defendant to a conditional discharge of one year.


HEADNOTES

Motor Vehicles -- Driving while Intoxicated -- Constitutionality of Minimum $ 350 Fine

The statute mandating the imposition of a minimum $ 350 fine upon a conviction for driving while intoxicated (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 1192, subd 5) is violative of the constitutional prohibition against "excessive fines" (US Const, 8th Amdt; NY Const, art I, § 5) as applied to defendant, who has no economic resources presently available, or in reasonably near expectancy, with which to pay the $ 350 fine mandated by law (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 1192, subd 5); excessiveness within the meaning of the constitutional provisions is gauged by a relative comparison between the amount of the fine and the wealth of the defendant, and, even though a defendant can be resentenced by proving his inability to pay a fine ( CPL 420.10), the constitutional provisions require that the question of excessiveness be answered before or during the imposition of sentence, not afterward.

 

1818 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Davis


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Onondaga County


114 Misc. 2d 1002; 452 N.Y.S.2d 1007; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3601



July 13, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated cases, petitioners, a guardian and parents, sought approval of their petition for voluntary placement of their children and challenged the requirement of respondent, a county social services department, that the parents voluntarily agree to relinquish custody of their handicapped children before the department would provide appropriate services under N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law §§ 358-a; 384-a.


OVERVIEW: One of the children had a guardian. The parents and the guardian filed separate actions to require the department to furnish services to the handicapped children without termination of their custody of the children. The department failed to answer. The court consolidated the actions. A prior federal court had held in a class action on behalf of all handicapped children that N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law §§ 358-a; 384-a violated § 504 of the Rehabilitation Act, codified as 29 U.S.C.S. § 794, and U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court found no evidence that the federal case was not still good law. Therefore, the children were members of the class, and N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law §§ 358-a; 384-a were without effect. Therefore, the department had no statutory authority to require the transfer of the children's custody as a prerequisite to provide services.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petition and ordered that the parents and guardian retain custody of the children.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Social Services -- Residential Treatment of Handicapped Children -- Surrender of Custody as Prerequisite

Sections 358-a and 384-a of the Social Services Law violate the Federal Rehabilitation Act (US Code, tit 29, § 794) as well as the Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution in that New York's statutory scheme discriminates against handicapped children by requiring their parents to relinquish custody in order to receive residential rehabilitative services for their children. Accordingly, since it is in the best interests of the handicapped children before the court that they receive residential treatment and since the Department of Social Services is required to provide residential and rehabilitative services for the children, voluntary placement of the children with the department is approved, with custody of the children to remain in their parents.

 

1819 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


C. on behalf of C. v. G.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Queens County


114 Misc. 2d 764; 452 N.Y.S.2d 523; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3562



June 25, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court was a paternity proceeding instituted by petitioner, a child's maternal grandmother, against respondent, the alleged father, to have the alleged father declared to be the father of the child. The child's mother had died. Respondent, the named father on the birth certificate, was joined as a necessary party. Both respondents acknowledged the child.


OVERVIEW: The grandmother also filed a petition for custody against the named father, who was directed to file a custody proceeding since the mother was deceased. Therefore, the named father filed a petition against the grandmother. The named father then moved to dismiss the paternity petition on the grounds that the action had abated by reason of the mother's death. The court found, however, that it had the statutory authority to proceed upon the death of the mother by virtue of N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 522, which authorized the guardian or next of kin to initiate a paternity action on behalf of the child. To accept the named father's construction of N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 518 to require the abatement of the action because of the mother's death would have run counter to United States Supreme Court holdings, and to hold that the child's right to status or to support abated and that his grandmother, as his next of kin who had the responsibility for his care, could not file the action less than a year after the death of the mother would have been unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that the paternity proceeding continue to trial.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Children Born out of Wedlock -- Filiation Proceeding -- Death of Mother

A paternity proceeding instituted by the maternal grandmother of a child born out of wedlock after the child's mother died, may proceed to trial in the Family Court (Family Ct Act, § 518), where the petition has been amended to add as a second respondent the man who was named as the child's father on the birth certificate and who commenced a proceeding to regain custody of the child after the mother's death; the proceeding may be construed as an action by the child against the two named respondents (Family Ct Act, § 522) and may continue unabated despite the death of the mother, all necessary parties being before the court.

 

1820 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Ribotsky v. Lupkin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


114 Misc. 2d 913; 452 N.Y.S.2d 806; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3586



June 22, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner marshal challenged the application of a directive which prevented any former marshal convicted of a crime related to the performance of official duties from being employed by any present marshal when the employment had a direct relationship to the criminal offense for which the former marshal was convicted. The marshal had been terminated from office after he was convicted of certain crimes relating to official duties.


OVERVIEW: The marshal began working for another city marshal sometime after his removal from office. Respondent department alleged that the marshal's new duties went beyond clerical. The marshal alleged that the directive was unfair and its application to him was unreasonable. The court held its final determination in abeyance until a referee reported the acts that constituted the marshal's offenses and the nature of his new duties. The court held that the department was empowered to issue the directive and the directive itself was neither arbitrary nor capricious. The court determined that the directive did not violate equal protection when applied to the marshal because he forfeited his right to hold such an office and did not have a right to be employed in the specific position from which he had been lawfully removed. The court concluded, however, that without information as to the underlying acts and the nature of the marshal's duties, it could not determine whether the department had acted arbitrarily and capriciously.


OUTCOME: The final determination was held in abeyance pending submission of a report by a referee as to the underlying acts claimed to have been engaged in by the marshal that constituted the offenses and the exact nature of his duties in his new position.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Public Officers -- City Marshals -- Prohibition against Rehiring Convicted Marshals

A directive issued by respondent Department of Investigation of the City of New York and department officials, which provides that "any former Marshal who has been convicted of a crime related to the performance of his/her official duties may not be employed by any present City Marshal when such employment bears a direct relationship to the criminal offense for which the former marshal was convicted", is constitutional. Moreover, the directive is constitutional as applied to petitioner, an ex-city marshal currently employed in a marshal's office who was convicted of "racketeering-based" crimes said to have been committed while he was employed as a city marshal and in connection with his official duties; however, a final determination in the proceeding to compel respondents to vacate their determination prohibiting petitioner's employment is held in abeyance pending submission of a report by a referee to establish the duties presently performed by petitioner and whether they bear a "direct relationship" to the acts underlying petitioner's conviction.

 

1821 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Lasky v. Van Lindt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


115 Misc. 2d 259; 453 N.Y.S.2d 983; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3670



June 21, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the petition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner applicant sought to annul the determination of respondent board that denied the applicant a license to become a thoroughbred racehorse owner. The applicant also sought to compel the board to issue him a license.


OVERVIEW: The board unanimously determined to deny the applicant a license to become a thoroughbred racehorse owner based upon its conclusion that the applicant was a tout. The board viewed materials published by the applicant and concluded that his publications tended to create the impression that he had inside information as to which horses would win particular races. Thus, the board found that the applicant was not entitled to a license pursuant to the provisions of 1951 N.Y. Laws ch. 324, § 4. In denying the applicant's petition to annul the board's decision, the court held that the board's conclusion that the applicant was a tout, as that term was defined in the context of racing, and that his activities were not in the best interests of racing was neither arbitrary, capricious, nor an abuse of discretion.


OUTCOME: The court denied the applicant's petition.


HEADNOTES

Horse Racing -- Licensing Racehorse Owners -- Denial of License to Professional "Tout"

Respondent State Racing and Wagering Board did not act arbitrarily or capriciously in denying petitioner's application for a license as a thoroughbred racehorse owner on the ground that he is a professional "tout" (L 1951, ch 324, § 4, as amd) whose activities are not in the best interests of horse racing since a "tout" is generally defined as a person who purports to sell inside information on racehorses and their prospects to bettors and petitioner's publications tend to create the impression that he has inside information as to which horses will win particular races; the State has a vital interest in protecting the integrity of horse racing, the only sport in which wagering is legal, and the board's denial of petitioner's license is consistent with its responsibilities to the sport, the public and the State; the statute authorizing the denial of a license to a "tout" is not unconstitutionally overbroad, vague and irrational since the statute clearly gives adequate warning of the type of conduct prohibited and owning, training and racing [***2] horses, not being one of life's "common occupations", may be subject to the strictest type of regulation; since there is no absolute right to a racehorse owner's license, petitioner has no "claim of entitlement" that would require the board to grant him a hearing.

 

1822 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Bay Ridge Community Council v. Carey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


115 Misc. 2d 433; 454 N.Y.S.2d 186; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3705



June 21, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion for discovery by petitioner Le Blanc is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Two consolidated actions and a separate action were before the court in a special proceeding in which petitioners, a community council and two individuals, challenged the reapportionment plan found in 1982 N.Y. Laws 111, as amended, and sought injunctive relief prohibiting respondents, New York State Governor, Attorney-General, and others, from implementing the plan. Respondents cross-moved to dismiss the petitions or for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners challenged the constitutionality of the reapportionment plan found in 1982 N.Y. Laws 111, and sought injunctive relief. Respondents filed cross motions for dismissal or for summary judgment. The court held that the challenges were justiciable because pursuant to N.Y. Const. art. III, § 5 and 1911 N.Y. Laws 773, § 1 an apportionment by the legislature was subject to immediate judicial review. The court granted summary judgment for respondents, holding that 1982 N.Y. Laws 111 was in accord with all of the subsisting provisions of N.Y. Const. art. III, and thus was a valid and constitutional apportionment statute. The court rejected petitioners' contention that the oddly shaped designs of the reapportionment plan violated the State Constitution's antigerrymander provisions, which required that legislative districts were "compact", "contiguous," and "convenient." Petitioners' challenge on the ground of "partisan gerrymandering," raised a "nonjusticiable issue." Petitioners did not contest that the plan complied with the overriding principle of "one person, one vote." Moreover, the court noted that with respect to Assembly districts there was no county line requirement.


OUTCOME: The court granted a summary judgment in respondent's favor, declaring and acknowledging that the law at issue was a valid and constitutional apportionment statute.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Reapportionment of Legislature -- Judicial Review

1. A reapportionment plan for State legislative districts (L 1982, ch 111, as amd) is subject to immediate judicial review (NY Const, art III, § 5) and the fact any reapportionment plan affecting Kings County must be submitted to the United States Attorney General for approval under the Voting Rights Act of 1965 does not make a challenge to the reapportionment plan premature and thus nonjusticiable; a judicial review of a State reapportionment statute is not a contest to select the "best" reapportionment but is limited to the sole question as to whether the statute passes constitutional muster with due regard to the rule that a presumption of constitutionality attaches to every statute.

Constitutional Law -- Reapportionment of Legislature -- "Partisan Gerrymandering"

2. The reapportionment plan for State legislative districts (L 1982, ch 111, as amd) which divides the four Assembly districts in the Bay Ridge area in Kings County into various elongated and disfigured shapes and segments thereby allegedly diffusing the political [***2] power of the community, is not violative of the State Constitution's antigerrymander provisions requiring that legislative districts be "compact", "contiguous" and "convenient" (NY Const, art III, § 5) since the districts are "contiguous" inasmuch as it is not necessary to travel through an adjoining district to keep within the boundaries of any district in the plan and geometric nicety of design must give way to the overriding goal of population equality leaving the Legislature with discretion to determine the degree of compactness which is possible in the total representation picture; a challenge to the constitutionality of the reapportionment plan upon the ground of partisan political gerrymandering raises a "nonjusticiable issue" which courts decline to consider and even assuming that a claim of partisan gerrymandering presents a justiciable issue, petitioners' unsupported allegations are insufficient to sustain their burden of proof on this issue nor have petitioners adduced any proof to sustain the allegation of racial gerrymandering; the legislation is not unconstitutional for separating Hamilton and Fulton Counties into separate Assembly districts.

 

1823 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re H.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Queens County


114 Misc. 2d 475; 451 N.Y.S.2d 983; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3500



June 16, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] As to the allegation of child abuse, the court, therefore, finds that the clear and convincing standard must be applied and accordingly, the petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner society for prevention of cruelty to children alleged that respondent father had placed his child in imminent danger in that he touched and rubbed his child's genitals with his hands, frequently beat and pushed his other child leaving contusions about her body, and respondent father drank to an excess and took valium.


OVERVIEW: The mother testified that the father sexually abused their four year old daughter. She testified that the four year old described acts of sexual abuse in age appropriate language. Additionally, the mother testified that the father had continuously beaten the children. The father categorically denied all allegations. The court found the child protection proceeding was complicated by a custody/visitation petition also pending before the court and a divorce action pending in the supreme court. The court held that the clear and convincing standard applied and dismissed the petition.Both parties appeared to the court to be devoted parents who had the welfare of their children (as each separately perceived it) as their primary concern. The court observed that an older child who testified appeared already to have been seriously affected by the "battling" between the parents. The court concluded that a clear and convincing burden of proof satisfied the "governmental interest" in a neglect petition, which lacked the criminal aspects, the clear threat of termination, and the stigma which attached when the more serious finding of child abuse was made.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition for child abuse against the father. The society failed to show by clear and convincing evidence that the children were abused.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child -- Abused or Neglected Child -- Standard of Proof

The standard of proof in a child abuse proceeding is clear and convincing proof (see Santosky v Kramer, 455 U.S. 745) rather than a preponderance of the evidence (Family Ct Act, § 1046, subd [b]). The private interests affected by the proceeding, which include the stigma of child abuse, possible criminal prosecutions and possible termination of parental rights; the risk of error created by the State's chosen procedure, and the countervailing governmental interest supporting use of the challenged procedure require application of the clear and convincing standard of proof.

 

1824 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Muller v. Muller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


116 Misc. 2d 660; 456 N.Y.S.2d 918; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3938



June 16, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff husband filed a divorce action, which he later withdrew, against defendant wife who filed a counterclaim to impress a trust on the husband's interest in a closed corporation. The wife filed a second action, in which she sought a divorce and the equitable distribution of marital property.


OVERVIEW: The court awarded the wife a judgment of divorce. The primary issue that remained was the valuation of the husband's interest in a closed corporation. The marriage by itself did not provide a basis for an implied promise that would support a constructive trust, and the wife's action was barred by the statute of limitations. The court found that N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law §§ 236(B)(5)(d) and (6)(a) were not unconstitutionally vague. The court found that the effective date for evaluation of marital property was the date the husband began his divorce action. The method of valuation found in the Rev. Rul. 59-60, 1959 C.B. 237 (1959) was the most comprehensive method to value the closed corporation. After analyzing the 8 enumerated factors in the regulation, the court determined that the wife was entitled to 35 percent of the husband's interest, which the court awarded as a distributive award.


OUTCOME: The court granted the divorce to the wife and awarded her 35 percent of the husband's interest in the closed corporation as a distributive award.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Trusts -- Constructive Trust -- Matrimonial Actions

1. Defendant wife has failed to establish her right to a constructive trust on plaintiff husband's interest in a closely held corporation, a claim she interposed in the parties' divorce action; defendant is unable to sustain her burden of proof, as there is no evidence of a promise, or any testimony of a reliance on any promise implied or otherwise, and the marriage of the wife and husband does not by itself provide a basis for an implied promise which would support the imposition of a constructive trust. Furthermore, the action is barred by the six-year Statute of Limitations pursuant to CPLR 213, as the statute runs from the commission, rather than the discovery, of the alleged fraud in an action to impose a constructive trust.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Equitable Distribution Law

2. Under section 236 (part B, subd 5, par d; subd 6, par a) of the Domestic Relations Law, which sets out nine specific factors to be considered by the court in making awards for equitable distribution of marital property and maintenance for a spouse, the tenth "catch-all" provision, which states that the court shall [***2] consider "any other factor which the court shall expressly find to be just and proper", is not unconstitutional for vagueness.

Husband and Wife -- Equitable Distribution -- Date for Valuing Marital Property

3. The date on which to base valuation of marital property under the Equitable Distribution Law is upon "the execution of a separation agreement or the commencement of a matrimonial action" (Domestic Relations Law, § 236, part B, subd 1, par c); thus, where the parties never executed a separation agreement and the only prior court proceeding was a Family Court proceeding instituted by the wife for support, the commencement of the husband's action for divorce in February of 1979 is the date on which to base valuation of marital property, notwithstanding the withdrawal without prejudice of the husband's action at the trial of the wife's divorce action, which she commenced in August of 1980. Accordingly, the husband's share of certain rental property, acquired after the date he commenced his action for divorce, is not marital property; however, said property is considered by the court in connection with the husband's ability to pay maintenance and child support.

 [***3] Husband and Wife -- Equitable Distribution -- Method for Valuing Marital Property

4. In valuing one spouse's interest in a closely held corporation as marital property for purposes of equitable distribution in a matrimonial action, the terms of a stockholder buy-out agreement are not binding with respect to valuation, although such an agreement is properly considered by the court.

Husband and Wife -- Equitable Distribution -- Method for Valuing Marital Property

5. The court valued the husband's interest in a closely held corporation as marital property for purposes of equitable distribution in a divorce action (Domestic Relations Law, § 236, part B, subd 1, par c) utilizingRevenue Ruling 59-60 of the Internal Revenue Service to capitalize earnings, with certain adjustments: (1) the weighted average income of the company over the five years preceding the valuation date was arrived at after the income reported for each year was increased by 13% to reflect the percentage of variance between actual and reported profit determined by the court after certain corporate business deductions were disallowed as unverified in the most recently reported tax year, and (2) an earning [***4] power multiplicant of 6, rather than 8 as proposed by the wife, was applied to the resulting average weighted income to reflect a lower capitalization rate appropriate to the "key man" character of the husband's business. Furthermore, the wife was awarded 35% of the value of her husband's share of the business as a distributive award, based on her contributions to her husband's advancement in his business in her role of spouse, mother and helpmate, according to a schedule of payments.

 

1825 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Green


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, New York County


114 Misc. 2d 339; 451 N.Y.S.2d 970; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3480



June 15, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the People's predicate felony offender information on the ground that because the prior felony sentence was unconstitutional, the predicate felony offender information was nullified.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to the offense of attempted robbery in the first degree with an option to withdraw the plea if he were determined to be a second felony offender. The People filed a statement of predicate felony conviction alleging that defendant was convicted of housebreaking in South Carolina. The South Carolina court suspended defendant's five-year prison sentence and placed him on probation for five years with the additional condition that he was banished from South Carolina during the probation period. Defendant alleged that the sentence was unconstitutional. The court found that defendant received two sentences: (1) a five-year prison term, which the South Carolina court suspended, and (2) a five-year probation. The court held that even though the banishment portion of defendant's sentence was illegal as cruel and unusual punishment, the remainder of the judgment and sentence were valid. The court rejected defendant's argument that the South Carolina sentence violated his right to interstate travel, holding that a convicted felon's right to travel was not absolute. Jail sentences and certain probation conditions were permissible restrictions on travel.


OUTCOME: The court declared defendant a second felony offender.


HEADNOTES
