 [***1] Crimes -- Sentence -- Banishment

Defendant's conviction, upon his plea of guilty, to a felony in South Carolina, is valid and may serve as the predicate felony for sentencing defendant in New York as a second felony offender, despite the fact that the suspended sentence of five years' imprisonment and five years' probation imposed in South Carolina was illegal, void and unenforceable to the extent that, as a condition of probation, it was agreed that defendant would go to New York and would not return to South Carolina during the period of probation; although a conviction requires a valid sentence for second felony offender purposes (Penal Law, § 70.06, subd 1, par [b], cl [ii]), here, the invalid "banishment" portion of the sentence is separable from the valid portion of the sentence.

 

1826 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bakolas


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Monroe County


113 Misc. 2d 832; 451 N.Y.S.2d 622; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3386



June 8, 1982 



DISPOSITION: The order of the Rochester City Court is reversed, and the matter remitted for further proceedings on the information.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the State, challenged an order from the City Court of Rochester (New York) that dismissed so much of an information as charged defendants with disorderly conduct.


OVERVIEW: Defendants filed a motion in the city court to dismiss the accusatory instruments on the grounds that the information was defective and that N.Y. Penal Law § 240.20(1), (2), and (5) were unconstitutional for vagueness. The appeal was confined to only that portion of the trial court's order dismissing the charge under § 240.20(2) as unconstitutional. The court reversed the dismissal of that part of the indictment because the city court, as a court of first instance, should not have set aside as unconstitutional that statute as such conclusion was not inescapable, particularly as a court of appeals had implicitly, if not explicitly, upheld the validity of the statute. There was a strong presumption of constitutionality of statutes and the unconstitutionality of the statute had to be demonstrated beyond a reasonable doubt.The statute prohibited making unreasonable noise with intent to cause public inconvenience, annoyance, or claim or recklessly creating a risk. The statute did not make it a crime to merely make an unreasonable noise, the unreasonable noise had to be with intent to cause public inconvenience, annoyance, or alarm, or recklessly creating a risk.


OUTCOME: The court reversed that part of the city court's order that dismissed part of an information charging defendants with disorderly conduct and remitted the matter for further proceedings on the information.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Disorderly Conduct -- Unreasonable Noise

On an appeal to County Court, an order of a City Court dismissing so much of an information as charged disorderly conduct under subdivision 2 of section 240.20 of the Penal Law, which prohibits making unreasonable noise with intent to cause public inconvenience, annoyance or alarm or recklessly creating a risk thereof, on the ground that the statute is unconstitutional, is reversed and the matter is remitted for further proceedings on the information. A statute should not ordinarily be set aside as unconstitutional by a court of original jurisdiction; there is a strong presumption of constitutionality of a statute and unconstitutionality must be demonstrated beyond a reasonable doubt.

 

1827 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Kenny


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Kings County


114 Misc. 2d 203; 450 N.Y.S.2d 1003; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3456



June 8, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner nonmarital child filed a claim for distributive status to prove that she was the natural, nonmarital daughter of the decedent.


OVERVIEW: In this proceeding for letters of administration, the nonmarital child submitted voluminous and facially compelling evidence to prove that she was the natural, nonmarital daughter of the decedent. The court stated that it had to be determined whether or not all prospect of the nonmarital child's eventual success under N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 4-1.2(a), para. 2, cl. C, was foreclosed as a matter of law by the enactment's provision that it would take effect on the first day of September next succeeding the date on which it would have become law. The court held that with respect to reciprocal rights of succession of a child born out of wedlock and his or her natural father, the benefit of N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 4-1.2(a), para. 2, cl. C, was available in any proceeding pending upon September 1, 1981, or arising thereafter, provided that distribution of the estate had not been accomplished and that no decree determining distributive status which as incompatible with the invocation of clause (C) was then in existence. As the nonmarital child's claim met these conditions, the court set a hearing date.


OUTCOME: The court granted the nonmarital child a hearing in order to decide her distributive interest in the decedent's estate.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Descent and Distribution -- Inheritance Rights of Nonmarital Children -- "Retrospective" Application of Inheritance Statute

The benefit of EPTL 4-1.2 (subd [a], par [2], cl [C]), which establishes a third method to legitimize a nonmarital child for purposes of intestate succession by conferring distributive status upon such nonmarital child if "paternity has been established by clear and convincing evidence and the father of the child has openly and notoriously acknowledged the child as his own", is available in any proceeding pending upon the statute's effective date of September 1, 1981 or arising thereafter and is not confined to the intestate property of those who died on or after that date provided that distribution of the estate had not been theretofore accomplished and that no decree determining distributive status which was incompatible with the invocation of the statute was then in existence, and further provided that the decedent died on or after March 1, 1966, the date on which inheritance rights between nonmarital children and their fathers were created; "retrospective" application of the statute is appropriate in view of its remedial purpose and the fact [***2] that it is primarily adjective in nature, only incidentally affecting substantive rights, and does not entail substitution of an intrinsically distinct class of person in place of those who qualified as distributees at the instant of death but rather merely creates a third method of proving the ultimate fact of an individual's membership in the theretofore recognized distributive category of persons born out of wedlock whose paternity can be established with an acceptable degree of certainty.

 

1828 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Sackinger v. Nevins


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Cattaraugus County


114 Misc. 2d 454; 451 N.Y.S.2d 1005; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3497



June 4, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate filed a petition pro se to enforce his rights allegedly violated when he was a prisoner in the county jail. The inmate sought the institution of criminal charges against a correction officer he claimed struck him with a ring of keys. The court appointed an attorney to represent the inmate. Respondent sheriff and former county district attorney filed a motion to dismiss the petition under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 7804(f).


OVERVIEW: The court held that the petition brought under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 was an appropriate procedural vehicle and that the technical procedural shortcomings did not call for dismissal. The court pointed out that it could not have ordered the former district attorney to file criminal charges. The court added that the sheriff undertook an investigation of the prisoner's complaint with timely dispatch, and the court found his actions commendable. The court further found no basis for concluding that the manner in which sheriff delivered a report of investigation to the prisoner, orally, was arbitrary, capricious, tyrannical, unreasonable, or not in the exercise of honest discretion. Although the court denied the inmate's request for orders directed at the sheriff and the former district attorney, it issued a declaratory judgment on what it determined was a justiciable controversy. The court held that the inmate had a right to request a criminal proceeding, and noted that the same books, forms, and personnel which were previously available to prisoners to initiate proceedings to get out of jail would henceforward have been available to initiate proceedings to get others in jail.


OUTCOME: The court entered a declaratory judgment that the inmate had a right of access to the state criminal courts for the purpose of initiating a criminal proceeding against the correction officer he accused of hitting him with a set of keys. In all other respects, the court denied the petition the inmate filed against the sheriff and former district attorney.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Proceeding against Body or Officer -- When Remedy Appropriate -- Proceeding Seeking Institution of Criminal Prosecution

1. A petition brought pursuant to CPLR article 78 is an appropriate procedural vehicle to compel the District Attorney and the County Sheriff to take what petitioner contends to be officially required action in criminal court in connection with petitioner's allegation that a correction officer struck petitioner with a ring of keys while he was incarcerated in the county jail since such a petition does not seek a review of a determination in a criminal case but rather seeks to compel performance of an act which the court is empowered to do by means of an article 78 proceeding.

Pleading -- Petition -- Form of Verification

2. A petition in a CPLR article 78 proceeding which did not contain the formal language of a verification but which was sworn to by petitioner before a notary public and bore the signature and stamp of the notary is sufficient to satisfy the verification requirement of CPLR 7804 (subd [d]).

Process -- Service of Process -- Service by Mail where Court Orders Personal Service

3. Service of a petition commencing a CPLR [***2] article 78 proceeding by mail contrary to a direction for personal service contained in an order to show cause did not void such service where petitioner was incarcerated in various penal facilities during the time within which the order to show cause was procured and service was affected since such confinement made it impossible for him to effect personal service on the respondents and there was no evidence that he was financially able to hire process servers to effect personal service on his behalf.

Limitation of Actions -- Four-Month Statute of Limitations -- Proceeding Commenced by Prisoner Appearing Pro Se

4. A petition placed in the United States mail one day before the expiration of the Statute of Limitations which was not received by respondents until after such expiration will be deemed to have been timely served where petitioner, a prisoner, was acting pro se, since pro se petitions by prisoners must be liberally construed in petitioner's favor and a determination of the rights of a prisoner to access to the criminal courts should not be made on technical procedural grounds.

Prisons and Prisoners -- Complaint of Mistreatment -- Duty to Investigate

 [***3] 5. Petitioner prisoner's request for a written report of an investigation conducted by a County Sheriff into alleged mistreatment of the petitioner by a correction officer is denied since there is no requirement under State law that prisoners' complaints be investigated and, furthermore, there is no authority which obligates the Sheriff to deliver a written report of such investigation if conducted.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process -- Prisoner's Right of Access to Courts

6. A prisoner has a constitutional right of access to the courts for the purpose of instituting a criminal charge on his own initiative against a correction officer who is alleged to have committed a criminal act against him since a denial to a prisoner of freedom to communicate to the courts serves no public interest and, therefore, would be an arbitrary exercise of power prohibited by the due process clause of the Fourteenth Amendment; moreover, such holding is buttressed by the fact that the criminal act complained of, being struck by a ring of keys, could, if proven, constitute a violation of the prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment provided by the Federal and State Constitutions.

 [***4] Crimes -- Commencing Criminal Prosecution -- Who May File Accusatory Information

7. Anyone who is directly harmed by alleged criminal conduct can file an accusatory information unless the statute defining the illegal conduct specifically states otherwise, or the asserted illegal conduct is too remotely connected to the interest to be protected and, therefore, prisoners should be included among those so eligible since deprivation of access to the courts is not a necessary or reasonable concomitant of incarceration.

 

1829 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Rosado v. McGuire


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


114 Misc. 2d 652; 452 N.Y.S.2d 277; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3538



May 27, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner accused sought an order of mandamus directing respondent, Commissioner, New York City Police Department (NYPD), to return personalty seized during his arrest and requested summary determination of an order of mandamus. The NYPD sought dismissal of the action claiming the property seized was surrendered to the Federal Task Force on Terrorism (task force) pursuant to a subpoena.


OVERVIEW: The accused was arrested and charged with criminal mischief. His property was taken from him when he was arrested. He was released on his own recognizance, but the NYPD refused to return the seized property. Subsequently, the property was surrendered to the task force pursuant to a subpoena. The accused brought suit to recover the property seized and the NYPD sought to dismiss the action. The court denied the accused's motion for summary determination of an order of mandamus without prejudice to renew and denied the NYPD's motion to dismiss. It found that various factual questions were unanswered which precluded summary disposition. The NYPD did not plead that the property was suspected of being employed in the criminal mischief alleged and the seized property did not reasonably appear to be contraband or instrumentalities to commit a crime. Further, the NYPD did prove that any of the seized property was held as evidence in any pending criminal investigation or proceeding. In addition, as the property was now in the possession of the task force, it was unclear what jurisdiction, if any, the court had to grant the requested relief.


OUTCOME: The court denied the accused's motion for summary determination of an order of mandamus without prejudice to renew after joinder of issue, conduct and completion of any pretrial discovery procedures. The court also denied the NYPD's motion to dismiss as a matter of law.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Proceeding against Body or Officer -- When Remedy Available -- Compelling Return of Personal Property

A motion by respondent police commissioner to dismiss a CPLR article 78 proceeding in the nature of mandamus is denied, where petitioner seeks to recover an attache case, keys, an appointment, telephone and address book, a list of friends and associates and other personal papers taken from him following his arrest on a misdemeanor charge for spray painting a wall, which personal property was subsequently transferred to the United States Attorney pursuant to a subpoena, and respondent is given leave to serve and file an answer; respondent has not pleaded or proved that the property initially seized for "safekeeping" was used in the commission of a crime, that it is contraband or that it was held as evidence in a pending criminal investigation or proceeding and, further, no proof has been submitted that the property which was transferred to the United States Attorney was used as evidence of violations of Federal statutes or in any pending investigation or proceeding. Petitioner's motion for summary judgment is denied without prejudice to renewal after answer, discovery and trial.

Smith v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Special Term, New York County


114 Misc. 2d 564; 451 N.Y.S.2d 954; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3518



May 21, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiff's motion for summary judgment is granted; defendant's motion for summary judgment is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff former employee and defendant city and officials both brought motions for summary judgment, in a suit seeking a declaration that personnel orders to preclude payment to plaintiff constituted an unlawful and unconstitutional deprivation of property without due process of law, in violation of the Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution, U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff worked for the city and was involuntarily terminated. Prior to her involuntary termination, which plaintiff claims was without cause, plaintiff was not advised that she should use up the hours standing to her credit in her "overtime bank" or she would not be compensated for those hours. Plaintiff was not given an opportunity to take advantage of accrued and unused time which resulted from work performed by plaintiff at the request of her superiors. The court granted plaintiff's motion for summary judgment and denied the city's motion for summary judgment. The city did not dispute that plaintiff had earned a certain number of authorized overtime credit, and that plaintiff would have been able to take advantage of the accumulated overtime had she not been terminated. The court found that toe the degree that the personnel orders in question prevented the city from paying plaintiff for the time she put in, such orders were an unconstitutional deprivation of plaintiff's rights and property.


OUTCOME: The court granted plaintiff former employee's motion for summary judgment and denied the city's motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service -- Termination of Employment -- Entitlement to Cash Value of Accrued Vacation Time

A municipal employee dismissed against his will, without notice and without an opportunity to utilize accrued vacation time, is entitled to the cash value of such vacation time since failure to so compensate the employee would constitute an unconstitutional deprivation of property without due process of law.

 

1831 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lewis


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Trial and Special Term, Rockland County


113 Misc. 2d 1091; 450 N.Y.S.2d 977; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3424



May 19, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, Count No. 1 of the indictment charging the defendant with "Burglary in the second degree" is amended to read "Burglary in the third degree."


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant brought a motion to dismiss an indictment against him for burglary in the second-degree, pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 140.25(2), on the grounds that a recent amendment to the burglary statute, which reclassified a daytime burglary of a dwelling as a violent C felony with a mandatory prison sentence, was violative of defendant's right to due process and constituted cruel and unusual punishment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the daytime burglary of a dwelling unconstitutionally constituted the commission of the crime of burglary in the second-degree, a violent C felony, after an amendment to § 140.25(2) removed the requirement that the offense occur "at night." Prior to the amendment, the acts alleged in the indictment against defendant would have only constituted the commission of the crime of burglary in the third-degree, a class D felony. On consideration of defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment, however, the court instead ordered the indictment amended to charge defendant with burglary in the third-degree. Specifically, the court held that the amendment to § 140.25(2) violated defendant's right to due process of law and made defendant's conduct disproportionate to his offense, thus constituting cruel and unusual punishment. Nevertheless, the court held that the conduct alleged in the indictment still constituted the crime of burglary in the third-degree, pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 140.20, before or after the amendment at issue. Accordingly, the court ordered the indictment amended to charge defendant with burglary in the third-degree.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the indictment amended to charge defendant with burglary in the third-degree.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Burglary

An indictment charging defendant with burglary in the second degree in that he knowingly entered or remained unlawfully in a dwelling during the daytime on February 6, 1981, is amended to read burglary in the third degree, since the September 1, 1981 amendment to subdivision 2 of section 140.25 of the Penal Law, which deleted the phrase "and the entering or remaining occurs at night" from the statute and reclassified a daytime burglary of a dwelling from a class D felony to a class C felony with a mandatory State prison sentence violates defendant's rights to due process and constitutes cruel and unusual punishment; the sentencing implications of the amendment were not foreseen and other crimes which the Legislature has classified as violent C felonies all involve either the actual use of force against a person or the potential for such force and violence by the possession or display of a weapon. A legislative act must bear a rational relationship between the classification employed in the statute [***2] and some legitimate State purpose and by making a daytime dwelling burglary a violent C felony the Legislature has failed to acknowledge the difference in culpability and potential for personal confrontation between a day and night burglary and has thus mandated a punishment greatly disproportionate to the offense actually committed.

 

1832 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Blumberg v. North Hempstead


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


114 Misc. 2d 8; 450 N.Y.S.2d 698; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3426



May 10, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Submit judgment dismissing the petition.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner councilwoman sought a judgment directing respondent town to provide for her defense in an action instituted against her as a result of a proceeding that she brought against the board of zoning appeals.


OVERVIEW: The councilwoman brought an article 78 proceeding challenging the board of zoning appeals' grant of a use variance to a developer. The developer commenced an action against all of the petitioners in the article 78 proceeding including the councilwoman for malicious prosecution and abuse of process. When the town attorney refused to provide for her defense, she retained private counsel and commenced the instant proceeding. Town Code § 23-15.2(A) provided that the town would provide for the defense of an employee in a civil action or proceeding arising out of any alleged act or omission which occurred or was alleged in the complaint to have occurred while the employee was acting within the scope of his public employment or duties. The court held that a town councilman was not employed to sue the board of appeals and such an action was not within the scope of his employment or duties unless he has been directed to bring the action in the name of the town by the town board. Thus, the councilwoman was not acting within the scope of her public employment or duties when she brought the article 78 proceeding against the board of appeals. The court dismissed the petition.


OUTCOME: The court submitted judgment dismissing the councilwoman's petition.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Reimbursement of Town Board Member's Legal Expenses -- Scope of "Official Duties"

Reimbursement by a municipality of the expenses incurred by a public officer in defending an action would constitute an unconstitutional gift of public funds for a purely private purpose without the benefit of a statute authorizing payment for such expenses arising out of the officer's "official duties", the test for determining an "official duty" being whether the officer is acting within the scope of what he is employed to do; accordingly, petitioner, a member of the town board, was not acting within the scope of her public employment or duties when she brought an article 78 proceeding against the town's zoning board of appeals on her own initiative and without board authorization challenging the grant of a use variance to a developer since a town board member is not employed to sue the board of appeals in the name of the town absent a direction to do so by the town board and, therefore, petitioner is not entitled to have the town provide for her defense in the developer's action brought against her for malicious prosecution [***2] and abuse of process nor does the fact that a member of the town board is authorized by statute to seek judicial review of a board of appeals' determination (Town Law, § 267, subd 7) compel a different result inasmuch as such statute only goes to the issue of whether a party has standing to bring the proceeding and cannot be construed as defining a scope of public employment or duty.

 

1833 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Mobil Oil Corp. v. Tully


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


113 Misc. 2d 885; 449 N.Y.S.2d 921; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3396



May 4, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer and defendant members of the state tax commission both filed motions for summary judgment on the fifth cause of the taxpayer's complaint regarding the constitutionality of N.Y. Tax Law § 182, which discussed a taxation method for oil companies, such as the taxpayer.


OVERVIEW: The parties' argument centered on the constitutionality of N.Y. Tax Law § 182. The court found that the amendment to § 182 resulted in the taxation of certain oil companies, but not others. In defining vertically integrated companies for the purposes of § 182, no tax was imposed upon the activity of production and refining. The tax was not enacted solely upon volume of sales of certain oil companies rather than others. Because there was no evidence of an arbitrary classification in the statute, the taxpayer's argument that certain oil companies were not subject to the tax was a question for the legislature to decide rather than a court. The taxpayer failed to demonstrate that the creation of the class of vertically integrated was without a rational basis.


OUTCOME: The court denied the taxpayer's motion for summary judgment, but the court granted the motion for summary judgment filed by the members of the state tax commission.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Taxation -- Gross Profits Tax on Petroleum Producers

An action by plaintiff oil company against defendant members of the State Tax Commission challenging the constitutionality of section 182 of the Tax Law, as amended by section 68 of chapter 1043 of the Laws of 1981, is dismissed, since the amended statute, which imposes a 2% gross profits tax on certain vertically integrated petroleum corporations that exceed threshold amounts of crude oil production and refining capacity, does not impose a tax on the activity of production and refining; reference to minimum thresholds of production and refining capacity is utilized only to define and set the parameters of the class itself, which class is not defined solely by the sales volume of a company.

 

1834 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Uplinger


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Erie County


113 Misc. 2d 876; 449 N.Y.S.2d 916; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3394



May 3, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The judgments against Robert Uplinger and Fredericka Sanders are affirmed; and the order dismissing the charge against Susan Butler is reversed and the case remitted to the City Court of Buffalo for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed judgments from the trial court (New York), which convicted them of loitering in public for the purpose of soliciting another to engage in deviate sexual intercourse in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35, subd. 3.


OVERVIEW: Three defendants, whose cases were unrelated, were charged with loitering in public for the purpose of soliciting another to engage in deviate sexual intercourse in violation of § 240.35, subd. 3. Two defendants were convicted of the offense while the third defendant's charge was dismissed upon a determination, following a hearing, that § 240.53, subd. 3 violated her constitutional rights. Defendants appealed claiming that § 240.35, subd. 3 violated their rights protected by the Federal and State Constitutions because it prohibited lawful conduct. In addition, the State appealed the dismissal of the third defendant's case claiming that § 240.35, subd. 3 was valid. The court ruled that the issue was not whether the prohibited act under § 240.35, subd. 3 was criminal but whether its proscription was rationally related to the interests of public morality. And the court concluded that the statute's proscription agasint loitering for the purpose of solicitation of others for deviate sexual activity was in fact rationally related to the interests of public morality.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments against the two defendants convicted of loitering and reversed the order dismissing the loitering charge agasint the third defendant, and the court remitted the case to the trial court for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute

Subdivision 3 of section 240.35 of the Penal Law, which proscribes loitering in a public place "for the purpose of engaging, soliciting another person to engage, in deviate sexual intercourse or other sexual behavior of a deviate nature", is constitutional; the purpose of the statute is to suppress public nuisance rather than to regulate morality, and the protection of citizens from undesired annoying behavior in public places provides a valid and rational basis to interdict the conduct described; the statute reasonably and narrowly limits one's opportunity to solicit others to engage in deviate sex, without trespassing upon the rights of free speech; the statute is not directed to a mental operation alone, as it must be interpreted to require either an overt act or other conduct unambiguous evidencing the proscribed purpose; the statute is not violative of equal protection, as the Legislature may have rationally [***2] concluded that solicitation by loiterers for deviate sexual activity is more offensive to the standards of public morality than solicitation for other purposes and there is no showing of knowing and intentional selective enforcement, and, finally, the language "or other sexual behavior of a deviate nature" does not render the statute void for vagueness, as the phrase adequately establishes sufficient warning as to the conduct to be avoided.

 

1835 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Correa


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


113 Misc. 2d 919; 450 N.Y.S.2d 266; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3403



April 28, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree after a jury trial. Defendant filed a motion to controvert the people's persistent violent felony offender statement.


OVERVIEW: After defendant was convicted of criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree, the people filed a persistent violent felony offender statement in 1982. The statement alleged two prior convictions from 1976, one for attempted murder in the second degree and the second for robbery in the first degree. Defendant was sentenced concurrently for the two prior convictions. Defendant filed a motion to controvert the statement, arguing that the plea allocution for the robbery conviction was legally insufficient to support a predicate violent felony finding, and that the convictions could not be used because they were prior to the enactment of the violent offender statute. The court held that defendant's contention with regard to the robbery conviction was without merit. However, the court held that the convictions could not be used as predicate violent felonies because the statute on its face did not provide for the use of a conviction that was obtained prior to the enactment of the violent felony statute. The court did find that the prior convictions could be used as predicate felonies, and adjudicated defendant a second felony offender.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to controvert the people's persistent violent felony offender statement.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Persistent Violent Felony Offender -- Predicate Violent Felony

Crimes committed prior to the enactment of the persistent violent felony offender statutes, effective September 1, 1978, may not be used as predicate violent felonies for the purpose of increasing punishment (Penal Law, § 70.08), since the statutes on their face only permit the use of a conviction which at the time of its commission was defined as a violent felony offense (Penal Law, § 70.04, subd 1, par [b], cl [i]; § 70.02), and do not permit the use of a conviction which at the time of its commission was not so defined; the omission of language in the statutes providing for a comparison of the elements of crimes committed in the State prior to the enactment of the persistent violent offender statutes with the elements of crimes designated as violent felonies as of the date of the commission of the offense for which the defendant is being sentenced as a basis for predicate violent felony determination prevents an interpretation that would allow such a comparison to be made in determining whether a conviction is a predicate violent felony.

Abdullah v. Smith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Wyoming County


115 Misc. 2d 105; 453 N.Y.S.2d 541; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3639



April 28, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoners filed a proceeding pursuant to N.Y.C.P.L.R. art. 78 seeking to contest the constitutionality of certain disciplinary sanctions imposed for the performance of certain religious rituals.


OVERVIEW: Several Moslem inmates at a penitentiary were disciplined for performing a ritual prayer in the recreation yard. Prison officials asserted that these prayers violated a directive of the Department of Correctional Services allowing group or demonstrative prayer by inmates only in the privacy of their cells or during an authorized religious service. An action was initiated alleging that the imposition of such disciplinary proceedings was unconstitutional. The court determined that because the conduct sought to be banned was not disruptive and only lasted 10 to 12 minutes, disciplining the inmates for such activities was improper.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the prison officials to cease enforcing the directive prohibiting religious activities in the recreation yard.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Prisons and Prisoners -- Discipline of Inmates -- "Keeplock" Confinement Prior to Hearing

1. The record of disciplinary proceedings against petitioner correctional facility inmate is ordered expunged, where he was locked in his cell for praying in the recreation yard in violation of a facility rule and he did not appear before an adjustment committee until three days later, since no finding was ever made that confinement prior to the holding of a due process hearing was necessary; "keeplock" is a form of special confinement which may not be imposed in the absence of due process protections.

Prisons and Prisoners -- Prayer in Recreation Yard

2. Respondent correctional facility superintendent is ordered to cease enforcing a facility rule banning religious services in the recreation yards so as to punish Sunni Moslem inmates for saying 10-minute individual prayers in an orderly fashion in the recreation yard at those times of the year when the mandatory prayer time falls during recreation periods, or to allow them access to their cells during such times without forfeiting the balance of the recreation period in order to worship in their cells; considering the size [***2] of the yard and the brevity of the prayer, this restriction on religious worship to avoid forcing petitioners' religion upon other inmates is unreasonable, although a different result might obtain if petitioners sought to perform such rituals in the dining area, work area or classroom where it might be disruptive (NY Const, art I, § 3; Correction Law, § 610).

 

1837 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Life Science Church


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


113 Misc. 2d 952; 450 N.Y.S.2d 664; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3406; 82-1 U.S. Tax Cas. (CCH) P9414



April 27, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In addition to restitution, petitioner attorney general sought a permanent injunction to prevent respondents, purported religious foundation and members, from violating N.Y. Exec. Law § 63(12) in seeking fraudulent church-related charitable income tax exemptions, violating N.Y. Bus. Law art. 23-A et seq. by engaging in an unlawful pyramid scheme, and violating N.Y. Jud. Law art. 15 et seq. by engaging in the unlawful practice of law.


OVERVIEW: As an initial matter, the court held that purported religious foundation and members had engaged in fraud by claiming certain charitable federal income tax exemptions, as both the inurement and operational requirements of 26 U.S.C.S. § 501(c)(3) were violated by the operation, in whole or part, of sanctioned subsidiary churches for the private purposes of members. The court thus held that any attempt to deduct non-exempt contributions to the parties was a fraudulent and deceptive practice in violation of N.Y. Exec. Law § 63(12). The court further found that attorney general was authorized to seek relief on proof of a pyramid scheme in violation of N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 359-fff, additionally noting that the giving of tax advice constituted the unlawful practice of law in violation of N.Y. Jud. Law § 478. Although the court did dismiss the petition with respect to members for whom no prima facie case was made, it then granted the requested relief as to the remaining parties, concluding that the claim of free exercise of religion was not a shield to an unlawful claim of tax exemption, the improper holding out as an attorney, and the fraudulent pyramid scheme.


OUTCOME: The court granted the request for permanent injunctive relief and appointed a permanent receiver to oversee the process of providing restitution to those who had paid sums to members of purported religious foundation.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Religious Corporations and Associations -- Unlawful Sale of Ministers' Credentials for Tax Avoidance -- Unlawful Pyramid Scheme

Respondent Life Science Church and certain named church officials, in addition to being guilty of criminal contempt for willful disobedience of a prior court order, are permanently enjoined (1) from engaging in fraudulent and deceptive practices (Executive Law, § 63, subd 12) based upon representations to the public claiming that a complete legal tax exemption could be obtained after paying $ 3,500 for minister's credentials and attending three training sessions at the church's "college" since neither the church nor any of its ministers is entitled to a tax exemption in view of the fact that the church operates in whole or in part for the private purposes of the respective ministers and their families (US Code, tit 26, § 501, subd [c], par [3]; § 170, subd [c], par [2], cl [C]; 26 CFR 1.501 [c] [3]-1 [c] [2]); (2) from engaging in an unlawful pyramid scheme (General Business Law, § 359-fff) based upon the payment of commissions to member ministers for the recruitment of new ministers; (3) from engaging in the unauthorized practice [***2] of law (Judiciary Law, § 478) based upon representations that the church would provide legal advice to its ministers on tax and other matters; and (4) from continuing to use the name "college" without proper authorization (Education Law, § 224); the constitutional protection of the free exercise of religion is not a license for unrestricted conduct and cannot be used as a shield to an unlawful claim of tax exemption, an illegal pyramid scheme, the improper practice of law and misuse of the term "college".

 

1838 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lee


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Special Term, Sullivan County


113 Misc. 2d 977; 450 N.Y.S.2d 259; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3407



April 22, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The conviction is affirmed, on the law.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from his conviction by the Village of Monticello Justice Court (New York) for violating Village of Monticello, N.Y., Code § 39-3 by possessing an open container of an alcoholic beverage. Defendant had been given a conditional discharge after pleading guilty once his motion to dismiss on constitutional grounds was denied.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the statute in question was unconstitutionally vague and criminalized conduct that an ordinary citizen could not reasonably understand as prohibited. He further argued that the statute was not worded to prevent arbitrary or discriminatory enforcement. The court found that: (1) the statute sought to discourage improper conduct arising from the consumption of alcohol in public places; (2) the intent of the prohibition of possession of alcoholic beverages was to prevent possession with the intent to consume in a public place; (3) the failure of the statute to proscribe possession for the purpose of consumption explicitly did not render the statute unconstitutionally vague where the intent of the statute was clear; and (4) the statute, which served a legitimate public good, had been constitutionally applied to defendant's possession of an open container for the purpose of consuming alcohol in public.


OUTCOME: The court construed the statute as prohibiting possession with intent to consume, held the statute constitutional, and affirmed the conviction.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating Liquors -- Open Container Ordinance -- Possession of Open Container with Intent to Consume in Public Place

An ordinance of the Village of Monticello, which proscribes possession of an open container of an alcoholic beverage in "any public place" within the village as part of a statutory scheme designed to eliminate the consumption of alcoholic beverages in public places, is not unconstitutionally vague since, although the ordinance does not specifically proscribe possession "for the purposes of consumption", the clear intent of the "possession" statute is to prohibit possession with the intent of the possessor or another to consume in a public place and not mere possession without a showing of such intent; the statute was constitutionally applied to defendant who was convicted for having an open can of beer with a straw in it in his hand and defendant failed to demonstrate the invalidity of the law beyond a reasonable doubt inasmuch as the ordinance bears some reasonable relation to the public good in seeking to prevent public drunkenness.

 

1839 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Darcy


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Yates County


113 Misc. 2d 580; 449 N.Y.S.2d 626; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3344



April 19, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, it is hereby ordered that the indictment be and the same is hereby dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant brought an omnibus motion, which among other things, sought to inspect the Grand Jury minutes and dismissal of an indictment against her. Defendant was charged with felony misuse of food stamps, in violation of N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 147, and with a misdemeanor of offering a false instrument for filing in the second degree, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 175.30.


OVERVIEW: Both charges were based upon the contention that defendant was a recipient of the food stamp program and failed to disclose the receipt of an educational loan in the amount of $ 2,470. In determining that the indictment required dismissal, the court held that although it entertained serious reservations about the constitutionality of N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law §§ 147, 95, insofar as they represented penal statutory provisions, it was unnecessary to decide those issues. The court found that whether based upon constitutional grounds or not, it was clear that in order for §§ 147, 95 to take on any meaning, reference to federal and/or state regulations, or the federal statute was necessary. The court found that the Grand Jury was not provided with sufficient information as to the regulations to enable it to intelligently decide whether or not a crime had been committed. The falsity of the information provided by defendant was based upon a definition of income as specified in the regulations or 7 U.S.C.S. § 2014, the substance of which was not provided to the Grand Jury. The court held that a social welfare examiner could not supply the deficiency in the Grand Jury instructions.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the indictment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Misuse of Food Stamps -- Sufficiency of Indictment

1. An indictment charging defendant, a recipient of food stamps, with misuse of food stamps (Social Services Law, § 147) in that she failed to disclose the receipt of a New York State Higher Education Service Corporation loan is dismissed; although section 147 of the Social Services Law purportedly makes the acquisition of food stamps with a value more than $ 250 a felony if done in a manner not authorized by section 95 of the Social Services Law, which merely places certain affirmative duties upon the Commissioner of Social Services, the statute fails to give notice that a recipient of food stamps is required to report income, or assuming such a duty, that income would encompass an educational loan. Additionally, to the extent that sections 147 and 95 of the Social Services Law incorporate Federal and State regulations, criminal prosecution raises serious constitutional questions concerning due process and delegation of legislative powers (see NY Const, art III), and in any event, the Grand Jury was not provided with [***2] sufficient information to enable it to intelligently decide whether or not a crime had been committed because it was unaware of the substance of any of the applicable regulations.

Crimes -- Offering False Instrument for Filing -- Sufficiency of Indictment

2. An indictment charging defendant, a recipient of food stamps, with offering a false instrument for filing in the second degree (Penal Law, § 175.30) in that she failed to disclose the receipt of a New York State Higher Education Service Corporation Loan is dismissed, since the recertification application defendant was required to fill out contained no express provision for receipt of an educational loan and the falsity of the information contained in the document is necessarily based upon a definition of income, as specified in the applicable State and Federal regulations, the substance of which were not provided to the Grand Jury. Furthermore, one of the Grand Jury witnesses testified that State and Federal regulations required the defendant to report her loan as income, and CPL 190.25 (subd 6) expressly provides that the only legal advisors of the Grand Jury are the court and the District Attorney.

 

1840 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Solomon


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


113 Misc. 2d 790; 449 N.Y.S.2d 875; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3379



April 19, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree. The indictment charged the weapon counts as felonies rather than misdemeanors because of defendant's prior conviction for misdemeanor assault. Defendant challenged the assault conviction as unconstitutional for inadequate plea allocution and argued that it could not be the basis for the elevation of the misdemeanor charge.


OVERVIEW: The state had filed a motion to amend the date on the special information of defendant's previous conviction, stating that the incorrect date was a typographical error. The motion to amend was granted, and defendant brought his constitutional challenge to the conviction. The court allowed the attack. There was no statutory authority for the challenge, and the evidence did not fall into any of the enumerated categories of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 710.20. Therefore, the court treated the challenge as a motion to controvert, found the plea unconstitutional, and held that it could not be used against defendant on the charges for possession of weapons. Defendant could not have commenced the challenge before trial of his weapons charges because he did not have actual knowledge that the state was relying on the conviction until after trial began. The court could not have delayed the motion because the issue went to the nature and degree of the crime, a decision that was necessary before defendant commenced his defense. The guilty plea for assault was unconstitutional because it was apparent to the that court that defendant was delusional and incapable of assisting his counsel.


OUTCOME: The court allowed the challenge, charged the jury accordingly, and defendant was convicted of attempted assault in the second degree and criminal possession in the fourth degree.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Special Information -- Amendment

1. A special information filed after the commencement of trial, which alleges the defendant's conviction of a prior crime so as to elevate a misdemeanor to a felony, such as criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree (Penal Law, § 265.02, subd [1]), may be amended to reflect the correct date of the prior conviction notwithstanding the fact that there is no express statutory authority for amending a special information as there is with an indictment or a bill of particulars since a special information is in the nature of a bill of particulars and logic and reason dictate that the court has the inherent power to permit the amendment of a special information after first inquiring into and confirming that the Grand Jury did in fact indict defendant for a felony by reason of the proper prior conviction; legislative action is recommended to provide for the amending of a special information.

Crimes -- Possession of Weapon as Felony -- Challenge to Constitutionality of Prior Conviction

2. Since subdivision (1) of section 265.02 of the Penal Law, which elevates the crime of possession of a weapon from a misdemeanor level [***2] to felony status where the defendant has been convicted of a prior crime, is an enhancement of punishment statute, the prosecution may not rely on an unconstitutionally obtained predicate conviction and, therefore, a challenge to the predicate conviction on constitutional grounds must be permitted notwithstanding the fact that there is no statutory provision providing for a challenge to the special information which alleges the prior conviction; the court has the power to establish a remedy to enable the defendant to enforce constitutional guarantees and since defendant's constitutional challenge most closely resembles a motion to controvert a second felony offender statement ( CPL 400.21) "a motion to controvert" the special information is the most appropriate mechanism to test the constitutionality of defendant's prior conviction with the People having the burden of proving the constitutionality of the prior conviction beyond a reasonable doubt; since defendant can only be arraigned on a special information after the commencement of trial ( CPL 200.60, subd 3) the "motion to controvert" cannot be made until after the commencement of trial and should not be converted to a posttrial [***3] motion to set aside the verdict ( CPL 330.30) nor does defendant's failure to have appealed his prior conviction constitute a waiver of his right to now raise the issue of its constitutionality.

Crimes -- Possession of Weapon as Felony -- Unconstitutionally Obtained Predicate Conviction -- Legislative Recommendations

3. Since the plea court in a prior proceeding addressed no inquiry to the defendant personally relating to his guilty plea and did nothing to insure that defendant understood the consequences or alternatives to the plea notwithstanding the fact that the plea court had information from the psychiatric examination report that defendant's view of the case was "rigid and unrealistic", the taking of the plea violated defendant's due process rights and may not be used as a predicate crime to elevate the possession of weapon charge from a misdemeanor to a felony (Penal Law, § 265.02, subd [1]); the statutory scheme with respect to a special information alleging a prior conviction so as to elevate the level of the crime charged ( CPL 200.60) should be amended to specifically authorize amendment of a special information, to specify the number of days after defendant's [***4] arraignment on the indictment the special information should be filed, to provide that the arraignment on a special information occur when the special information is filed or within a reasonable time thereafter, to provide a mechanism for attacking the constitutional validity of prior convictions to set forth a time limitation for making challenges to the constitutionality of the prior conviction and to set forth the burden of showing the constitutionality or unconstitutionality of the prior conviction.

 

1841 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Boudin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Rockland County


114 Misc. 2d 523; 452 N.Y.S.2d 496; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3509



April 19, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The plaintiff state moved for an order directing defendant to appear in a corporeal lineup pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L. 240.40 (2)(b)(i). The defendant cross-moved for an order granting a stay of discovery.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the facts submitted on the application established probable cause for the "necessity" of conducting the requested lineup procedure. Accordingly, the court ordered that the state may conduct a corporeal lineup of defendant in the presence of defendant's counsel, defendant to be viewed only by witnesses to the events which occurred, which are the subject of the instant indictment. Witnesses who previously participated in a corporeal lineup of defendant shall not be permitted to participate in this corporeal lineup. The court noted that with respect to the conduct of the lineup, the stated consented to the presence of defendant's counsel, to the photographing and stenographic recording thereof, to permit defense counsel to choose defendant's position, and to giving three days' prior notice. The other requests made by defendant with respect to the conduct of the lineup were denied. The defendant's cross motion for an order staying the conduct of the requested corporeal lineup procedure was denied. The court found that the completion of pretrial discovery and the conduct of pretrial hearings are not a condition precedent to the granting of relief requested.


OUTCOME: The court granted the state's motion for an order directing defendant to appear in a corporeal lineup and denied defendant's cross-motion for an order granting a stay of discovery.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Identification of Defendant -- Lineup

The People may conduct a corporeal lineup of a defendant charged with felony murder, robbery, assault and grand larceny in a highly publicized case for those eyewitnesses who have previously identified defendant only through photographic arrays, pursuant to CPL 240.40 (subd 2, par [b], cl [i]), which provides that the court may order the defendant to provide nontestimonial evidence including appearing in a lineup; requiring defendant to appear in a lineup does not run afoul of her Fifth Amendment rights regarding evidence of a testimonial or communicative nature, and furthermore, the People have demonstrated that the defendant was at the scene of the crime, and that the requested lineup procedure is necessary in view of the admissibility of evidence of a previous corporeal identification procedure as evidence in chief at trial and the inadmissibility of evidence of a prior photographic identification procedure.

 

1842 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Abbott


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


113 Misc. 2d 766; 449 N.Y.S.2d 853; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3378



April 15, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to a jury verdict, the court convicted defendant of manslaughter in the first degree. Before the court was the issue of whether defendant's two previous felony convictions were unconstitutionally obtained and therefore could not be used to sentence him as a persistent violent felony offender pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 70.08 and N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 400.16.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that his Utah conviction for assault with a deadly weapon by one convict upon another and his federal guilty-plea conviction for bank robbery were unconstitutionally obtained. The court disagreed and held that the convictions could be used for sentence enhancement purposes. The absence of the Utah trial transcript, which was destroyed after 10 years as a result of established administrative procedure in Utah, did not constitute denial of due process because defendant had been afforded his right to appeal. Under principles of collateral estoppel and full faith and credit, defendant could not contest an alleged Miranda violation in the Utah proceedings because the issue had already been resolved by the highest Utah appellate court. Moreover, defendant had failed to overcome the presumption of regularity of the Utah proceedings. The 10-year tolling provision of N.Y. Penal Law § 70.06 did not violate defendant's equal protection rights because it was not irrational to distinguish between a prisoner and a non-prisoner. The federal guilty plea was not improper because the record indicated that it was knowing and voluntary and that defendant was competent to plead guilty.


OUTCOME: The court held that defendant's prior felony convictions could be used for sentence enhancement purposes and that the court was thus mandated to sentence defendant as a persistent violent felon.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Persistent Violent Felony Offender -- Absence of Trial Transcript of Previous Conviction

1. While a defendant is entitled to the transcript of a trial or its reconstructed equivalent for the purpose of implementing a clear right to appeal, there is no concomitant right to preserve a trial transcript long after the defendant has been afforded the right to appeal; accordingly, in a proceeding to determine whether defendant's previous felony convictions require that he be sentenced as a persistent violent felony offender ( CPL 400.15, subd 7, par [b]; 400.21, subd 7, par [b]), the absence of the transcript of a 1966 Utah trial, which resulted in defendant's felony conviction for assaulting another prison inmate with a knife, does not constitute denial of due process of law that automatically precludes a fair assessment of the events surrounding the Utah trial since the facts have otherwise been adequately reconstructed and defendant's allegation that additional constitutional errors would become apparent if the trial transcript were available is pure speculation and is no basis for controverting the Utah conviction.

Crimes -- Persistent Violent Felony Offender  [***2] -- Collateral Estoppel -- Burden of Proof -- Tolling Provision

2. In a proceeding contesting the constitutionality of an out-of-State predicate felony for purposes of determining whether defendant's previous felony convictions require that he be sentenced as a persistent violent felony offender ( CPL 400.15, subd 7, par [b]; 400.21, subd 7, par [b], defendant is collaterally estopped from controverting a 1966 Utah conviction for stabbing another prison inmate on the ground that his constitutional rights were violated by the admission of two statements at his trial since that very issue has already been decided against defendant in Utah's highest appellate court and the fact that an out-of-State conviction is at issue does not mandate a de novo review inasmuch as collateral estoppel may properly be invoked in predicate felony proceedings regardless of whether the conviction is a New York or out-of-State conviction; the People do not have the burden of proving the constitutionality of defendant's previous convictions beyond a reasonable doubt since the statutory provisions merely require the People to establish beyond a reasonable doubt the existence or fact of a predicate [***3] felony conviction and it is then incumbent upon the defendant to come forward with some quantum of evidence that the conviction was unconstitutionally obtained; even if collateral estoppel is inapplicable, defendant has failed to rebut the presumption of regularity of the Utah proceedings and, lastly, the provision which excludes periods of incarceration from the 10-year period within which a prior conviction can be used as a predicate felony (Penal Law, § 70.06) is not unconstitutional as applied to a defendant who has lived his entire life in a prison and who has committed a crime that can only be committed in prison.

Crimes -- Persistent Violent Felony Offender -- Collateral Estoppel -- Burden of Proof

3. In a proceeding under the persistent violent offender statutes contesting the constitutionality of a previous Federal bank robbery conviction ( CPL 400.15, subd 7, par [b]; 400.21, subd 7, par [b]) on grounds of an insufficient plea allocution at the time of defendant's guilty plea and an insufficient inquiry into defendant's mental competency to enter a guilty plea, the doctrine of collateral estoppel precludes a de novo review of the validity of the guilty [***4] plea since such issue was fully and fairly litigated in the Federal courts which properly utilized the same standard for determining competency to plead guilty as is used to test the competency of a defendant to proceed to trial, a standard acceptable under New York law, and properly determined that defendant's plea is not rendered invalid merely because the trial court failed to advise defendant of all the rights he was waiving by pleading guilty since the plea was knowingly and voluntarily made and that is all that is required under New York law; the People do not have the burden of proving the constitutionality of the previous conviction beyond a reasonable doubt but are only required to prove the fact of the previous felony conviction beyond a reasonable doubt and it is then incumbent upon the defendant to establish the unconstitutionality of the previous conviction by factual support; it was not error for the Federal habeas court to have denied defendant the opportunity to be present at the hearing (US Code, tit 28, § 2255) and to have precluded defense counsel from presenting live testimony and confronting witnesses since defendant had no constitutional or statutory right to [***5] be present at such hearing and the procedures utilized adequately elicited the necessary facts.

 

1843 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Francis v. Mulholland


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Chautauqua County


113 Misc. 2d 821; 449 N.Y.S.2d 848; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3384



April 8, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the Allegany County District Attorney, brought a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding seeking a judgment ordering the board of legislators of the county and the County Treasurer to pay him the minimum salary mandated by N.Y. Jud. Law § 183-a. Respondent county officials moved to have the hearing take place in Allegany County.


OVERVIEW: The court first denied a change of venue because there were no facts in questions, only questions of law, and no hearing was necessary. The court then held that the State of New York mandated that full-time district attorneys be paid certain minimum salaries, based on the population of the county. Allegany County had enacted a law stipulating a lower salary. The court held that N.Y. Jud. Law § 183-a was not a special law and that it applied to all full-time district attorneys. Thus, Allegany County's attempt to amend § 183-a was invalid. That certain counties with a small population could choose to make the position part time did not effect the statutory compensation scheme. The salary of a district attorney was not just a matter of local concern. Effective fighting of crime in one county inured to the benefit of the whole State. Finally, the court held that it was not an arbitrary act on the part of the legislature to insist that full-time district attorneys make at least as much as the judges before whom they practice.


OUTCOME: The court entered judgment granting the District Attorney the relief demanded.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] District and Prosecuting Attorneys -- Compensation

Petitioner, the full-time District Attorney of Allegany County, is granted judgment ordering the county board of legislators and the County Treasurer to pay him the minimum salary mandated by section 183-a of the Judiciary Law. The statute is a general law, which applies to all full-time District Attorneys in the State (NY Const, art IX, § 3, subd [d], par [1]), not a special law, and an attempt to amend section 183-a by a local law setting petitioner's salary at a lower figure is invalid; respondents failed to sustain the heavy burden of proving the statute unconstitutional, as it was not an arbitrary act on the part of the Legislature to insist that full-time District Attorneys make at least as much as the County Judges before whom they practice, and although the variation and disparity amongst the County Judges' salaries as established by the Legislature may be irrational, unfair or even unconstitutional, it does not invalidate that section requiring District Attorneys to be paid as much as those Judges.

 

1844 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Rutuelo v. State


Claim No. 61657


Court of Claims of New York


113 Misc. 2d 467; 449 N.Y.S.2d 419; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3320



April 7, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] By reason of the foregoing, the claim is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action for false arrest and malicious prosecution, plaintiff driver and defendant officer were before the court for its decision on the merits of the driver's claim.


OVERVIEW: The officer stopped the driver and issued him a traffic ticket. The traffic regulation that the driver violated was found unconstitutional in the underlying action and the ticket dismissed. The court dismissed the driver's complaint for false arrest and malicious prosecution. The court held that the officer had probable cause to stop the driver and issue the ticket and the subsequent finding of unconstitutionality did not vitiate the reasonableness of the officer's action. The court also concluded that an action for false arrest did not lie for the mere issuance of a traffic ticket.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the driver's complaint for malicious prosecution and false arrest against the officer.


HEADNOTES

Malicious Prosecution -- Probable Cause

1. Where claimant was issued a uniform traffic ticket for violating a regulation prohibiting pickup campers from using a parkway (9 NYCRR 415.6 [e]), which charge was dismissed on the ground that the regulation was unconstitutional, the subsequent finding of unconstitutionality does not vitiate the reasonableness of the officer's actions, claimant having clearly fallen within the ambit of the prohibition, and, therefore, the probable cause was not invalidated and an action for malicious prosecution will not lie.

False Imprisonment -- False Arrest

2. A cause of action for false arrest does not lie when predicated on the mere issuance of a traffic ticket; moreover, a finding of probable cause for a proceeding on a uniform traffic ticket issued pursuant to an unconstitutional regulation mandates a dismissal of the claim.

 

1845 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Pratt v. Allen


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Chemung County


116 Misc. 2d 244; 455 N.Y.S.2d 904; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3862



March 31, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, who were adjacent property owners, brought an N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 action against respondent, a town's superintendent of highways, seeking a judgment prohibiting the creation of a private road that was requested by intervenors, who were property owners, pursuant to N.Y. High. Law § 300 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Intervenors applied for a private road link between their property and a public highway that would pass across petitioners' property. Petitioners' property contained no right of way for the benefit of intervenors' property. In their action to prohibit the superintendent from creating the private road, petitioners contended that the state and federal constitutions prohibited the taking of private property for the gain of private interests and that § 300 et seq. authorized private individuals to use the public power of eminent domain to take private property for exclusively private use. In denying petitioners' requested relief, the court held that the connecting of private property with the public highways was in the public interest; the taking of private property for such a purpose was a public use sufficient to sustain § 300 et seq. as constitutional even in the absence of N.Y. Const. art. I, § 7(c), which authorized the construction of private roads. The court also determined that the underlying petition was not defective for failing to provide a survey of the proposed roadway in courses and distances because N.Y. High. Law §300 et seq. did not require such a survey.


OUTCOME: The court denied relief to petitioners.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Statute Authorizing Private Interests to Condemn Rights of Way Across Private Property

1. The public benefit to which the taking of property by eminent domain is constitutionally limited is not restricted to sole occupancy and use by the public. Accordingly, section 300 et seq. of the Highway Law, which, under certain conditions, permits the taking of the property of one landowner for the construction of a private road to provide access between a public highway and the property of a second landowner, is constitutional since the access provided by such a taking is in the public interest and is a public use sufficient to sustain the statute under both the New York and Federal Constitutions.

Eminent Domain -- Sufficiency of Petition for Private Road

2. A landowners' petition to condemn a right of way across respondent's property pursuant to section 300 et seq. of the Highway Law is legally sufficient despite a failure to allege the necessity of such roadway or to provide a survey of the proposed roadway since the statute contains no requirement that necessity be demonstrated in the petition and only requires a description [***2] of the land to be taken, and a tax map, application and appraisal included with petition adequately fulfill the description requirement; moreover, such a petition need not set forth consequential damages, the correct measure of damages or the existence of other rights of way or easements which provide access to petitioners' property, and is the correct vehicle for the relief sought regardless of whether or not there already exists a right of way over the servient estate.

 

1846 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Civil Service Bar Asso., etc. v. Schwartz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


114 Misc. 2d 849; 452 N.Y.S.2d 478; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3575



March 29, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendants' cross motion for an order awarding summary judgment in their favor dismissing the complaint is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff union filed an action for declaratory judgment against defendants, corporation counsel and related individuals. Both parties moved for summary judgment in their favor.


OVERVIEW: The corporation counsel promulgated a regulation that prohibited attorneys employed by the city's law department from engaging in the private practice of law. The union contended that the corporation counsel lacked the authority to promulgate the challenged regulation. The union claimed that the regulation violated N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law §§ 200, 204 in that it allegedly changed the terms and conditions of employment, a change that could only be effected through the collective bargaining process. The corporation counsel asserted that he derived his authority from the provisions of the New York City Charter §§ 1105, 1120 and the New York City Administrative Code. The court granted defendants' motion for summary judgment and dismissed the complaint. The union's motion was denied. The court held that the Board of Collective Bargaining had exclusive, nondelegable jurisdiction over improper labor practices. Because the city had a comprehensive labor law designed to adjudicate such improper charges, the court lacked subject matter jurisdiction to resolve the union's claim of violation of the Civil Service Law.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendants' cross motion for an order that awarded summary judgment in their favor and dismissed the union's complaint for a declaratory judgment.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service -- Prohibition on Outside Employment

Defendant Corporation Counsel of the City of New York is granted summary judgment dismissing an action by plaintiff civil service attorneys' union for judgment declaring void a regulation promulgated by defendant which prohibits attorneys employed in the office of Corporation Counsel from the private practice of law except in "unusual circumstances" and with defendant's permission; the establishment of such a rule is within defendant's authority to regulate the manner in which the law business of the city shall be conducted (NY City Charter, §§ 391, 394, subd a; § 1105, subd a; § 1120). Further, with respect to plaintiff's contention that the regulation violates sections 200 and 204 of the Civil Service Law by changing the terms and conditions of employment, a change that can only be effected through the collective bargaining process, the Supreme Court lacks jurisdiction to resolve the claim, as the Board of Collective Bargaining has exclusive, nondelegable jurisdiction over improper labor practice charges.

 

1847 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Restuccia


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Oswego, Oswego County


113 Misc. 2d 224; 449 N.Y.S.2d 16; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3280



March 19, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the defendant's motion is denied and a trial date of Wednesday, April 28, 1982 at 2:00 p.m. is hereby set.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the accusatory instrument against her on the ground that the animal control ordinance was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the accusatory instrument against her on the ground that the animal control ordinance was unconstitutionally vague. The court denied the motion and set a trial date. The court held that although what constituted persistent or constant barking, howling, whining, snarling, or growling could have been subject to some judicial interpretation, the terms were certainly understandable so that persons of average education could appreciate the conduct which was prohibited. The court held that the fact that many homes in the city were located in very close proximity to each other, some even a matter of inches apart, made the ordinance all the more reasonable for the protection of those persons who might have been persistently or constantly awakened or otherwise disturbed by an animal which might have persistently or consistently barked, howled or whined, snarled or growled.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion and set a trial date.


HEADNOTES

Animals -- Animal Control Ordinance

A motion to dismiss an accusatory instrument on the ground that a local animal control ordinance is unconstitutionally vague is denied inasmuch as the terms of the ordinance are understandable and persons of average education can appreciate the conduct which is prohibited; furthermore, the fact that many homes in the locale are in close proximity to each other makes the ordinance all the more reasonable for the protection of those who might be constantly disturbed by the persistent barking, howling, whining, snarling or growling of an animal.

 

1848 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Rosa v. Mohan Kulkarni Unibell Anesthesia, P.C.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


113 Misc. 2d 39; 448 N.Y.S.2d 400; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3252



March 10, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, anesthesiologist, physicians, and others, in three separate actions that were brought by plaintiff patients, filed motions for an order that directed the referral of each action to a medical malpractice panel pursuant to N.Y. Judiciary Law § 148-a. In a fourth action, plaintiff, patient's executor (executor), filed a motion requesting that his action against defendant doctor be set for trial without submission to a § 148-a panel.


OVERVIEW: In the first action, the patient claimed that the anesthesiologist's delay in reporting to the delivery room compelled her to give birth without any anesthesia. In the second action, the claim arose out of the physician's lack of investigation into the patient's complaint of rectal bleeding that was eventually diagnosed as colon cancer. In the third action, the patient claimed that he was misinformed about the malignancy of a node in his left groin. In the fourth action, the executor claimed that the doctor refused to hospitalize the patient, despite the fact that the patient had a tumor. Defendants in the first three actions filed motions to have the matters referred to a § 148-a panel, while the executor in the fourth action sought a trial of his case without its submission to a § 148-a panel. All actions were consolidated for decision and the court denied defendants' motions in the first three actions and conditionally granted the executor's motion. The court held that in each action there were factual disputes that were raised by the parties that precluded a finding by a § 148-a panel. It was constitutionally impermissible for a panel to make factual findings.


OUTCOME: In the first three actions, the court denied defendants' motions for an order that directed the referral of the actions to a medical malpractice panel. In the fourth action, the court conditionally granted the executor's motion for a trial without the submission of the matter to a medical malpractice panel.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Physicians and Surgeons -- Medical Malpractice Mediation Panel
