The Supreme Court has the discretion to determine that certain medical malpratice cases should not be submitted to a medical malpractice panel under section 148-a of the Judiciary Law, and said discretion was properly exercised when, in several medical malpractice cases where there were basic factual disputes between the parties which would preclude the panel from functioning constitutionally in making any finding, the court ordered that the cases proceed to trial without first being submitted to a medical malpractice panel.

 

1849 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Young v. Binghamton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Broome County


112 Misc. 2d 1017; 447 N.Y.S.2d 1017; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3233



March 9, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In view of the observations made, the motion for summary judgment is granted, without motion costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city filed a motion for summary judgment to dismiss an action initiated by plaintiff landowners who alleged that they suffered damages due to a delay in construction that was negligently or maliciously wantonly, and unlawfully caused by the city, depriving the landowners of their civil rights under the United States Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The landowners applied to the city to rezone their property. The city council eventually voted in favor of the rezoning after hearing both opposition and approval of the rezoning. The mayor vetoed the action and the city council voted to override the mayor's veto. The city's corporation counsel filed an opinion with the county clerk, stating that the veto override was invalid. As a result, the landowners were denied a building permit. A proceeding was held, finding that the override vote was valid, which was affirmed on appeal, and a building permit was eventually issued. The landowners brought an action against the city for damages, alleging that due to a construction delay negligently or maliciously, wantonly, and unlawfully caused by the city, they were deprived of their constitutional civil rights. The city filed a motion for summary judgment. The court granted the city summary judgment, ruling that the evidence established that although the landowners may have experienced some deprivation of their property interests due to the opinion of the city's corporation counsel, there was no intrusion on their right to contract and no denial of any of their constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court granted the city's motion for summary judgment in an action brought by the landowners alleging that they were deprived of their constitutional civil rights due to a construction delay negligently or wantonly, unlawfully and maliciously caused by the city.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Liability for Refusal to Issue Building Permit

The wrongful refusal of defendant city to issue a building permit to plaintiffs based upon an opinion of the city's Corporation Counsel that the vote of the city council overriding the Mayor's veto of the council's approval of plaintiffs' application for a rezoning of the land in question was invalid, the procedures involving the council's override vote having subsequently been ruled valid in court, cannot give rise to an action against the city under State law for damages suffered by plaintiffs as the result of the delay in construction since the act of the Corporation Counsel was discretionary and quasi-judicial in character and the city and the Corporation Counsel are thus both immune from liability for such act however erroneous or maliciously inspired it might have been; although a municipality is not immune from suit for alleged Federal civil rights violations (US Code, tit 42, § 1983), the city's refusal to issue the building permit did not violate any constitutional rights of plaintiffs, inasmuch [***2] as only the land was affected and no contract right was unconstitutionally impaired, nor is there any deprivation of property without due process of law since there is nothing to indicate that the value of the use of property was reduced or that there was any inhibition imposed upon other applications of the property to fruitful enterprises and the fact that the land may not have been put to its most profitable use does not make the activities of the municipality improper or unconstitutional.

 

1850 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Bates v. County of Steuben


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Steuben County


113 Misc. 2d 68; 448 N.Y.S.2d 625; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3255



March 5, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the court grants the relief requested by the petitioner.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner district attorney sought mandamus relief from respondent county's refusal to pay the district attorney's salary pursuant to N.Y. Jud. Law § 183-a. In response, the county claimed that the action was barred by N.Y. C.P.L.R. 217's time limitation and that N.Y. Jud. Law § 183-a was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: After the district attorney's office was made full-time, pursuant to N.Y. County Law § 700(8), (10) and N.Y. Jud. Law § 183-a, the district attorney requested payment in accordance with § 183-a. When the county failed to respond, the district attorney continued to accept the established payment. After a year, however, the district attorney sought mandamus relief from the county's refusal to pay the requested statutory salary. In response, the county claimed that the action was barred by N.Y. C.P.L.R. 217's time limitation and that N.Y. Jud. Law § 183-a was unconstitutional. In granting the requested relief, the court held the action was not time barred because: 1) the district attorney had not known of the refusal until the year's final paycheck; and 2) the time barrier had been inapplicable as the county had failed to comply with a continuing statutory duty. The court also rejected the contention that § 183-a was an unconstitutional exercise of legislative authority in that it did not qualify as a general law under N.Y. Const. art. IX, §§ 2(b)(2), 3(d)(1) because N.Y. Jud. Law § 183-a was a general rather than a special law, notwithstanding its population classification.


OUTCOME: The court granted the district attorney relief from the county's refusal to pay the district attorney's statutory salary.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of Actions -- Proceeding against Body or Officer

1. A CPLR article 78 proceeding in the nature of mandamus, commenced in January of 1982 by petitioner District Attorney to compel respondent county to pay his salary for 1981 in accordance with the provisions of section 183-a of the Judiciary Law, is not time barred, as respondent failed to reply to a written demand for said salary made in April of 1981 by petitioner and the four-month period of limitation imposed under CPLR 217 commences at the time petitioner's demand is refused; furthermore, petitioner cannot be charged with knowledge that his claim had been refused until December 31, 1981, when, in his last paycheck, he did not receive an amount sufficient to place his annual salary at the level dictated by section 183-a and, even if respondent had communicated a refusal to the claim, the time barrier would not apply since the claim involves a failure by respondent to comply with a continuing constitutional or statutory duty.

District and Prosecuting Attorneys -- Statutory Compensation Plan -- Population Based Classification

 [***2] 2. Petitioner, the full-time District Attorney of Steuben County, whose term of office commenced January 1, 1981, is entitled to be paid in accordance with section 183-a of the Judiciary Law at the same annual rate as a Steuben County Court Judge, as set for the years 1981 and 1982 by section 14 of chapter 881 of the Laws of 1980; section 183-a of the Judiciary Law is a validly enacted general law binding upon respondent local government (NY Const, art IX, § 2, subd [b], par [2]) and the population based classification of counties under the statute has a rational basis. Furthermore, said compensation plan is not inconsistent with section 201 of the County Law, which authorizes the county board of supervisors to set the salaries of county officers, inasmuch as, even assuming that section 201 applies to petitioner's position, the board is still free to set his salary above the minimum level established under section 183-a of the Judiciary Law.

 

1851 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


City of Buffalo v. Rein


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


120 Misc. 2d 611; 466 N.Y.S.2d 108; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4116



February 24, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant Rein's motion to dismiss plaintiff City of Buffalo's complaint is denied and defendant is directed to submit an order in conformance with this memorandum decision.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant taxpayer filed a motion to dismiss plaintiff city's complaint and the taxpayer challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 926(1) as violating the equal protection of the laws for unreasonably discriminating between residents and nonresidents in its impositions of personal liability for real property tax.


OVERVIEW: The court denied the taxpayer's motion to dismiss the complaint. The taxpayer argued that exempting nonresidents from personal liability deprived residents of the equal protection of the laws. The court disagreed and held that § 926(1) did not distinguish between state and non-state residents for which long-arm in personam considerations were applicable but rather only between residents or nonresidents of the city or town in which the real property was situated. The legislative intent regarding real property taxation was for there to have been a distinction between personal liability for local residential owners and in rem liability for nonresidential owners to the extent of the value of the real property. The court determined that the taxpayer had not met his burden.


OUTCOME: The court denied the taxpayer's motion to dismiss the city's complaint.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Assessment -- Personal Liability for Taxes

A taxpayer who asserted that subdivision 1 of section 926 of the Real Property Tax Law was an unconstitutional violation of equal protection because it imposes personal liability for real property taxes on residents of a taxing district but only in rem liability on property owned by nonresidents, has failed to meet his heavy burden to negate every conceivable basis upon which the rationale of the legislative classification was based, since throughout the history of real property taxation the distinction between resident and nonresident has existed as a way of recognizing the differing benefits residents personally received from the taxes, the limits of jurisdiction possessed by the assessment officers, and the difficulty of notifying nonresidents regarding changes in assessments and methods of grieving them; although today's nonresident is capable of receiving adequate notice, the fact that only one day per year is set aside for review of assessments [***2] produces similar due process considerations upon which the distinction may be maintained.

 

1852 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Mass v. Blum


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


112 Misc. 2d 898; 447 N.Y.S.2d 660; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3210



February 22, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The cross motion of the defendants for judgment is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action by plaintiff for a declaratory judgment and related relief, which placed in issue the constitutionality of N.Y. A.P.A. Law § 301(2), plaintiff and defendant commissioner filed cross motions for judgment.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff's position was that § 301(2) impermissibly denied judicial review with respect to the sufficiency of the notice given of an adjudicatory proceeding. The court held that the statute was not inescapably unconstitutional. The court held that its inquiry must end if any state of facts, known or to be assumed, justify the law. The court held that the statute did not limit due process because the very arguments that prompted the action could have been made at the administrative hearing where they would have been considered and ruled upon. The court held that a preliminary inquiry with respect to the constitutionality of the statute was unwarranted.


OUTCOME: The court granted the commissioner's cross motion for judgment.


HEADNOTES

Administrative Law -- Judicial Review

Subdivision 2 of section 301 of the State Administrative Procedure Act does not unconstitutionally deny judicial review with respect to the sufficiency of the notice given of an adjudicatory proceeding, insofar as it provides that the agency's finding as to the sufficiency of definiteness or detail of the statement furnished or its failure or refusal to furnish a more detailed statement shall not be subject to judicial review. Procedural due process does not provide for judicial review of each separate determination which may be involved in an ultimate finding; the purpose of the statute is to insulate preliminary or intermediate rulings from judicial review for purposes of expediency and to preserve the finality doctrine long associated with appeals from administrative determinations.

 

1853 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Pecoraro v. Buffalo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


112 Misc. 2d 985; 447 N.Y.S.2d 842; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3227



February 22, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a vending company and its owner, brought an action against defendant city officials, seeking to obtain a judgment under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 30 that Buffalo, N.Y., Ordinance ch. 7, § 27(9), which governed amusement arcades, was unconstitutional. The company sought summary judgment. The court issued a preliminary injunction enjoining the officials from taking any action to enforce the ordinance pending a determination.


OVERVIEW: The vending company contended that the passage of the ordinance constituted an ex post facto law, in violation of U.S. Const. art. I, § 9. The ordinance defined amusement parks, imposed licensing conditions and fees, limited the hours children could attend, and placed limits on the hours of operation. The court concluded that the company failed to carry the burden to show that the enactment violated the United States Constitution. The court denied the request for summary judgment and lifted the preliminary injunction, but it did rule that one section of the ordinance was invalid. The court held that a provision prohibiting certain hours of operation was arbitrary and not reasonably related to some manifest evil sought to be controlled. However, the court found that the provision was separable from the remainder of the ordinance, so the ordinance was not otherwise rendered invalid.


OUTCOME: The court denied the request for summary judgment, except that it found one provision of the ordinance to be invalid. Nevertheless, it held that the remainder of the ordinance was valid because removal of the invalid provision did not impair the remainder of the ordinance.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal Corporations -- Regulation of "Amusement Arcades"

The provision of an ordinance of the City of Buffalo regulating amusement arcades which defines an amusement arcade as a place having four or more coin-operated amusement devices is not violative of equal protection since a decision that only those places having four or more machines need regulation is not unreasonable in view of the intent of the ordinance of discouraging truancy nor does the fact that other forms of "amusement" are not similarly regulated violate equal protection since a legislative body may deal with one part of a problem without addressing all of it; the provision of the ordinance prohibiting all persons under the age of 16 years from being in an amusement arcade during school hours on any weekday the city's public schools are in session is not lacking a rational basis and does not constitutionally infringe First Amendment rights; however, the provision of the ordinance prohibiting the operation of amusement devices between the hours of 11:00 p.m. and 10:00 a.m. bears no rational relationship to the purpose of the ordinance and is therefore invalid; since the legislative body retains the power [***2] to grant licenses, the absence of formulated licensing standards in the ordinance does not render the ordinance unconstitutional; plaintiffs have not met their burden of showing that the $ 75 licensing fee for every coin-controlled amusement device located in an amusement arcade is excessive and not reasonably related to the cost of administering the ordinance.

 

1854 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Debra B. v. Manuel R.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Queens County


113 Misc. 2d 152; 448 N.Y.S.2d 353; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3261



February 18, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Commissioner of Social Services filed suit seeking enforcement of an accumulated child support arrears against respondent father after both natural parents gave up all parental rights to the minor child in question under N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 398(6)(f). The arrears was based on a prior order directing the father to pay child support for the benefit of the Department of Social Services.


OVERVIEW: The minor child was in foster care placement because both biological parents had surrendered their parental rights. The Commissioner brought the petition as the assignee of the mother. The court found that: (1) while the statute in question relieved a surrendering mother of any and all liability for the support of an out-of-wedlock child, it failed to mention any provision for a surrendering father; (2) if the statute were given a literal reading, constitutional problems would arise because of its failure to treat both the mother and the father equally; (3) it was proper, therefore, to read the statute as also ceasing the financial liability of the father as to a minor child who was the subject of a surrender instrument; and (4) therefore there was no basis for the petition for accumulated arrears.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition for the accumulated arrears.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Children Born out of Wedlock -- Support

Section 398 (subd 6, par [f]) of the Social Services Law, which relieves a surrendering mother of any and all liability for the support of an out-of-wedlock child, but makes no mention of a surrendering father, must be read in accordance with principles of gender neutrality so that the father's financial liability also ceases upon execution of surrender instruments. Accordingly, a proceeding by the Commissioner of Social Services, as assignor of the mother, seeking enforcement of accumulated arrears of $ 2,600 against respondent father, is dismissed where both natural parents have executed surrender instruments giving up all parental rights.

 

1855 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Buffalo v. Wysocki


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


112 Misc. 2d 543; 447 N.Y.S.2d 386; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3164



February 11, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a class action suit brought by plaintiff city, defendants, individual and corporate taxpayers, filed a motion for summary judgment against the city for the refund of real property taxes paid from 1974 through 1978.


OVERVIEW: The city commenced a class action against the taxpayers. The court certified two major classes of taxpayers: those who allegedly paid their taxes under protest and those who did not. The taxpayers answered and counterclaimed for a refund of their taxes illegally collected by the city from 1974 through 1978. The city uniformly replied to each counterclaim by asserting the affirmative defense of the one-year limitation in the city charter. First, the court held that the taxpayers' claims for tax refunds were timely asserted because an action to recover taxes paid on an unconstitutional levy was based upon an implied contractual obligation that was subject to a six-year statute of limitations which accrued when the taxes were paid. Second, the court held that only those taxpayers who paid their taxes under appropriate legal protest were entitled to refunds. Illegal taxes voluntarily paid were not recoverable. Moreover, taxpayers could recover refunds for each year that their checks or receipts were stamped "paid under protest."


OUTCOME: The court granted summary judgment to those taxpayers who could prove that they paid their taxes under protest, but the city was entitled to summary judgment against those taxpayers who did not make appropriate legal protest of their taxes.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Limitation of Actions -- What Statute Governs -- Action for Refund of Unconstitutional Tax

1. Inasmuch as an action to recover taxes paid on an unconstitutional levy is based upon an implied contractual obligation and thus subject to a six-year Statute of Limitations, a provision of the Charter of the City of Buffalo which provides a one-year limitation period for the commencement of an action to test the validity of a tax does not bar an action in which defendants seek a refund of property taxes which the city had unconstitutionally levied from 1974 through 1978, since the action is not one to test the validity of the tax but rather to recover taxes paid which had already been held unconstitutional for each of the four years in question.

Judgments -- Collateral Estoppel -- Application to Multiple Taxpayer Suits

2. The doctrine of collateral estoppel has no application to multiple taxpayer suits; accordingly, although the City of Buffalo failed to assert a defense of a one-year Statute of Limitations contained in its city charter in any prior taxpayer suits, it is not collaterally estopped from asserting that defense in a pending multiple taxpayer suit.

 [***2] Taxation -- Illegal Taxes -- Payment under Protest or Duress

3. A taxpayer may not recover for payment of illegal taxes unless they were paid under protest or duress; accordingly, defendant taxpayers who failed to protest their payment of taxes unconstitutionally levied by the City of Buffalo are not entitled to refunds since the city's knowledge of the illegality of the tax does not obviate the protest requirement and the nonprotesting defendants did not pay under duress since they were neither confronted with a severe interest penalty nor was their liberty or the immediate possession of their property threatened.

Taxation -- Illegal Taxes -- Manner of Protest

4. The protest required of a taxpayer in order to recover for payment of an illegal tax must be made in writing, at the time of payment, in each year for which recovery is sought, but need not specify the legal basis upon which the taxes are involuntarily paid; accordingly, in an action to recover for payment of taxes unconstitutionally levied by the City of Buffalo, taxpayers who sent letters of protest with their checks, indorsed "paid in protest" on their checks or requested that the taxing authority similarly [***3] stamp their tax receipts are entitled to refunds for the tax years in which protest was made.

 

1856 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Kelsey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, Third District, Suffolk County


112 Misc. 2d 927; 447 N.Y.S.2d 637; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3215



February 10, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In view of the above, the court finds that the ordinance, section 166-17 of the Code of the Town of Huntington, is unconstitutional and, therefore, the defendant's motion to dismiss is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss after defendant was charged with violating a city ordinance requiring persons dealing with shellfish to obtain a permit. In moving to dismiss, defendant claimed the ordinance was unconstitutional because the state had pre-empted legislation of the shellfish industry.


OVERVIEW: A city ordinance required all persons dealing with shellfish to obtain a permit. When defendant was charged with having violated the ordinance, defendant moved to dismiss, claiming the ordinance was unconstitutional because the state had pre-empted legislation of the shellfish industry. In granting defendant's motion, the court held the ordinance unconstitutionally prohibited state law because the ordinance prohibited an individual from dealing in the wholesale trade of shellfish even if the individual was licensed by the state and permitted to deal in the shellfish industry under state law.


OUTCOME: In granting defendant's motion to dismiss that was filed after defendant was charged with violating a city ordinance requiring persons dealing with shellfish to obtain a permit, the court held the ordinance was unconstitutional because the state had pre-empted legislation of the shellfish industry.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- State Pre-emption -- Local Law which Prohibits Activity Permissible under State Law

Where a provision of the Code of the Town of Huntington prohibited defendant from dealing in the wholesale trade of shellfish unless he first obtained a permit as prescribed by the code even though he was licensed to engage in such business by the New York State Department of Environmental Conservation pursuant to ECL 13-0315 (subd 3, par d), defendant's motion to dismiss a charge of violating said provision on the ground that it is unconstitutional and otherwise invalid is granted; where the extension of a principle of State law by means of a local law causes that which is permissible under the former to be a violation of the latter, the local law is unauthorized; moreover, where as here, the State has surveyed the field to be regulated, manifested an intent to occupy the entire field and set up standards which are proper as evidenced by ECL article 13 [***2] and the regulations promulgated thereunder (6 NYCRR Ch 1, Subch F), those standards may not be made more rigorous or extensive by local authority.

 

1857 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Brian S.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Richmond County


112 Misc. 2d 561; 447 N.Y.S.2d 382; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3166



February 10, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, commissioner and guardians, filed an action to determine whether a summons initiating a child abuse proceeding under Fam. Ct. Act § 10 could be served outside New York on respondent nonresident father, where all the alleged acts of abuse took place in Illinois.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners filed an action to determine whether a summons could be served on the non-resident father. Petitioners argued that service could be made in Illinois, where the non-resident father resided. Upon review, the court held that the summons could not be served on the non-resident father, an Illinois resident, in that state. The court noted that petitioners' argument was without merit. The court further noted that it could not ignore the obvious meaning of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 314. The court recognized that Rule 314, which authorized out-of-state service in certain actions, applied only to in rem actions. The court further noted that if it applied petitioners' interpretation of Rule 314, the statute would be rendered unconstitutional. The court also recognized that Fam. Ct. Act § 1036 permitted the substituted service of a summons in a child protective proceeding. However, the court noted that the substituted service had to take place in New York.


OUTCOME: The court found that the summons could not be served on the non-resident father in a child abuse proceeding.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Process -- Service on Nonresidents -- Child Protective Proceeding

A summons initiating a child abuse proceeding under article 10 of the Family Court Act may not be served outside New York on a nonresident natural father where all the alleged acts of abuse took place in Illinois since section 154 of the Family Court Act limits service of process to this State and a Family Court proceeding is not an action in rem so as to permit out-of-State service under section 165 of the Family Court Act and CPLR 302 and, also, because the substituted service permitted in a child protective proceeding by subdivision (c) of section 1036 of the Family Court Act concerns service within this State.

 

1858 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Camme


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Queens County


112 Misc. 2d 792; 447 N.Y.S.2d 621; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3194



February 8, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants filed a motion to suppress physical evidence obtained by the police pursuant to a search warrant they obtained after determining that a defendant that owned an auto salvage yard did not have a dismantler's license as required by N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 415-a.


OVERVIEW: A defendant that owned the auto salvage yard argued that N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 415-a was unconstitutional. The court rejected the argument because pursuant to that law the auto dismantling industry was pervasively regulated, and therefore warrantless administrative searches were an exception under U.S. Const. amend. IV. Furthermore, § 415-a was not vague because it gave notice to auto dismantlers that their premises were subject to inspection. Thus a search of the auto salvage yard upon the police observing the dismantling of a car and finding that the defendant owner of the salvage yard did not have a dismantler's license was proper. One defendant that was physically made to return to the salvage yard, prior to the police observing the dismantling of the car, was seized within the meaning of the Fourth Amendment, and the seizure was unlawful because at that point there was no reasonable suspicion that a crime had been committed. A search warrant obtained by police was not defective for having the wrong address when it contained the right name of the business, and even though undated it was executed within 10 days of issuance as required by N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 690.30(1).


OUTCOME: The court upheld the constitutionality of the Vehicle and Traffic Law as it permitted warrantless administrative searches of auto dismantlers. The search warrant obtained by the police was deemed valid. The court found one defendant who was arrested before evidence of a crime was found had his constitutional rights violated and the seizure of property against him was improper and suppressed. Otherwise, the motion to suppress was denied.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute

1. The Legislature has demonstrated its intention to pervasively regulate the vehicle dismantling industry by the enactment of section 415-a of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, and since the performance required under the statute is set forth with sufficient clarity so that a person of common intelligence can ascertain its meaning, said statute is constitutional and a warrantless administrative search conducted pursuant thereto was proper.

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure

2. The seizure of defendant's person as he was walking away from a vehicle dismantling shop which was suspected of operating illegally, by a police officer who was investigating the shop, was unlawful and the fruits thereof must be suppressed where the officer did not have probable cause or even a reasonable suspicion to believe that defendant had committed a crime.

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Probable Cause

3. An experienced police officer, who observed defendant as the latter was cutting up a relatively new car in a vehicle dismantling shop which was suspected of operating illegally, acted reasonably by seizing defendant and [***2] taking him to the shop's office while the car and some license plates which had been near the car were being investigated, since the officer's observation gave rise to probable cause to believe that a crime had been or was being committed.

Crimes -- Search Warrant -- Technical Errors

4. A search warrant which improperly listed the premises to be searched as "Jasons II Auto Salvage" at "94-40 Merrick Blvd." instead of "Jasons Auto Salvage II" at "94-04 Merrick Blvd." was proper and valid where it was clear that the premises were authorized to be searched, that they could be identified with reasonable effort and that there was no probability of a search being made of premises other than those intended to be searched under the warrant, since technical errors in a portion of the description of the premises to be searched will not invalidate a warrant under those circumstances.

Crimes -- Search Warrant -- Undated Warrant

5. An undated search warrant is valid where the time of execution is not an issue, since nowhere is it required that there be a date on the warrant itself (see CPL 690.45), and since an unintentional dating error has been held not to affect the validity [***3] of the warrant ( People v Horton, 32 AD2d 707).

 

1859 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. McMillan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Monroe County


112 Misc. 2d 901; 447 N.Y.S.2d 626; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3211



February 8, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The defendant, who has been charged with two counts of driving while intoxicated and two traffic violations, sought to suppress the result of his breath analysis.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, pulled over for speeding, was able to walk normally and to pass the finger to nose field test, but the officer nevertheless requested defendant to submit to an alco-sensor prescreening test and he complied. The prescreening test result indicated that the blood alcoholic content of the defendant was more than the legal limit, and he was arrested for driving while intoxicated. Defendant was given the Miranda warnings, followed by a breathalyzer test and completion of an alcoholic influence report form. Defendant claimed that when the police officer stopped his vehicle to issue him an appearance ticket for speeding, he was in custody and entitled to Miranda warnings. The People argued that a stop of this nature did not constitute custody. The court found that the issuance of a speeding ticket was not tantamount to an arrest and, therefore, the Miranda warnings were given at an appropriate time. Thus, the evidence of the breathalyzer test was admissible.


OUTCOME: The result of the breathalyzer test administered to defendant was deemed admissible at trial.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Motor Vehicles -- Driving while Intoxicated -- Need for Miranda Warnings after Traffic Stop -- Alco-Sensor Prescreening Test

Defendant's statement that he had been drinking beer made after a police officer stopped defendant for speeding, observed defendant's glazed and bloodshot eyes, smelled the odor of alcohol and then inquired of defendant if he had been drinking, is not suppressed because of the absence of constitutional warnings since the traffic stop of defendant's vehicle for the purpose of issuing defendant an appearance ticket for speeding is not an arrest but merely an investigatory rather than custodial situation not requiring Miranda warnings even though defendant could not have left the scene until the traffic ticket was handed to him; the extraction of breath from a defendant for purposes of an alco-sensor prescreening test is equivalent to a search and seizure requiring probable cause but the issues of probable cause and the constitutionality of the statute authorizing the test (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 1193-a) need not be reached since defendant voluntarily submitted to the test without coercive threats; accordingly, the police officer's observations [***2] of defendant, defendant's admission and the results of the prescreening test, taken together, provided sufficient probable cause for defendant's arrest for driving while intoxicated and the result of the breathalyzer test administered to defendant can therefore be admitted at trial.

 

1860 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hedges


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, First District, Suffolk County


112 Misc. 2d 632; 447 N.Y.S.2d 1007; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3177



February 5, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought dismissal of the criminal charges against him for possession of untagged shellfish, N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 13-0317, and unlawful possession of seed clams, N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 13-0325. He also sought suppression of evidence on the ground of an illegal search.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with possession of untagged shellfish, N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 13-0317, and unlawful possession of seed clams, N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 13-0325. He sought dismissal of the informations, or, in the alternative, suppression of evidence. Defendant's motion to dismiss was denied pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 170.30. Police had entered defendant's place of work and searched for contraband without a warrant under the entry powers of N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 71-0525. That statute permitted police to enter without a warrant any building, other than a dwelling, to inspect for the protection of shellfish or crustacea. The court granted the motion to dismiss. No state may require, as a condition of doing business, a blanket submission to warrantless searches at any time and for any purpose. Section 71-0525 did limit the scope of the search. But it did not limit the time or place at which a search was permitted. Where the search was not routine, but undertaken because police had cause to believe that they would find evidence of a crime, a warrant was required.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the charges against him for unlawful possession of untagged shellfish, and unlawful possession of seed clams. The court granted his motion to suppress evidence.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Warrantless Search of Pervasively Regulated Industry

1. Motion by defendant, charged with violating certain provisions of the ECL pertaining to the pervasively regulated shellfish industry, to suppress evidence gathered pursuant to a warrantless search and seizure authorized by ECL 3-0301 (subd 2, par g), 13-0319, 71-0525 and 6 NYCRR 42.5 (b) is granted; inasmuch as a warrantless search of a pervasively regulated industry is invalid unless authorized by a statutory scheme that is carefully limited in time, place and scope, the instant search and seizure is invalid since ECL 3-0301 (subd 2, par g) and 71-0525 provide no limitations as to time or place and ECL 13-0319 and 6 NYCRR 42.5 (b) provide no limitations at all; moreover, the search cannot be deemed to have been consented to on the ground that 6 NYCRR 42.5 (b) requires acquiescence to searches as a condition to the grant of a permit to engage in shellfishing since no State may require a blanket submission to warrantless searches as a condition of doing business.

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Warrant Required for Search Based upon "Cause to Believe" Crime is Being  [***2] Committed

2. ECL 71-0525 (subd 1, par b), which authorizes warrantless searches of buildings used in connection with the shellfish industry where government officials have "cause to believe" that there has been or is an ongoing violation of the ECL, is constitutionally infirm since, although a routine, periodic search of a pervasively regulated industry may be undertaken without a warrant if authorized by a valid statutory scheme, a search warrant must be obtained prior to a search undertaken by government officials who have "cause to believe" they will find evidence of a crime; accordingly, defendant's motion to suppress evidence seized during a warrantless search conducted by police officers who allegedly had "cause to believe" that violations of the ECL were being committed is granted.

 

1861 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Picciotto v. McGuire


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


112 Misc. 2d 763; 447 N.Y.S.2d 615; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3189



February 5, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Therefore the determination of the Trustees is annulled and respondent is directed to retire petitioner on accident disability retirement retroactive to the date of retirement on ordinary disability.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner police officer, brought a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding, seeking a judgment to annul a determination of respondent board of trustees of a pension fund, which denied the officer's application for an accidental disability retirement pension.


OVERVIEW: While descending the front stairwell of the precinct building, the officer slipped and fell down the stairs, injuring his knee. The medical board of the police pension fund examined the officer and determined that he was disabled to due a knee injury; the medical board unanimously recommended that he be granted accidental disability status. The board of trustees denied the officer's application for an accidental disability retirement pension on the ground that his injury was not accidental because there had been no intervention of an external fortuitous event, proximately causing the injury. In annulling the board of trustees' determination, the court held that the board of trustees acted capriciously in failing to rely on the unanimous findings of the medical board and in relying upon a standard that had no statutory basis. Specifically, the court found that the board of trustees' standard that an injury be caused by the intervention of an external fortuitous event flew in the face of New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § B18-43.0, which did not require such proof.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the board of trustees' determination and directed that the board of trustees retire the officer on accident disability retirement.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service -- Retirement and Pension Benefits -- Accidental Disability Retirement

The Board of Trustees of the Police Pension Fund acted without authority when it promulgated a resolution defining accident or accidental injury as one proximately caused by an external, unexpected and unusual fortuitous event, since the intended effect of said resolution was to change the clear language of section B18-43.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York, which requires only that a police force member be incapacitated for the performance of city service, as a natural and proximate result of such city service to qualify for accidental disability retirement. Accordingly, where petitioner, who injured his knee when he slipped and fell down several stairs, was concededly injured while on duty and in the line of duty and was incapacitated from performing his duties, the board of trustees, in failing to rely on the unanimous findings and report of its medical board that petitioner [***2] be granted accidental disability status, acted capriciously and unreasonably, and it acted in an arbitrary manner in denying petitioner's application for accidental disability retirement on the ground that an external proximate cause was not proved; the determination is annulled and respondent is directed to retire petitioner on accident disability retirement retroactive to the date of retirement on ordinary disability.

 

1862 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Hunyadi v. Smith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Wyoming County


112 Misc. 2d 484; 447 N.Y.S.2d 226; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3151



February 4, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate filed a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding against respondent correctional superintendent seeking to expunge a disciplinary report against him based on statements made by the inmate in a grievance filed against a correctional officer. The petition was initially dismissed, but the court sua sponte granted reconsideration.


OVERVIEW: The initial dismissal of the petition was based on a finding that the disciplinary report had not been filed as a reprisal, but because the officer had sincerely believed the inmate's statements in the grievance to be lies. The grievance had been based on the allegation that the officer was failing to consistently honor a pass authorizing the inmate to go to certain areas. The court found that: (1) the disciplinary report had been based solely on the grievance statements; (2) a grievance was privileged and immune from use as the basis of a disciplinary report for lying; (3) the potential to use a grievance as the basis of a disciplinary report would threaten the inmate's First Amendment rights; (4) the filing of the grievance was protected under N.Y. Correct. Law § 139; and (5) it was therefore proper to expunge the disciplinary report.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that the disciplinary report based on the grievance should be expunged from the inmate's record and that any material drawn from the grievance should be barred from use as the basis of a disciplinary report or sanction.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Prisons and Prisoners -- Discipline of Inmates -- Grievance Procedures -- Privilege Accorded Material Contained in Grievance

A disciplinary report for lying filed by a correction officer against a prisoner based solely upon alleged false statements made by the prisoner in a grievance filed against the officer is an unconstitutional abridgement of First Amendment rights and thus cannot stand since, although the State has an interest in protecting the integrity of its correctional system through disciplinary reports, there is no showing that the concomitant effect of discouraging the filing of grievances provided for by statute (Correction Law, § 139) is necessary to that purpose; material contained in a grievance must be privileged and immune from use as a basis for a disciplinary report for lying since any possibility of reprisal must be avoided in order to have the system of informal settlement of inmate grievances operate smoothly.

 

1863 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Diocese of Buffalo v. Buczkowski


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


112 Misc. 2d 336; 446 N.Y.S.2d 1015; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3135



January 29, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the foregoing reasons, the decision of the zoning board in denying a use permit to the petitioner for the proposed intermediate care facility is upheld.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner religious diocese and intervening petitioner disabilities agency filed an action seeking to overturn respondent government agents' denial of a use permit applied for by the diocese and disabilities agency. The purpose of the use permit was to change the program of a building from a residential care institution for predelinquent and delinquent young men to an intermediate care facility for the developmentally handicapped.


OVERVIEW: The diocese and disabilities agency argued that the proposed use permit did not constitute a change in use that required approval of the government agents. They also argued that the denial of the use permit contravened the state's policy of favoring implementation of intermediate care facilities and that denial of the permit unconstitutionally interfered with the diocese's religious function. The court rejected such arguments and upheld the denial of the use permit. The court found that the proposed change in use was not a more restrictive change under the zoning ordinance. Also, the court rejected the argument that the proposed use was not a change in use at all, as the facts and circumstances demonstrated that the use of the facility would be changed. The court also noted that a state policy favoring implementation of programs such as the one at issue did not require that local laws be disregarded. Finally, the court noted that the denial of the proposed use permit did not unconstitutionally interfere with any religious function because the proposed use was not actually a religious use.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the denial of the use permit requested by the diocese and the disabilities agency.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Change to More Restrictive Use Classification

1. Petitioner's application for a use permit to change the program of a building from a Boys Town, a permissible nonconforming use, to an intermediate care facility for the developmentally disabled was properly denied by the Buffalo Zoning Board since the Buffalo Zoning Ordinance prohibits a change in use of a building devoted to a nonconforming use unless the change is to a more restrictive use classification and a change in use from one nonprofit institution, permitted in zone R4, to another such institution, also permitted in zone R4, is merely a change to an equally restrictive use; moreover, such change does not entail a change to a more restrictive use classification since, although the use of the particular building as a Boys Town might not be permitted in zone R4, the use of a building as a Boys Town is permitted and thus the use of the premises before was one permitted in zone R4 and the use proposed is permitted in zone R4.

 [***2] Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Change in Use of Nonprofit Institution

2. A provision of the Buffalo Zoning Ordinance which governs changes in nonconforming uses is applicable to a proposed change of the program of a building from a Boys Town to an intermediate care facility for the developmentally disabled since, although a change from one type of nonprofit institution to another does not constitute a change in use classification under the ordinance, the proposed change would result in an increase in the intensity of supervision and activity and, therefore, does constitute a change in use.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Input of Municipality into Site Selection of Intermediate Care Facility

3. The Buffalo Zoning Board's denial of a use permit for an intermediate care facility intended to house 30 developmentally disabled persons with 53 supervisory attendants on the ground that the proposed change in use violates the city's zoning ordinance does not contravene State policy favoring implementation of such facilities since the statutes which form the basis of that policy do not expressly or by any reasonable inference require that local law be disregarded [***3] and, since it is the policy of the State as evidenced in section 41.34 of the Mental Hygiene Law to allow municipalities input into the site selection of all intermediate care facilities which are to house 4 to 14 mentally disabled persons (14 NYCRR 681.3 [c]), a municipality must have at least as much input into a facility intended to house 30 developmentally disabled persons and 53 supervisory attendants.

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Religion -- Constitutional Protection of Use Instituted by Religious Society

4. The Buffalo Zoning Board's denial of a use permit for an intermediate care facility for the developmentally disabled does not unconstitutionally interfere with petitioner Diocese of Buffalo's religious function because constitutional protection is afforded only to religious uses, not all uses instituted by a religious society and petitioner's proposed use is not religious, since it is not simply ancillary or accessory to a traditionally religious use by petitioner, but solely charitable and therefore subject to the same regulations as any other charitable organization.

People v. Godek


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Suffolk County


112 Misc. 2d 512; 447 N.Y.S.2d 214; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3158



January 28, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, defendant's motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to dismiss the indictment brought against him pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 263.10 for promoting an obscene sexual performance by a child.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 263.10, defendant was charged with promoting an obscene sexual performance by a child. Defendant moved to dismiss the indictment arguing that § 263.10 was unconstitutional. The court stated that it was well established that the legislature had the power to mandate stiffer penalties for illegal conduct that adversely affected the lives of children. The court held that § 263.10 was constitutional on its face and as applied to defendant's case.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Promoting Obscene Sexual Performance by Child

 Section 263.10 of the Penal Law, which proscribes "promoting an obscene sexual performance by a child", is constitutional on its face; the Court of Appeals, in holding unconstitutional the statute which proscribes promoting a sexual performance by a child (Penal Law, § 263.15), implicitly upheld the constitutionality of section 263.10 (see People v Ferber, 52 NY2d 674); the Legislature may lawfully mandate stiffer penalties for illegal conduct which in addition adversely affects the lives of children.

 

1865 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Steiner v. Steiner


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Albany County


112 Misc. 2d 300; 447 N.Y.S.2d 118; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3127



January 27, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Therefore, the motion of the defendant to dismiss this action on the grounds of defective service is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The appellate division (New York) ordered a jurisdictional hearing on review of a decision and order of the supreme court which denied defendant husband's motion to dismiss an action brought against him by plaintiff wife for an absolute divorce.


OVERVIEW: In the wife's action for an absolute divorce from her husband, she had a summons personally served on him in the State of North Carolina by a deputy sheriff. However, the service occurred on a Sunday. The husband filed a motion to dismiss for lack of subject matter jurisdiction, which was denied. The appellate division ordered a jurisdictional hearing on review of the supreme court's decision. The court reversed and granted the husband's motion to dismiss. The court held that N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 11 directed that Sunday service in a civil action was absolutely void for any and every purpose and service on the husband in a foreign state on a Sunday did not give the court jurisdiction even if Sunday service was not prohibited there. When service was authorized to be made outside New York it must be made in the foreign state or country as though that foreign state or country were New York. The court also held that the prohibition against Sunday service was not unconstitutional. The statute was not ambiguous and was related to its avowed purpose and there was a strong presumption of constitutionality to which the statute was entitled which had not been overcome by the wife.


OUTCOME: The husband's motion to dismiss the wife's divorce action on the grounds of defective service was granted.


HEADNOTES

Process -- Service of Process -- Sundays and Holidays -- Service of Civil Process without State

Inasmuch as section 11 of the General Business Law prohibits service of civil process in New York on Sunday and CPLR 313 requires that service made outside New York must be made in the same manner as service within New York, service on a nonresident defendant in North Carolina on Sunday is void even though North Carolina law authorizes Sunday service; moreover, section 11 of the General Business Law is not unconstitutional since the statute is neither ambiguous nor has plaintiff overcome the strong presumption of constitutionality to which it is entitled.

 

1866 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Alicia P.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


112 Misc. 2d 326; 446 N.Y.S.2d 1009; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3134



January 27, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For all the reasons stated in this decision, the petition against Alicia P. is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The matter before the court involved a delinquency case in which respondent was charged with possession of a dangerous knife by a person under the age of 16 in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 265.05. Respondent raised several constitutional challenges to the statute.


OVERVIEW: Respondent was arrested in a subway station after a police officer found an ordinary, unmodified kitchen knife tucked in respondent's waistband. Respondent was charged under N.Y. Penal Law § 265.05, which prohibited possession of a dangerous knife by a person under the age of 16. Respondent challenged the statute's constitutionality. The court held that the statute did not deny respondent due process of law as a finding of a violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 265.05 was the predicate to a dispositional hearing under Fam. Ct. Act §§ 731, 746. Also, the statute's prohibition against a child's possession of a dangerous knife did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment as the legislature had the power to make certain rights unavailable to minors. The court, however, held that the portion of N.Y. Penal Law § 265.05 that referred to dangerous knives, and under which appellant had been charged, was void for vagueness. The statute failed to define what constituted a dangerous knife, and the prohibition itself was so essentially vague that it could only be remedied by inserting an intent requirement, which the court was not free to do. Thus, the petition against respondent was dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition against respondent because that portion of the penal statute that referred to dangerous knives, and under which she had been charged, was void for vagueness because it failed to give ready notice to children of the prohibited action and the vagueness of the statute was inseparably linked to the absence of unlawful intent.


HEADNOTES

Infants -- Juvenile Delinquents -- Possession of Dangerous Knife by Juvenile -- Due Process of Law

1. Section 265.05 of the Penal Law which prohibits possession of a dangerous knife by a person under the age of 16 has been consistently interpreted as incorporating the requirements of article 7 of the Family Court Act, so that a finding of a violation of section 265.05 is simply the predicate to a dispositional hearing pursuant to sections 731 and 746 of the Family Court Act to establish that a child charged with being a juvenile delinquent is, by a preponderance of the evidence, in need of supervision, treatment or confinement, and therefore the statute does not violate due process.

Infants -- Juvenile Delinquents -- Possession of Dangerous Knife by Juvenile -- Cruel and Unusual Punishment

2. The prohibition against possession of a dangerous knife by a person under the age of 16, found in section 265.05 of the Penal Law, does not constitute cruel and unusual punishment, given that the Legislature has legitimately made numerous rights unavailable to minors: the right to [***2] vote, to marry, to consume alcoholic beverages, and to drive automobiles, all of which are short-lived prohibitions, hardly cruel and anything but unusual.

Infants -- Juvenile Delinquents -- Possession of Dangerous Knife by Juvenile -- Vagueness -- Equal Protection of Laws

3. Any sharp knife is dangerous if used with intent to do harm, but without unlawful intent, the term dangerous is so vague as to make it impossible to know whether a knife may or may not be so described; thus, section 265.05 of the Penal Law, prohibiting possession of a dangerous knife by a person under the age of 16 without a showing of unlawful intent, is unconstitutionally vague and intertwined with a denial of equal protection of the law since unlawful intent is explicitly required before adults can be held criminally liable for possessing dangerous knives. Section 265.05 which fails to give notice as to what knives are prohibited can only be remedied by inserting a requirement of intent, and legislation may not be reworded by a court to remedy a flaw.

Infants -- Juvenile Delinquents -- Possession of Dangerous Knife by Juvenile -- Proof beyond Reasonable Doubt

4. An ordinary kitchen [***3] knife, freely available to be purchased in stores by adults and children alike, not modified in any manner so as to make it a weapon more suitable for aggressive use and carried by a 15 year old who made no threatening gestures or attempts to use it against another is not a dangerous knife, so that even if section 265.05 of the Penal Law, referring to possession of dangerous knives, were not void for vagueness, a delinquency case with those facts would be dismissed for failure of proof beyond a reasonable doubt that the knife is dangerous.

 

1867 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Glotzer v. Glotzer


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


112 Misc. 2d 851; 447 N.Y.S.2d 603; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3204



January 25, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relying on the provisions of N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 244, plaintiff wife sought the entry of judgment against defendant husband for arrears of unpaid child support and spousal support and maintenance. The husband had not appeared.


OVERVIEW: The wife was granted a divorce against the husband in California. The wife was currently a resident of New York. The California judgment of divorce was filed with a county clerk in New York. The court held that the act of filing the foreign judgment did not make it a New York judgment. The court denied the wife's motion because it lacked jurisdiction to grant the relief requested because there was no showing that the husband had ever been a resident of the state, that he was served within the state, or that any of the events described in N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 230 occurred entitling the court to assert jurisdiction. The wife sought a judgment for obligations that accrued following the entry of the California judgment. In order to grant such relief, the court would have been required to make a finding that the husband failed to make certain payments and that the payments aggregated a specific sum of money for which a further judgment would have been entered. Thus, in personam jurisdiction was required. The husband did not have certain minimum contacts with the state such that the maintenance of the action did not offend traditional notions of fair play and substantial justice.


OUTCOME: The court denied the wife's motion that sought the entry of judgment against the husband for arrears of unpaid child support and spousal support and maintenance.


HEADNOTES

Execution -- Enforcement of Foreign Judgments

A party who has procured a matrimonial judgment in a foreign jurisdiction and filed said judgment in this State, pursuant to the provisions of CPLR article 54, may not, without first acquiring jurisdiction in personam over the other party to the judgment, obtain further money judgments for delinquency in payment under that foreign decree; such additional fact finding regarding obligations which accrued following the entry of the foreign judgment was never contemplated by the Legislature in enacting article 54.

 

1868 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Milio


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Yonkers


112 Misc. 2d 949; 447 N.Y.S.2d 809; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3219



January 25, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant landlord brought a motion to have Yonkers, N.Y., City Code §§ 38.40, 38-44, pertaining to heating of buildings, declared unconstitutional and to have an information against him dismissed as violative of a citizen's rights.


OVERVIEW: Yonkers, N.Y., City Code §§ 38.40, 38-44, provided that parties responsible for heating any building occupied as a home or a place of business had to heat such buildings to 70 degrees at all times when the street temperature was below 55 degrees during the day, and provided for civil and criminal fines and up to 10 days' imprisonment for violations. The court found that the burden imposed upon one asserting the unconstitutionality of a statute was heavy, that unconstitutionality had to be shown beyond a reasonable doubt, and that as a court of limited jurisdiction it should not set aside a statute unless a statute was facially invalid. The court denied the motion and found that the heating regulations were a valid exercise of the city's police power and that duties flowed from ownership of real property. The court ruled that the potential punishment associated with § 38-44's criminal sanctions did not give defendant the right to a jury trial because they were violations pursuant to N.Y Penal Law § 10.00(1), (3), and the right to a jury trial did not extend to crimes classified as less than misdemeanors.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion in its entirety.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Violation of City Heating Code by Landlord

Defendant landlord's motion to dismiss the information against him and to declare unconstitutional certain sections of a city heating code under which he is charged is denied, where the statutes require landlords to provide heat in rental properties when the temperature drops below a certain point and provide a civil penalty for landlords who fail to commence work within 24 hours or to complete the same within 72 hours of notification of a violation and a criminal penalty of a more substantial fine and up to 10 days' imprisonment for willful or reckless violations, with subsequent violations of the same section pertaining to the same premises punishable as a misdemeanor; said provisions, which do not improperly interfere with defendant's right of contract in relation to his business and which provide a reasonable method and procedure of notification of violations that meet due process requirements, are a valid exercise of the city's police power. Furthermore, since only a repeat offense is a misdemeanor, defendant's right to trial by jury, which does not apply to those crimes classified as less than a misdemeanor, is not [***2] violated by nonjury trial on the initial charge.

 

1869 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Blackburn v. Blackburn


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Delaware County


113 Misc. 2d 619; 449 N.Y.S.2d 827; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3350



January 21, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff wife filed a motion for summary judgment in her action for various relief based upon a judgment of divorce which she obtained against defendant husband in an out-of-state court.


OVERVIEW: The wife and husband entered into a separation agreement in 1973, which was incorporated into a judgment of divorce the following year. At the time of the divorce judgment, the parties were residents of another state. Approximately six years later, the wife sought enforcement of provisions within the divorce decree, including child support arrearages owed by the husband. In particular, the wife requested the court make the judgment of the out-of-state court a judgment of a state court. On the wife's motion for summary judgment, filed in lieu of a complaint, the court stated the out-of-state decree was considered as a judgment of the state because the husband did not provide a reason why conversion should have been denied. The court also found the decree was not dormant because the wife had shown that she intended to keep the action alive. The court then held the wife was not collaterally estopped from bringing the action because the issue earlier considered by family court was distinct from the issue of her right to child support under the out-of-state decree.


OUTCOME: The motion for summary judgment was granted insofar as the out-of-state divorce decree was recognized as a judgment of the court. The motion was denied in all other respects.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional Law -- Full Faith and Credit -- Finality of Judgment

1. Plaintiff is permitted to convert a Georgia divorce decree, which incorporated the parties' separation agreement, into a New York judgment in the exercise of permissive full faith and credit, where there is no showing that this would be violative of defendant's rights or against the public policy of this State, notwithstanding the fact that the Georgia decree may be modifiable and nonfinal; the finality of a sister State decree is a condition precedent to its mandatory recognition in this State under the full faith and credit clause (US Const, art IV, § 1), but is not a condition to permissive recognition. Further, plaintiff's action to enforce the Georgia decree is not time barred by a provision of Georgia law that a bona fide effort to enforce the judgment and an entry of such effort on the execution docket be made within seven years of the judgment date or the judgment becomes dormant and unenforceable, as plaintiff's New York action was commenced within seven years of the judgment date and, despite her failure to enter the pendency of the instant action on the docket in Georgia, she has substantially [***2] complied with the terms of the statute; plaintiff's action is also not barred by laches.

Parent and Child -- Uniform Support of Dependents Law

2. Various New York Family Court orders, made pursuant to the Uniform Support of Dependents Law (Domestic Relations Law, § 30 et seq.), directing defendant to pay plaintiff child support and setting arrears therefor, do not affect plaintiff's ability to enforce the financial terms of the parties' Georgia divorce decree in the Supreme Court in an action to convert said sister State decree into a New York judgment; plaintiff is not collaterally estopped from bringing the present action because the issue that has been considered by the Family Court is distinct from the issue at bar and, accordingly, the action can be entertained pursuant to the Supreme Court's plenary and constitutional powers (NY Const, art VI, § 7).

 

1870 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Venus B. v. Danillo S.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


112 Misc. 2d 195; 446 N.Y.S.2d 894; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3116



January 20, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, respondent's motion is dismiss on the ground that the time limitations of section 517 of the Family Court Act have passed is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, who was an alleged father, moved to dismiss petitioner mother's paternity proceeding on the ground that it was time-barred pursuant to N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act. § 517.


OVERVIEW: In opposition to the alleged father's motion to dismiss, the mother contended that her action was not time-barred because the alleged father, by actions constituting an informal acknowledgement of paternity, fraudulently induced her to forego legal proceedings until the statutory time limit had been passed. The mother also contended that N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 517 was unconstitutional because it discriminated on the basis of gender by allowing a putative father to file a paternity petition at any time up to a child's 18th birthday but restricting the mother to a two-year statute of limitations. In granted the alleged father's motion to dismiss, the court held that the mother's allegations of fraudulent inducement were irrelevant because the statute limited acknowledgement to two forms: a writing or a showing of support. Because the mother had presented neither form of acknowledgement, her action was time-barred. The court also rejected the mother's allegation of impermissible gender-based distinction because a mother proceeded solely for her benefit and a male petitioner proceeded solely to his own detriment.


OUTCOME: The court granted the alleged father's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Children Born out of Wedlock -- Filiation Proceeding -- Statute of Limitations

1. A paternity proceeding commenced by petitioner mother four years after her child was born out of wedlock is time barred under section 517 of the Family Court Act, which provides that such a proceeding by the child's mother shall not be brought after the lapse of more than two years from the birth of the child, unless paternity has been acknowledged by the father in writing or by furnishing support; her allegation that respondent's informal acknowledgment of paternity fraudulently induced her to forego legal proceedings until the statutory time limit had passed is not sufficient to avoid the two-year period of limitation, since petitioner's offer of proof does not meet either of the two statutory criterion for acknowledgment.

Children Born out of Wedlock -- Filiation Proceeding -- Statute of Limitations

2. The fact that subdivision (a) of section 517 of the Family Court Act restricts the mother of a child born out of wedlock to a two-year [***2] period from the birth of the child in which to commence a paternity proceeding, while a putative father is permitted to file a paternity proceeding at any time up until the child's eighteenth birthday under subdivision (c) of section 517, does not amount to unlawful discrimination against a petitioner mother and her child; since the relief sought by a female petitioner is to the detriment of the male respondent it is reasonable to afford him prompt notice of the claim, while a male petitioner is proceeding only to his own detriment, at least insofar as support is concerned, and a longer Statute of Limitations for his proceeding is, therefore, reasonable.

 

1871 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Joye v. Schechter


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Nassau County


112 Misc. 2d 172; 446 N.Y.S.2d 884; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3112



January 14, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The court therefore denies respondent's motion to dismiss the herein petition.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent mother filed a motion to dismiss petitioner putative father's complaint in his paternity action. The mother argued that the action was barred by the Statute of Limitations in N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 517(c), which she alleged was unconstitutional. The mother sought to dismiss the paternity action on the ground that she was not seeking support from the putative father.


OVERVIEW: For four years, the putative father regularly supported and visited the child. When the mother prevented the putative father from seeing the child, he filed a paternity action. The mother argued that §517(c) was unconstitutional because it provided different time limitations for the putative father than it did for the mother. In denying the mother's petition to dismiss the paternity action, the court held that § 517(c) was constitutional and that the paternity action could not be dismissed on the ground that the mother was not seeking support from the putative father. The reasonable basis to justify a different time for instituting a paternity proceeding for a father included: the putative father's knowledge of the child depended on being told by the mother and the putative father's objective to establish his right to visitation differed from the mother's objective to seek support. The putative father's paternity action was a separate proceeding that could cumulate in an order of filiation separate and apart from any order of support, visitation, or protections. The mother could not have the paternity petition dismissed because she was not seeking a support order.


OUTCOME: The court denied the mother's motion to dismiss the putative father's paternity action. Specifically, the court held that the statute of limitations for putative fathers in the Family Court Act was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Children Born out of Wedlock -- Filiation Proceeding -- Proceeding Originated by Putative Father

1. Subdivision (c) of section 517 of the Family Court Act, which grants a putative father the right to bring a paternity proceeding within 18 years of the birth of the child, does not deny the mother of a child born out of wedlock equal protection of the laws, although subdivision (c) of section 517 limits her right to institute such a proceeding to two years after the birth of the child, since the difference in the time periods within which to commence the proceeding is not based on sex, but upon a reasonable basis to achieve an important governmental objective; the mother of a child born out of wedlock is given custody of the child from the time it is born and the facts concerning paternity are within her knowledge, which, if the statute limited the time in which the putative father could institute a proceeding to a short period, would enable her to withhold the information or lull him into a feeling of security about his relationship with the child until his proceeding is time barred, while a [***2] father's objective is to establish his visitation and other rights to the child, knowing he also assumes the legal financial responsibility for the child, and it is reasonable to grant a putative father who wishes to assume these obligations a long period of time within which to commence a paternity proceeding.

Children Born out of Wedlock -- Filiation Proceeding -- Proceeding Originated by Putative Father

2. A motion by respondent, the mother of a child born out of wedlock, to dismiss the putative father's paternity petition on the grounds that she is not seeking support from him and that there is no likelihood that she will become a public charge is denied; the purpose of a paternity proceeding is to determine whether the male party is the father of the child, which proceeding may culminate with an order of filiation separate and apart from any order of support or visitation, and, therefore, neither party may have a paternity proceeding dismissed on said grounds.

 

1872 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Davis Constr. Corp. v. County of Suffolk


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


112 Misc. 2d 652; 447 N.Y.S.2d 355; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3178



January 8, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, company and president, filed an action against defendants, county and others, seeking a judgment declaring Suffolk County, N.Y., Local Law No. 8-1981 to be invalid. The action was initially stayed pending the outcome of an action in federal court, and the company and president filed a motion to reargue or renew.


OVERVIEW: The company argued that the district court action had been adjourned pending criminal actions and that a judgment in the district court would not be dispositive of the issue. The court granted the motion to reargue and granted summary judgment in favor of the company and president. The statute was invalid as it attempted to alter the statutorily created powers of the district attorney. The state, by statutorily defining the rights and duties of a district attorney, preempted the possibility of local regulation relating to the duties of the office. While the district attorney was a constitutional officer chosen by the electors of a county, the duties of the office were prescribed by statute as opposed to the state constitution. The county could not, by local law, expand those duties in order to give the district attorney the power to litigate actions outside the scope of his duties as defined by statute. The court determined that the federal action, now stayed, would not adjudicate the validity of the local law and, thus, would not provide the company with an adequate remedy. Additionally, the county's assertion that the company failed to join necessary parties was without merit.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion to reargue filed by the company and the president and granted summary judgment in their favor in an action to have a local law declared invalid.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Declaratory Judgments -- When Remedy Appropriate -- Another Action Pending

1.
