As a general rule, it is an abuse of discretion for a court to entertain jurisdiction of a declaratory judgment action when another action is pending in which all factual and legal issues can be determined but a declaratory judgment may not be refused because of the pendency of another suit if all legal and factual issues cannot be determined in such suit and a declaratory judgment action may be maintained notwithstanding the availability of another remedy where such remedy is not as adequate or effective; accordingly, plaintiffs' action to declare invalid a Suffolk County local law authorizing the District Attorney to prosecute civil actions under the Racketeer Influenced and Corrupt Organizations Act (RICO) (US Code, tit 18, § 1964, subd [c]) need not be dismissed because of the pendency of the county's Federal civil action against plaintiffs for treble damages in connection with the construction of a sewer project since all legal and factual issues will not be determined in the pending action and the remedy sought there is not as adequate or effective as that sought here inasmuch as plaintiffs'  [***2] counterclaim in the Federal action might not meet with much success.

Parties -- Necessary Parties -- Justiciable Controversy

2. An action to declare invalid a Suffolk County local law authorizing the District Attorney to prosecute civil actions under the Racketeer Influenced and Corrupt Organizations Act (RICO) (US Code, tit 18, § 1964, subd [c]), the county having brought a Federal RICO action against plaintiffs in connection with the construction of a county sewer project, need not be dismissed because of the failure to join the other defendants in the RICO action since the missing defendants are not indispensable parties ( CPLR 1001) inasmuch as their interests can be adequately protected by the instant plaintiffs nor should the action be dismissed for failure to state a cause of action since the issue of the power of the county legislature to enlarge the scope of the District Attorney's statutorily prescribed jurisdiction and duties presents a justiciable controversy.

District and Prosecuting Attorneys -- Local Legislation Imposing Additional Duties -- Prosecution of Civil Action

3. A Suffolk County local law which authorizes the District Attorney to bring [***3] civil actions under the Racketeer Influenced and Corrupt Organizations Act (US Code, tit 18, § 1964, subd [c]) is invalid upon the ground that its enactment was beyond the power of the Suffolk County Legislature; the office of District Attorney is so clearly a matter of State concern that local legislation affecting the office may be deemed valid pursuant to article IX of the State Constitution and section 10 of the Municipal Home Rule Law only if the State Legislature has not restricted the adoption of such legislation and the statutory provisions providing for the imposition of additional duties for the District Attorney as "prescribed by law and directed by the board of supervisors" (County Law, § 700, subd 1) is such a State restriction in view of the fact that the State Legislature has adopted many "laws" imposing such additional duties thereby precluding local legislation affecting the office of District Attorney; there is also some merit in plaintiffs' argument that the local law constitutes an improper amendment to the county charter which imposes upon the County Attorney the duty of prosecuting and defending all civil actions brought by or against the county.

 

1873 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Wild Oaks Utilities, Inc. v. Green


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


112 Misc. 2d 53; 445 N.Y.S.2d 941; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3405



December 21, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In order to prevent the plaintiff from being compelled to operate at the mercy of the defendants at a rate unilaterally fixed by them, a trial preference is granted in this action.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action against defendants, a town board and a municipality, concerning rate fixing, plaintiff sewage corporation sought an order for a declaratory judgment that certain sewage rates proposed by the corporation were fair and reasonable, a judgment pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, and a judgment declaring N.Y. Transp. Corp. Law § 121 unconstitutional and ineffective.


OVERVIEW: The sewage corporation made an application for a preliminary injunction establishing interim rates. The defendants contended that there was no authority to set interim rates nor to impose a one-time special assessment to recoup past unrealized revenues. The court granted a preliminary injunction in favor of the sewage corporation because preliminary injunctive relief had to be granted when necessary to prevent intervening irreparable damage. Furthermore, the court reasoned that although a court had no rate-fixing power per se, it had the right and the duty to prevent the confiscation of the sewage corporation's property without due process of law. Finally, the court determined that a refusal of defendants to allow collection of even the amount it considered fair and reasonable under the guise of statutory sanction could bankrupt the sewage corporation and defeat the intended purpose of the enactment.


OUTCOME: The court granted a preliminary injunction in favor of the sewage corporation upon a condition that the sewage corporation post a bond.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Rates for Private Sewage Works Corporation

Plaintiff, a private sewage works corporation governed by a statute which requires that it agree with the local governing body on the rates to be charged but which fails to provide any procedure to resolve a stalemate or fix interim rates (Transportation Corporations Law, § 121), defendant town having refused to allow plaintiff to collect the rates proposed by the town for the years 1979 through 1981, is entitled to at least collect the proffered amount since, although the court has no rate-fixing power per se, it has the right and the duty to prevent the confiscation of property without due process of law and the refusal of the municipality to allow collection of even the amount it considers fair and reasonable under the guise of statutory sanction could bankrupt the utility and defeat the intended purpose of the statute to promote private enterprise and solve a vexing problem for growing communities; plaintiff is entitled [***2] to collect a one-time assessment from its customers to pay tax arrears and avoid in rem foreclosure and provide operating revenue during the pendency of the action and, to the extent that the final rates established after trial are different, appropriate adjustments can be made in the customers' bills.

 

1874 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Johnson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, New York County


112 Misc. 2d 590; 447 N.Y.S.2d 341; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3434



December 1, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion for an order suppressing the statements made by him because the statements were obtained during a custodial questioning on less than probable cause for arrest and were involuntary under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 60.45. Defendant was charged with the crime of manslaughter in the first degree. The statements were made after several polygraph tests were administered.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was questioned about his whereabouts on the date that the victim was stabbed to death in her apartment. Each time, defendant told the same story. He had seen the victim that night, but then left the apartment. Defendant agreed to a lie detector test. The first test results were inconclusive, but the test giver told defendant that he had failed. Several subsequent tests were given, which defendant "passed," although each time defendant was told that he had failed. Finally, after a lengthy custodial interrogation, defendant confessed. The court decided that the interrogation was without probable cause for arrest and had violated defendant's Fourth Amendment rights. Because of the close causal connection between the illegal seizure of defendant and the subsequent "confessions," the statements were suppressed. The seizure, because it was not supported by probable cause, was unreasonable. The same rule applied to the custodial interrogation. The confession was involuntary under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 60.45 because of the improper conduct that impaired defendant's mental condition. The court looked at the totality of the circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion to suppress all of the statement made by defendant.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Probable Cause for Detention and Custodial Interrogation

1. Incriminating statements made by defendant are suppressed on the ground that they were obtained in violation of his Fourth Amendment rights where the statements were obtained by police after lengthy interrogation, during which defendant maintained his innocence, and following defendant's voluntary submission to polygraph tests which proved inconclusive, since the police had no reasonable basis to believe that defendant was lying and, therefore, lacked the requisite probable cause to detain defendant and subject him to custodial interrogation.

Crimes -- Confession -- Voluntariness

2. Where a defendant voluntarily submitted to polygraph tests, the results of which proved inconclusive, and was detained and subjected to lengthy interrogation by the police, who deceived defendant regarding the polygraph results and accompanied the interrogation with promises of leniency, threats pertaining to his child's future welfare and physical deprivations until defendant eventually confessed to a homicide, the confession is suppressed, since, under the totality of the circumstances,  [***2] it was obtained through coercion and duress and was, therefore, involuntary as a matter of law; moreover, since the confession is excluded as being taken in violation of the Fourteenth Amendment because it resulted from duress or coercion, it is suppressed both as evidence in chief and as a means of impeachment against the defendant.

 

1875 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Commissioner of Social Services v. B.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


111 Misc. 2d 617; 444 N.Y.S.2d 556; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3325



November 27, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Commissioner of Social Services filed an action against respondent alleged father seeking to establish the paternity of a child. The issue before the court was who would pay for a second, very expensive, blood test to help in the determination.


OVERVIEW: The Commissioner brought a paternity proceeding against the alleged father, who requested and paid for a blood grouping test. The results were admissible only to exclude him as the father, and the Commissioner requested a more sophisticated test. The alleged father did not object, but stated that he could not afford to pay for the second lab test. The court directed that the Commissioner pay for the second test, holding that it would not impose on the alleged father the burden of paying for the production of evidence sought by the Commissioner. There was no requirement in N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 532 that the less expensive test be ordered first, and no requirement that the mother present evidence of a relationship with the alleged father before obtaining the more expensive test at his expense, but a totally innocent alleged father could be taxes with a substantial expense, over and above the cost of appearing in court and contesting the petition, and that was not the legislature's intent. The alleged father was a working man with a wife and two children and was significantly in debt because of medical expenses. For him to pay for the test would impose genuine hardships on his family.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the Commissioner to pay for the second test instead of having the alleged father do so.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Children Born out of Wedlock -- Filiation Proceeding -- Costs of Blood Grouping Tests

 Section 532 of the Family Court Act which grants any party in a paternity proceeding the right to request a human leukocyte antigen (HLA) blood test, the results of which may be introduced as evidence that the respondent is the father of the child in question in contrast to the prior law which allowed blood test results to be admitted into evidence only to exclude paternity, but which still requires that the cost of the HLA test be borne only by the alleged father unless he is financially unable to pay, should be judicially construed so as to avoid an unconstitutional gender-based discrimination that could threaten implementation of the statute as a whole and thereby frustrate the overriding statutory purpose of making the most reliable medical evidence available to the parties and to the trier of facts, especially in view of the fact that payment for the lab tests is clearly a secondary issue and the original rationale for the assignment of expense to the alleged father has disappeared with the introduction of the HLA blood test; accordingly, a reasonable construction of the statute provides [***2] for payment in the first instance by the party requesting the HLA test and if paternity is then established the costs of the test can be included as part of the order of support.

 

1876 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Mary P.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Queens County


111 Misc. 2d 532; 444 N.Y.S.2d 545; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3310



November 25, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother filed a petition alleging that her daughter was a person in need of supervision within the meaning of N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 712(b).


OVERVIEW: The mother chiefly alleged, among other things, that her daughter's refusal to obey the mother's directive to abort her pregnancy rendered her incorrigible, ungovernable, and habitually disobedient. The court struck the allegation from the petition as legally insufficient as a matter of law. The court issued an order, to protect the daughter's right to give birth pursuant to § 759, which ordered the mother neither to interfere with daughter's determination to deliver her child nor to attempt to force the daughter to have an abortion. The court concluded that the mother's directive was neither lawful nor constitutional and that no absolute parental veto existed over a child's decision to give birth. The court found that the daughter's right to give birth was within the zone of privacy protected by the Fourteenth Amendment.


OUTCOME: The court struck the mother's first allegation against her daughter, issued a protective order in assistance of the daughter's decision to give birth, and adjourned the case in contemplation of dismissal provided that the daughter complied with the terms of the order.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Infants -- Persons in Need of Supervision -- Refusal to have Abortion

Petitioner's 15-year-old daughter may not be adjudged a person in need of supervision (Family Ct Act, § 712, subd [b]) for refusing to comply with petitioner's directive that she have an abortion since the freedom of personal choice to have an abortion or to give birth is a "fundamental" right within the zone of privacy protected by the Fourteenth Amendment and, thus, parents may not utilize the State court system to exercise or enforce a blanket veto over their daughter's fundamental right to give birth; to the extent that parents, either directly or indirectly, fail to respect their child's right to make the ultimate decision on whether to give birth, their conduct may be labeled "offensive" to the child thereby warranting the issuance of an order of protection (Family Ct Act, § 759, subd [a]) prohibiting parental interference with their daughter's constitutional right to give birth.

 

1877 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Covenant Community Church, Inc. v. Gates Zoning Bd. of Appeals


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


111 Misc. 2d 537; 444 N.Y.S.2d 415; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3311



November 19, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Therefore, the relief sought by petitioner is granted to the extent that respondent's determination of April 13, 1981 is hereby annulled and the matter remanded to respondent with the direction that it grant petitioner's application upon such reasonable conditions as will permit the construction of petitioner's church while minimizing any adverse effects upon the neighboring properties and the community at large.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner church commenced an action under article 78 to compel respondent zoning board of appeals to issue a conditional use permit for the construction of a church, parochial school, and day care center on the church's land after the board made factual findings and denied the application. A group of residents in the vicinity of the church site were permitted to intervene as respondents.


OVERVIEW: The church argued that the board's denial was arbitrary, capricious, an abuse of discretion. Respondent argued that the church did not state constitutional claims with sufficient specificity to raise any constitutional issues. The court granted the church's petition, deeming the zoning ordinance under which the board denied the application to be valid because the article 78 proceeding was not the proper mechanism for testing its constitutionality. However, the church's petition was sufficient to raise the issue of whether the zoning code was applied unconstitutionally by alleging that the board's decision was affected by errors of law to the extent that it imposed unconstitutional restrictions on the church. The board's conclusions were arbitrary, irrational, and affected by errors. It made factual findings as to the increased traffic and noise, effect on use and enjoyment of the neighboring properties, aesthetics, change in character of the neighborhood, and diminished property values. These factors were impermissible to justify the outright denial of the application. The board also erred in finding of the current size of the congregation and the intended use of the premises.


OUTCOME: The court granted the church's petition, annulled the board's decision, and remanded to the board with the direction that it grant the church's application.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Construction of Church in Residential District

The determination of respondent town zoning board of appeals denying outright the application of petitioner church for a conditional use permit for the construction of a church, school and day care center in a residential district because of increased traffic noise, the detrimental effect on the use and enjoyment of the neighboring properties, aesthetics, change in the character of the neighborhood and diminished property values, without any attempt to minimize the adverse effects upon the neighboring properties and the community at large is constitutionally infirm as well as arbitrary, capricious and an abuse of discretion since considerations which may justify the exclusion of [***2] commercial structures from residential areas cannot justify the exclusion of religious structures, although they can be considered for the purpose of minimizing the impairment of surrounding areas or the danger of traffic hazards; in order to sustain a zoning determination, it must be "convincingly shown" that the proposed religious use "will have a direct and immediate adverse effect upon the health, safety or welfare of the community" and the court must carefully scrutinize the reasons advanced for a denial to insure that they are real and not merely pretexts used to preclude the exercise of constitutionally protected privileges.

 

1878 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Shell Oil Co. v. New York State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Albany County


111 Misc. 2d 460; 444 N.Y.S.2d 392; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3295



November 16, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state tax commission moved, and plaintiff oil company cross-moved, for reargument and renewal of a prior decision of the court, in which the oil company's motion for summary judgment upon its first, second and third causes of action was denied and the commission's motion for summary judgment was partially granted. The commission also moved for summary judgment dismissing the oil company's fourth, fifth and sixth causes of action.


OVERVIEW: The commission's motion to reargue was granted to the extent that it sought deletion of reference to validity of the tax only through November 10, 1980. The oil company's motion to reargue and renew its prior motion for summary judgment on the first, second and third causes of action set forth in the complaint was denied because such a motion could not be utilized to authorize an unsuccessful party to argue again the precise issues previously determined. A review of the challenged statute, 1980 N.Y. Laws 271, revealed that as to this plaintiff, it did not violate either the federal or state constitutional provisions set forth in causes of action numbered four, five and six. Finally, the commission's request that a judgment be entered in its favor in a specific dollar amount, representing the tax alleged to be due and owing, was denied. The court held that the State of New York could not collect unpaid estimated taxes without a prior assessment.


OUTCOME: The commission's motion to reargue was partially granted, but its motion that a judgment in a specific dollar amount be entered was denied. The oil company's motion to reargue and renew its prior motion for summary judgment on the first, second and third causes of action set forth in the complaint was denied.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Motions and Orders -- Motion to Reargue and Renew -- Summary Judgment

1. A defendant's motion for reargument and renewal that is supported by additional decisions and orders in the companion Federal litigation which were unavailable upon the defendant's original motion for summary judgment wherein it sought and was granted a declaration that a certain gross receipts tax on corporations doing business within the State is constitutional and validly enforceable for a limited time ending November 10, 1980, is granted to the extent that it seeks deletion of reference to the validity of the tax through November 10, 1980 only.

Motions and Orders -- Motion to Reargue and Renew

2. A motion to reargue and renew a prior motion for summary judgment may not be utilized to authorize an unsuccessful party to argue again the precise issues previously determined.

Judgment -- Summary Judgment -- Burden of Proof

3. A party opposing a motion for summary judgment cannot defeat that motion without presenting its proof in evidentiary from that there is or are material fact(s) in dispute and an assertion that the moving party has failed to prove that a challenged taxing statute [***2] is constitutional will not preclude the granting of summary judgment since there is a presumption that the statute is constitutional and it is the opposing party that has the burden of proving it is unconstitutional beyond a reasonable doubt; that burden of proof cannot be shifted to the moving party despite deficiencies in the movant's papers such as unsworn allegations of fact, generalizations and assumptions.

Taxation -- Assessment -- Judgment

4. The State of New York may not collect unpaid estimated taxes without a prior assessment; therefore, the New York State Tax Commission as the initial moving party on a motion for reargument and renewal of a prior decision declaring that a gross receipts tax on corporations doing business within the State is constitutional and validly enforceable during a time period which the movant seeks to have extended to date cannot have a judgment entered in its favor in a specific dollar amount representing the tax alleged to be due and owing by the opposing party.

 

1879 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


New England Petroleum Corp. v. State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Albany County


111 Misc. 2d 471; 444 N.Y.S.2d 532; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3297



November 16, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action by plaintiff corporate taxpayer challenging a franchise tax imposed by defendant State of New York, the parties each filed motions for reargument and renewal of the court's decision denying the taxpayer's summary judgment motion as to certain counts and granting the State's summary judgment motion to the extent that it sought a declaration that the tax imposed was constitutional for a certain period.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer filed a suit against the State, challenging a retroactive franchise tax. The court denied the taxpayer summary judgment as to certain counts and granting summary judgment to the State to the extent of declaring the tax constitutional from its effective date through a certain date. The parties moved for reargument and renewal. The taxpayer sought to shorten the cessation date of the tax and oppose summary judgment to the State because the taxpayer's motion had been limited to certain counts; the State sought to lengthen the valid period from the effective date to the current date. The court granted the State's motion to the extent of lengthening the valid period because the tax was valid until there was a final order from the temporary emergency court of appeals or the federal supreme court; The court then held that the taxpayer's remaining counts were meritless. The tax did not violate the Import-Export Clause under U.S. Const. art. I, § 10(2) because it was not directed at imports based on their place of origin; further, the tax did not violate due process because its retroactive application for a short period was valid absent of proof of inequity to the taxpayer.


OUTCOME: The court granted the State's motion to reargue to the extent that it sought deletion of reference to the validity of the tax only through a certain date; the court granted the taxpayer's motion to reargue but then dismissed the taxpayer's remaining causes of action.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Taxation -- Gross Profits Tax on Oil Companies

The "gross receipts tax" imposed on profits generated by certain oil companies in New York, retroactive to January 1, 1980 (L 1980, ch 271), does not violate the constitutional prohibition against State imposition of import-export duties (US Const, art I, § 10, subd 2) for sales of imported oil which occur at dockside since the tax is nondiscriminatory, being applicable to oil company receipts regardless of whether the products sold are from foreign or domestic sources, and it does not conflict with the free flow of imported goods among the various States in that plaintiff's imported oil is delivered for use directly in New York and it thus cannot be said that the goods are merely in transit through this State when the tax is assessed; the retroactive nature of the tax is not unconstitutional in view of the Legislature's "nearly unconstrained authority in the design of taxing measures" and the fact that retroactivity provisions in tax statutes are generally valid if for a short period; the "gross receipts tax" does not constitute an unlawful tax on presumed wealth, profits or income; the State is not entitled to judgment in a [***2] specific dollar amount for the taxes allegedly due and owing since the State may not collect unpaid estimated taxes without a prior assessment.

 

1880 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Pace


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


111 Misc. 2d 488; 444 N.Y.S.2d 529; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3302



November 13, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to reargue is granted and upon reargument, the original decision denying the motion is adhered to.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, co-owners of an auto salvage yard, were both charged with three counts of criminal possession of stolen property in the first degree and 19 counts of criminal possession of stolen property in the second degree. The matter was before the court on a renewed motion to suppress physical evidence and to controvert a search warrant on the ground that New York City, N.Y., Charter § 436 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: New York City, N.Y., Charter § 436 provided in part that the police commissioner had powers of general supervision and inspection over dealers in second-hand merchandise. The court noted that there was a presumption of constitutionality to all legislation and that a party that attacked the constitutionality of laws must demonstrate the invalidity beyond a reasonable doubt. The court found (1) that § 436 was not void for vagueness because it gave a person of ordinary intelligence fair notice as to what was required and what was forbidden; (2) that it met time, place, and scope guidelines and was not overbroad because the police commissioner did not have unfettered discretion to conduct administrative searches but rather was restricted to reasonable times by New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § B32-132.0(d); and (3) that auto junk yards were pervasively regulated businesses and that anyone who chose to engage in the business did so with the knowledge that the business was subject to inspection and supervision under the police power, so that consent to warrantless searches was implied. The court held that defendants failed to show that the statute was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion to reargue. Upon reargument, the court adhered to its original decision that denied the motion to suppress physical evidence and to controvert a search warrant.


HEADNOTES

Searches and Seizures -- Warrantless Search or Inspection of Junkyards

Upon reargument of a motion by defendants, who are charged with criminal possession of stolen property, for an order to suppress physical evidence recovered from their business premises, whereon they operate an automobile wrecking and salvage company, on the ground that section 436 of the New York City Charter, which gives the Commissioner of the New York City Police Department powers of general supervision and inspection over certain trades, including the power to search and supervise junkyards, is unconstitutional, the court adhered to its original decision denying the motion; the State has a legitimate interest in supervising businesses such as defendants, which have been pervasively regulated, the statute provides a person of ordinary intelligence fair notice as to what it requires and/or forbids and searches thereunder are limited in time, place and scope (Administrative Code of City of New York, § B32-132.0, subd d); moreover, defendants have implicitly consented to such warrantless searches [***2] by engaging in a business known to be pervasively regulated, despite the fact that failure to comply with the provisions of the statute may result in fine or imprisonment or both.

 

1881 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Graham


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, New York County


111 Misc. 2d 666; 444 N.Y.S.2d 988; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3337



November 13, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: At a hearing to determine whether defendant should have been sentenced as a persistent violent felony offender pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 400.20, defendant contended that his two prior felony convictions, subsequently reclassified as violent felonies, should not have been used as predicate offenses. Defendant also challenged the constitutionality of one of the prior convictions.


OVERVIEW: The court noted that defendant's two prior convictions for third degree attempted criminal possession of a weapon were classified as felonies at the time of the convictions, but were now classified as violent felony offenses under N.Y. Penal Law § 70.02, subd. 1, para. (d). The court held that the legislature intended N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 400.20 to be read so that reclassified felonies qualified as violent felonies. The court, therefore, concluded that defendant's two prior felony convictions, subsequently reclassified as violent felonies, were properly considered predicate offenses to his being sentenced as a persistent violent felony offender. The court rejected defendant's challenge to the constitutionality of his first conviction. The court held that because defendant failed to challenge the constitutionality of the conviction at his first hearing to determine his predicate felony offender status, he was thereafter precluded from doing so at any subsequent hearing.


OUTCOME: The court found defendant to be a persistent violent felony offender.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Sentence -- Persistent Violent Felony Offender -- Reclassified Predicate Violent Felonies

Prior felony convictions occurring prior to September 1, 1978, the effective date of the violent felony offender law, which have been reclassified since that date as violent felony offenses (Penal Law, § 70.02), may lawfully serve as predicates in determining whether a defendant recently convicted of a violent felony offense should be sentenced as a persistent violent felony offender since considering reclassified violent felonies in determining whether a defendant is a persistent violent felony offender is not unconstitutional as ex post facto inasmuch as a sentence under a second felony or habitual criminal statute does not constitute punishment for the prior conviction and it is the defendant's present conviction that is the determinative one; furthermore, a defendant who fails to challenge the constitutionality of a previous conviction at a prior predicate felony hearing (see CPL 400.20) is thereafter precluded from challenging the constitutionality of such previous conviction at any subsequent hearing to determine whether he should be sentenced as a persistent violent felony [***2] offender; since the sentencing statute for persistent violent felony offenders (Penal Law, § 70.08) omits any provision for the sentencing of a class E persistent violent felony offender, corrective legislation is needed to rectify the situation.

 

1882 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Morgenthau v. Erlbaum


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


112 Misc. 2d 30; 445 N.Y.S.2d 997; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3402



November 10, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a prostitution prosecution, respondent judge declared N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 340.40(2) unconstitutional in that it denied a jury trial to persons charged with the crime of prostitution. The judge held that the defendants in the underlying proceeding were entitled to a jury trial. Petitioner district attorney brought a motion for a judgment pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 to prohibit enforcement of the order.


OVERVIEW: The court determined that prohibition was not an appropriate remedy in the case but held that it was not foreclosed from exercising its jurisdiction under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 30. The district attorney thereafter sought to convert the proceeding to an action for declaratory judgment. The court granted the motion to convert the proceeding and declared that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 340.40(2) was constitutional. The court reasoned that the state had the right to limit the availability of jury trials to preserve the benefits that resulted from speedy and inexpensive nonjury adjudications. The court found that the statute's classification of a "serious offense" as one that was punishable by more than six months in prison was reasonable. The court was not persuaded by the judge's characterization of prostitution as a "serious" crime. The court concluded that the defendants in the underlying prosecution were not entitled to a jury trial.


OUTCOME: The court declared the statute constitutional and determined that the defendants in the underlying prosecution were not entitled to a jury trial.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Courts -- Collateral Appeal of Interlocutory Order of Criminal Court -- Constitutionality of Denial of Jury Trial in Prostitution Cases

When a Criminal Court Judge holds a statute to be unconstitutional, thereby prejudicing the People and creating delay in the criminal justice system, the People should be able to collaterally appeal that Judge's decision in the Supreme Court by way of a declaratory judgment action, relief in the nature of a writ of prohibition not being an appropriate remedy in this type of case; accordingly, the People's article 78 proceeding to prevent implementation of a decision of a New York City Criminal Court Judge holding the statutory denial of a jury trial in prostitution cases ( CPL 340.40, subd 2) to be unconstitutional on the ground that prostitution is a "serious" offense for which jury trials are mandated, although punishable by only three months' imprisonment (see 107 Misc 2d 973), is properly converted to an action for a declaratory judgment since the Judge's actions can be characterized as an excess of authority, and CPL 340.40 (subd 2) is declared to be constitutional in view of the State's classification of prostitution as a class B misdemeanor [***2] not "serious" enough to bear the expense of jury trial under the standard set by the United States Supreme Court defining a "serious" offense as one which is punishable by more than six months in prison.

 

1883 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


McKinney v. State


Claim No. 63267


Court of Claims of New York


111 Misc. 2d 382; 444 N.Y.S.2d 386; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3281



November 9, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant administratrix filed an action against defendant state on the ground that it had acted negligently and unconstitutionally when it permitted a peace officer to carry a weapon and hold dual employment. The administratrix filed a motion to compel the completion of a deposition previously ordered by the court and for discovery, inspection, and copying of certain records.


OVERVIEW: The administratrix's intestate decedent had been killed by an employee of the state, who worked as a court officer in the Supreme Court and as a bus driver. The administratrix contended that the state knew of the court officer's violent nature and sought discovery of various employment records. The court had ordered the examination of a state employee with knowledge of the court officer's work history but maintained that certain records were immune from disclosure under N.Y. Pub. Off. Law §§ 84-90. When the ordered discovery was not completed the administratrix filed a motion to compel. The court granted the motion because the personnel records of a court officer was not entitled to the protection or benefits of the N.Y. Civ. Rights Law § 50-a (1976) privilege that protected corrections and police officers. Furthermore, there was no question that the records were in the possession and control of the state under N.Y. Jud. Law § 39 (1976). Finally, the court noted that the rules governing discovery were broader that those governing admissibility and that it had made no ruling on the latter issue.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion to compel completion of a deposition and for the production of the requested documents.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Civil Rights -- Disclosure -- Material Exempt from Disclosure

1. According to section 50-a of the Civil Rights Law, a privilege does exist with respect to discovery of personnel records of police officers and correction officers but it does not extend to personnel records of court officers since they are not police officers by definition and though generally regarded along with correction officers as peace officers, the Legislature did not intend that that similarity entitle their records to be privileged from discovery; if the section 50-a privilege were made available to court officers, discovery of personnel records would still be available under certain circumstances upon the express written consent of the subject officer or as mandated by lawful court order or under the State's Freedom of Information Act (Public Officers Law, art 6, §§ 84-90).

Disclosure -- Discovery and Inspection -- Court Officers

2. Official records sought to be disclosed must be material and necessary, not privileged from disclosure, and in the possession, custody or control of the party to be examined or the party upon whom demand for production is made ( CPLR art 31); where a court officer's [***2] personnel records made prior to April 1, 1977 are demanded for discovery, those records being material and necessary and not subject to a claim of privilege, the State, as employer, must produce them since it came into possession, custody or control of the records as of April 1, 1977 when nonjudicial court employees became State employees, and the fact that the records had been made and kept by the City of New York prior to the State's ownership is coincidental and irrelevant; also, it makes no difference that upon acquisition by the State the records were kept at one of two State agencies because these agencies have no existence independent of the State; since the incident involving the court officer which is the basis of the negligence claim against the State occurred while the State had ownership of personnel records, any material, nonprivileged records made following that incident are discoverable.

 

1884 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Uplinger


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Buffalo


111 Misc. 2d 403; 444 N.Y.S.2d 373; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3285



November 9, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with a violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(3), which prohibited loitering in a public place for the purpose of engaging in or soliciting another person to engage in deviate sexual intercourse. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the charge on the ground that § 240.35(3) violated the Equal Protection Clause of U.S. amend. XIV. The court issued its ruling on the motion.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a male homosexual, was arrested after he solicited an undercover policeman on a public street. Defendant argued that § 240.35(3) was unconstitutional because people had lawful right to engage in deviate sexual intercourse. He also contended that homosexuals had a right to associate with one another in public and one aspect of this right was the opportunity to make discreet inquiries of a prospective companion to have sex in private. The court found defendant guilty as charged. The court held that § 240.35(3), as applied to male homosexuals, was constitutional. The court found that (1) male homosexuals who solicited in front of and near neighborhood residents' homes and businesses significantly interfered with the use and enjoyment and worth of the property of those in the neighborhood, and (2) there was a sufficient connection between the public's loss of the use and enjoyment of its streets, businesses, and homes and the activity at issue to warrant the ban. The court stated that the activity of homosexuals who loitered on the streets to solicit one another was akin to the loitering of prostitutes in its effect on the public.


OUTCOME: Defendant was found guilty as charged for loitering in a public place for the purpose of engaging in or soliciting another person to engage in deviate sexual intercourse. The court found that the statute that prohibited that activity was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Loitering -- Deviate Sexual Intercourse

The statute which prohibits loitering in a public place for the purpose of engaging in or soliciting another person to engage in deviate sexual intercourse (Penal Law, § 240.35, subd 3) is not unconstitutional as applied to male homosexuals despite the fact that persons have a lawful right to engage in deviate sexual intercourse since loitering in public for this purpose may be constitutionally proscribed depending on its effect on the public welfare so long as there is some connection shown between the proscription and a legitimate public concern and, inasmuch as the activity of homosexuals who loiter on the streets to solicit one another is akin to the loitering of prostitutes in its effects on the public, a sufficient connection exists between the public's loss of the use and enjoyment of its streets, businesses and homes and the activity at issue to warrant the proscription; accordingly, defendant, who solicited a plainclothes policeman on a street to commit oral sex with him at his apartment, is guilty of violating the statute.

 

1885 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Dann


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Cattaraugus County


111 Misc. 2d 279; 443 N.Y.S.2d 993; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3261



November 4, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In separate proceedings, petitioner decedents' representatives opposed claims for recoupment of medical assistance benefits that were brought by respondent Commissioner of Social Services pursuant to N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 369(1)(b).


OVERVIEW: The commissioner made separate claims for recoupment of benefits that were paid for medical assistance to each of two decedents during their lifetime, but after each decedent had attained the age of 65, pursuant to N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 369(1)(b). The decedents' representatives opposed payment on grounds that a requirement of payment under § 369(1)(b) would have represented a denial of equal protection of the laws in violation of provisions of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution, as they were transposed to N.Y. Const. art. I § 11. The court held that there was no abridgement of the decedents' rights because they, from whom recoupment had been authorized by the state legislature, were not similarly circumstanced to those under 65 from whom recoupment was not permitted. Further, the court found that the legislature's classification, although made unknowingly, nonetheless had a reasonable basis. Therefore, the commissioner's claims against the decedents' estates were allowed.


OUTCOME: The court allowed the commissioner's claims against the decedents' representatives for the recoupment of medical assistance benefits that had been paid to each decedent after each had attained the age of 65.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Social Services -- Recoupment -- Equal Protection Clause

1. Section 369 (subd 1, par [b]) of the Social Services Law which allows for recoupment by a County Commissioner of Social Services of Medicaid payments made only to recipients over age 65 who died leaving no surviving spouse and no child who is under 21 years of age or is blind or disabled does not violate the equal protection clauses of the Federal and State Constitutions in that it does not treat persons similarly circumstanced differently; those persons over 65 years of age from whom recoupment is authorized are not similarly circumstanced to those recipients under 65 who suffer from a catastrophic illness or are eligible for home relief and from whom recoupment is not authorized because of the stiffer income restrictions imposed on eligibility of those under 65 and the fact that those over 65 qualifying for Medicaid may retain and maintain substantially greater real and personal property.

Social Services -- Recoupment

2. Since the State in recognizing its responsibility to care for those needy and aged over 65 during their lives permits them to own a home, have cash or other assets not exceeding $ 1,850 [***2] in total value, own an automobile with a net equity up to $ 4,500, and still qualify for Medicaid, it is not unreasonable for the State to recoup those medical payments furnished to them from the unexpended assets of their estates where there is no spouse or minor or permanently disabled child surviving and where the only losers in the event of recoupment are the heirs at law or potential legatees or devisees whose inheritance is possibly lost; furthermore, it is the adult children of the Medicaid recipient, the heirs and potential legatees or devisees, whom the State benefited in 1966 (L 1966, ch 256, § 18) by relieving them of their pre-existing responsibility of financial support of their impoverished parent and thus it is they rather than the taxpayers who must make some sacrifice for the medical care of their deceased.

Statutes -- Construction

3. While there may be some inequality in a statutory classification by age, if facts may be conceived to justify such classification, the court will defer to the legislative determinations that have been made; the statutory discrimination in allowing recoupment of Medicaid payments from the estates of deceased recipients over [***3] 65 who are not survived by a spouse or a minor or permanently disabled child but not allowing recoupment from recipients under age 65 is reasonable insofar as those over 65 cannot be expected to return to wage-earning status whereas those under 65 may recover from a catastrophic illness and return to work and those receiving home relief may improve in financial status in which case the medical assistance burden on the State would be temporary.

 

1886 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Burke


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Monroe County


111 Misc. 2d 296; 443 N.Y.S.2d 1003; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3262



November 4, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant social services department sought the reimbursement of Medicaid benefits paid on behalf of the decedent. Petitioner executrix rejected the claim.


OVERVIEW: The decedent had received Medicaid benefits for several years. Pursuant to N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law §§ 104, 369, the department sought reimbursement for this assistance. The executrix rejected the claim and argued that the state recovery statute, N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 369(1)(b), was unconstitutional because it excluded recoupment against estates of persons who died under the age of 65. The executrix argued that this unequal treatment was an infringement of a property right and violated U.S. Const. amend. V and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6. The court ordered the executrix to pay the claim. The court held that the recovery provisions did not directly or indirectly impair the rights of the decedent to qualify for or receive Medicaid benefits during her lifetime. Just because recovery was to be taken from her estate to the detriment of the beneficiaries under her will did not limit or abridge any of their fundamental constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the executrix to pay the claim filed by the department for the reimbursement of Medicaid benefits paid on behalf of the decedent.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Social Services -- Recoupment of Medicaid Benefits -- Recovery of Correctly Paid Benefits from Estate of Recipient

The State statute which, as authorized by the Federal enabling statute (US Code, tit 42, § 1396a, subd [a], par [18]), permits recoupment of correctly paid medical assistance benefits from the estate of a recipient if the receipient was age 65 or older when the benefits were received and then only if there is no surviving spouse, or a child who is under 21 or is blind or disabled (Social Services Law, § 369, subd 1, par [b]), thereby precluding recovery where there are "dependents" who might otherwise be in need of assistance, does not, by reason of the age distinction for recovery purposes between Medicaid recipients who are under 65 years of age and those who are 65 years of age and older when the benefits are received, unconstitutionally infringe or abridge the right of a decedent to dispose of property by will; accordingly, the claim filed by the Monroe County Department of Social Services against the estate of decedent, who died at the age of 90, for medical assistance rendered to decedent is valid and enforceable.

 

1887 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Pilgrim


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Nassau County


111 Misc. 2d 842; 445 N.Y.S.2d 868; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3369



October 30, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The case came before the court on defendant's motion pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.10(1)(h), which allowed for vacatur of a conviction obtained in violation of the state or federal constitutions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had been represented in two cases by a layman masquerading as an attorney. Those convictions were vacated. However, those convictions had been admitted in a third case of drug dealing. The trial court permitted the district attorney to introduce evidence of prior convictions in the third case for three reasons: to demonstrate the predisposition of defendant to commit the crimes charged in the indictment, to attempt to overcome the entrapment defense raised; to demonstrate defendant possessed the requisite criminal intent, to attempt to overcome the agency defense raised; and to help establish the identity of defendant as the perpetrator of the crimes charged. The court held that the introduction at trial of the uncounseled convictions did not automatically warrant a reversal because the overwhelming evidence of guilt was decisive. The incredibility of defendant's testimony, the fact that defendant's testimony was discredited by other evidence, and the limited use of the uncounseled convictions at trial were sufficient to render the error harmless.


OUTCOME: The conviction was affirmed, but defendant's motion for resentencing without consideration of the prior uncounseled convictions was granted.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Harmless and Prejudicial Error -- Right to Counsel -- Admission of Evidence Concerning "Uncounseled" Prior Convictions

Although defendant's two prior convictions for drug sales must be vacated because of his representation in those cases by a layman masquerading as an attorney, the admission into evidence of such prior "uncounseled" convictions on the People's direct case in defendant's drug sale trial for the proper limited purposes of overcoming defendant's entrapment defense by demonstrating his predisposition to commit the crimes charged, overcoming the agency defense by demonstrating that defendant possessed the requisite criminal intent and establishing the identity of the defendant as the perpetrator of the crimes charged, constitutes harmless constitutional error that does not warrant a reversal of defendant's conviction in view of the overwhelming evidence of guilt established by the testimony of the undercover police officer, corroborated by other witnesses and recorded conversations, and the fact that defendant's testimony, after admitting that he was a "small time dealer", was incredible as a matter of law; however, due process mandates that defendant [***2] be resentenced pursuant to CPL 440.20 without consideration of the prior "uncounseled" convictions.

 

1888 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Harvey v. Finnick


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Ontario County


111 Misc. 2d 197; 443 N.Y.S.2d 980; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3249



October 29, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner district attorney brought a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding to compel respondent county to pay him the compensation as established by N.Y. Jud. Law §§ 183-a, 221-d.


OVERVIEW: The court denied the district attorney's petition for an order compelling the county to increase his pay according to N.Y. Jud. Law §§ 183-a, 221-d. The court held that the compensation scheme of § 183-a could not be upheld as it did not give consideration to the differing duties and responsibilities of county court judges and district attorneys nor did it allow for any meaningful accommodation of each county's particular situation. The court concluded that the compensation provisions of § 183-a, which equated the salaries of certain district attorneys to those of a county court judge lacked a rational basis and must be declared unconstitutional. Therefore, the court held that the county was not obliged to pay the district attorney in accordance with the salary provisions of § 183-a and N.Y. Jud. Law § 221-d. The court went on to address the issue of whether a district attorney was a state officer or a local officer. The court held that the office of district attorney was actually a hybrid. For purposes of compensation, district attorneys had to be considered to be state officers.


OUTCOME: The court denied the district attorney's petition.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] District and Prosecuting Attorneys -- Statutory Compensation Plan -- Population Based Classification

While section 183-a of the Judiciary Law, which requires a county such as Ontario County to pay its full-time District Attorney a salary equivalent to that of its County Court Judge, controls over an earlier enacted statute authorizing the county to establish the salary for the District Attorney ( County Law, § 201), and constitutes a general, not a special, law, inasmuch as there is a reasonable basis for the population based classification and providing full-time District Attorneys an adequate compensation is of importance to the State generally, the compensation plan of paying certain District Attorneys the same as County Court Judges is unreasonable in view of the subsequent adoption of the Unified Court System under which such Judges are given additional duties and responsibilities with a minimum salary of $ 60,900 per year ( Judiciary Law, § 221-d) as opposed to the $ 25,000 per year minimum salary which existed when the compensation system was first enacted; the District Attorney is a State officer for purposes of the constitutional provision prohibiting the compensation [***2] received by State officers from being increased or diminished during their term of office (NY Const, art XIII, § 7).

 

1889 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Pestar v. Remington Arms Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Herkimer County


111 Misc. 2d 364; 443 N.Y.S.2d 987; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3277



October 29, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee filed an action to for a temporary injunction to restrain defendant employer from withholding income tax from his pay. The employer filed a cross-motion to dismiss the action for failure to state a cause of action.


OVERVIEW: The employee applied to the court for a temporary injunction to restrain the employer from withholding income tax from his pay. The employer filed a cross-motion to dismiss for failure to state a claim. The court dismissed the action finding that the employee was not deprived of his right to due process because federal income tax withholding did not constitute a taking of property without due process. The court found that the Federal Anti-Injunction Act, 26 U.S.C.S. § 7421 was a bar to the proceeding because it stated that no suit for the purpose of restraining the assessment or collection of any tax could be maintained unless it fell into one of the listed exceptions. The employee's action did not fall within any of the exceptions. The court found that because the Anti-Injunction Act barred the action, the court lacked subject matter jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The court granted the employer's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Judges -- Disqualification

1. The Judge hearing plaintiff's action for a permanent injunction restraining his employer from withholding any income tax from his pay need not disqualify himself under the Rules Governing Judicial Conduct merely because his son is employed by plaintiff's employer since the son has reached the age of majority and has no interest that could be substantially affected by the outcome of the proceeding (22 NYCRR 33.3 [c]) inasmuch as the employer would not be financially burdened by an adverse decision.

Taxation -- Federal Income Tax -- Injunction to Restrain Withholding of Income Tax

2. State Supreme Court lacks subject matter jurisdiction over plaintiff's action for a permanent injunction restraining his employer from withholding any income tax from his pay, plaintiff's withholding certificate claiming exemption from income tax having been rejected by the Internal Revenue Service (IRS) pursuant to its regulations (26 CFR 31.3402 [f] [2]-1 [g]), since such action is barred by the Federal "Anti-Injunction Act" which generally prohibits the maintenance of any suit "restraining the assessment or collection of any tax" (US Code, tit [***2] 26, § 7421, subd [a]) and is applicable to actions which seek to restrain the withholding of income taxes; in order to avoid the statutory bar, plaintiff must demonstrate that under no circumstances could the employer prevail and also that he will be irreparably harmed if the injunction is not granted and plaintiff has advanced no claim that he could succeed on the merits with respect to the exemption since the IRS regulation is not violative of due process and has not demonstrated the existence of any irreparable harm inasmuch as he could institute a suit for refund upon exhaustion of administrative remedies.

 

1890 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Codd v. Barbaro


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


111 Misc. 2d 135; 443 N.Y.S.2d 683; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3239



October 27, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In light of the above, the petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner aggrieved candidate brought an action to invalidate the petition of respondent, nominated independent candidate for mayor.


OVERVIEW: The aggrieved candidate challenged the petition of the nominated candidate, arguing that some witnesses were not qualified to vote on the first day for circulating the petition and that some witnesses later voted in the primary. The nominated candidate argued that the court lacked jurisdiction because service was improper. On the last day on which to effect service, the server was unable to obtain admission to the nominated candidate's apartment. He taped the papers to the wall above the intercom bells which were two to five feet from the front door. The nominated candidate testified that although the papers were attached to the wall at the building's main entrance, he used another entrance and did not learn of the service until several days later. The court dismissed the aggrieved candidate's petition, validating the signatures based on a percentage limited sampling. Service was proper. The front entrance was as far as the server was able to proceed using due diligence, and he had only a few hours before the deadline. The signatures of the witnesses who were not qualified to vote were not counted. However, the signatures of persons who later voted in the primary were valid.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the aggrieved candidate's petition.


HEADNOTES

Process -- Service of Process -- Affixing Order to Show Cause to Door

1. The process server, after being unable to gain admittance to respondent's apartment building, properly effected service on respondent by taping the order to show cause and petition challenging respondent's independent nominating petition as a candidate for Mayor of the City of New York to the wall above the intercom bells located near the front door of the building in compliance with the terms of the order which authorized service by "affixing a copy thereof to the outer door" of respondent's residence, since no improper means are required to obtain admission and, inasmuch as the front entrance was as far as the process server was able to proceed using due diligence, it was proper to leave the papers above the intercom bells especially considering that the process server only had a few hours before the jurisdictional deadline to effect service would have passed.

Elections -- Independent Nominating Petition -- Validity of Signatures

2. Otherwise valid signatures on respondent's independent nominating petition as a candidate for Mayor of [***2] the City of New York filed on September 5, 1981 are not rendered invalid merely because the witnesses to those signatures subsequently voted in the Mayoral primary on September 22, since the retroactive invalidation of properly filed petitions is unjust and not mandated by the statutory provisions which provide that a person may not be counted as a valid signature if he voted "at a primary election where a candidate was nominated for an office for which such petition purports to nominate a candidate" (Election Law, § 6-138, subd 1) and further provide that a person may only witness an independent petition if he is qualified to sign it (Election Law, § 6-140) inasmuch as the criteria for persons who are qualified to sign independent nominating petitions are those that must exist on the date the petition is signed; signatures obtained by witnesses who were not qualified to vote on August 4, 1981, the first day for circulating such petitions, may not be counted, but signatures on a page inserted between consecutively numbered pages are not invalid nor are petitions obtained through "street corner petitioning" and not in any order by county; once a specific objection to a subscribing [***3] witness is overruled, the individual signatures cannot be subject to challenge unless the subject of a listed specific objection.

Elections -- Independent Nominating Petition -- Use of Sampling to Ascertain Validity of Signatures

3. In view of the close proximity of the New York City Mayoral election and the large number of signatures on respondent's independent nominating petition being challenged, the validity of the challenged signatures may be determined by means of a limited sampling of actual line-by-line objections notwithstanding the fact that both parties oppose the use of the sampling method; accordingly, based on a 3% sampling of the 15,000 challenged signatures and a validation of 12% as found by the court-appointed Referee, 1,800 signatures can be validated making a total of 18,938 valid signatures thereby exceeding the 7,500 signatures needed to uphold respondent's nominating petition (Election Law, § 6-142, subd 2, par [b]).

 

1891 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Merit Oil of New York, Inc. v. New York State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


111 Misc. 2d 118; 443 N.Y.S.2d 604; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3236



October 26, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] To the extent that plaintiff seeks summary judgment on its first cause of action and dismissal of the defendants' counterclaim, said motion shall be granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer filed a motion in the trial court (New York) for an order directing the entry of summary judgment on its causes of action and dismissing defendant State Tax Commission's counterclaim. The taxpayer challenged the constitutionality of the tax imposed pursuant to N.Y. Tax Law § 182, which taxed the gross profits of oil companies which sold more than 60 million gallons of petroleum in the preceding year.


OVERVIEW: The gross profits tax applied only to those which sold more than 60 million gallons of petroleum in New York during the preceding taxable year. For those retailers selling less, a complete exemption from the tax was granted. The taxpayer argued that the taxing scheme was arbitrary, unreasonable, and violated the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11. The court found that there was no rational basis for treating members within the class of oil companies differently. The court held that N.Y. Tax Law § 182(2)(a) was unconstitutional to the extent that it defined "oil company" differently regarding the amount of gross sales. The court invalidated only the unconstitutional portion of § 182 and not the entire statute. The court deleted the invalid portion of the statute to include, within the exemption from tax, all those engaged solely in the sale of retail petroleum.


OUTCOME: The court granted the taxpayer's motion for summary judgment and dismissed the Commissioner's counterclaim.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Additional Franchise Tax on Certain Oil Companies -- Gasoline Retailers

Section 182 (subd 2, par [a]) of the New York Tax Law, which imposes a 2% gross profits tax on certain oil companies, is unconstitutional to the extent that the definition of an "oil company" subject to the tax includes those retailers with gross sales of 60 million gallons of gasoline or more and excludes those retailers with lower gross sales volumes; although the statutory scheme may properly treat the class of corporations engaged in the business of extracting, producing, refining, manufacturing or compounding petroleum as a separate entity subject to the tax and, similarly, exempt from such taxation all corporations engaged in the selling of residential fuel oil, no rational basis supports the different treatment accorded those companies within the class of gasoline retailers. Accordingly, the invalid portion of the act shall be deleted so as to include all those engaged solely in the sale of petroleum at retail within the [***2] exemption from tax.

Dixon v. Coughlin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Orange County


111 Misc. 2d 93; 443 N.Y.S.2d 598; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3230



October 20, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought a declaration by the court that N.Y. Correct. Law § 851(2) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was serving a 15-year sentence for manslaughter in the first degree. He had been twice convicted of escape in the state of Georgia and it was contended that those convictions prohibited defendant's eligibility for temporary release under the statute. The court remanded after finding defendant eligible for consideration for temporary release. The court determined that the acts committed by defendant were as a youth and it was apparent that the conviction for escape in Georgia would not have been either permissible or mandatory in New York at the time of such occurrences. The court found that the record did not sufficiently disclose the crimes of which defendant was convicted and which served as a basis for his imprisonment and subsequent escapes.


OUTCOME: The court found defendant eligible for consideration for entry into the temporary release program and remanded the matter.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Prisons and Prisoners -- Temporary Release Program -- Ineligibility Due to Escape Conviction

Plaintiff's more than 30-year-old escape convictions in the State of Georgia when he was under 17 years of age do not prohibit his eligibility for participation in the State's temporary release program which denies eligibility to any person convicted of any escape or absconding offense (Correction Law, § 851, subd 2), since plaintiff's convictions for escape in Georgia would not have been either permissible or mandatory in New York at the time of such occurrences because of plaintiff's age and New York courts, in appropriate circumstances, have declined to apply in New York as determinative of questions involving personal liberty, the incidents of convictions of crime in other jurisdictions whose laws and policies may not be as enlightened as those of New York.

 

1893 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Barbour


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


111 Misc. 2d 266; 443 N.Y.S.2d 815; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3259



October 19, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After defendant was found guilty of criminal possession of a weapon in the first degree, the People filed a statement pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 400.16 alleging that defendant was a persistent violent felony offender. The People requested that defendant be sentenced to a maximum sentence of life imprisonment. The court issued its opinion on the requested sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had prior convictions for robbery in the second degree, a class C felony, and criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree, a class D felony. At the time defendant was convicted of those offenses, they were classified as ordinary felonies. Subsequently, they were reclassified as violent felonies. The State argued that the instant charge of criminal possession of a weapon in the first degree coupled with the prior convictions for violent felonies required defendant to be sentenced to a maximum term of life imprisonment. The court held that insofar as N.Y. Penal Law § 70.02 was retroactively applied to make offenses which at the time of commission were only felonies into violent felonies (for purposes of finding defendant a second violent felony offender or a persistent violent felony offender) it was unconstitutional as ex post facto and in violation of U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, cl. 1. The court stated that defendant's priors could be used to determine whether or not he was a persistent felony offender pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 70.10.


OUTCOME: The court found that defendant was a persistent felony offender, not a persistent violent felony offender, and sentenced him accordingly.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Persistent Violent Felony Offender -- Ex Post Facto Law

Defendant may not be classified as a persistent violent felony offender (Penal Law, § 70.08, subd 1) upon his conviction for the violent felony offense of criminal possession of a weapon since defendant's two predicate felony convictions, a 1973 robbery conviction and 1976 weapons conviction, were not classified as violent felonies until 1978 and 1980, respectively, and, insofar as the persistent violent felony offender statute is retroactively applied to make offenses which at the time of commission were only felonies into violent felonies, it is an unconstitutional ex post facto law inasmuch as the reclassification of the earlier crimes to previously nonexistent categories before imposing sentence effectively "changes the legal consequences of acts completed before" enactment of the violent felony offender statute thereby increasing the penalty for criminal acts committed prior to its enactment.
