P. v. S.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


110 Misc. 2d 978; 443 N.Y.S.2d 343; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3196



October 1, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother sought child support in the Family Court of New York, New York County, from respondent father.


OVERVIEW: The mother, an unmarried airline flight attendant, lived with the father and thereafter saw him approximately once a year. Besides her testimony that she had sexual intercourse only with the father during the period for conception of the child, the blood-matching test showed the high probability of the father's paternity. The mother, before the parties had sexual intercourse, advised the father that she was using birth control pills, when she was not using them or any other contraception. However, she denied recollection of any question by the father about contraception. The court determined that the mother's planned and intentional deceit barred her from financial benefit at the father's expense. Thus, it was constitutional to deny her application for child support, provided that the basic objective of satisfying the child's fair and reasonable needs could have nevertheless been met.


OUTCOME: Because a support order benefiting the mother would raise constitutional doubt under the Fourteenth Amendment, it should be denied. However, the court scheduled a hearing to determine whether the financial facts justified an entry of a support order.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Children Born out of Wedlock -- Paternity Proceeding -- Misrepresentation of Mother as to Contraception

The planned and intentional deceit of petitioner mother in deliberately and falsely representing to respondent putative father that she was using contraception provides an exception to respondent's otherwise unequivocal obligation to support his out-of-wedlock child (Family Ct Act, § 545) and bars petitioner from financial benefit at respondent's expense and the usual rule in apportioning support that parents must make equivalent financial sacrifices to support their child is thus inapplicable; in addition, a support order benefiting petitioner, enforceable by the court's contempt power, would condone, encourage, and put the "imprimatur of the State" upon interference with the constitutional right to reproductive choice and should be denied; however, since enforcement of the parental support duty is a compelling State interest that justifies diminution of the right to free procreative choice, respondent's duty of support cannot be entirely eliminated and a support order will be entered against him only if petitioner's means are insufficient to answer the child's fair and [***2] reasonable needs according to respondent's standard of living.

 

1895 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Fujishima v. Games Management Services


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


110 Misc. 2d 970; 443 N.Y.S.2d 323; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3195



September 28, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, the holder of an allegedly winning lottery ticket, brought an action against defendants, an authorized sales agent who sold the ticket and a lottery manager, to recover damages based on negligence, fraud, and breach of contract. The New York State Division of the Lottery (division) was permitted to intervene. The lottery manager and division filed motions for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The holder was informed that, although her copy of the ticket correctly noted the winning numbers, the ticket could not be considered a winner since the original had not been microfilmed at Lotto Central in accordance with the Lotto rules and regulations. She brought her action based on negligence, fraud, and breach of contract and sought to declare the microfilm rules unconstitutional. The court granted the motions for summary judgment filed by the manager and the division. The court held that: 1) the microfilm regulation was not violative of due process because it was rationally related to the purpose of the legislation; 2) the holder's conclusory allegations of fraud were legally insufficient to state a cause of action; and 3) the holder could not recover on theories of negligence, breach of contract, or bailment because the limitation of liability printed on the back side of the ticket was enforceable.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motions for summary judgment filed by the manager and division in the ticket holder's negligence, fraud, and breach of contract action.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Lotteries -- Rules of Lotto Game -- Microfilm Requirement

Plaintiff cannot be considered a $ 250,000 Lotto prize winner since the original of her $ 250,000 winning Lotto ticket had not been recorded on microfilm at the designated central station (Lotto Central) as required by the rules and regulations of the State Division of the Lottery (21 NYCRR 2817.1 et seq.) referred to on the face of the Lotto ticket which provide that no Lotto bet is valid unless the top or original of the three-part Lotto ticket is received from the authorized sales agent and then recorded on microfilm at Lotto Central prior to the weekly drawing of winning numbers; the microfilm requirement is a reasonable exercise of the power of the Division of the Lottery to assure the internal security and integrity of the Lotto game and is thus not violative of due process even though better or different means could have been found in establishing an internal security plan; plaintiff is not entitled to any recovery against the manager of the Lotto game since her purchase of the Lotto ticket constituted an acceptance of all the terms and conditions disclosed on the face of the ticket including a disclaimer [***2] of liability and notice that all tickets, in order to be eligible for the weekly drawing, must be on microfilm at Lotto Central.
