New York Bus Tours, Inc. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Bronx County


111 Misc. 2d 10; 443 N.Y.S.2d 309; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3214



September 22, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On a motion for summary judgment, plaintiff express bus companies asked the court to declare that defendant city and related officials had violated the equal protection clauses of the state and federal constitutions by selectively enforcing the utility tax, New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § QQ46-1.0(10). The city's cross-motion asserted tax deficiencies, argued a rational basis for the tax, and cited pending administrative actions.


OVERVIEW: New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § QQ46-1.0 (10) imposed a utility tax on the revenue derived by bus companies solely within the city; but limited fare bus companies were excluded from the tax. In practice, the tax was imposed on express bus companies and not on non-express bus companies. The city then allowed both kinds of companies to raise their rates in excess of the fare limit imposed by the tax law but collected the tax only from the express bus companies. The express bus companies filed an action under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 and under the equal protection clauses of the state and federal constitutions. The city argued that there was a rational basis for its application of the tax and that the express bus companies were bound by administrative review. The court disagreed. To challenge a specific assessment, the administrative hearing was an appropriate forum. To challenge a taxing authority's jurisdiction on the grounds of a statute's unconstitutionality, the court was the correct forum. Review of the legislative history of the statute in light of the highly unequal application of the law proved a discriminatory motive. The court granted judgment for the bus companies.


OUTCOME: The court held that the city had violated the equal protection clause by selectively enforcing the utility tax against some bus companies and not others. The court held that the express bus companies were not bound to seek constitutional remedies in an administrative forum that addressed other issues and denied the city's motion. The court granted the express bus companies' motion for summary judgment and enjoined tax enforcement against them.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Taxation -- New York City Utility Tax -- Unlawful Selective Enforcement against Express Bus Companies

The selective enforcement by the City of New York of its utility tax on the gross receipts of private omnibus companies against plaintiffs, franchised bus companies providing express bus service, but not against other nonexpress bus operators which should have lost their exemption from the tax as limited fare bus companies in December, 1975 when they increased their fares to 50 cents (see Administrative Code of City of New York, § QQ46-1.0, subd 10), unlawfully and improperly discriminates against plaintiffs and establishes a cause of action for damages under the Federal civil rights statute (US Code, tit 42, § 1983); the selective imposition of the tax against plaintiffs must be deemed intentional and not due to mere administrative auditing delays in view of the city's proposal to amend the utility tax statute so as to retroactively provide the nonexpress operators with the exemption from the tax enjoyed by them prior to their fare increase thereby acknowledging the current unequal selective enforcement of the utility tax law; plaintiffs need not await determination of their [***2] administrative remedies since resort need not be had to the method of review prescribed in the taxing statute when the constitutionality of the tax law is in issue.

 

1897 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Metz v. Hempstead


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


111 Misc. 2d 27; 443 N.Y.S.2d 316; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3217



September 22, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, village and others, filed a motion for an order granting summary judgment dismissing plaintiff property owners' complaint based upon the affirmative defense of the statute of limitations at N.Y. C.P.L.R. 217. The property owners possessed real property within the village and requested a declaratory judgment that the water rate structure passed by the village board of trustees was void and ineffective and to restrain its enforcement.


OVERVIEW: The village had not board of water commissioner, but the activities of such a board were carried out by the village board of trustees by simple resolution. The property owners brought the declaratory judgment action after the village board of trustees at a special meeting increased the water rate. Defendants claimed that the four-month statute of limitations of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 217 was applicable and therefore that the action was untimely filed. The court disagreed and denied defendants motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint. The court held that N.Y. C.P.L.R. 217 was not applicable. The court held that the village's action, whether labeled as a resolution or otherwise, completely altered the water rate structure for the entire village on a permanent basis, and the fixing of rates was a legislative act not subject to review by way of a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding. The court further held that the action was governed by the statute of limitations in N.Y. C.P.L.R. 213(1) and that the action was therefore timely commenced.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motion for an order granting summary judgment dismissing the property owners' action, in which they sought a declaratory judgment that the water rate structure passed by the village board of trustees was void and ineffective and to restrain its enforcement.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Limitation of Actions -- What Statute Governs

A motion by defendant village to dismiss the complaint in an action to declare a village resolution void and ineffective on the ground that the action is barred by the four-month Statute of Limitations ( CPLR 217) since the challenged resolution was reviewable by way of an article 78 proceeding is denied; inasmuch as the resolution completely altered the water rate structure for the village on a permanent basis, it may be deemed a legislative act and, as such, is not subject to review by way of an article 78 proceeding; accordingly, the period of limitations for plaintiff's declaratory judgment action is governed by CPLR 213 (subd 1) and the action was timely commenced.

 

1898 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Alacqua v. Baudanza


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Special Term, Kings County


110 Misc. 2d 774; 443 N.Y.S.2d 792; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3159



September 14, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: This automobile negligence case between plaintiff claimant and defendant litigant was noticed for trial in the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York). The supreme court transferred the case to the court under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 325(d). The litigant filed a motion to strike the action from the court's calendar and to reduce the ad damnum clause to $ 10,000, or alternatively to estop the claimant from claiming an ad damnum clause in excess of $ 10,000.


OVERVIEW: The case was transferred in May 1978. In April 1981, the court, under a different judge, granted the litigant's motion for an order to stay all proceedings for 30 days pending the litigant's motion to the supreme court for an order remanding the case to the supreme court pursuant to Rule 325(d). The 30-day period expired and no motion was made in the supreme court to remand the case and the decision was never appealed. The court held that with the failure of compliance with N.Y. C.P.L.R. 1012(b) and N.Y. Exec. Law § 71 the constitutionality of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 325(d) was not fully tested by inasmuch as it deprived the Attorney-General an opportunity to intervene in support of the constitutionality of that statute; therefore, the courts April 1981 decision was null and void. The court denied the motion, holding that: (1) the matter must be retained as a transfer pursuant to Rule 325(d) with the amount of recovery limited to that demanded in the complaint; and (2) N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 15(d) related to those actions initiated and originating in the lower court and that section was not applicable to cases transferred to the court pursuant to N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 19.


OUTCOME: The court denied the litigant's motion to strike the action from the court's calendar and to reduce the ad damnum clause to $ 10,000, or alternatively to estop the claimant from claiming an ad damnum clause in excess of $ 10,000. The court scheduled the matter for trial on October 13, 1981.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Courts -- Transfer of Action to Other Court -- Monetary Jurisdictional Limitation

The prior order of a former Judge of the New York City Civil Court declaring unconstitutional CPLR 325 (subd [d]), which allows the Supreme Court to remove an action to a court of limited jurisdiction without consent with the judgment being subject to the "limitation of monetary jurisdiction of the court in which the action was originally commenced" when such removal is authorized by rules promulgated by the Appellate Division, is a nullity due to the court's failure to notify the Attorney-General of the challenge to the constitutionality of the statute ( CPLR 1012, subd [b]; Executive Law, § 71) and it cannot, therefore, be used by defendant as a basis for limiting plaintiff's ad damnum clause to the Civil Court's $ 10,000 monetary limitation (NY Const, art VI, § 15, subd b) following the transfer of the action from the Supreme Court; in any event, CPLR 325 (subd [d]) is constitutional since the elimination of calendar congestion and backlog is a public policy of the State authorized by constitutional provisions which allow the Supreme Court to transfer actions to lower courts having [***2] jurisdiction but for the amount demanded and further authorize the Legislature "to provide that the verdict or judgments in actions and proceedings so transferred shall not be subject to the limitation of monetary jurisdiction" of the transferee court (NY Const, art VI, § 19, subds a, k).

 

1899 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Beaudoin v. Tilley


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Rensselaer County


110 Misc. 2d 696; 442 N.Y.S.2d 914; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3144



September 14, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the Commissioner of Social Services, filed an action seeking support from respondent husband for his wife and unborn child. The husband alleged that he was not the father of the unborn child and requested, pursuant to N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 418, a human leukocyte antigen (HLA) blood grouping test.


OVERVIEW: The court noted that pursuant to a recent amendment to § 418, 1981 N.Y. Laws 9, §§ 1,2, HLA tests were available in support proceedings at the discretion of the court. The court further noted that the amendment failed to address the issue of who was responsible for the payment for such test. The husband contended that N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 418, as amended, was unconstitutional in that the payment provisions as applied to the husband were discriminatory and denied equal protection of the law in that they were gender based and not based on an important governmental objective nor substantially related to an important governmental objective. The court rejected the husband's contention, holding that § 418, as amended, was constitutional. The court granted the husband's request of the HLA test and determined that the husband was financially able to contribute towards the cost of the test. The court directed the Department of Social Services to pay the amount remaining toward the cost of the test because the child was a recipient of public assistance.


OUTCOME: The court granted the husband's request for an HLA blood grouping test but required the husband to pay for a portion of the cost of the test.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Parent and Child -- Support -- Responsibility for Payment of Blood Grouping Test

A request for a blood grouping test, specifically a human leucocyte antigen (HLA)test, made pursuant to section 418 of the Family Court Act (as amd by L 1981, ch 9, § 1), which allows the court to exercise discretion in deciding whether such a test should be ordered, is granted, and respondent husband, who requested the test in response to a demand for support by the county social services agency, is found financially available to contribute the sum of $ 250 for and towards the cost of the test, but unable financially to pay for the entire test. The payment provisions of section 418 as applied to the respondent are constitutional and do not deny equal protection of the law in that they are gender based and not based on or substantially related to an important governmental objective; the purpose of the statute is not to provide a means by which a respondent can be cleared of a false claim of paternity, but rather the discretion given the court in allowing the test and determining whether its results should be received in evidence indicates a legislative purpose of protecting the children of the [***2] parties from spurious claims of a father designed to avoid or delay paying support, a legitimate governmental objective, particularly where, as here, mother and child are recipients of public assistance.

 

1900 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Board of Education v. Carey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


110 Misc. 2d 580; 442 N.Y.S.2d 1010; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3127



September 8, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion of plaintiff and plaintiff-intervenors for summary judgment is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff board of education filed a motion for summary judgment in its declaratory judgment action challenging changes in New York statutes that affected the funding of the state's health insurance fund.


OVERVIEW: Various boards of education filed a declaratory judgment action seeking to withdraw from the state's health insurance fund (HIRRF) and claiming that the impact of a new statutory scheme designed to fund the state's employee health insurance fund was to force a school district to remain in the state health insurance plan or be faced with a "two-fold" decrease in their state aid occasioned by a reduction in aid equal to the HIRRF funds and by the state withholding moneys to repay the amount of the school district's default. The court held that another statute, enacted the same day as the funding statute, did not effectively repeal the funding statute. The court noted that no cogent reason was advanced for the application of the middle tier scrutiny, and therefore the case should have been determined after due inquiry as to whether there was a rational basis for the distinction between school districts which remained in the health plan after March 25, 1977 and those who withdrew prior to that date. The court found that these were questions of fact, which could not have been determined on a motion for summary judgment.


OUTCOME: The court denied the summary judgment motion of the boards of education.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- School Districts -- Reduction in State Aid Apportionment

Chapter 55 of the Laws of 1977 (State Finance Law, § 99-c), which provides for the withholding of State aid upon the failure of municipalities or school districts to repay moneys advanced from the health insurance reserve receipts fund (HIRRF), was not repealed by implication by chapter 71 (§ 8, subd b) of the Laws of 1977, which was enacted on the same day and provides for a reduction in State aid to school districts electing after March 25, 1977 not to receive such funds in the amount of the distribution they would have received and for an annual reduction in aid to those districts receiving HIRRF moneys; in fact, the enactments indicate a harmony of purpose, since chapter 71 provides for a loss of State aid if the school district does not accept a distribution from the HIRRF, thereby placing that body in no better position than one who accepts the distribution and, pursuant to the provisions of chapter 55, is later required to return some or all of that portion of the aid it represents. Further, summary [***2] judgment is inappropriate on the constitutional challenge addressed to the distinction drawn between school districts that elect not to participate in the insurance plan prior to March 25, 1977 and suffer no reduction in State aid and those which so elect after said date and have their State aid reduced; accordingly, the matter should be determined after due inquiry as to whether there was a rational basis for the distinction.

 

1901 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Butler


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Buffalo


110 Misc. 2d 843; 443 N.Y.S.2d 40; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3173



September 8, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the charge against the defendant is hereby dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with loitering in a public place for the purpose of engaging in or soliciting another person to engage in deviate sexual intercourse, N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35, subd. 3. Defendant challenged the constitutionality of the statute.


OVERVIEW: The court noted that defendant, a known prostitute, was seen waving at cars at a public intersection, approaching a stopped car, and getting in. The police later found defendant parked nearby engaging in deviate sexual intercourse with the co-defendant. The court noted that defendant and the co-defendant were not married. The court found that the term "deviate sexual intercourse" was defined only once in the Penal Law and that was as certain types of sexual conduct between persons not married to one another. Therefore, the court concluded that what is proscribed § 240.35, subd. 3, was loitering involving parties not married to one another.The court held that § 240.35, subd. 3, was unconstitutional because it violated the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment in that it prohibited such conduct between unmarried persons but not married persons. The court also found that the statute was unconstitutionally vague because, by allowing the police wide discretion in implementing the statute, it encouraged arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement of the law.


OUTCOME: The court declared the statute unconstitutional and dismissed the charge against defendant.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Loitering -- Loitering for Purpose of Engaging in Deviate Sexual Intercourse

In view of People v Onofre (51 NY2d 476), which struck down as unconstitutional the consensual sodomy statute which prohibited persons not married to one another from engaging in deviate sexual intercourse, the statute prohibiting loitering in a public place for the purpose of engaging in or soliciting another person to engage in deviate sexual intercourse is likewise unconstitutional since it is violative of the equal protection clause and unconstitutionally vague when used as a means to fight prostitution in that it allows the police unfettered discretion in determining who is or is not to be arrested as a prostitute or prostitute's customer thereby encouraging arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement of the law; laws against prostitution and loitering for the purpose of prostitution constitute adequate safeguards against open displays of sexual conduct that offend the public.

 

1902 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Menna v. New York City Employees' Retirement System


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


110 Misc. 2d 1065; 443 N.Y.S.2d 574; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3206



September 4, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the subject article 78 petition is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee filed a motion for an order to review, amend, and set aside respondent agency's administrative determination denying an accident disability retirement application as arbitrary, capricious, and contrary to law.


OVERVIEW: The employee became physically incapacitated while removing a spare tire from the trunk of a patrol car. He alleged that the agency's medical board approved his application for accidental disability retirement benefits, but the recommendation was "laid over" by the agency's board of trustees. The board of trustees directed its medical board to apply a narrower definition of "accident" relating to on-the-job injury and, upon application of the new definition and re-evaluation, the medical board denied the employee's application. The court granted the petition. The court found that while it was the duty of the board of trustees to instruct the medical board upon the proper definition of "accident," the prior definition in effect at the time of the injury and the employee's application should have fairly, reasonably, and equitably governed. The court determined that revising retroactively the definition of "accident" under the facts and circumstances impaired and diminished the employee's constitutionally vested interest, and thus the action of the agency's board of trustees was arbitrary, capricious, and contrary to law.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petition.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service -- Retirement and Pension Benefits -- On-the-Job "Accident"

Respondent board of trustees of the New York City Employees' Retirement System acted arbitrarily, capriciously and contrary to law in overruling the original report of its medical board approving petitioner's application for accidental disability retirement benefits, petitioner, a Housing Authority police officer, having become physically incapacitated while removing a spare tire from the trunk of a patrol car, and then denying petitioner's application after directing the medical board to apply a new narrower definition of "accident" relating to on-the-job injury requiring the "intervention of an external, unexpected, and unusual fortuitous event proximately causing the injury (Administrative Code of City of New York, § B3-40.0), since the definition of "accident" in effect at the time of petitioner's injury which only required that the injury be the "natural and proximate" result of city service, should govern petitioner's application and retroactively revising the definition of "accident" may unconstitutionally impair and diminish petitioner's [***2] vested pension interest (NY Const, art V, § 7).

 

1903 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Delgado


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, Bronx County


110 Misc. 2d 492; 442 N.Y.S.2d 748; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3111



August 24, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with first degree robbery, second degree robbery, first degree criminal use of a firearm, and fourth degree criminal use of a weapon. At defendant's arraignment, the State brought an oral motion for an order authorizing the in-court seizure of defendant's sneakers. Defendant opposed the motion.


OVERVIEW: The State contended that the sneakers were required for the purpose of enabling the police department to make a comparison with a sneaker print left atop a bank counter at the crime scene. Defendant argued that the proper procedure for seizing the sneakers as evidence was by written demand to produce pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 240.30, that it was not the proper function of the court to assist the State in preparing and prosecuting the case, and that the seizure would have been violative of defendant's Fourth and Fifth Amendment rights. The court rejected defendant's contentions and granted the State's motion. The court said that, in light of the existence of exigent circumstances, in-court seizure of the sneakers was appropriate. The court noted that § 240.30 was inapplicable because it permitted discovery on demand only where a defendant was charged by indictment, superior court information, prosecutor's information, or information. The statute was not available to the State in the instant matter because defendant was charged by felony complaint and was awaiting possible felony hearing or grand jury consideration.


OUTCOME: The court granted the State's motion for an order authorizing the in-court seizure of defendant's sneakers.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Warrantless In-Court Seizure of Defendant's Sneakers

The court, upon the prosecutor's arraignment-stage oral application, may properly order the postarrest, preindictment in-court seizure of defendant's sneakers without a written demand to produce ( CPL 240.30) for comparison with a sneaker "print" allegedly left atop a bank counter during the commission of the robbery since, given both the urgency of the seizure and its in-court nature, any court-imposed requirement that the People proceed by written search warrant to seize evidence in plain view is inappropriate inasmuch as such an application would impede the truth-finding function by exposing potentially admissible evidence to loss or alteration; the seizure order in open court did not constitute improper judicial assistance to the People in preparing and prosecuting the case against defendant; the seizure of defendant's sneakers falls outside the scope of Fifth Amendment guarantees against self incrimination since the seizure does not involve testimonial compulsion nor did the seizure violate defendant's Fourth Amendment right to be free from unreasonable searches and seizures since [***2] defendant had no logical expectation of privacy.

 

1904 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Church of St. Francis de Sales


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


110 Misc. 2d 511; 442 N.Y.S.2d 741; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3115



August 20, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner church submitted an application to the court for approval of a contract to sell a building to respondent purchaser under N.Y. Not-For-Profit Corp. Law § 511. Prior to the petition, the seller commenced an action for specific performance of the contract but different appraisals of the property were submitted. The court held a hearing on evidence offered by the church, the purchaser, and another potential buyer.


OVERVIEW: The seller, pursuant to § 511, which provided that a religious corporation could not sell any real property without leave of court, submitted an application for approval of a contract to sell a building to the purchaser for $ 2 million, with an amendment for an additional $ 200,000 to be paid by the purchaser if the transfer occurred by a specific date. The purchaser filed an action for specific performance and a hearing was held. Upon review of the petition, the court approved the amended petition and ordered for the transfer of title to the purchaser. The court held that the Not-For-Profit Corporation law set forth two issues to be considered by the court: (1) the fairness and reasonableness of the terms of the transaction, and (2) whether the sale would benefit the petitioner or promote the interests of its members. The court, after evaluating the appraisal reports and testimony related to other offers, ruled that the offer by the purchaser was within the range of the fair market value of the property and that the conveyance would benefit the parish because the property was underutilized and been shown to many other prospective purchasers.


OUTCOME: The court approved the petition submitted by the church, as amended, to include the increased offer by the purchaser to meet the standards of the applicable law, and the church was directed to arrange for transfer of title.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Religious Corporations and Associations -- Judicial Approval of Sale of Real Property -- Market Value at Time of Contract

In deciding whether to approve a contract for the sale of real property by a religious corporation (Religious Corporations Law, § 12; Not-For-Profit Corporation Law, § 511), the court must first determine the fairness and reasonableness of the purchase price and other terms of the transaction as of the time of the making of the contract and must then determine whether the sale would benefit the petitioner or promote the interests of its members as of the time of consideration of the petition for approval of the contract since confirmation of a contract that meets the test of "fair and reasonable at the time it was made" will guarantee to church transactions security and reliability which will give the church the benefit of a firm contract; accordingly, since the fair market value of the premises as of the contract date was in the area of $ 2,000,000, objectants' evidence as to another offer being an unreliable measure of market value, the amended contract price of $ 2,200,000 is within the range of fair market value and otherwise meets the standards of [***2] the applicable law for approval.

 

1905 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


White River Paper Co. v. Ashmont Tissue, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Special Term, Bronx County


110 Misc. 2d 373; 441 N.Y.S.2d 960; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3094



August 17, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiff's application for the ex parte order is denied because its attorney is not authorized to appear.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff paper company sought an ex parte order to permit it to file a summons nunc pro tunc under N.Y. City Civ. Ct. Act § 411 in its action against defendant tissue company. The question was whether the paper company's attorney, admitted to practice in New York but lacking an office in the state, was authorized to appear.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff paper company filed an action against defendant tissue company. The paper company's attorney was admitted to practice in New York but maintained his only office in Pennsylvania. The paper company sought an ex parte order to file a summons nunc pro tunc, pursuant to City Civ. Ct. Act § 411. The court held that: (1) N.Y. Jud. Law § 470 provided that an attorney admitted to practice in New York but who resided out of state could practice in New York only if he maintained an office in New York; (2) the holding of the unconstitutionality of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 9406(2), in its requirement that an applicant for admission to practice be an actual resident of the state of New York, did not affect the New York office requirement of N.Y. Jud. Law § 470; (3) a resident could obviously employ his home as his office, and there was no statutory requirement of an office for a resident; and (4) the New York office requirement was not discriminatory against nonresidents because it placed them in the same position as residents.


OUTCOME: The paper company's application for an ex parte order to permit it to file a summons nunc pro tunc in its action against the tissue company was denied.


HEADNOTES

Attorney and Client -- Nonresident Attorneys -- Local Office Requirement

Although a nonresident attorney may now be admitted to the New York Bar and may practice law in this State following admission to practice, a nonresident attorney admitted to practice in this State must maintain an office in New York in order to appear as an attorney of record for a party without regard to whether he resides in an adjoining or nonadjoining State (Judiciary Law, § 470); the fact that the nonresident must also maintain a residence and/or office elsewhere does not mean he is being discriminated against in the State of New York, since the requirement of a New York location does not result in disparate treatment, but places the nonresident in no different position than a resident.

 

1906 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cortez


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, New York County


110 Misc. 2d 652; 442 N.Y.S.2d 873; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3137



August 13, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The Grand Jury minutes are therefore legally insufficient to establish concealability with respect to each of the defendants and the indictment is therefore dismissed. 13



13 The court wishes to express its appreciation to the following people for their generous co-operation: H. G. Williams, retired president of the Browning Corp., Morgan, Utah; Robert Sears, Senior Associate Technical Editor, "The American Rifleman"; Asst. District Attorney Elizabeth Marsh, who surveyed the laws relating to sawed-off shotguns in every State and under Federal law; and Lael Scott of the Cardozo Law School, whose research efforts were invaluable.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were indicted for criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree. They filed a motion to inspect the grand jury minutes on the question of what size a sawed-off shotgun had to be before it qualified as a prohibited "firearm" under N.Y. Penal Law § 265.00(3).


OVERVIEW: Defendants were indicted on charges of criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree. The weapon was a .12 gauge Browning semiautomatic shotgun loaded with four live rounds. Both the barrel and the stock had been cut down. The barrel was 13 3/8 inches in length and the over-all length was 26 3/8 inches. Both defendants were 5 feet 4 inches tall. The primary question on defendants' motion to inspect the grand jury minutes was what size a sawed-off shotgun had to be before it qualified as a prohibited "firearm" under N.Y. Penal Law § 265.00(3). Defendants claimed that the statute was unconstitutionally vague, and that the evidence before the grand jury was insufficient as a matter of law to show that the weapon in question was capable of being concealed upon the person. The court concluded that the law in New York as to the concealability of sawed-off shotguns was unclear. Therefore, the grand jury minutes were legally insufficient to establish concealability with respect to each of defendants and the indictment was therefore dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the indictments against defendants for criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Possession of Weapon -- Concealability of Sawed-Off Shotgun

The statute proscribing possession of a "sawed-off shotgun * * * of a size which may be concealed upon the person" (Penal Law, § 265.00, subd 3) is not unconstitutionally vague, but the absence of any objective standard as to concealability requires legislative action that would prohibit the possession of a sawed-off shotgun with a barrel of less than 18 inches or an over-all length of less than 26 inches; based upon prior case law, any sawed-off shotgun up to 18 inches [***2] in length is deemed to be presumptively concealable, any sawed-off shotgun between 18 and 24 inches in length presumptively creates a question of fact for Judge or jury and any sawed-off shotgun in excess of 24 inches is presumptively not concealable in the absence of special circumstances relating to dress or other factors which manifest concealability; accordingly, in light of such criteria, the evidence before the Grand Jury was insufficient to show that the sawed-off shotgun 26 3/8 inches long, approximately half the height of each of the defendants, seized from defendants' car was capable of being concealed upon the person and the indictment is therefore dismissed.

 

1907 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Sanders


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Buffalo


110 Misc. 2d 398; 442 N.Y.S.2d 46; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3099



August 12, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] This court therefore holds that the subject statute is, in its entirety, constitutional and has denied defendant's motion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss a charge of loitering on the ground that the information against her was defective because the statute defining the offense charged was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the statute was constitutional in its entirety and denied the motion. The court determined that the statute was explicitly limited to public conduct and was, therefore, outside the ambit of the "right of privacy." The court also determined that equal protection of the law was not denied because the marital status distinction inherent in the phrase "deviate sexual intercourse" was complemented by the phrase "or other sexual behavior of a deviate nature," leaving the statute applicable without regard to marital status.


OUTCOME: The court found that statute constitutional and denied defendant's motion.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Loitering

Subdivision 3 of section 240.35 of the Penal Law, pursuant to which defendant was charged with loitering "in a public place for the purpose of engaging * * * in deviate sexual intercourse or other sexual behavior of a deviate nature" is not unconstitutional even though section 130.38 of the Penal Law, prohibiting "deviate sexual intercourse" as defined in subdivision 2 of section 130.00 of the Penal Law, has been declared unconstitutional as violative of the rights to privacy and to equal protection of the laws. The subject statute is explicitly limited to public conduct and is therefore outside the ambit of the right to privacy, and the marital status distinction inherent in the phrase "deviate sexual intercourse" is complemented by the phrase "or other sexual behavior of a deviate nature", leaving the statute applicable without regard to marital status.

 

1908 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re South Bronx Neighborhood Dev. Plan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Bronx County


110 Misc. 2d 571; 442 N.Y.S.2d 869; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3124



August 7, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Condemnee property owners challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 3-a (2) which limited the prejudgment interest arising out of a condemnation award.


OVERVIEW: The property owners challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 3-a (2) which limited the prejudgment interest arising out of a condemnation action. The court found that interest rates had consistently been too far above the statutory amount to find that it was judicially adequate and found that the rate provided by the statute was unfair and unconstitutional. Based upon current interest rates the court determined a fair interest rate to be utilized in order to satisfy the constitutional requirement of just compensation.


OUTCOME: The court found that the interest rate was unconstitutional and set a higher rate.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Eminent Domain -- Interest on Award -- Constitutionality of Statutory Rate

Subdivision 2 of section 3-a of the General Municipal Law, which limits prejudgment interest arising out of a condemnation award to 6%, results in constitutionally unjust payment. While interest rates fluctuated around 6% during the 1972-1977 period, such rates have consistently been too far above 6% during the last four years to find that statutory rate judicially adequate, with medium-term public securities providing the basis for calculating the proper interest rate; accordingly, a rate of 9% is "fair" and under the circumstances must be utilized for the 1978-1981 period in order to satisfy the constitutional requirement of just compensation.

 

1909 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


English v. Lefever


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Rockland County


110 Misc. 2d 220; 442 N.Y.S.2d 385; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3068



August 7, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, the town, and county voters, filed motions for summary judgment in actions for combined declaratory and injunctive relief against defendant Rockland County Board of Elections (board), that sought a declaration that the 18-member Rockland County Legislature, and the 20-member legislature created by county resolution, violated the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution and N.Y. Const. art. I, §§ 1, 11.


OVERVIEW: A previous reapportionment case that involved the board had proceeded through the New York courts and the United States Supreme Court. The board had adopted a resolution that provided for five legislative districts based upon the existing boundaries of five towns, with each town to elect a varying number of legislators based upon their respective populations. Although the plan permitted a total voting inequality of 11.9 percent, it was approved by all of the courts that considered it. It changed each time census results were published. When the changes required by the census results were made the total voting inequality had increased to 29.3 percent. Plaintiffs sought to prevent the election and to have the court impose a plan of reapportionment or order the board to produce one. The court granted plaintiffs summary judgment to the extent that the plans were unconstitutional violations, ordered the board to produce a constitutional plan, but allowed the election to proceed because there was no effective method of timely relief. The voting inequality was an unconstitutional violation of the requirement of one person, one vote.


OUTCOME: The court awarded summary judgment to plaintiffs to the extent that it declared the 18-member legislature and the 20-member legislature created pursuant to resolution violated the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution and N.Y. Const. art. I, §§ 1, 11, and ordered the county to adopt a plan of reapportionment which eliminated discrimination and provided for fair representation. The court did not enjoin the pending election.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Counties -- Reapportionment

1. A plan to implement court-ordered reapportionment of county legislative districts, which was passed by resolution of the county board of supervisors following court disapproval of the board's original proposal 10 days prior to the effective date of section 10 (subd 1, par [ii], cl a, subcl [13]) of the Municipal Home Rule Law, which permits the county to reapportion itself subject to a permissive referendum, is not subject to section 10 (subd 1, par [ii], cl a, subcl [13]), section 23 (subd 2, pars f, g) or section 24 (subd 2, par j) of the Municipal Home Rule Law, is valid under subdivision 2 of section 22 of that law and, further, does not deprive plaintiffs taxpayers of a franchise right, to wit, the right to vote at a referendum; as of the effective date of the legislation calling for a permissive referendum, the judicial process which culminated in the approval of the resolution was extant and the court had already disapproved one reapportionment plan and, in addition, although section 23 of the Municipal Home Rule Law required a mandatory referendum thereon, the final plan, which was approved by the court and was affirmed in all [***2] New York appellate courts and in the United States Supreme Court, did not so provide.

Elections -- Certification of Offices to be Filled

2. The requirements of section 4-106 of the Election Law that the County Clerk transmit to the board of elections a certificate stating each county officer to be voted for at a general election at least eight months before such election and notify the board of any vacancy in an office required to be filled at the next general election within three days after the occurrence of the vacancy does not bar a County Clerk, who timely certified 10 open seats in the county legislature in an upcoming election, from later certifying two additional legislators to be voted for at the election in an amended certificate, where final census counts indicating the possibility of adding two additional seats were not received until after the eight-month deadline and, moreover, the board of elections was aware of the need to fill the two additional seats at the coming election since it was the board of elections that acted pursuant to a reapportionment plan to add the two seats after the county board of supervisors failed to act thereon.

Counties --  [***3] Reapportionment

3. A court-approved formula for reapportionment of county legislative districts that attempts to preserve traditional town boundaries within the county and which, as originally adopted, resulted in an 18-member legislature and an 11.9% total voting inequality deviation, but results in a 26.4% deviation for an 18-member legislature when applied to 1980 census figures and a deviation of 29.3% for a 20-member legislature, violates the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution and sections 1 and 11 of article I of the New York Constitution; however, although the plan is constitutionally unacceptable, the court will not enjoin the imminent election of a 20-member legislature, nor will it mandate that the county adopt a specific alternate plan without affording the legislators an additional opportunity to discharge this obligation themselves. Accordingly, the county legislature is ordered to adopt a plan of reapportionment which will comply with constitutional standards within 90 days.

 

1910 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Tobias v. College Towne Homes, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Richmond County


110 Misc. 2d 287; 442 N.Y.S.2d 380; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3078



August 6, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, summary judgment is granted to the defendant City of New York, dismissing the complaint.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff mortgagee brought an action to foreclose a mortgage on certain property because of defaults in the payment of installments of interest by the mortgagor. Defendant city claimed unencumbered title to the subject premises as the result of an in rem foreclosure, which was instituted in 1975 for tax arrears.


OVERVIEW: The mortgagee alleged that the city's claim of title was a nullity, so such claim could not defeat the action to foreclose the mortgage. The court granted summary judgment to the city, dismissing the complaint. The court determined that notice requirements were in conformity with constitutional due process. The court concluded that the Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation (FDIC) was not entitled to have tax bills or notice of foreclosure action mailed to it because of its failure to register its interest in the property. The court noted that the city did not violate due process requirements because the recording of the deed under the old block designation should have brought realization to any interested party that the subject premises could be involved and affected. Therefore, the city's error in recording the tax foreclosure deed was not of such magnitude as to mislead a person making a diligent title search. The court determined that the right to foreclose vested before the FDIC held the mortgage lien and thus the foreclosure could nullify the lien, so the assignment from the FDIC to the mortgagee conveyed nothing more than a nonexistent mortgage.


OUTCOME: The court granted summary judgment to the city, dismissing the mortgagee's complaint.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Tax Liens, Tax Sales and Tax Titles -- Extinguishment of Mortgage Lien of Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation

Plaintiff, who, in November, 1979, became the assignee of a mortgage previously assigned to the Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation (FDIC) in October, 1974 as the receiver of the defunct mortgagee bank, the City of New York having recorded a foreclosure deed for the subject premises in January, 1977 without service of any notice of foreclosure upon FDIC as the result of an in rem tax foreclosure proceeding commenced in October, 1975, is barred by the two-year conclusive presumption of regularity of all tax foreclosure proceedings (Administrative Code of City of New York, § D17-12.0, subd b) from challenging the legality of the city's tax title since the FDIC's mortgage lien was conclusively foreclosed in January, 1979; in any event, the city's tax foreclosure proceedings did not effect a deprivation of procedural due process since the FDIC was not entitled to have tax bills or notice of the foreclosure action mailed to it because of its failure [***2] to register with the city as the holder of the mortgage lien, the inadvertent recording of the deed under the property's old block designation was not of such magnitude as to mislead a person making a diligent title search and, lastly, the city's tax foreclosure validly nullified the lien of the FDIC inasmuch as the city's right to foreclose for three years' tax arrears vested before the FDIC held the mortgage lien and the fact that the city did not begin the in rem tax foreclosure proceeding until after the FDIC acquired the mortgage lien is of no consequence.

 

1911 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Cass v. State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


109 Misc. 2d 107; 442 N.Y.S.2d 1005; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2361



July 24, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, former and present judges, sought summary judgment in their request for injunctive relief and a declaration that the Unified Court Budget Act (Act), N.Y. Law §§ 966 (1976), 55 (1979), 881 (1980), enacted by defendants, state, comptroller, and chief administrator, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Former and present judges brought three separate actions against the state, comptroller, and chief administrator, seeking summary judgment on their request for injunctive relief and a declaration that the Act was unconstitutional. They claimed they were entitled to retroactive salary payments. The issue before the court was whether §§ 966 (1976), 55 (1979), and 881 (1980) violated the Equal Protection Clauses of the federal and state constitutions by providing the former and present judges lower salaries than granted to other judges of the same courts performing identical duties. The court ruled that the complaints stated viable claims for declaratory relief. The court held that the state, comptroller, and chief administrator failed to present proof of facts contrary to the claims against them. Finally, the court concluded that there was no governmental objective advanced by the classification, which contravened the purpose of the enactment of a unified court budget. Summary judgment was granted to the comptroller, dismissing the complaint against him. The court granted the summary judgment against the state and chief administrator.


OUTCOME: The court granted the summary judgment as to the comptroller, dismissing the complaint against him. Summary judgment was granted against the state and chief administrator.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Judges -- Disparity in Judicial Salaries

1. The Unified Court Budget Act (L 1976, ch 966; L 1979, ch 55; L 1980, ch 881) violates the equal protection clauses of the Federal and State Constitutions insofar as plaintiffs, 109 named Judges who hold or held the positions of Family, County and/or Surrogate Court Judge in various counties throughout the State, are provided lower salaries than granted to other Judges of the same courts performing similar duties, and plaintiffs are entitled to receive retroactive salary payments from April 1, 1977 to the present in order to correct the disparity. Pursuant to the Unified Court Budget Act, it was provided that, commencing April 1, 1977, salaries of Family, County and Surrogate Court Judges would be placed on the State payroll and judicial personnel henceforth would be State employees (L 1976, ch 966, § 2, adding Judiciary Law, former § 220, subd 6, renum § 39, subd 6); the differences in pay attributable to local government discretion and funding prior to the takeover were continued and the disparity increased by subsequent legislation (L 1979, ch 55; L 1980, ch 881). The basis for classification of salaries contained in the Unified [***2] Court Budget Act is purely a vestige of local politics unrelated in a rational manner to population, case load, jurisdiction or any other relevant criteria; moreover, no legitimate governmental objective is advanced by the classification, which directly contravenes the purpose behind the movement to a unified court system and the enactment of a unified court budget.

Parties -- Proper Parties

2. In an action to declare a judicial salary plan enacted by the Legislature unconstitutional, the Supreme Court lacks jurisdiction of a claim for money damages against the State, and a claim against the State Comptroller should be dismissed; however, the State is a proper party to an action to declare a statute unconstitutional, and, therefore, the Chief Administrator of the Courts is an appropriate party to an action by Judges to declare a legislative classification to be in violation of the equal protection clause.

Declaratory Judgments -- When Remedy Appropriate

3. A declaratory judgment action is the proper vehicle to test the constitutionality of a legislative act, and upon an appropriate record summary judgment is in order. When there is a proven claim that salary [***3] differentials violate the equal protection clause, the declaratory judgment thereon may declare that the plaintiffs are entitled to receive retroactive payments to correct the injustice.

 

1912 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Highland Falls v. Highlands


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Orange County


110 Misc. 2d 130; 441 N.Y.S.2d 903; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3050



July 24, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner village commenced a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding that sought to vacate the imposition of real estate taxes levied by respondent town and its officials upon an approximately five-acre parcel of property located in the village. The village further sought that it be granted an exemption from real estate taxes, and to stop any tax sale of the real estate.


OVERVIEW: As a preliminary issue on personal service, the court determined that although personal service upon a town could be obtained by service on the supervisor or clerk pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 311(5), a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding could be commenced by an order to show cause pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 7804(c), and notice could be given in the manner specified by the court pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 2214(d). Service was properly made pursuant to an order to show cause issued by a justice of the supreme court. Although the acreage at issue was initially taken by the village's condemnation proceedings, the condemnation was set aside upon application of a mortgagee, which order in turn was reversed by the appellate division. The court held that when the condemnation was first set aside the property did not revert to the prior owner's non-exempt use, because the order of the appellate division would have dated back to time from which the order was appealed; that is, the intervening order that set aside the condemnation was void, and in no way affected the village's title or their tax exempt ownership of the property.


OUTCOME: In the village's action that sought to vacate the imposition of real estate taxes against it by the town, the court canceled all such taxes assessed and the property at issue was stricken from the assessment rolls of the town. The town was further stayed from selling the property because the taxes were canceled.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Process -- Service of Process -- Service upon Town

1. Commencement of a CPLR article 78 proceeding challenging a tax levy by respondent town, pursuant to an order to show cause, by personal service upon an attorney who, although he represents the town on various matters including subject proceedings, is not the Town Attorney, and personal service of the petition and the order to show cause upon the assessor of taxes for the town and a member of the town board, neither of whom is a town officer, is effective; although personal service upon a town may be obtained by service on the supervisor or clerk ( CPLR 311, subd 5), an article 78 proceeding may be commenced by order to show cause ( CPLR 7804, subd [c]) and notice shall be given in the manner specified by the court ( CPLR 2214, subd [d]), and the manner of service effected herein has afforded more than the minimum due process requirements of notice and opportunity to defend to the respondent.

Limitation of Actions -- Proceeding against Body or Officer

2. A CPLR article 78 proceeding wherein petitioner village seeks to vacate the imposition of real estate taxes levied by respondent town upon a five-acre parcel [***2] of property located in the village which the village has resolved to use as a public park and to which parcel it obtained title in condemnation proceedings that resulted in litigation wherein the condemnation order was first vacated and later reinstated, during which interim period the parcel was listed on the tax rolls, is timely, although it was commenced approximately 22 months after the parcel was placed on the tax rolls and 18 months after the Appellate Division order reinstating the condemnation order was entered; the four-month Statute of Limitations set out in CPLR 217 for article 78 proceedings does not bar the proceeding, since the assessing officer originally acted without his jurisdiction in placing said exempt property (Real Property Tax Law, § 406, subd 1) on the tax rolls and the assessment was a nullity.

 

1913 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Shell Oil Co. v. New York State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


110 Misc. 2d 71; 441 N.Y.S.2d 595; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3042



July 15, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer sought a declaration that N.Y. Tax Law § 182 was unenforceable and void from its inception. It sought summary judgment against defendant New York State Tax Commission on causes of action which asserted that the provision was in violation of both the commerce and supremacy clauses of the Federal Constitution and that its enactment and implementation resulted in a denial of due process.


OVERVIEW: The challenged tax imposed a two percent gross profits tax on profits generated within the state and partially prevented the taxpayer and other oil companies from passing this additional tax on to the consumers. The "anti-pass through" provision had been invalidated. The court denied the taxpayer's motion. The court found that a review of the tax in question revealed that it met the applicable criteria and was not unconstitutional. The court determined that the taxpayer had the burden of proving that the statute was unconstitutional beyond a reasonable doubt and failed to meet that burden.


OUTCOME: The court denied the taxpayer's motion for summary judgment, but granted the Commission's motion for summary judgment to the extent that it sought a declaration that the tax was constitutional and validly enforceable.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Taxation -- Gross Profits Tax -- "Anti-Pass Through" Provision

Although the "anti-pass through" provision of section 182 of the Tax Law, which imposes a 2% gross profits tax on profits generated by certain oil companies in New York State and partially prevents plaintiff and other oil companies from passing this additional tax on to the consumers of New York State, is invalid as unconstitutional, the tax itself is constitutional and is enforceable from January 1, 1980 through November 10, 1980 according to the terms of an amendment to the legislation whereunder it was enacted, which provides that said tax be paid until 10 days after any decision invalidating the anti-pass through provision, thus exposing the consumers of this State to what may be termed a limited liability for increased prices at the gas pump (L 1980, ch 271, § 12, as amd by L 1980, ch 272, § 5); a State may impose a gross receipts tax upon corporations doing business in this State, if the tax is applicable to activity having a constitutional nexus herein, is fairly apportioned, does not discriminate against interstate commerce and is reasonably related to the services provided by the State.

 

1914 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Fort


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Syracuse


109 Misc. 2d 990; 441 N.Y.S.2d 357; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2505



July 14, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendant's motion to exclude identification evidence is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to exclude identification evidence on the ground that the prosecution's "blank notice" of intention to use the evidence did not comply with N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 710.30.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with criminal possession of stolen property, and the prosecution gave a notice of intention to use identification evidence, stating that "the evidence will consist of testimony regarding an observation of the defendant either at the time or place of the commission of the offense or upon some other occasion relevant to the case, to be given by a witness who has previously identified him." Such notice was called a "blank notice." Defendant moved to exclude the testimony on the ground that the blank notice was not sufficiently specific under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 710.30. The court denied the motion, but the court required the prosecution to comply with the plain meaning of § 710.30 by providing the name of the witness and the time, place, and manner of the identification. The court held that a blank notice did not meet the requirements of § 710.30(1)(b) and would not give defendant the necessary information to move to suppress the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to exclude the evidence but ordered the prosecution to provide proper notice containing the required specifics, holding that the blank notice did not comply with the statutory requirements.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Identification of Defendant -- "Blank" Notice of Intention to Offer Identification Evidence

A District Attorney's "blank notice" of intention to offer identification evidence at defendant's trial fails to meet the CPL 710.30 (subd 1) requirement that such notice, "specifying the evidence intended to be offered", be served within 15 days of arraignment and prior to trial, which requirement must be read as a direction that the notice contain, at a minimum, the name of the witness who made the previous identification, the time and place of the identification and the manner in which the identification was conducted; it is improper to rely on a discovery motion by defendant to ensure that he will be able to obtain further information, since that relief may be time barred when sought ( CPL 255.20) and, in any event, the practice places an unnecessary burden on the courts, and such a "blank notice" also fails to provide defendant with enough information to make a proper pretrial suppression motion ( CPL 710.60). Further, the District Attorney's failure to provide the proper information due to his mistaken [***2] belief that it was more appropriately sought through a discovery motion by defendant does not amount to "good cause" for permitting late notice or amendment to the original notice ( CPL 710.30, subd 2); however, inasmuch as the sufficiency of such notice has not been previously addressed by the court, preclusion will not be employed in this instance, and the District Attorney is ordered to supply the necessary information to defendant.

 

1915 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Klebetz v. Ramapo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Rockland County


109 Misc. 2d 952; 441 N.Y.S.2d 216; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2498



July 10, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff homeowners filed a motion for summary judgment and to strike defendants' answer in an action seeking a declaratory judgment to void the Ramapo Town Zoning Local Law § 376-33.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff homeowners filed a suit against defendants, a town and the town board, alleging that the Ramapo Town Zoning Local Law § 376-33 was in violation of N.Y. Town Law § 262. However, defendants claimed that N.Y. Mun. Home Rule Law § 10(1)(ii)(d)(3) granted defendants the ability to amend or supersede the provisions of N.Y. Town Law. The court found that the provisions of the Ramapo Town Zoning Local Law § 376-33 were inconsistent with the provisions of N.Y. Town Law § 262. Further, § 376-33 violated N.Y. Town Law because it did not apply uniform regulations for two-family semiattached residences within the R-15 district or for uniform regulation of the same structures throughout the remainder of defendants' R-15 districts.


OUTCOME: The court granted plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment because there were no triable issues of fact as defendants did not have the authority to enact a town law inconsistent with the provisions of another town law. The court submitted judgment on the notice declaring the zoning local law void and illegal.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- District Uniformity Requirements

A town zoning law, which authorizes the erection of two-family, semiattached residences in one portion of the town's R-15 districts but makes no similar provision for the other R-15 districts in the town, is void, since the provisions of the zoning law are "inconsistent" with the provisions of section 262 of the Town Law, which requires that a zoning ordinance be uniform throughout a district, and a town lacks the power to enact local zoning laws that are inconsistent with the general laws of the State; the State has specifically circumscribed a town's zoning power by requiring uniformity both within and throughout identical zoning districts and absent an express statutory delegation, a town lacks the power to enact, amend or enforce zoning or other land use regulations and it must strictly adhere to the State-wide statutory zoning mandate; the power delegated to local governments to enact local laws which relate to their "property, affairs or government" (Municipal Home Rule Law, § 10, subd 1, par [ii], cl d, subcl [3]) does not constitute a delegation of authority to the local government to enact zoning [***2] regulations and therefore does not grant respondent town the power to amend or supersede the provisions of the Town Law which relate to zoning.

 

1916 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Brown v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


110 Misc. 2d 164; 441 N.Y.S.2d 781; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3056



July 10, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In view of the foregoing, the plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment is denied and the complaint is dismissed on the defendants' cross motion for dismissal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff residents filed a motion for summary judgment in an action by the residents to declare N.Y. Educ. Law § 2023 unconstitutional in that it violated equal protection, was a deprivation of due process under the law, and a denial of the residents' constitutional right to an education.


OVERVIEW: The court could not agree with the contention of the residents and dismissed the complaint. The court found that the statute complied with the constitutional mandate of N.Y. Const. art. XVI, § 1. The court determined that the state constitutional classifications based on size and nature of the municipalities and school districts were not of the suspect type and, the appropriate standard being the rational basis test, a rational basis existed for the differences in classifications between a city school district and a noncity school district. The court found that the mere existence of voter approval or disapproval did not necessarily guarantee that a student would be provided the right to participate in interscholastic sports, additional library programs, marching band activities, etc. The court concluded that such activities or programs were not required by statute to be provided and the decision to make such additional activities available was discretionary with each individual board of education.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion for summary judgment and dismissed the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Levy of Tax by School District in Absence of Voter Approval

 Section 2023 of the Education Law, which specifies that in the event the voters refuse to vote the sums estimated to be necessary for teachers' salaries or "ordinary contingent" expenses, then the board of education may levy a tax for same, is not an unconstitutional delegation of taxing powers to a school district in violation of section 1 of article XVI of the New York State Constitution; a tax may be levied for legal obligations of the district and those items necessary to maintain the educational program, preserve property and assure the health and safety of the students and staff. Moreover, the classifications based on size and nature of the municipalities and school districts set forth in section 10 of article VIII of the New York State Constitution are not inherently suspect so as to require utilization of the "strict scrutiny" standard of review; the appropriate standard is the rational basis test and a rational basis exists [***2] to justify the distinction between sections 2516 to 2519 of the Education Law, where city school districts are allowed to appropriate funds and levy taxes for "ordinary contingent" expenses without voter approval, while under section 2023, noncity school districts require voter approval. Nor does section 2023 amount to a deprivation of due process and a denial of plaintiffs' right to an education, since the mere existence of voter approval or disapproval does not necessarily guarantee that a student will be provided the right to participate in activities such as interscholastic sports, marching band and additional library programs, which are not statutorily required.

 

1917 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ayers


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Nassau County


109 Misc. 2d 870; 440 N.Y.S.2d 1019; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2487



July 9, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Based upon the foregoing, the defendant's motion is in all respects denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion for an order to dismiss the indictment for criminal usury, N.Y. Penal Law § 190.40 on the grounds that the prosecution violated his constitutional rights in that he was denied equal protection of the laws and deprived of liberty without due process of law under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law. § 210.20(1), para.(h).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was accused of making a $ 3,500 loan at an interest rate of approximately 208 percent per annum. He filed a motion to dismiss, alleging that prosecution under this statute violated the constitution. The trial court denied the motion in all respects. It held that he had failed to meet his burden of demonstrating the unconstitutionality of N.Y. Penal Law § 190.40 beyond a reasonable doubt. The court used the rational basis test. It could not say that treating this defendant differently from a large corporate lending institution was wholly irrelevant to achievement of the statute's objectives. As there were valid state objectives and a rational basis for distinguishing between the two classes of lenders, defendant's constitutional attack based on equal protection was rejected. The court held that no fundamental sense of justice had been denied the defendant by subjecting him to prosecution. In affirming, the court held that merely subjecting a defendant to a possible hard result based upon the legislation involved did not constitute any violation or deprivation of any of fundamental, statutory or constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss his indictment for criminal usury.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Usury

Prosecution of defendant under section 190.40 of the Penal Law for criminal usury in the second degree upon the allegation that he made a $ 3,500 loan to another individual at an annual interest rate of approximately 208%, while section 5-501 (subd 6, par b) of the General Obligations Law allows interest rates in excess of 25% on loans greater than $ 2.5 million, is not a violation of defendant's constitutional right to equal protection, nor is prosecution under said statute a violation of his substantive due process rights; there is rational basis for treating defendant differently from a large corporate lender to further the State's valid interests in protecting the poor from loansharks and in preserving and protecting the State's legitimate financial industry, and, further, notwithstanding the rise in interest rates and the changes in economic conditions since section 190.40 was enacted, there is still a just and fair connection between said criminal usury statute and the promotion of the public good, which precludes a finding of denial of substantive due process.

 

1918 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Faieta


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, First District, Nassau County


109 Misc. 2d 841; 440 N.Y.S.2d 1007; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2483



July 8, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to show cause seeking to have a temporary order of protection vacated or modified on grounds that provisions of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 530.12 were unconstitutional and that he was not afforded an opportunity to be heard, was not represented, and there was a lack of due process.


OVERVIEW: An accusatory instrument alleged that defendant committed assault in the third degree in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 120.00 (1). The trial court issued a temporary order of protection requiring defendant to stay away from the complainant's home, school, business, or place of employment. Defendant had multiple sclerosis. Defendant filed a motion to show cause seeking to have the temporary protection order vacated or modified. The court modified the order to the extent that defendant was to stay away from the complainant, but that defendant could remain in his own home because it was equipped to accommodate his multiple sclerosis. The court did not find the provisions of N.Y. Penal Law § 120.00 unconstitutional because the failure of the statute to provide for a hearing prior to the issuance of the protection order did not result in a lack of due process.


OUTCOME: The court denied that branch of defendant's motion seeking an order declaring provisions of the applicable statute unconstitutional because they were not constitutionally defective. However, the court granted defendant's motion to extent of modifying the temporary order of protection by retaining all terms and conditions except that defendant could have access to his own home and residence.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Family Offenses -- Temporary Order of Protection

CPL 530.12, which empowers the court in a criminal proceeding involving certain family offenses to issue a temporary order of protection without a prior hearing, is not constitutionally defective since the temporary order of protection is issued as a condition of bail or pretrial release and requiring that a hearing be held before any such issuance would be onerous, impractical and in most instances inappropriate; a defendant's right to due process is adequately protected since a defendant who is the object of a temporary order of protection may at any time request a hearing for the purpose of determining the merits of such an order notwithstanding that such a procedure is not specifically provided for in the statute; accordingly, in order to avoid an injustice under the circumstances, the temporary order of protection directing defendant, a victim of multiple sclerosis who is charged with assaulting his wife, to stay away from his home, which is equipped with special facilities to accommodate his physical incapacity, is modified to the extent of permitting defendant to re-enter his own residence without interference.
