Miller v. Miller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


109 Misc. 2d 982; 441 N.Y.S.2d 339; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2502



June 30, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In plaintiff wife's divorce action, defendant husband filed a motion to stay further action until the New York Court of Appeals decided whether it would review an appellate court ruling regarding the applicability of the New York Equitable Distribution Law to out-of-state property.


OVERVIEW: The wife brought a divorce action against the husband. After filing suit, the wife sought to compel the husband to file an affidavit of financial disclosure. The husband requested a stay until such time as the New York Court of Appeals determined if it would review an appellate court ruling that declared the New York Equitable Distribution Law to be applicable to out-of-state property. The court granted the wife's motion and declined the husband's request for a stay, holding that the mere fact that an application for discretionary review was filed did not in itself justify such an action.


OUTCOME: The husband's motion for a stay was denied and he was ordered to file a financial disclosure affidavit.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Actions -- Stay

1. While courts are authorized to grant a stay of proceedings where a point of law applicable to certain litigants is about to be decided by an appellate court, a stay should be granted only where the decision is imminent, taking into consideration when the appeal was taken and when the arguments are to be heard. Accordingly, where only a motion for leave to appeal has been made and there has been no decision thereon, a stay is denied.

Husband and Wife -- Equitable Distribution

2. Pursuant to the Equitable Distribution Law (Domestic Relations Law, § 236, part B, subd 5), a court must determine what constitutes the marital property of the parties and make an equitable distribution thereof and, while the court may not affect title to real property situated outside the State, it may order one of the parties to convey that property.

 

1920 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Folk


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, New York County


109 Misc. 2d 738; 440 N.Y.S.2d 984; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2461



June 22, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the defendant's motion to vacate his plea of guilty on grounds of the unconstitutionality of section 263.05 of the Penal Law is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to vacate his plea of guilty to attempted use of a child in a sexual performance in violation of N.Y. Penal Law §§110, 263.05 on grounds that section 263.05 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant moved to vacate his plea of guilty to attempted use of a child in a sexual performance in violation of N.Y. Penal Law §§110, 263.05 on grounds that section 263.05 was unconstitutional. The court denied defendant's motion to vacate, holding that the law was constitutional as applied to defendant. It rejected the contention that defendant's conduct could be equated with speech within the meaning of U.S. Const. amend. I. Defendant was not prosecuted for the probable content of the photographs that he might have subsequently produced, but for the deleterious effects of his conduct upon the well being of minors. Defendant's conduct in soliciting juveniles to have sex with other juveniles for pay was clearly within state's police power to punish. To the extent that defendant's proposed production of a film evoked First Amendment concerns, this potential communicative interest was clearly outweighed by state's interest in protecting juveniles. The court also held that defendant was not a proper party to raise the constitutionality of the statute as applied to third persons.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to vacate, holding that defendant's conduct could not be equated with speech within the meaning of U.S. Const. amend. I. Defendant's conduct in soliciting juveniles to have sex with other juveniles for pay was clearly within the police power of the state to punish.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Use of Child in Sexual Performance

 Section 263.05 of the Penal Law, which prohibits use of a child in a sexual performance, is not unconstitutional as applied to defendant; defendant's conduct in soliciting juveniles to have sex with other juveniles for pay, asking them to undress and showing them sexually explicit photographs, is clearly of a sort that is within the police power of the State to punish, and defendant's conduct was not a form of protected speech or expression. Defendant is not a proper party to raise the constitutionality of the statute as applied to third persons, i.e., the juvenile performers, since he is not a member of the class of child performers, nor does the record warrant the conclusion that he is entitled to any representative standing, inasmuch as there is no agency relationship between defendant and the child performers, nor does defendant appear to have any personal, parental or substantial economic relation to any child performer.

 

1921 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


L. v. S.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Queens County


109 Misc. 2d 628; 440 N.Y.S.2d 825; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2443



June 22, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed a motion for an order directing the mother, child, and respondent putative father to submit to a Human Leucocyte Antigen blood tissue test (HLA) by a qualified physician, pursuant to N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 532, and for an order directing that the payment be borne by the Department of Social Services or the putative father. The putative father opposed the motion.


OVERVIEW: Upon review, the court determined that in amending § 532, the legislature made a policy decision on the reliability and efficacy of the HLA test and that before making the decision the legislature considered a myriad of evidence and balanced the conflicting interest. The court found that there was no allegation that the legislature acted in an impermissible or unconstitutional manner in enacting the HLA legislation and therefore deferred to the legislative determination on the reliability of the HLA test and did not engage in further scrutiny of the procedure's scientific reliability. The court held that the HLA test did not violate the Fourth Amendment's right to privacy or the Fifth Amendment's privilege against self-incrimination. The court also determined that § 532 mandated that the putative father pay for the test because he was not indigent. The court recognized that making the putative father bear the cost of the HLA test led to inequitable results, but the court concluded that § 532 mandated that he pay for the test because he was not indigent. However, the court divided the costs of the test equally between the parties because petitioner consented to pay half of the cost.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner's motion, and ordered that the parties and the child submit to an HLA test by a qualified physician. The court also ordered that the cost of the HLA test be paid one half by the putative father and one half by the Department of Social Services.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Children Born out of Wedlock -- Filiation Proceeding -- HLA Blood Test

1. A motion by the Commissioner of Social Services, as the assignee of petitioner mother, for an order directing the mother, her child, who was born out of wedlock, and respondent putative father to submit to a Human Leucocyte Antigen (HLA) blood tissue test pursuant to section 532 of the Family Court Act is granted; the statute leaves no discretion in the court to deny motions by either party for such tests. Further, respondent's challenge to said HLA test on the grounds that it is not sufficiently reliable and that submission to the test violates his right to privacy under the Fourth Amendment and his privilege against self incrimination under the Fifth Amendment is dismissed; the court will defer to the legislative determination on the reliability of the HLA blood test and must also reject respondent's constitutional objections, since the minimal privacy intrusion involved is justified by the significant State interest in the welfare of children born out of wedlock and the blood test does not implicate the Fifth Amendment privilege, which is limited to criminal cases.

Children Born out of Wedlock  [***2] -- Filiation Proceeding -- HLA Blood Test

2. Section 532 of the Family Court Act, when amended to allow the Human Leucocyte Antigen (HLA) blood tissue test into evidence to aid in the determination of whether the alleged father is or is not the father of a child, was otherwise unchanged and, therefore, the existing provision that the cost of a blood grouping test be borne by respondent putative father unless he is indigent is also applicable to the HLA amendment; while it was fair to have a nonindigent putative father bear the cost of the original standard blood grouping test permitted by the statute, since it was only admissible to exclude the respondent as the father, the apparent oversight in the amendment of the statute may lead to the inequitable result that respondent will have to pay for the newer HLA blood test, which must be ordered on the mother's motion for said test and is admissible as evidence that respondent is the father. However, upon the consent of the movant Commissioner of Social Services, petitioner mother's assignee, the cost of said test will be borne equally by the nonindigent respondent and the Department of Social Services.

 

1922 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hamza


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Justice Court of New York, Town of Gates, Monroe County


109 Misc. 2d 1055; 441 N.Y.S.2d 579; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3025



June 15, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendant's motion to dismiss is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant brought a motion to dismiss the state's charge that he violated N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1193-a by refusing to take a breath screening test, on the grounds that § 1193-a violated his Fifth Amendment right against self-incrimination.


OVERVIEW: A police officer stopped defendant as he was driving and charged him with speeding, unsafe lane changes, driving without a license, and refusal to take a breath screening test in violation of § 1193-a. Defendant brought a motion to dismiss the charge of refusing to take a breath test, arguing that § 1193-a violated his right against self-incrimination. The court granted the motion, holding that the sole purpose of § 1193-a was to enable police officers to gather evidence of drinking and driving from a defendant where the police officer did not have grounds to request a chemical test under N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1194. Because N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1193-a carried with it a penalty of fine and/or imprisonment for failure to take the breath test, it violated the Fifth Amendment.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to dismiss the state's charges for failing to take a breath screening test.


HEADNOTES

Motor Vehicles -- Chemical Tests -- Validity of Statute

 Section 1193-a of the Vehicle and Traffic Law violates rights conferred by the Fifth Amendment of the United States Constitution since it carries with it a penalty of fine and/or imprisonment for failure to take the breath test and the sole purpose of the test is to enable a police officer to gather evidence of drinking and driving from the defendant in situations where the circumstances do not otherwise show such proscribed conduct; if there were reasonable grounds to believe that the defendant had been driving under the influence of alcohol in violation of section 1192 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, the police officer could merely proceed under section 1194 with the chemical test demand and section 1193-a would be unnecessary. Further, section 1193-a is unconstitutional as applied where the officer who requested the breath test under section 1193-a and arrested the defendant for driving while intoxicated under section 1192 thereafter requested the defendant to take the chemical test under section 1194, since, if the officer was correct in making the arrest and demanding [***2] the chemical test, then it was completely unnecessary for him to utilize section 1193-a.

 

1923 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Baraka


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


109 Misc. 2d 271; 439 N.Y.S.2d 827; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2388



June 10, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to vacate, reduce, or modify his sentence of 90 days' imprisonment for his conviction for resisting arrest in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 205.30.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of resisting arrest and sentenced to 90 days' imprisonment. Defendant filed a motion to vacate, reduce, or modify his sentence, and the court denied the motion. The court held that it had no power to alter the sentence under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 430.10 because it was a lawful sentence and defendant had already commenced serving the sentence. Also, the fact that defendant was remanded to custody immediately after the imposition of the sentence did not lift the § 430.10 bar on the court's modification of the sentence. The sentence was within the proper sentencing range for defendant's offense; therefore the sentence was not unlawful. Finally, there was no evidence in the record of the trial court uttering any unconstitutional remarks during the imposition of the sentence.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to vacate, reduce, or modify his sentence for his conviction of resisting arrest.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Sentence -- Sentence of Imprisonment not to be Changed After Commencement

Since a sentence of imprisonment "may not be changed, suspended or interrupted once the term or period of the sentence has commenced" ( CPL 430.10) unless the sentence was "unauthorized, illegally imposed or otherwise invalid as a matter of law" ( CPL 440.20) and since defendant had commenced service of his 90-day sentence for resisting arrest prior to obtaining an order granting stay of execution and bail pending appeal, defendant's conviction having been affirmed, and he has not shown that the sentence imposed was unauthorized, illegal or otherwise invalid, the sentencing court is without power to disturb the sentence originally imposed; the fact that defendant was remanded immediately after sentence does not bar application of CPL 430.10 since the appropriate mechanism would have been to seek an immediate stay of execution of sentence from a Justice of the Supreme Court.

 

1924 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Morris


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Westchester County


109 Misc. 2d 475; 439 N.Y.S.2d 1013; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2415; 441 N.E.2d 616



June 10, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On Reargument July 20, 1981.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss an indictment charging defendant with the crimes of robbery in the third degree and grand larceny in the second degree based upon an alleged lack of speedy trial.


OVERVIEW: The period of time elapsed from the issuance of a warrant of arrest and the announcement by the People of their readiness for trial totaled 269 days. This was three months past the six-month standard required by N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§ 30.30, 210.20(1)(g). However, from the time the arrest warrant was issued in August 1980 and this final arrest in February 1981, defendant's whereabouts were unknown as he was avoiding apprehension. Also, the police were unable to determine his location by due diligence. The court made the factual finding that the period of delay related to the defendant's voluntary absence from the local jurisdiction was excluded under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 30.30(4)(c) as the delay was caused by the absence or unavailability of defendant. Accordingly, the People was regarded as being ready for trial within the six-month provisions of § 30.30(1)(a). On a further motion to reargue its decision, the court ordered a rehearing to determine whether the police exercised due diligence in attempting to locate defendant. If so, the prior holding that such period was chargeable to defendant would remain in effect.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment, but subsequently granted his motion for a rehearing on his unavailability.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Right to Speedy Trial -- Unavailability of Defendant

Although more than six months elapsed from the time of the filing of a local court felony indictment against defendant, during which time the People did not obtain an indictment against him or express their readiness for trial, dismissal of the indictment on the ground that defendant was denied his right to a speedy trial ( CPL 30.20, 210.20, subd 1, par [g]) is not required where the defendant could not be found during this period despite due diligence on the part of the police to locate him and he was not located, arrested or arraigned on the felony complaint until after the six-month period had run; it would be absurd to require the People to proceed with the indictment of an absentee defendant in such a case, necessitating a tremendous administrative effort, wasted paperwork, court time and expense in order to demonstrate that the delay in prosecution is not attributable to the People but to the defendant's absence.

 

1925 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Rose


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


109 Misc. 2d 960; 441 N.Y.S.2d 161; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2499



June 4, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, an acquittee of the crime of murder by reason of mental disease or defect, sought a rehearing after a different New York supreme court justice denied his petition for a writ of habeas corpus and a petition by the Commissioner of Mental Hygiene (commissioner) for release on conditions pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. § 330.20(12).


OVERVIEW: The petitioner had been hospitalized in various psychiatric institutions since the commission of the homicides in 1973. In July 1976, he was acquitted of the charges by reason of mental disease or defect, and pursuant to law, had been retained in the custody of the commissioner. The court granted the petition for a rehearing, and appointed two psychiatrists to examine the petitioner. The court found that the very inclusion in N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 330.20(16) of N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law §§ 9.35, 15.35 impelled the construction that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 330.20(16) accorded the petitioner on the denial of an application for a release order (in effect, the equivalent of a retention order) a right of rehearing and a right of a jury trial. The court concluded that a criminal acquittee had the same and no lesser rights than that of a detainee under a civil commitment. The court further determined that the standard of proof, clear and convincing, was the same for each.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petitioner's request for a rehearing of his release from a psychiatric facility and appointed two psychiatrists to examine the petitioner. The court ordered that a jury be summoned for the hearing and directed that the people's standard of proof was by clear and convincing evidence.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Insanity -- Rehearing on Retention Order

CPLR 330.20 (subd 16), which grants a person in the custody of the Commissioner of Mental Hygiene following an acquittal of the crime of murder by reason of mental disease or defect the right to a rehearing and review of a commitment order, retention order or recommitment order in accordance with other statutory provisions which grant an involuntary detainee the right to a rehearing and review before a jury and a different Judge (Mental Hygiene Law, §§ 9.35, 15.35), is construed so as to accord an acquittee on the denial of an application by the Commissioner of Mental Hygiene for a release order ( CPL 330.20, subd 12), the right to such a rehearing before a jury; the scope of the rehearing is not limited to evidence presented at the first hearing nor is the prior decision denying the commissioner's application binding on the rehearing court as the "law of the case"; the proper standard of proof upon the rehearing is "clear and convincing" evidence of the need for continued confinement, such standard of proof being applicable to both those persons acquitted of crimes and those detained under a civil commitment.

 

1926 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


McMinn v. Oyster Bay


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Nassau County


111 Misc. 2d 1046; 445 N.Y.S.2d 859; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3388



May 29, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, owners and tenants, brought an action to declare unconstitutional Oyster Bay, N.Y., Building Zoning Ordinance art. I, § 1. The ordinance prohibited occupancy of a one-family dwelling by other than a family, which was defined as persons related by blood, marriage, or adoption, living and cooking together as a single nonprofit housing unit; or any two unrelated persons, both over 62 years of age, living and cooking as a single unit.


OVERVIEW: Defendant town charged the owners with violating the ordinance due to their rental of a one-family dwelling to the tenants, who were four unrelated men. The owners and tenants claimed that the ordinance violated the equal protection and due process clauses of the state constitution, and that it was superseded by state law. The court found that the ordinance lacked a rational purpose in permitting occupancy in one instance based upon the relationship of the occupants and in another based upon attainment of a certain age. The court held that the effect of the ordinance was to discriminate in the occupancy of one-family housing solely on the basis of age. The court directed that the ordinance was not enforceable against two unrelated persons who resided in a one-family dwelling as a single housekeeping unit within the town's unincorporated areas. The court also found that the ordinance conflicted with N.Y. Exec. Law § 296(5)(a)(1), which prohibited discrimination in housing on the basis of marital status. Thus, the ordinance was ruled invalid to the extent that it prohibited occupancy of a one-family dwelling by persons not married to each other.


OUTCOME: The court prohibited the town from enforcing the ordinance against two unrelated persons who occupied a one-family dwelling within the unincorporated areas of the town. The court also prohibited enforcement to the extent that the ordinance prohibited occupancy by unmarried persons.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Restrictions on Occupancy of Single-Family Dwelling

A zoning ordinance of the Town of Oyster Bay which restricts occupancy of a single- or one-family dwelling to persons "related by blood, marriage or legal adoption, living * * * together as a single nonprofit housing unit" or to two unrelated persons living as a single housekeeping unit if they are both 62 years of age or older, is not unconstitutional on the ground of exclusionary zoning in regards to young unmarried adults; however, the ordinance, to the extent that it permits two unrelated persons to live in a single-family dwelling only if they are 62 years of age or older without making any allowance for two unrelated younger persons who might live in a relationship that possesses characteristics of family life, bears no rational relationship to a proper governmental objective and is thus an unconstitutional exercise of the town's police power; in addition, the ordinance is invalid to the extent that it seeks to prohibit occupancy of a residence in a one-family dwelling solely on the ground that the proposed tenants are not married to each other in violation of the State policy prohibiting [***2] an owner from refusing to "rent * * * a housing accommodation because of * * * marital status" ( Executive Law, § 296, subd 5); accordingly, the zoning ordinance may not be enforced against those unrelated persons, not exceeding two in number, who may reside in a one-family house as a single housekeeping unit.

 

1927 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Devitt v. Heimbach


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Orange County


109 Misc. 2d 463; 440 N.Y.S.2d 465; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2413



May 28, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding, petitioner, a county resident and district legislator, sought to annul a resolution by respondents, a county legislature and it executive, authorizing the executive to enter into a contract of private sale of certain county-owned properties. Respondents asserted a cross-motion seeking, inter alia, to dismiss the petition for failure to state a cause of action and for summary judgment in respondents' favor.


OVERVIEW: Because of lack of evidence, the court rejected the petitioner allegation that a closed executive session prior to adopting, by majority vote in open session, a resolution authorizing the sale of county-owned lands violated the Open Meetings Law, N.Y. Pub. Off. Law art. 7. The permissible limited judicial review of legislative acts did not permit the court to substitute its judgment for that of the legislature as to the price or the adequacy of the consideration for the bona fide sale of the properties. The lack of a legislative resolution stating that the property was no longer needed for public purposes was not void for violation of N.Y. County Law § 215 as the county operated under an optional or alternative form of county government. The county legislature did not meet the legal requirement of determining that its action had no environmental significance on the area merely by passing a local law authorizing the sale of the county-owned property. Accordingly, all further proceedings were stayed under the contract of sale pending compliance with the applicable provisions of the State Environmental Quality Review Act (SEQRA).


OUTCOME: The court stayed the proposed sale pending compliance with the applicable provisions of SEQRA and a final determination by the appropriate county authorities as to the environmental significance of the proposed sale.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Public Officers -- Open Meetings Law -- Closed Executive Session of County Legislature

1. The fact that the Orange County Legislature improperly conducted a closed 30-minute executive session prior to adopting by majority vote in open session a resolution authorizing the sale of county-owned property pursuant to a local law is an insufficient ground to annul the action taken by the legislature since, although it has not been demonstrated that publicity would have substantially affected the value of the property so as to justify the closed executive session under the Open Meetings Law (Public Officers Law, § 100, subd 1, par h), petitioner legislator has failed to prove that good cause exists to invoke the granting of judicial relief (Public Officers Law, § 102) nor has sufficient proof been presented that improper informal private meetings occurred to such an extent as to place the validity of the legislative process in question.

Courts -- Limited Review of Legislative Acts -- Sale of County-Owned Property

2. The court cannot review the question of whether the Orange County Legislature properly placed a resolution authorizing the sale of certain county-owned property [***2] on the legislative agenda after the legislative session had commenced since the placing of items on the agenda is within the exclusive prerogative of the legislature and the court cannot interject itself into the internal procedures of the legislature or review the acts of another co-ordinate and equal department of government; the permissible limited judicial review of legislative acts does not permit the court to substitute its judgment for that of the legislature as to the price or the adequacy of the consideration for the bona fide sale of the subject parcel of property.

Counties -- Private Sale of County Property

3. A local law adopted by the Orange County Legislature which authorizes the county to conduct private negotiations for the sale of certain county-owned property without public bid and without the necessity of a legislative resolution stating that the property is no longer needed for public purposes is not void for violating section 215 of the County Law since Orange County is operating under an optional or alternative form of county government and, thus, its local law supersedes the provisions of the County Law which are in conflict with it (County Law, § [***3] 2, subd [b]).

Environmental Conservation -- Environmental Impact Statement -- Sale of County Property

4. Orange County did not comply with the requirements to the State Environmental Quality Review Act (SEQRA) and the regulations promulgated thereunder ( ECL 8-0113, subd 3, par [a]; 6 NYCRR 617.12 [b]) when it determined that the proposed sale of more than 100 contiguous acres of county-owned land did not require an environmental impact statement without identifying the relevant areas of environmental concern or making a reasoned elaboration of the basis for its determination that the proposed sale would have no "significant" environmental effect on the area; accordingly, the proposed sale is stayed pending compliance with the applicable provisions of SEQRA and a final determination by the appropriate county authorities as to the environmental significance of the proposed sale.

 

1928 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Kuczka v. Clark


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


110 Misc. 2d 273; 441 N.Y.S.2d 854; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3076



May 21, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner candidate sought a judgment to require respondent commissioner to place petitioner's name on the commissioner's list of eligible candidates for the position of police officer. The commissioner moved for summary judgment on the ground that the cause of action was without merit. The state attorney general intervened and also moved for summary judgment or a declaration that N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 58 was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: A candidate for appointment as a county police officer sought a judgment requiring the commissioner of personnel to place the candidate's name on the certified list of eligible candidates for the position. The candidate challenged provisions of N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 58 as unconstitutional and invalid. The commissioner moved for summary judgment. The state attorney general intervened and sought judgment dismissing the petition with regard to the constitutional challenge. The court found that the commissioner refused to put the candidate's name on the eligibility list for two reasons: age and place of residence. The court found that N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 58(1) precluded appointment of police officer candidates more than 29 years of age. The candidate reached his 29th birthday but was not yet 30. The court held that the candidate should be considered 29 until his next birthday, and that he fell within the age limitations of the statute. The court also held that the durational residency requirement imposed on civil service applicants by a county rule penalized nonresidents without adequate justification and violated the state and federal constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motions for summary judgment of the commissioner and the state attorney general. The court ordered the commissioner to place the candidate's name on the list of certified eligible candidates for the position of police officer. The court held that the four-month county durational residency requirement for civil service candidates was unconstitutional under the state and federal Constitutions.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Civil Service -- Appointment and Promotion -- Appointment as Police Officer -- Age Ceiling of 29

1. Section 58 of the Civil Service Law, which provides that "no person shall be eligible for provisional or permanent appointment * * * as a police officer * * * unless * * * he is not less than twenty nor more than twenty-nine years of age", operates to disqualify from appointment as police officers only those persons who have reached their 30th birthday since it is proper to treat the words "more than twenty-nine years of age" as being the same as "over" 29 years of age and courts have held that persons are not "over" a certain age until their next birthday is reached; in any event, since the town board would have had the opportunity to appoint petitioner to the position of police officer before this 29th birthday had the Erie County Civil Service Commission established the eligible list earlier, the prompt establishment of a list of eligible candidates being critical for any position which has an age ceiling, any town board appointment of petitioner to the position of police officer is deemed to have taken place before petitioner's 29th birthday.

Civil Service -- Residence  [***2] Requirement

2. The four-month durational residence requirement for recruits to municipal positions contained in rules of the Erie County Civil Service Commission heavily penalizes nonresidents without justification, the payment of taxes being an impermissible basis for a durational residency requirement, and is, therefore, unconstitutional as violative of the equal protection and privileges and immunities clauses of the State and Federal Constitutions and may not be invoked to refuse to place petitioner's name on the certified list of candidates for the position of police officer.

 

1929 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


10 Lafayette Square Holdings v. Buffalo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


108 Misc. 2d 960; 439 N.Y.S.2d 289; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2322



May 19, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer filed suit against defendant city to recover taxes allegedly exceeding the city's constitutional taxing powers by excluding annual pension and retirement liabilities in reliance upon unconstitutional statutes. The taxpayer filed a motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer contended that its taxes were paid involuntarily and under protest by virtue of the timely filing, prior to payment, of proceedings for review of the assessments of the city pursuant to N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law art. 7. Denying the motion for summary judgment, the court held that the taxpayer's allegations that the assessment was not made in accordance with the law and statutes was not the specificity required under N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 706 as the taxpayer failed to allege that the reason the tax was illegal was the city's exclusion of annual pension and retirement liabilities in reliance on unconstitutional statutes. The court found that the taxpayer failed to allege that its taxes were paid under protest. The court held that such failures gave the city no reason to assign any portion of the taxpayer's taxes into a reserve to meet the contingency of refunds.


OUTCOME: The court denied the taxpayer's motion for summary judgment in its claim to recover taxes.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Taxation -- Assessment -- Payment under Protest

Illegal taxes involuntarily paid are recoverable, but illegal taxes voluntarily paid are not. Accordingly, plaintiff taxpayer's motion for summary judgment in an action to recover the amount of taxes it paid in certain years on the ground that the said taxation exceeded defendant city's constitutional taxing powers is denied; plaintiff's timely filing, prior to payment of the taxes, of proceedings for review of the assessments pursuant to article 7 of the Real Property Tax Law, wherein it did not specify that its taxes were paid in protest of the constitutionality of the statutory exclusions upon which the taxes were computed, on which claim of illegality it now relies, does not constitute involuntary payment or payment under protest.

 

1930 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Liberati v. Axelrod


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


108 Misc. 2d 820; 439 N.Y.S.2d 82; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2299



May 14, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner funeral director opposed a motion filed by respondent Commissioner of Health of the State of New York to dismiss the N.Y C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding commenced by the funeral director to review and annul the Commissioner's determination, made pursuant to N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 3450, on the ground that the determination was arbitrary and capricious and that there was no opportunity for a hearing.


OVERVIEW: The funeral director was convicted a felony for robbery and armed feature. The Commissioner revoked the license pursuant to § 3450(4)(a). The funeral director challenged the revocation. The Commissioner responded that the statute mandated a duty which in no way involved a discretionary action or the exercise of judgment on the part of the Commissioner. The statutory mandate did not require a hearing. The court held that the directive in § 3450(4)(a) was clear and unqualified in that a license should be revoked upon the holder's conviction of a felony. The conviction constituted an abridgement of the license automatically terminating its duration. The statute struck a balance in favor of the public's right to rest assured that its funeral directors were individuals of moral integrity in whom they could place their confidence and trust. Because the statute did not provide for a review of revocations, the court had no jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The court held that the action taken by the Commissioner was constitutionally mandated and it granted the Commissioner's motion to dismiss the funeral director's art. 78 proceeding.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Licenses -- Funeral Directors

Section 3450 (subd 4, par [a]) of the Public Health Law, which permits the license of a funeral director to be automatically revoked upon conviction of a felony, is constitutional since in weighing the interest of a funeral director convicted of a felony, whether justly or unjustly, against that of the public, the balance must be struck in favor of the public's right to rest assured that its funeral directors are individuals of moral integrity in whom they may place their confidence and trust; furthermore, inasmuch as there is no provision for review of revocations pursuant to the statute, a court has no jurisdiction to hear a petition brought by a funeral director, convicted of a felony, challenging the revocation of his license without a hearing.

 

1931 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Romano


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Kings County


109 Misc. 2d 99; 438 N.Y.S.2d 967; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2360



May 7, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate informally submitted an application, pursuant to N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 114, seeking to gain access to adoption records. The inmate also sought leave to proceed in forma pauperis, to have an attorney appointed, and to have notification of his natural mother. Respondent attorney general appeared in opposition to the inmate's claim that § 114 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The inmate sought to unseal records pertaining to his adoption. The court denied the inmate's application to proceed as a poor person, finding that the inmate failed to establish sufficient facts so that the merits of his contentions could have been ascertained as required by N.Y. C.P.L.R. 1101(a). The court held that the inmate was required to prove and not merely allege a concrete and compelling need to learn the facts of his ancestry. The court also rejected the inmate's request that his natural mother receive notice of the proceedings. The inmate cited a case wherein the natural parent was notified and advised of her legal rights to determine whether or not she wished to oppose a petition. The court noted a different case, which implicated that a showing of good cause had to precede the giving of notice to the natural parent. Thus, the court declined to follow the course of action prescribed by the case cited by the inmate until the inmate had initially made a showing of entitlement to inspect the sealed records. The court established a deadline before which the inmate was to notify the court that a hearing on the merits of his petition was desired.


OUTCOME: The court denied the inmate's request to proceed in forma pauperis. The court also denied the inmate's request that his natural mother receive notification of the proceedings. The inmate was ordered to notify the court before a set date if he desired a hearing on the merits of his petition.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Adoption -- Inspection of Sealed Adoption Records -- Appointment of Attorney for Poor Person

Petitioner, a prisoner at a State correctional facility whose adoptive parents have died, is not entitled to proceed as a poor person and to have an attorney appointed to him ( CPLR 1101, 1102) on his application to unseal his adoption records for "good cause" (Domestic Relations Law, § 114) since uncompensated counsel should only be assigned to a poor person in civil cases if the court is satisfied that there is merit in the applicant's contentions and petitioner's unsupported allegations that his psychological well-being and need to "relate to himself as a whole person" require that he learn the identities of his natural parents, are insufficient to establish merit to his contentions as a predicate to the conferring of poor person status upon him; petitioner is not entitled to have his natural mother notified of the proceeding until he initially makes a showing of entitlement to inspect the sealed adoption records.

 

1932 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


AUTOMOBILE CLUB OF NEW YORK, INC. v. CITY OF NEW YORK


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, New York County


1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3518



May 4, 1981  
May 28, 1981, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, automobile club and garage owners, filed a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding, seeking a declaration that a municipal regulation promulgated by respondents, city, mayor, and Commissioner of the Department of Transportation, was void. Petitioners also sought an injunction prohibiting the implementation of the regulation.


OVERVIEW: In an effort to improve traffic into the city, respondents promulgated a municipal regulation banning the use of four bridges into the city by single occupancy private passenger cars on weekday mornings. Petitioners filed an action seeking a declaration that said regulation was void and filed a motion for an injunction seeking to prohibit its implementation. The court granted the petitioners' motions. While the city had authority to adopt local laws regulating the use of streets pursuant to N.Y. Const. art IX, § 2(c)(ii)(6), it could not do so in conflict with general laws. The New York Legislature adopted a general law with regard to traffic regulation, N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1604. Section 1604 stated that a city could not limit the free use of streets absent a grant of specific authority to do so. The Vehicle & Traffic Law did not give specific authority to regulate the number of passengers that could be in a vehicle while using a street. As the municipal regulation in question was in conflict with § 1604, it was void. The court also issued an injunction prohibiting its implementation.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioners motions, declaring that the municipal regulation was void and issuing an injunction prohibiting the implementation of the regulation.

 

1933 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


L. v. B.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


108 Misc. 2d 709; 438 N.Y.S.2d 726; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2277



May 1, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner child filed a motion for an order directing respondent alleged father to undergo a human leucocyte antigen (HLA) test, which in the uniform view of experts was capable of proving the alleged father's paternity to a high degree of probability.


OVERVIEW: Near the end of trial in the contested paternity case, legislation became effective authorizing the admission into evidence in family court paternity proceedings of the HLA test. The court determined that the HLA test results were in the nature of rebuttal evidence that would have been particularly appropriate because of its independence from the conflicting testimony. The court determined that the HLA test was constitutional. The court explained that there was no evidence that was comparable to an HLA test in effectiveness to prove paternity. The court noted that the HLA test involved only the withdrawal of a small amount of blood from the alleged fathers. The court concluded that a justification for the imposition of the HLA test was the state's deep, persuasive, and abiding interest in the welfare of its children. The court explained that the slight intrusion on individual privacy resulting from the HLA test was justified by the public interest in reliable evidence of paternity, both for the sake of child welfare and of conservation of public assistance funds, and it was also justified by the interest in equality of discovery devices for both parties in paternity litigation.


OUTCOME: The court determined that the HLA test was constitutional. The court concluded that the slight intrusion on individual privacy resulting from the HLA test was justified by the public interest in reliable evidence of paternity and the interest in equality of discovery devices for both parties in paternity litigation.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Children Born out of Wedlock -- Filiation Proceeding -- Blood Grouping Tests

1. Petitioner mother is entitled to an order, pursuant to an amendment (L 1981, ch 9) to section 532 of the Family Court Act, directing respondent to undergo a human leucocyte antigen (HLA) blood tissue test, which is capable of proving his paternity to a high degree of probability, notwithstanding the fact that the HLA amendment became effective near the end of the paternity proceeding, inasmuch as the procedure in an action is governed by the law regulating it at the time any question of procedure arises and the Legislature may change the practice of the court, and the change will affect pending actions in the absence of words of exclusion; the Judge who denied petitioner's previous motion for an HLA test need not, pursuant to CPLR 2221, hear the present motion, since that denial was based solely on the absence of a statute of the very type now enacted such that the policies of judicial inefficiency and deference underlying CPLR 2221 are inapplicable.

Children Born out of Wedlock -- Filiation Proceeding -- Blood Grouping Tests

2. An amendment (L 1981, ch 9) to section 532 of the Family [***2] Court Act, which authorizes the court in a paternity proceeding, on motion of any party, to order the mother, her child and the alleged father to submit to a human leucocyte antigen (HLA) blood tissue test and to receive the results of the test in evidence to aid in the determination of whether the alleged father is or is not the father, is constitutional inasmuch as the slight intrusion on individual privacy resulting from the HLA test is justified by the public interest in reliable evidence of paternity, both for the sake of child welfare and of conservation of public assistance funds; furthermore, the amendment is justified by the interest in equality of discovery devices for both parties in paternity litigation.

 

1934 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Granatelli


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Suffolk County


108 Misc. 2d 1009; 438 N.Y.S.2d 707; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2327



April 30, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought to have the assignment of his case to the court by the supervising judge of the Criminal Branch of the Superior Courts in Suffolk County (New York) declared void. Defendant contended that the judge's appointment, as supervising judge did not meet certain filing requirements under N.Y. Const. art. IV § 8 and N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. 22 § 20.8.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that because the appointment of the supervising judge of the Criminal Branch of the Superior Courts in Suffolk County was not properly filed with the office of the Secretary of State of New York, that the judge's assignment of his case to the court should be declared null and void. The court disagreed and held that N.Y. Const. art. IV, § 8 did not set forth any "textual commitment" which required the judiciary to file rules or regulations made in the course of its operation and administration of New York State's Unified Court System. The court noted that the chief administrative had determined that the designation of an individual as an administrative judge was neither an "initial assignment of judicial personnel" nor a "substantive court procedure" which needed to be filed under N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 22, § 20.8, which was an internal "housekeeping" rule. The administrator's powers regarding assignments were complete and needed no approval. The court held that there was no requirement to publish what did not need to be filed.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's application to have the supervising judge's assignment of his case to the court declared null and void.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Courts -- Unified Court System

1. There is no constitutional, statutory or regulatory impediment to the exercise of the powers of Supervising Judge of the Criminal Branch of the Superior Courts in Suffolk County by the individual so designated by the Chief Administrator of the Unified Court System in consultation with the Presiding Justice of the Appellate Division, Second Department, and with the approval of the Chief Judge of the Court of Appeals, which designation was not filed with the Department of State or the Administrative Board and was not published in the Official Compilation of Codes, Rules and Regulations of the State of New York.

Courts -- Unified Court System -- Criminal Parts

2. Assignment of all cases which the District Attorney designates "rackets" cases directly to Part II of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County, Criminal Term, by the Supervising Judge of the Criminal Branch of the Superior Courts in Suffolk County, without first having the matter calendared and called at Part I (22 NYCRR 1280.2 [a]), does not violate defendant's right to equal protection (US Const, 14th Amdt); defendant's assertion that the District Attorney, by arbitrarily designating [***2] a case as a "rackets" case, in effect, chooses the Judge to whom the case will be assigned, is not supported by any factual assertions and there is no evidence that the designation of cases is manipulated to affect assignment to a particular part of the court.

 

1935 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Malerba v. Warren


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


108 Misc. 2d 785; 438 N.Y.S.2d 936; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2291



April 28, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners and town filed an action against defendant adjacent property owners (adjacent owners) for trespass, fraud, breach of covenant, violation of zoning ordinances, and an injunction. The action was filed in response to the adjacent owners' placement of a cottage on their property that obstructed the property owners' view of the ocean.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the property owners were entitled to money damages from the adjacent owners for trespass. The court determined that the adjacent owners unlawfully entered upon the property owners' land and removed ground cover, dunes, and shrubbery. The court allowed punitive damages for the trespass. The court also held that the adjacent owners were guilty of fraud but the court determined that damages for fraud were impermissible. The court further held that the adjacent owners were guilty of maintaining a private nuisance. The court awarded the property owners damages for the private nuisance for the loss of income sustained. The court held that the property owners were entitled to an injunction that directed the adjacent owners to remove the cottage. Finally, as to the property owners the court held that they were entitled to enforcement of the deed of covenants and restrictions. The court further determined that the town was entitled to the injunctive relief that it sought and that the adjacent owners could not remove a utility pole without bearing the costs of removal.


OUTCOME: The court granted damages in favor of the property owners and issued an injunction against the adjacent owners after finding the adjacent owners guilty of trespass, fraud, breach of covenant, and zoning ordinance violations.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Trespass -- Damages

1. Plaintiffs are entitled to money damages from defendants for trespass where defendants entered 10 to 15 feet onto plaintiffs' property and removed a portion of a sand dune and assorted shrubbery, and through their excavations interfered with the lateral support of plaintiffs' premises; furthermore, plaintiffs are entitled to punitive damages where they proved that the defendants acted with reckless disregard of plaintiffs' rights.

Fraud -- Damages

2. Plaintiffs, the purchaser of real property from defendants, are not entitled to damages based on the fraudulent conduct of defendants, who placed a cottage on their property in such a manner as to encroach on plaintiffs' adjacent property and obstruct plaintiffs' view of the ocean, where equitable relief requiring defendants to remove the cottage is being granted.

Nuisance -- Private Nuisance

3. Defendants are guilty of maintaining a private nuisance and plaintiffs are entitled to recover for defendants' violation of town zoning and building regulations where defendants, despite repeated requests by the plaintiffs and the town that they stop construction of a cottage on their property [***2] which was encroaching on plaintiffs' property and obstructing plaintiffs' view of the ocean, continued construction of the cottage and thereby acted in a manner that was both illegal and injurious to plaintiffs.

Injunctions -- Injunction to Remove Obstruction to Ocean View

4. In an action for an injunction directing defendants to remove a cottage they placed on their property in such a manner as to encroach on plaintiffs' adjacent property and obstruct plaintiffs' view of the ocean, plaintiffs are entitled to the requested relief where it would not be impossible, impracticable or unequitable.

Covenants -- Declaration Defining Water Frontage

5. Covenants that are entered into with the design to carry out a general scheme for the improvement and development of real property are valid and enforceable; accordingly, plaintiffs, the purchasers of real property from defendants, are entitled to an injunction directing defendants to take all steps necessary to comply with all the covenants and restrictions contained in a declaration which defined the minimum water frontage of four adjacent lots to be 100 feet where defendants unilaterally filed a new map reducing the [***3] frontage of the lot they eventually sold to plaintiffs to 90 feet.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning

6. A town zoning ordinance is not unconstitutionally vague where the intent of the ordinance is absolutely clear in its desire to prohibit two separate residential buildings on one parcel of land in a certain district, and the town or an adjoining landowner is entitled to a mandatory injunction to remove a structure or use which violates the ordinance.

Easements -- Implied Easement

7. Plaintiffs, who purchased real property from defendants, are entitled to an injunction restraining defendants, the owners of an adjacent parcel of property, from in any way interfering with a utility pole located on defendants' property which serves the plaintiffs with electricity, or, in the alternative, that if the defendants want it moved, they pay the cost and do so in a manner acceptable to plaintiffs, where the apparent physical situation at the time of the conveyance indicated an implied easement giving to the plaintiffs, as grantees, the right to have the use or burden on defendants' property as a benefit to the property they purchased.

 

1936 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Carr v. North Hempstead


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


108 Misc. 2d 654; 438 N.Y.S.2d 456; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2263



April 27, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Based on all of the foregoing, the motion by the Town of North Hempstead to dismiss the complaint herein is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211(a)(7), defendant Town of North Hempstead (town) filed a motion to dismiss the complaint filed by plaintiff injured person, under which the injured person sought to recover damages for personal injuries allegedly sustained as a result of the negligence of the town in failing to inspect and prune a tree located in the public way.


OVERVIEW: The injured person sought to recover damages for personal injuries sustained when, while riding in a truck, he was struck by the branch of a tree located in the public way. He claimed that the town had allowed a nuisance and failed to take reasonable measures to avoid the claimed accident. The town argued that the action was barred due to the failure to comply with N.Y. Town Code § 26-1, which required actual written notice by the town as a condition precedent to a civil action for personal injury damages sustained in consequence of any highway, bridge, or culvert being defective, out-of-repair, unsafe, dangerous or obstructed. The court further held that that more relevant and applicable statute was N.Y. Town Code ch. 20A also know as Local Law No. 2-1978. The statute created in the town a specific duty concerning trees in or adjoining public rights of way in order to protect the public against tree-caused unsafe or hazardous conditions. N.Y. Town Code § 26-1 did not apply to defeat the claim merely because the tree was near a highway, bridge, or culvert. Therefore, the court denied the motion to dismiss.


OUTCOME: The court denied the town's motion to dismiss the injured person's complaint, under which the injured person sought damages for injuries allegedly sustained as a result of the town's negligence in failing to inspect and prune a tree located in the public way.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Trees on Public Way -- Nuisance

A local law wherein defendant town specifically assumes the duty to regulate and control all trees in or adjoining public rights of way in order to protect the public against tree-caused unsafe or hazardous conditions throughout the town, creates a specific duty owed to all persons, which duty is not contingent upon the town's prior receipt of written notice of such a condition, as it is required under another local statute where a defective or otherwise unsafe, dangerous or obstructed highway, bridge or culvert might be involved. Accordingly, said highway provision cannot be made to apply so as to defeat a valid claim for personal injuries allegedly sustained as a result of the negligence of the defendant in failing to inspect and prune a tree located in the public way, thereby allowing a nuisance, simply because the offending tree happens to be near a highway, nor is lack of actual written notice to the town of the condition prior to plaintiff's accident fatal.

 

1937 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Goddard


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, Kings County


108 Misc. 2d 742; 439 N.Y.S.2d 71; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2284



April 24, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of attempted criminal mischief and sentenced to a conditional discharge and a fine. He was brought before the court on a warrant when the fine remained unpaid. The state requested incarceration. The court found that defendant was indigent. Defendant sought either revocation of the fine pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 420.10(4)(c) or not to be resentenced to the possible prison term authorized under § 420.10(4)(d).


OVERVIEW: Defendant's sentence for his attempted criminal mischief conviction included a conditional discharge and a $ 250 fine. He failed to pay the fine and the state requested incarceration. After a hearing, the court concluded that defendant was indigent. Defendant then sought either total revocation of the fine under § 420.10(4)(c) or not to be resentenced to the possible prison term authorized by § 420.10(4)(d). It was not clear whether the conditional discharge was independent of the fine or whether the fine was a term of the discharge. The court held that in either instance vacating the entire fine or resentencing to a term of imprisonment was not proper. Therefore, the court lowered the amount of the fine pursuant to § 420.10(4)(a) and resentenced defendant to a conditional discharge plus a $ 50 fine separate and apart therefrom. The court concluded that resentencing defendant to the original term of incarceration because of clear financial inability to pay would have been legally inequitable, constitutionally impermissible, and socially undesirable. Moreover, it would have been tantamount to the court sitting as an appellate court on the judgment of the sentencing judge.


OUTCOME: The court lowered the amount of the fine that was imposed on defendant as part of his sentence for his attempted criminal mischief conviction.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Sentence -- Failure to Pay Fine

To the extent that CPL 420.10 (subd 4, par [d]) permits resentencing of an indigent defendant who is unable to pay a fine previously imposed with a conditional discharge, to the original term of incarceration for said offense, it is legally inequitable, constitutionally impermissible (US Const, 14th Amdt), socially undesirable and tantamount to the court's sitting as an appellate court on the judgment of the sentencing Judge; however, revocation of the entire fine is unauthorized, leaving the court the choice of adjusting the terms of payment ( CPL 420.10, subd 4, par [a]) or lowering the amount of the fine ( CPL 420.10, subd 4, par [b]). Accordingly, defendant is resentenced to a conditional discharge plus a lower fine, separate and apart from said discharge.

 

1938 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Crouse v. First Trust Union Bank


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Cattaraugus County


109 Misc. 2d 89; 438 N.Y.S.2d 438; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2359; 31 U.C.C. Rep. Serv. (Callaghan) 1494



April 21, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff borrower filed a motion for summary judgment in his action against defendant lender that sought, in addition to further relief prayed for, a declaration that the repossession of the borrower's automobile was improper because N.Y. U.C.C. Law § 9-503 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The borrower fell behind in his loan payments to the lender. The lender repossessed the borrower's automobile that was purchased with the money it had loaned to the borrower and that was also pledged as collateral security. The lender gave the borrower no advance written notice of the repossession. Also, the lender did not advise the borrower of any opportunity to be heard before the repossession, and no judicial hearing was noticed or held as to the repossession. In his action against the lender, the borrower filed a motion for summary judgment that sought a declaration that § 9-503 was unconstitutional. The court granted the motion and held that § 9-503 and N.Y. U.C.C. Law § 9-504, which was included therein by reference, violated N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6 because they permitted the seizure and sale of the borrower's automobile without giving the borrower the opportunity to be heard or to otherwise challenge the validity of the lender's lien. The borrower deserved to be heard before his interest in the property was extinguished.


OUTCOME: The court granted the borrower's motion for summary judgment insofar as it declared that the self-help statute for repossession of the borrower's automobile was unconstitutional, but denied the motion in all other respects.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Secured Transactions -- Seizure and Sale of Collateral without Opportunity to be Heard -- Due Process Violation

Sections 9-503 and 9-504 of the Uniform Commercial Code which provide that "a secured party has on default the right to take possession of the collateral * * * without judicial process if this can be done without breach of the peace" and to then dispose of the collateral by a sale without a prior judicial hearing are violative of the due process clause of the State Constitution (NY Const, art I, § 6) to the extent that they permit the seizure and sale of valuable personal property without giving the debtor the opportunity to be heard or otherwise challenge the validity of the creditor's lien (see Sharrock v Dell Buick-Cadillac, 45 NY2d 152).
