Smithtown v. Commack Gas & Washateria


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, Fourth District, Suffolk County


108 Misc. 2d 887; 438 N.Y.S.2d 930; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2309



April 20, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In view of the above, the motion to dismiss pursuant to CPL 170.35 (subd 1, par [c]) is granted since section 54-14D(3)(g6g) of the Code of the Town of Smithtown is unconstitutional insofar as it prohibits portable signs in filling stations.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who was charged with violations of Town of Smithtown, N.Y., Code §§ 54-12(A), 54-140(3), which prohibited the use of portable signs in filling stations, filed a motion to dismiss pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 170.35(1)(c), and alleged that the offense charged was unconstitutional or otherwise invalid.


OVERVIEW: Defendant maintained a portable sandwich sign at its premises, a filling station. Defendant was charged with the violation of Town of Smithtown, N.Y., Code §§ 54-12(A), 54-140(3), which prohibited the use of portable signs in filling stations. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 170.35(1)(c), and alleged that the offense charged was unconstitutional or otherwise invalid. The town submitted no opposing papers to the motion, apparently relying on the presumption that a statute was constitutional until proven otherwise beyond a reasonable doubt. Defendant's main claim was that the ordinance was discriminatory and was an unconstitutional regulation of commercial free speech as protected by the First and Fourteenth Amendments. The court noted that only filling stations had a prohibition against portable signs. The court found that a repair shop could have a portable sign, and since the main difference between the two activities was the retail sale of gasoline, the court concluded that the ordinance was an unconstitutional restriction on protected commercial speech aimed specifically at retail gasoline price information.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Restriction on Commercial Speech

A local ordinance which prohibits gasoline filling stations from displaying portable signs, but does not so limit display of portable signs in connection with other commercial activities regulated under the same chapter of the town code, including repair garages, is an unconstitutional restriction on the content of protected commercial speech aimed specifically at retail gasoline price information; moreover, alternative means of communicating the information would involve more cost and less autonomy and would be less likely to reach persons not deliberately seeking sales information.

 

1940 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Turbin v. Metropolitan Transp. Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


109 Misc. 2d 149; 439 N.Y.S.2d 578; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2367



April 16, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff instituted an action against defendants, County of Nassau, Metropolitan Transportation Authority (MTA), and Metropolitan Suburban Bus Authority (MSBA), alleging violation of plaintiff's civil rights under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The county filed a motion for an order granting summary judgment and dismissing the complaint and cross- claims against the county. Plaintiff, the MTA, and the MSBA filed cross-motions.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, a handicapped person, alleged that after he boarded a bus with a half-fare identification card issued by the City of New York, the bus driver refused to honor the card and had plaintiff arrested and prosecuted for theft of services. After the charges were dismissed, plaintiff filed an action against the county under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 based on an alleged "policy" which deprived plaintiff of his constitutional rights. The court determined that plaintiff failed to show that the county had a policy that proximately caused the alleged incident. The court found that a policy of charging full fare to handicapped non-county residents could not be a proximate cause of the incident, that there was no proof that the county had a policy of enforcing its regulation by causing non-county bus riders to be arrested, that plaintiff had not even demonstrated that the county had jurisdiction or undertook to formulate such a policy with regard to buses, and that an "official policy" could not be inferred from a single incident. The court did not grant the county's motion for summary judgment in order to permit plaintiff to take oral depositions of county supervisory employees.


OUTCOME: The court denied the county's motion for summary judgment with leave to renew the motion at the conclusion of depositions of its supervisory employees. The court granted the cross-motions of plaintiff and the Metropolitan Suburban Bus Authority to the extent of directing the examinations.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal Corporations -- Liability for Federal Civil Rights Violation -- Half-Fare Bus Rate for Handicapped Nassau County Residents Only

The alleged "policy" of defendant Nassau County of not permitting non-Nassau handicapped persons to receive the same half-fare bus rates afforded to handicapped Nassau County residents, which "policy" allegedly resulted in plaintiff's arrest and prosecution for "theft of services" (Penal Law, § 165.15) when a bus driver refused to honor the half-fare card issued to plaintiff by the City of New York, cannot be a proximate cause of the incident and thus cannot give rise to defendant's liability under the Federal civil rights statute (US Code, tit 42, § 1983) absent a showing that defendant maintained a policy "to arrest and detain handicapped persons on buses for failure to pay the full fare" since a municipality may be sued for damages under the Federal civil rights statute only when constitutional violations have been committed pursuant to an "official policy" or custom relating to the deprivation of a constitutional right, not the policy of a municipality's regulation of the fares on public transit; since defendant disregarded a prior [***2] court order directing that it submit to oral depositions, plaintiff is afforded an opportunity to examine the county to determine the county's relationship with the bus line operator and whether the county had a policy of enforcing its regulations by causing bus riders to be arrested for "theft of services".

 

1941 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jones


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Syracuse


108 Misc. 2d 574; 438 N.Y.S.2d 75; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2243



April 15, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to dismiss the charge of resisting arrest in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 205.30 that was filed against him.


OVERVIEW: Police followed defendant's car after noticing that there were no stickers of inspection or registration on it. As defendant exited the car and began walking away, a police officer called to him. Defendant then began to run, and the police officer pursued him. The police officer followed defendant's footprints into an apartment building and found him in an unlocked apartment. The police officer placed defendant under arrest. Defendant physically attempted to prevent his arrest. Defendant was subsequently charged with operating an unregistered vehicle and with resisting arrest. Defendant moved to dismiss the resisting arrest charge, and the court granted the motion. Pursuit was justified only if a police officer was endeavoring to complete the arrest of an individual the officer had probable cause to believe had committed or was about to commit a crime. The traffic offense committed by defendant was not a crime justifying pursuit.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Arrest -- Resisting Arrest

Where a police officer attempts to question a suspect in regard to a traffic infraction, the suspect flees and is followed into an apartment by the officer, and while being placed under arrest, pushes the officer, a charge of resisting arrest cannot be sustained since a search or seizure carried out on a suspect's premises without a warrant is per se unreasonable, absent exigent circumstances; although hot pursuit of a suspect is such a circumstance, it is only justified when the suspect is fleeing the scene of a crime, and neither flight from the attempted questioning of a police officer nor a traffic infraction constitutes a crime. No other exigent circumstances are present; therefore, the arrest of defendant was unlawful and violative of his Fourth Amendment rights. An authorized arrest is an indispensable element of a resisting arrest offense.

 

1942 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Gorsky v. Gorsky


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Queens County


108 Misc. 2d 583; 438 N.Y.S.2d 196; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2245



April 9, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant former wife filed a motion to dismiss the first, second, and third causes of action on the grounds of res judicata, for summary judgment dismissing the first, second, fourth, fifth, and sixth causes of action, and for an attorney's fee relative to the aftermath of a dual divorce.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff former husband alleged conversion by the former wife of personal property belonging exclusively to him. The former wife was awarded possession of the property in the divorce. The third cause of action was based on the judgment of divorce, which directed the parties to execute documents enabling a bank to split their joint bank account between them. The fourth and fifth causes of action were based on the alleged conversion by the former wife of half of the parties' 1977 joint income tax refund. The sixth cause of action was based on a judgment obtained by a department store against the former husband following the divorce. A decision on the posttrial motion regarding the tax refund did not constitute law of the case and neither it, nor the judgment of divorce, barred the interposition of the first three causes of action. Dismissing the third cause of action, the court noted that the action regarding the joint bank account was an action on a judgment, which, under the circumstances, was impermissible. Summary judgment was denied because N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 176 was found unconstitutional. Accordingly, the personal property could have been awarded to the former husband.


OUTCOME: The court held that the husband was entitled to maintain the action except for the third cause of action, and the request for summary judgment and attorney's fees were denied.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Husband and Wife -- Divorce -- Property Rights in Action by Wife

 Section 176 of the Domestic Relations Law, which provides that where a final judgment in a divorce action brought by the wife is rendered dissolving the marriage and the wife "has in her possession or under her control any personal property * * * which was left with her" by the husband, the husband "shall not have any interest therein, absolute or contingent, before or after her death", is an unconstitutional gender-based statute that does not serve a legitimate governmental interest; a neutral-gender construction of the statute would not preserve its constitutionality since the statute relates to the common-law estates of curtesy and dower which were radically altered in 1930 and such a construction would only have the effect of resurrecting a statute dead more than 50 years; accordingly, plaintiff husband is entitled to maintain an action against his former wife based upon allegations of conversion by defendant of furniture and other personal property belonging exclusively to plaintiff which were left in the marital premises over which defendant was awarded exclusive possession.

 

1943 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Heverly


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Cattaraugus County


113 Misc. 2d 925; 450 N.Y.S.2d 253; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3472



March 29, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In proceedings involving a decedent's estate, the decedent's brother filed a petition seeking limited letters of administration of the estate for the sole purpose of prosecuting an action for the decedent's wrongful death. The court-appointed guardian ad litem opposed the petition, contending that the decedent's natural daughter, who was adopted by her stepfather, was entitled to appointment as the special administrator to prosecute the action.


OVERVIEW: The decedent was killed in an accident while a passenger in a car driven by his ex-wife. The ex-wife, who was nominated as the executrix of the decedent's estate in his will, declined to institute an action for the decedent's wrongful death. The brother filed a petition seeking appointment as a special administrator under N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 5-4.1 to bring the wrongful death action. The court appointed a guardian ad litem, who determined that the decedent had a natural daughter with a previous wife and that the daughter was adopted by her stepfather. The guardian ad litem contended that the daughter was entitled to appointment as the special administrator to prosecute the wrongful death action. The court rejected the guardian ad litem's argument and granted the brother's petition for appointment as the special administrator. The right to damages for wrongful death accrued at the moment of the decedent's death. At the time of the death, the daughter was not entitled to share in the proceeds of the wrongful death action because, pursuant to N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 117, an adopted person's rights to inheritance through her natural parents terminated upon adoption.


OUTCOME: The court granted the brother's petition for appointment as a special administrator for purposes of prosecuting a wrongful death action for the decedent's death.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Death -- Wrongful Death -- Right of Adopted Natural Child to Be Appointed Special Administrator

Decedent's daughter, who was adopted by the husband of decedent's first wife in 1947 following decedent's divorce, may not be appointed as a special administrator to bring a wrongful death action ( EPTL 5-4.1) against the executor of decedent's estate, decedent's divorced second wife who was driving the car in which decedent was killed, since decedent's adopted natural daughter is not a distributee of the decedent entitled to share in the proceeds of the wrongful death action ( EPTL 4-1.1) inasmuch as the right to participate in damages in a wrongful death action is determined as of the date of the decedent's death and while in 1947 adopted children could inherit from both the natural parents and adoptive parents, the law in effect at the time of decedent's death in 1981 terminated the "rights of an adoptive child to inheritance and succession from and through his natural parents" (Domestic Relations Law, § 117); accordingly, since decedent's personal representative refuses to institute a wrongful death action against herself, decedent's brother, with the consent of the other distributees,  [***2] is appointed special administrator to bring the action.

 

1944 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Kountz v. State University of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Kings County


109 Misc. 2d 319; 437 N.Y.S.2d 868; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2396



March 17, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Appellate Division, Second Department (New York) reversed a summary judgment and remanded the action of plaintiff doctors against defendants, state university and teaching hospital (university), for trial as to whether article 8-AA of the Education Law, N.Y. Educ. Law §§ 385-a -- 385-i, applied to fees for services to private patients, or whether the law unconstitutionally deprived the doctors of due process and/or equal protection.


OVERVIEW: The doctors sought a declaratory judgment that article 8-AA of the Education Law, N.Y. Educ. Law §§ 385-a -- 385-I, did not apply to fees for medical services rendered to private practice patients or that the statute was unconstitutional on the grounds that it deprived them due process of law and equal protection. The court summarily declared that the law applied only to services rendered in connection with their employment as teaching professionals, but the appellate court reversed and remanded for trial. On remand, the court declared that article 8-AA encompassed a doctor's fees from private practice as patient care was an integral part of a doctor's teaching responsibilities, and the law was meant to implement a collective bargaining agreement intended to apply to all patient care services. The court further held that article 8-AA did not violate any constitutional rights because the law was rationally related to the legitimate state and university interests of fostering full-time devotion to teaching duties, and the doctors had no constitutional right to engage in the private practice of medicine while holding a public position of employment as teachers at the university.


OUTCOME: On remand from the appellate court, the court declared that the statute was applicable to all patient care services rendered by doctors employed by the university, including services rendered and fees earned in private practice, and that the statute did not unconstitutionally deprive the doctors of equal protection of the law or of property without due process of law.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Colleges and Universities -- State Medical College -- Private Practice Income of Teaching Physicians

1. Plaintiffs, who are licensed physicians employed by a medical college operated, managed and controlled by the State University of New York (SUNY), are not entitled to a declaration that article 8-AA of the Education Law, entitled "Clinical Practice Income Management Corporations", does not apply to fees for medical services rendered by the plaintiffs to their own private practice patients; article 8-AA, like the collective bargaining agreement which it was intended to implement, is applicable to all patient care services rendered by the teaching professionals (i.e., doctors) employed at a SUNY medical college, including the services rendered by the doctors, and the fees earned therefrom, to patients in their private practice.

Colleges and Universities -- State Medical College -- Private Practice Income of Teaching Physicians

2. Article 8-AA of the Education Law, entitled "Clinical Practice Income Management Corporations", which limits the amount of private practice income a physician teaching at a State University of New York (SUNY) medical college may receive,  [***2] is constitutional inasmuch as the enactment of a clinical practice plan and the inclusion of a teaching doctor's private practice within the purview of said plan are reasonably and rationally related to the legitimate State and university interests of fostering full-time devotion to teaching duties; article 8-AA does not violate due process since there is no constitutional right to engage in the unlimited private practice of medicine while holding a public position of employment as teachers at SUNY, and there is no violation of equal protection inasmuch as all licensed physicians on a SUNY faculty are treated similarly.

Statutes -- Presumption of Constitutionality

3. A legislative enactment carries with it an exceedingly strong presumption of constitutionality and a party who is attacking the constitutionality of a statute has the heavy burden of establishing unconstitutionality beyond a reasonable doubt; only as a last resort should a court strike down legislation on the ground of unconstitutionality, and while the law may not be arbitrary, it need only be reasonably related to some manifest evil which is reasonably apprehended.

 

1945 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Board of Education v. Ambach


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


108 Misc. 2d 632; 438 N.Y.S.2d 427; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2257



March 16, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The instant motions will be granted to the extent that the court will grant a judgment which declares section 3602 (subd 7, pars d, e) of the Education Law and the regulation issued pursuant thereto (8 NYCRR 175.9) to be constitutional, valid and enforceable.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a board of education and its officers individually as residents and taxpayers, filed a summary judgment motion. Plaintiffs' declaratory judgment action contended that "cap" legislation, N.Y. Educ. Law § 3602(7)(d), (e) was unconstitutional and its implementing regulation, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 8, § 175.9, was invalid. Defendant Commissioner of Education of the State of New York filed a cross-motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs filed a complaint claiming that the school district would be forced to purchase new school buses on credit. Plaintiffs asserted that the "cap" legislation and its implementing regulation would reduce the State's share of the cost of acquiring the buses from 90 percent to about four percent of their purchase price. The court only sustained the motions to the extent of its judgment that declared N.Y. Educ. Law § 3602(7)(d), (e) and its regulations constitutional, valid, and enforceable under N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 8, § 175.9. The court rejected the Commissioner's contention that the complaint was prematurely filed because the complaint sufficiently charged that the State was about to perform acts pursuant to the N.Y. Educ. Law § 3607 that were unconstitutional. The court rejected the claim of unconstitutionality as meritless. The court applied the rational basis test and concluded that a program designed to encourage cash rather than credit purchases of school buses was a rational one. The court could not find that the formula that the legislature and the Commissioner had adopted to pursue that objective was an irrational one that could not have been achieved.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motions to the extent that it rendered a judgment declaring the statute and the regulation issued pursuant thereto to be constitutional, valid, and enforceable.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- Transportation of Pupils -- State Aid for Pupil Transportation -- Exception to "Cap" on Spending for Purchase of School Buses for Cash

A statute and implementing regulation, which exclude from the "cap" on State aid to local school districts for pupil transportation, all purchases of school buses "other than by notes and bonds" (Education Law, § 3602, subd 7, pars d, e; 8 NYCRR 175.9 [a] [1]), thereby rewarding those local school districts which pay cash for new school buses and penalizing those districts which finance such purchases, does not deny equal protection of the law to those school districts that cannot afford to pay cash for new school buses since there is no constitutional requirement that all school districts be treated alike and, in any event, a program designed to encourage cash rather than credit purchases of school buses in times of high interest rates is a rational one and it cannot [***2] be said that the State aid formula the Legislature and the Commissioner of Education have adopted to pursue that objective is an irrational one; any judicial determination that a school district cannot "afford" to pay cash for its school buses necessarily involves impermissible judicial intrusion into areas of responsibility reserved for other branches of government; the regulation regarding the purchase of school buses was validly promulgated under the authority of the "cap" legislation.

 

1946 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Short v. Louis Ohland Meat Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Nassau County


108 Misc. 2d 338; 437 N.Y.S.2d 549; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2202



March 13, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, it is the decision of this court that the motion of the plaintiff to prohibit the use of the toxicology report in the autopsy of the decedent Joseph P. Short is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff executrix filed a motion prior to trial in her personal injury and wrongful death action against defendants concerning the admissibility of the toxicological part of the decedent's autopsy report.


OVERVIEW: The executrix initiated a wrongful death and personal injury action against defendants. Prior to trial, the executrix sought to prohibit the admission of the toxicology part of the decedent's autopsy report, which indicated the presence of .12 percent alcohol. The executrix's motion was based upon a rule in effect at the time the testing was done, which prohibited admission of such reports. The court found that the rule regulating the admissibility of a toxicological report, which was found in N.Y. County Law § 674(3)(b), was a rule of evidence only, and should have been applied as it read at the time of trial, rather than at the time the test was performed. Thus, use of the toxicology part of the autopsy report was permitted.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion of the executrix to prohibit the use of the toxicology report in the autopsy of the decedent.


HEADNOTES

Evidence -- Admissibility of Toxicological Autopsy Report in Negligence Action -- Alcoholic Content of Blood of Deceased Motorist

The proscription against the use in a negligence action of the toxicological autopsy report on the alcoholic content of the blood of a deceased motorist in effect at the time the examinations were conducted does not carry over to cases pending after the effective date of the 1978 amendment which eliminated the proscription against the use of the toxicological part of the autopsy report in a negligence action (County Law, § 674, subd 3, par [b]) since the admissibility of a toxicological report is an evidentiary rule only and, inasmuch as there is no vested right in a rule of evidence, a statute which is retroactive as to such a procedural matter is not unconstitutional; accordingly, since the statute should be applied as it now reads and not as it read in 1976, the toxicological part of the 1976 autopsy report relating to decedent which indicated the presence [***2] of 0.12% alcohol is admissible in the personal injury and wrongful death action.

 

1947 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Galaxy Rental Service, Inc. v. State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


108 Misc. 2d 237; 437 N.Y.S.2d 854; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2187



March 12, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff rental service sought a preliminary injunction to enjoin defendant state from enforcing, on constitutional grounds, recently enacted amendments to N.Y. Real. Prop. Law § 12-C, which required refund of certain advance paid fees for customers who were unable to secure acceptable housing through information provided by duly licensed rental services.


OVERVIEW: The court held that in order to obtain a preliminary injunction, the rental service assumed the burden of establishing the unconstitutionality of the statute beyond a reasonable doubt. The rental service was engaged in the business of providing relevant information concerning the availability of rental housing to prospective tenants in exchange for an agreed upon fee. The legislation and accompanying regulations were enacted in response to complaints about referrals to nonexistent or inappropriate apartments and to prevent the use of misleading advertisements. In denying the injunction and upholding the constitutionality of the contested statute and regulations, the court held the regulations were reasonably related and made uniformly applicable to a type of business conduct which the state could constitutionally limit or proscribe. Commercial speech enjoyed a more limited form of constitutional privilege than other types of protected expression and was subject to the strict requirements of truthfulness, and if found to false or misleading, could be totally prohibited.


OUTCOME: The court denied the preliminary injunction requested by the rental service and found the statute and regulations were constitutional.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Landlord and Tenant -- Regulation of Apartment Information Vendors

A motion by plaintiff "apartment information vendor" (Real Property Law, § 446-a, subd 2) for a preliminary injunction enjoining the State's enforcement of certain provisions relating to the licensing and regulation of such vendors, formerly referred to as "apartment referral agents" (L 1975, ch 772, § 1), made in an action seeking declaratory and injunctive relief invalidating, on constitutional grounds, recently enacted amendments to article 12-C of the Real Property Law (Real Property Law, § 446-b, subds 3, 4; § 446-c, subd 5, pars [a], [b]) and certain regulations of the Secretary of State (19 NYCRR 190.1, 190.2, 190.5, 190.6, 190.8), particularly 19 NYCRR 190.8, which prohibits advertising of specific apartments by such information vendors, is denied, since the contested statutes and regulations are reasonably related and uniformly applicable to a type of business conduct which the State may constitutionally limit or proscribe; while less drastic, but equally effective, measures might have been adopted, it is not the court's function, especially upon a motion for a preliminary injunction, to question [***2] the means chosen by the Legislature or the administrative agency to address the demonstrated evils of unverified referrals and referrals to nonexistent or uninhabitable housing which were prevalent in the referral industry.

 

1948 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Miller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Nassau County


108 Misc. 2d 528; 437 N.Y.S.2d 543; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2233



March 11, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution in a criminal proceeding moved the court for an order granting discovery of certain non-testimonial evidence from defendant, pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 240.40(2)(b).


OVERVIEW: The prosecution in a criminal proceeding requested discovery from defendant, including two business letters written to him and "other documents," which were records relating to the conduct of a hospital where defendant served as assistant administrator. The prosecution made this request pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 240.40(2)(b), which allowed it to request non-testimonial evidence in discovery. The court denied the request for the "other documents" because there was not a sufficient showing as to need and justification, and the request was too broad. As to the requested letters, the court was convinced of the specificity, special need, and justification for them, and ordered their production. There was little dispute of the possible probative value of these documents to the charges; their inaccessibility through other sources, primarily the hospital itself; and the reasonableness of the request. The court was satisfied that the U.S. Const. amend. V privilege did not protect the content of the documents, but held that no evidence could be adduced at trial that would require the defendant to testimonially authenticate the documents absent a waiver of his constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court denied the prosecution's request for the "other documents" because there was not a sufficient showing as to need and justification, and the request was too broad, but ordered that the requested letters be produced.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Discovery -- Nontestimonial Evidence

A motion by the People to compel discovery of two letters written to defendant in his capacity as assistant administrator of a hospital and "other documents" relating to the conduct of business at the hospital, pursuant to CPL 240.40 (subd 2, par [b]) which allows a court before which an indictment is pending to order a defendant to provide nontestimonial evidence, is denied with regard to the "other documents" since the prosecutor did not make a sufficient showing as to need and justification, the "property" to be discovered was not specifically designated ( CPL 240.10, subd 3) and the request was so overbroad as to possibly compel disclosure of defendant's attorney's work product in contravention of CPL 240.10 (subd 2) or require in camera viewing by the court; however, the motion is granted with regard to the letters since the Fifth Amendment privilege against disclosure of incriminating testimonial evidence does not extend to the personal business records and their production will not result in any unconstitutional compulsion upon the defendant to authenticate these records absent a waiver of his constitutional rights.

 

1949 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Ventura


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


108 Misc. 2d 281; 437 N.Y.S.2d 538; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2194



March 10, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought to vacate the suspension of his driver's license, claiming that N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1194(2) was unconstitutional because it mandated the suspension of his driver's license prior to a revocation hearing after he refused to submit to a chemical test upon arrest for driving while intoxicated.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for driving while intoxicated and his driver's license was suspended after he refused to submit to a chemical test pursuant to § 1194(2). Defendant claimed that § 1194(2) was unconstitutional because it mandated the suspension of his driver's license prior to a revocation hearing. The court ruled that defendant failed to sustain his burden of showing that the lack of a pre-suspension hearing was violative of his due process rights. The court held that the duration of any potentially wrongful deprivation of defendant's license and the timeliness of the post-suspension review was reasonable. The court ruled that the state had a compelling and legitimate interest in public safety in promptly removing drivers from the road that were driving while intoxicated.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that defendant's due process rights were not violated by the suspension of his driver's license prior to a revocation hearing after he refused to submit to a chemical test upon arrest for driving while intoxicated.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Statutes -- Presumption of Constitutionality

1. All statutes are entitled to a strong presumption of constitutionality, and any individual challenging a statute has the burden of establishing unconstitutionality beyond a reasonable doubt.

Motor Vehicles -- Revocation or Suspension of Operator's License

2. Subdivision 2 of section 1194 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, which mandates suspension of a driver's license prior to a revocation hearing because of the driver's refusal to submit to a chemical test upon arrest for driving while intoxicated, is not void on its face as being violative of the due process clause of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution and section 6 of article I of the State Constitution.

 

1950 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Baker


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Oneida County


106 Misc. 2d 994; 436 N.Y.S.2d 833; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2042



March 4, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Since the original neglect petition was adjourned more than one year ago, we order the original petition dated September 7, 1979, dismissed in the interest of justice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, the parent of a minor child that petitioner state government sought to take on grounds of neglect, moved to dismiss an adjournment-in-contemplation-of-dismissal violation petition and challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act art. 10, § 1039(e), pursuant to which the petition was filed.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner state government filed suit and alleged that respondent parent's minor child was neglected. Parties thereafter agreed that case could be adjourned in contemplation of dismissal (ACD). After ACD status was taken, child services agency became dissatisfied with respondent's conduct, and based on that alleged lack of cooperation, plaintiff sought to transform the ACD into a judgment of neglect. Respondent objected and challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 1039, which held that an ACD order in a child neglect action was automatically transformed into a judgment of neglect if the matter was not dismissed. The court agreed with respondent and held that the state constitution prohibited a state from removing a child from parental custody absent a judicial finding of unfitness or neglect. The court also held that putative waiver of the right to such a finding that was occasioned by entry of ACD status was invalid because it was coercive.


OUTCOME: The court granted respondent's motion and declared unconstitutional that portion of the Family Court Act that obviated petitioner's burden to prove neglect in certain child protective actions. The court held that a state could not constitutionally interfere with parental custody rights absent a judicial finding of neglect.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child -- Child Protective Proceeding -- Adjournment in Contemplation of Dismissal

Subdivision (e) of section 1039 of the Family Court Act, which permits a child to be removed from his parents without any actual finding of abuse or neglect but merely upon a finding of non-co-operation with a child protective agency when a child protective proceeding has been adjourned in contemplation of dismissal upon the condition the parents co-operate with the agency, is unconstitutional since the Constitution prohibits a court from interfering with parental custody rights unless they have violated a duty relating to their child's welfare and parents cannot, therefore, be deprived of the custody of their child without an actual finding of abuse or neglect; and adjournment in contemplation of dismissal is not tantamount to a knowing and voluntary surrender of custody since parents who are facing a child protective proceeding which may result in the loss of their child and who are given a choice of either consenting [***2] to the adjournment and taking their children home or facing the charges and risking the loss of their children cannot be said to be acting without threat or coercion.

 

1951 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bakolas


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Rochester


108 Misc. 2d 74; 436 N.Y.S.2d 956; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2158



March 3, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, this court holds subdivision 2 of section 240.20 of the Penal Law to be unconstitutional on its face and the defendants' application to dismiss as to this charge against each defendant is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were charged with, among other things, a violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.20(2), which provided that a person was guilty of disorderly conduct when he made unreasonable noise. Defendants argued that the statute was unconstitutionally vague, and plaintiff, the People of the State of New York, defended the statute. Defendants moved to dismiss the informations on the grounds that the statute was unconstitutional on its face.


OVERVIEW: Both defendants were charged with disorderly conduct for making "unreasonable noise," as prohibited in N.Y. Penal Law § 240.20(2). Defendants challenged the constitutionality of the provision, arguing that it was too vague. Defendants moved to dismiss the informations on the grounds that the statute was unconstitutional on its face. Defendants argued that the statute was constitutionally infirm because it was overbroad and vague. The court determined that what was unreasonable noise to some people was not unreasonable to others. The court held that the statute was unconstitutional. The court found that the statute failed to give fair warning of prohibited conduct. The statute sanctioned arbitrary application by law enforcement personnel and the courts because of its vagueness. Finally, the statute operated to inhibit exercise of basic First Amendment freedoms.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendants' application to dismiss as to the charge for "unreasonable noise."


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Disorderly Conduct -- "Unreasonable Noise"

A statute will be void for vagueness if its prohibitions are not clearly defined, thus encouraging arbitrary and erratic arrest and convictions; accordingly, the disorderly conduct statute which proscribes making an "unreasonable noise" with "intent to cause public inconvenience, annoyance or alarm, or recklessly creating a risk thereof" (Penal Law, § 240.20, subd 2) is unconstitutionally vague since it sanctions arbitrary application by law enforcement personnel and the courts in that what is "unreasonable noise" depends upon the ear and mind of the listener and what is "unreasonable noise" to some people is not unreasonable to others.

 

1952 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Roque


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, New York County


108 Misc. 2d 965; 437 N.Y.S.2d 527; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2323



March 2, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 330.30(1) to set a side the verdict convicting him of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the fourth degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted by a jury of the crime of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the fourth degree. He moved to set aside the verdict on the grounds that the chain of custody on the substance was not established, that the evidence against him was insufficient to support conviction and that the court's instruction shifting the burden to him to prove that he legally possessed the controlled dangerous substance violated his due process rights. Defendant argued that the proof of two of the elements of the crime charged, ownership of the car in which the controlled substance was found and his knowledge of the substance, were circumstantial in nature and that they fell short of the proof required. The court rejected defendant's arguments and denied the motion because the chain of custody adequately established the identity and unchanged condition of the substance, the jury was properly instructed on its right to make or reject inferences based upon the evidence presented, and defendant failed to raise the issue regarding the burden shifting at trial.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion because the change of custody was properly proven and circumstantial evidence was sufficient to support defendant's conviction.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Controlled Substances -- Unlawful Possession

1. Defendant was properly convicted of criminal possession of a controlled substance where there was a legal sufficient chain of custody regarding the contraband and the circumstantial evidence was sufficient to support the verdict; defendant was the driver and sole occupant of the car in which the contraband was found and told the arresting officer that he was the owner of the car.

Crimes -- Unlawful Possession of Controlled Substances

2. Subdivision 1 of section 3396 of the Public Health Law does not shift the burden of proof to the defendant in a trial for criminal possession of a controlled substance so as to require him to prove that it was lawful for him to possess the narcotics inasmuch as if the issue of lawful possession is raised, the People are then required to prove the contrary beyond a reasonable doubt.

 

1953 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re H.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


106 Misc. 2d 972; 436 N.Y.S.2d 814; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2040



February 24, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner foster parents brought a proceeding to terminate the parental rights of their foster child's biological mother pursuant to § 622 of the Family Court Act and § 392 of the Social Services Law.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to § 622 of the Family Court Act (Act), petitioner foster parents brought a proceeding for a finding that the biological mother of their foster child permanently neglected the child thereby allowing for a termination of her parental rights and their adoption of the child. The court held that under § 622 and the constitutional guarantees of due process, clear and convincing evidence was required to support a finding of permanent neglect. The court had jurisdiction over the matter notwithstanding the fact that the absent father was not served, because under N.Y. C.P.L.R. § 1001 he would not be adversely affected as his consent to the adoption would not be required under § 111 of the Act. The court found that there had been permanent neglect because mother's failure to maintain contact with the child for over a one-year period was not justified by her economic circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court made a finding that the biological mother of the child of petitioner foster parents permanently neglected the child and that her financial inability did not justify her failure to visit the child for over a one-year period.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Parent and Child -- Termination of Parental Rights -- Service on Unwed Father in Guardianship Proceeding

1. A guardian cannot be appointed for the purpose of a child's adoption (Social Services Law, § 384-b, subd 4, par [d]) unless a successful permanent neglect proceeding or other proceeding to terminate parental rights has been brought against an unwed father whose consent to the adoption is required (Domestic Relations Law, § 111) and a named father should be served if the known facts or gaps in knowledge show even a slight likelihood that he has a right to be heard in a proceeding to transfer a child's guardianship for the purpose of adoption but when it is established with complete clarity that a man is outside the classes of unwed fathers with a right to be heard because of his failure to maintain any contacts with his child, he need not be joined and served as a necessary party merely because the mother has named him; accordingly, the Family Court has jurisdiction to entertain a guardianship proceeding initiated by petitioners foster parents despite their failure to serve the named unwed father.

Parent and Child -- Abused or Neglected Child -- Permanent  [***2] Neglect -- "Clear and Convincing" Standard

2. Although the statute provides that a "fair preponderance of the evidence" suffices for a fact finding of permanent neglect (Family Ct Act, § 622), a parent's constitutional due process rights require the application of the higher "clear and convincing" standard of proof in a permanent neglect proceeding; accordingly, on the basis of such "clear and convincing" evidence, permanent neglect is found in that respondent mother "failed * * * substantially and continuously * * * to maintain contact" with her child for over a year of the child's foster care although financially able to do so (Family Ct Act, § 611; Social Services Law, § 384-b, subd 7, par [a]), the availability of public funds constituting financial capability from the standpoint of permanent neglect inasmuch as the purpose of the financial ability clause is to preclude a permanent neglect finding only when the parent's failure to maintain contact is attributable to external impossibility, and the fact that the respondent reestablished contact with her child after the statutory one-year "critical period" does not preclude a permanent neglect finding nor was the foster [***3] care agency, which made good faith efforts to encourage respondent's relationship with her child, under a duty to pressure respondent to visit her child during the "critical period".

Parent and Child -- Abused or Neglected Child -- Permanent Neglect

3. Due process of law does not require that a foster care agency warn a parent of the possibility of a permanent neglect proceeding (Family Ct Act, § 611) prior to the acts of neglect thereafter alleged and, in any event, the requirement of an advance warning cannot be judicially imposed as a condition of the rights of the other parties in the proceeding since it would frustrate the foster care review statutory scheme (Social Services Law, § 392); relegating major considerations of the child's best interests, including a purported desire for adoption by the foster parents, to the second stage of a permanent neglect proceeding so that it becomes the central focus only if the court first finds neglectful conduct by the parent, is a constitutional "attempt to balance the rights of the parents against those of the child".

 

1954 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Link


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


107 Misc. 2d 973; 436 N.Y.S.2d 581; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2120



February 23, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, who were charged with the crime of prostitution, filed a motion for trial by jury.


OVERVIEW: Defendants contended that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 340.40(2) was unconstitutional because prostitution was not a petty offense but a serious offense and that § 340.40(2) denied them equal protection because it withheld the right to trial by jury in a certain class of misdemeanor trials in a city but permitted jury trials of such cases in the remainder of a state. The court held that to the extent § 340.40(2) made a jury trial unavailable in a specific county, it contravened the Sixth and Fourteenth Amendments and was null because prostitution, no matter how lightly punished, was a serious crime and could not have been prosecuted without the right to trial by jury.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion. The court ordered a trial by jury, stayed the order for 30 days, and afforded the State adequate time for pursuit of the State's legal options.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Right to Jury Trial

The crime of prostitution (Penal Law, § 230.00), without regard of the fact that it carries a maximum term of imprisonment of under six months (Penal Law, § 70.15, subd 2), is a serious crime and may not be prosecuted without the right to trial by jury and, accordingly, to the extent that CPL 340.40 (subd 2) makes such a trial unavailable in New York County, said statute contravenes the Sixth and Fourteenth Amendments to the United States Constitution and is null; the six-month potential sentence dividing line was only intended to be the criterion of whether or not an offense is serious where it is not inherently serious, apart from the sentence to which the defendant is exposed.

 

1955 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re P.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Kings County


108 Misc. 2d 181; 437 N.Y.S.2d 225; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2176



February 15, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The clerk will enter a short form order that the motion by the attorney for the respondent is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed petitions in the Family Court of New York, Kings County, seeking to terminate the paternal rights of respondent mother, on the ground that she was unable to provide proper care for her children by reason of mental illness and that she has permanently neglected her children. The mother's attorney filed a motion for an order dismissing the petitions on the ground that N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 384-b was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner sought orders terminating the rights of the mother on the ground that she was unable to provide proper care for her children by reason of mental illness and that she has permanently neglected her children, pursuant to N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 384-b. The mother's attorney filed a motion for an order dismissing the petitions on the ground that § 384-b was unconstitutional. The court denied the motion. Many decisions had established the power of the state to deprive a parent of the custody of his or her child where there would be a danger of neglect to the child if the parent were allowed custody. A parent could be deprived of custody for the entire minority of the child. A child who was made a ward of the state could thus be kept in indefinite foster care. If the state could deprive such a parent of custody through annual proceedings, then the state may deprive such a parent who may be free of fault of the custody of his child for all time where the proof was clear and convincing.


OUTCOME: The court denied the attorney's motion.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child -- Termination of Parental Rights -- Mental Illness of Parent

A motion by respondent mother for an order dismissing petitions to terminate her parental rights, which petitions are based upon allegations that she is unable to provide proper care for her children by reason of mental illness and that she has permanently neglected her children, on the ground that section 384-b of the Social Services Law is unconstitutional, is denied; the State may terminate parental rights in cases of neglect if the proof is clear and convincing, even where the parent may be said to be free of fault because the conduct which endangers the child is the product of mental illness and, further, there is no constitutional requirement that the proof that a termination of parental rights is in the best interests of the child be at a separate dispositional hearing.

 

1956 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Felder v. Foster


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


107 Misc. 2d 782; 436 N.Y.S.2d 675; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2096



February 11, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, members of a class who had been found to have been unconstitutionally disallowed welfare benefits by defendant county legislators and county officials, filed a motion for attorney's fees pursuant to the Civil Rights Attorney's Fees Awards Act of 1976, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988. The class members had prosecuted their action under the Civil Rights Act of 1871, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983.


OVERVIEW: The class members sought attorney's fees on behalf of a nonprofit group that had served as their counsel. The legislators and county officials contended that any grant of attorney's fees was inappropriate and that even if they were in fact proper, the amount requested was excessive. The court held that only a showing of minimum causality between the class members' lawsuit and achievement of the remedy sought was necessary to trigger an award of fees under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988. The court found that because the class had in fact substantially prevailed, compensation even for time spent on the unsuccessful issue of punitive damages was within the ambit of § 1988. Further, the court ruled that an award of attorney's fees was not any less appropriate because the class members' attorney was a nonprofit legal organization which did not charge for its services. The court concluded that while reasonable attorney's fees should be awarded, an evidentiary hearing was necessary to determine the amount so that the various factors bearing on the issue could be properly evaluated.


OUTCOME: The court found that the class members were entitled to an award of reasonable attorney's fees and ordered that the case be set for an evidentiary hearing on the issue.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Civil Rights -- Federal Civil Rights Attorney's Fees Awards Act of 1976 -- Attorney's Fees for Legal Assistance Corporation

Plaintiffs, who, in a civil rights action (US Code, tit 42, § 1983) in which they were represented by staff attorneys of a legal assistance corporation, obtained an order determining that defendants unconstitutionally disallowed them welfare benefits but were denied class certification and punitive damages, are entitled to an award of reasonable attorney's fees as "prevailing parties" after an evidentiary hearing (US Code, tit 42, § 1988); the controlling rule is that so long as a party has substantially prevailed on the merits compensation for time spent on unsuccessful research or litigation should be allowed unless the positions asserted were clearly frivolous or made in bad faith and an award is not any the less appropriate merely because a prevailing party's attorney is a nonprofit legal organization which provided its services without charge; the statute does not require that in the absence of a trial, an award of attorney's fees must be passed upon in piecemeal fashion by each Judge that made determinations in the case; defendants are precluded [***2] from asserting as a bar any fee-generating guidelines that the legal assistance corporation may have transgressed since such issue must be raised administratively (US Code, tit 42, § 2996e, subd [b], par [1], cl [B]) and cannot affect the final disposition of a case; defendants have not been prejudiced by the delay of nine and one-half months between the dismissal of the last appeal taken (49 NY2d 800) and the making of the motion for attorney's fees.

 

1957 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Money Management, Inc. v. Vetere


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Special Term, Queens County


107 Misc. 2d 861; 436 N.Y.S.2d 158; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2105



February 10, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court was a motion for summary judgment in lieu of complaint brought under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3213 by plaintiff third assignee of a promissory note, based on a cognovit judgment from the state of Pennsylvania. The issue was whether the judgment should have been entered in New York in accordance with the full faith and credit provisions of U.S. Const. art. IV, § 1.


OVERVIEW: A cognovit judgment was entered by a Pennsylvania court prior to defendant debtor's default. The third assignee of the promissory note then filed a motion for summary judgment in lieu of complaint on the cognovit judgment. The debtor contended that if entered in New York, the judgment was almost certain to have become a lien on her principal residence, in contradiction to the agreement of the parties and the warrant of authority to confess judgment. The court denied the motion for summary judgment in lieu of complaint and dismissed the proceeding, finding that there were no issues of fact to be resolved and no valid basis for enforcement of the foreign judgment. If the judgment was entered in New York, there may never have been a determination of the dispute on the merits and the effect would have been deprivation of property without due process of law. As the Pennsylvania judgment lacked the degree of finality that it would have been given in New York, the court questioned whether it had the conclusiveness to evoke the full faith and credit requirement. The court also questioned whether Pennsylvania had constitutional jurisdiction to render the judgment initially.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion for summary judgment in lieu of complaint filed by the third assignee of a promissory note based on a cognovit judgment from Pennsylvania, and dismissed the proceeding.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Judgments -- Enforcement of Foreign Cognovit Judgment -- Full Faith and Credit

Although a cognovit clause in a promissory note that gives an unlimited warrant to a third party to confess judgment at any time without prior notice even before an action is commenced is not per se violative of due process, a foreign cognovit judgment that is found to result in a denial of due process because of a significant deprivation of a property interest without an opportunity to be heard is not entitled to full faith and credit in New York and will not be enforced; accordingly, a Pennsylvania cognovit judgment entered by the clerk of a Pennsylvania court prior to defendant's default on a promissory note which contained a cognovit clause excluding the debtor's "principal residence" from the lien of any confessed judgment, is not entitled to enforcement in New York since a significant deprivation of a property interest occurs upon the entry of judgment and, inasmuch as defendant has not yet been given an opportunity to be heard in Pennsylvania where a court may open the judgment if there is sufficient evidence to go to a jury, the entry of the judgment in New York and the resulting lien on [***2] real property ( CPLR 5018, subd [a]; 5203, subd [a]) would be an unconstitutional violation of defendant's due process rights and, in addition, the Pennsylvania judgment lacks the degree of finality and conclusiveness necessary to entitle it to full faith and credit.

 

1958 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re N. Children


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Kings County


107 Misc. 2d 763; 435 N.Y.S.2d 1018; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2095



February 10, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After finding clear and convincing evidence of respondent mother's mental illness, the court ordered a dispositional hearing the petition to terminate the mother's parental rights to determine whether she was, by reason of mental illness, unable for the present and foreseeable future, to provide proper and adequate care for her three minor children.


OVERVIEW: The father's parental rights were terminated for abandonment. The court found clear and convincing evidence that the mother suffered from chronic schizophrenia. The court found that a dispositional hearing was necessary to protect the rights of the mother and the best interests of the children. Because the mother was participating in an out-patient clinic, the court ordered an updated mental examination, subpoenaed the out-patient clinic records for use by a second examining psychiatrist, and ordered a home visit by the social services agency. The psychiatrist felt that the mother would not be able to tolerate the stresses of parenthood, with or without medication. Her potential for relapse represented a clearly dangerous situation to which young children should not be exposed. The court found the grandparents well-meaning but not viable resources to assist the mother. While the court had no doubt that the mother was sincere in her desire to do whatever the court required of her, the court found that the dispositional alternative attended by the fewest harmful effects on the children would be to terminate the mother's rights and commence adoption proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court found that termination of the mother's parental rights was in the best interests of her children because of her mental illness. The court ordered the Commissioner of Social Services and the agency to institute adoption proceedings for the children.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Parent and Child -- Termination of Parental Rights

Although the Social Services Law and the Family Court Act do not make special provision for a separate dispositional hearing in a proceeding to terminate parental rights based on allegations of mental illness, it is wholly within the sound discretion of the Judge to order such a hearing in appropriate cases; the statutory distinction contained in section 384-b of the Social Services Law between mentally ill or retarded parents and neglectful or abusive parents is rational considering the potential damage to a young child exposed to a mentally ill parent. Accordingly, a termination of respondent's parental rights is in the best interests of her children where the evidence at the dispositional hearing reveals that she would not be able to tolerate the stresses of parenthood due to schizophrenia.

 

1959 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Adoption of U.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Orange County


106 Misc. 2d 828; 435 N.Y.S.2d 659; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2025



January 30, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] It is therefore ordered, that the petition for the adoption of Robin U. by Terry C. and Roger C. be, and hereby is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the husband of the natural mother of a child, brought an action seeking to adopt the mother's child. Respondent, the natural father of the child, objected to the proposed adoption.


OVERVIEW: The natural parents never married, but they maintained a close relationship for several months after the birth of the child. The natural father had been adjudicated the father and had paid support until the mother ended visitation rights when the adoption proceedings were initiated. Although the amended N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111 did not equalize the rights of all fathers of children born out of wedlock with the rights of the mothers, it did provide two categories of fathers whose consent was required. Section 111 required the court to distinguish between unconcerned fathers and concerned fathers who had accepted responsibility. Since the child was over six months of age, the father fell within § 111(d), and his consent was required for the proposed adoption. Substantial uncontroverted testimony showed that the father held himself out to be the child's father and that he maintained a close and loving relationship with the child. Although the discontinuance of child support was improper, there was no evidence of abandonment of his financial responsibility toward the child. Further, petitioner failed to disprove the fact that respondent was a concerned father.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition for adoption.


HEADNOTES

Children Born out of Wedlock -- Adoption -- Consent of Natural Father

A petition to adopt a child born out of wedlock, instituted by the mother's husband when the child was over six months old, is dismissed for lack of the consent of the natural father, since he meets the requirements of section 111 (subd 1, par [d]) of the Domestic Relations Law, which mandates that the father's consent to the adoption of a child born out of wedlock be obtained if his relationship with his child meets certain standards; the father openly held himself out to be the child's father and maintained a close and loving relationship with his child, including regular visitation, communication and financial support, until visitation was cut off by the mother.

 

1960 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wehnke


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Rome, Oneida County


107 Misc. 2d 881; 436 N.Y.S.2d 137; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2107



January 26, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The informations against the defendants are hereby dismissed, the bail is released, and Local Law No. 3 of 1971 of the City of Rome is hereby declared unconstitutional and void.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a topless dancer and the owner of a restaurant allowing topless dancing, moved the court for an order dismissing the informations against them upon the basis that N.Y. Loc. Law 3 (1971) regulating topless entertainment was unconstitutional on its face.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, the owner of a restaurant, was charged with illegally permitting topless entertainment at his premises in the city of Rome, New York. Defendant dancer was charged with engaging in topless entertainment at the same time and place. Defendants moved the court for an order dismissing the information upon the basis that the local law regulating topless entertainment was unconstitutional on its face. The court granted defendants' motion, and held that such nude dancing was within the constitutional protection of free expression, and that the local government could not totally restrict topless dancing. The court also concluded that the anti-topless ordinance must also fall for being overbroad in its scope by prohibiting constitutionally protected non-obscene performances or dances, as well as performances that may have been legitimately restricted.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the informations against defendants on the ground that a local law regulating topless dancing was unconstitutional and overbroad in its scope by prohibiting constitutionally protected non-obscene performances or dances, as well as legitimately restricted performances.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Expression -- Local Law Prohibiting Topless Entertainment

A local law of the City of Rome, which regulates topless entertainment and provides that no female shall entertain or perform in a public place with her breasts uncovered or so thinly covered as to appear uncovered, and that no person shall knowingly conduct, own or manage any public place where a female so appears or entertains, is unconstitutional and void, since nonobscene topless dancing is a constitutionally protected form of expression which may not be prohibited in accordance with the freedom of expression guarantee of the First Amendment of the United States Constitution; nude dancing has been held to be within the constitutional protection of free expression, and although the protection afforded to nudity by the First Amendment is minimal, a State or local governing body cannot totally restrict topless dancing under all circumstances. Furthermore, the ordinance is overbroad in its scope by prohibiting [***2] constitutionally protected nonobscene performances or dances, as well as performances that may be legitimately restricted.

 

1961 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Yankelevitz v. Royal Globe Ins. Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Kings County


107 Misc. 2d 636; 435 N.Y.S.2d 900; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2074



January 23, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee initiated a declaratory judgment action against defendant insurance companies alleging that they were required to provide him liability coverage in a third party action initiated against him by his employer.


OVERVIEW: The employee was operating a vehicle that was owned by his employer. The employee's wife was riding in the vehicle. The employee was involved in an automobile accident and his wife was severely injured. The wife initiated a personal injury action against the employer. The employer initiated a third party action against the employee. The employee initiated a declaratory judgment action against his insurance companies to compel them to provide liability coverage. The insurance companies denied liability under N.Y. Ins. Law § 167(3). The court concluded that the insurance companies were required to provide the employee liability coverage. Section 167(3) automatically excluded coverage for injuries to an insured's spouse. The court found that the employee was unaware of the existence of § 167(3) and was given no opportunity to bargain for interspousal coverage. The insurance companies could not rely on § 167(3) to deny the employee coverage when they did not make the employee aware of such provision and did not specifically include an interspousal exclusion provision in their policies.


OUTCOME: The court held that the insurance companies were required to provide the employee liability coverage.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Insurance -- Automobile Liability Insurance -- Statutory Interspousal Exclusion

1. If it can be established that full liability coverage is not readily obtainable by a spouse so as to afford full protection against a righteous claim by the other spouse, then subdivision 3 of section 167 of the Insurance Law, which provides that no automobile liability policy "shall be deemed to insure against any liability of an insured for injuries to his or her spouse or for injury to property of his or her spouse, unless express provision for such insurance is included in the policy", must be reconsidered and re-evaluated for its irreconcilability with the Legislature's paternalistic consumer statutes and also as to its constitutionality in function; a spouse may not be effectively prevented from seeking proper owner insurance coverage, be it at a higher premium, and the statutory interspousal exclusion may not be welded into an instrument of discrimination, of deprivation of equal opportunity to be fully insured and of equal protection under the law.

Insurance -- Automobile Liability Insurance -- Statutory Interspousal Exclusion

2. The sale of insurance contracts comes within [***2] the scope of section 2-302 of the Uniform Commercial Code, which authorizes the court to refuse to enforce an unconscionable contract or clause; accordingly, the inclusion in an automobile liability insurance policy of the statutory exclusion for interspousal coverage (Insurance Law, § 167, subd 3) can constitute an unconscionable contract if it can be established that the insurer does not issue motor vehicle liability insurance policies with interspousal coverage and that the spouse for whom liability coverage was disclaimed was not given any notice nor had any knowledge of the existence of the statutory exclusion and was given no opportunity to bargain for interspousal coverage.

Insurance -- Automobile Liability Insurance -- Statutory Interspousal Exclusion

3. Subdivision 3 of section 167 of the Insurance Law, which provides that no automobile liability insurance policy "shall be deemed to insure against any liability of an insured for injuries to his or her spouse or for injury to property of his or her spouse, unless express provision for such insurance is included in the policy", although constitutional, cannot be applied to exclude plaintiff from liability coverage [***3] in his employer's third-party action against him, plaintiff's wife having been severely injured while riding as a passenger in an automobile operated by plaintiff and owned by his employer, since defendants insurers waived and bargained away their right to include the statute as an exclusion in their "complete" contract of "comprehensive" insurance by failing to include any reference to the statute or any language reiterating the interspousal exclusion; the subject insurance policies fully cover the named insureds, including plaintiff, and their respective spouses for "all hazards" except those specifically excluded and are thus valid and binding as written; the Legislature, in enacting the interspousal exclusion, never intended to present to the insurance industry a means by which all of the consumer protective legislation may be frustrated nor grant to an insurance company a talismatic sword that can destroy an honest claim of an innocent consumer.

 

1962 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re S.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Bronx County


106 Misc. 2d 770; 435 N.Y.S.2d 645; 1981 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2018



January 22, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The instant application being granted, the limited letters of guardianship of the infant heretofore issued by this court are sealed with the adoption file.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner grandparents filed an application for the private placement adoption of their grandchild.


OVERVIEW: The grandparents had custody of their grandchild. The mother was never married and did not know who the child's father was. The mother consented to the grandparents' adoption of the child, but died before the adoption was granted. The grandparents contended that the putative father was not entitled to notice. They further alleged that his consent was not required. The court granted the application for adoption. The court determined that the consent of the putative father was not required under N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111(1). The court noted that it was impossible to tell when the child in question was placed with her grandparents for the purpose of adoption because the grandparents had raised the child since birth. The father did not come within the ambit of the requirements of § 111(1)(d), (e); therefore, his consent was not required. Moreover, he was not entitled to notice because he did not meet any of the requirements of § 111-a. Finally, the court determined that the fact that § 111(1)(e) was previously found to be unconstitutional as applied to another case did not mandate that conclusion in the grandparents' action.


OUTCOME: The court granted the grandparents' application for the private placement adoption of their grandchild.


HEADNOTES

Adoption -- Consent of Father of Out-of-Wedlock Child -- "Significant Paternal Interest"

The consent of the unknown nonmarital father to the adoption of an eight-year-old girl is not required and no service of process or notice need be made on such putative father by publication or otherwise since the nonmarital father has never manifested any "significant paternal interest" in that the mother prior to her death did not know the identity of the child's father and no one has ever come forward and held himself out as the father of the child, and, thus the nonmarital father does not meet any of the statutory criteria necessary to require his consent whether or not the child was "placed for adoption" before or after the age of six months (Domestic Relations Law, § 111, subd 1, pars [d], [e]) and he is not even entitled to "notice" of the adoption (Domestic Relations Law, § 111-a).
