Respondent moved for an indefinite adjournment of the trial contending that her constitutional privilege against self-incrimination would be violated if the child abuse trial were held before the termination of the criminal case against her. Respondent also argued that she was incompetent to stand trial. The court held that respondent's child abuse trial, before the conclusion of the related criminal prosecution, would not violate her privilege against self-incrimination. The court found that N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 1014(d) afforded respondent all the protection from self-incrimination to which she was constitutionally entitled. The court further held that the trial could be held even if respondent was incompetent as long as a guardian ad litem was appointed for her to protect her interests.


OUTCOME: The court denied respondent mother's motion for an indefinite adjournment of child abuse proceedings.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Parent and Child -- Child Protective Proceeding -- Self Incrimination

1. Conducting civil Family Court child abuse proceedings concurrent with or prior to a criminal prosecution concerning the same underlying acts as provided by statute (Family Ct Act, § 1013, subd [b]; § 1014, subd [c]) is constitutional and does not impose an unconstitutional penalty upon a respondent's constitutional privilege against self incrimination since the choice imposed on a respondent by a forthwith child abuse trial between the right to testify therein and the right against self incrimination is constitutionally permissible in that the choice against testifying in the child abuse trial and risking self incrimination will not result in a direct and automatic loss of a benefit; burdening a respondent with the difficult choice between the right to testify and the right to silence is justified by profound considerations of public policy since delaying a child abuse trial for the period required to resolve the criminal case would leave the child's care in a suspended state for many months and prevent for an incalculable time the long-term planning essential for the child's welfare; in any event,  [***2] the Family Court's authority to grant a respondent "testimonial immunity in any subsequent criminal court proceeding" (Family Ct Act, § 1014, subd [d]) provides all the protection of the privilege against self incrimination to which a respondent is constitutionally entitled.

Parent and Child -- Child Protective Proeeeding -- Competency to Stand Trial

2. Child neglect or abuse proceedings in the Family Court are governed by the civil rule regarding competency to stand trial despite some similarity to criminal proceedings since the purpose of a child protective proceeding is remedial rather than punitive, and, thus, the appointment of a guardian ad litem for a respondent mentally incompetent to stand trial ( CPLR 1201) will permit the child abuse trial to proceed.

 

1987 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Viggiani


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


105 Misc. 2d 210; 431 N.Y.S.2d 979; 1980 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2466



September 22, 1980 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the defendant's challenge for cause based upon the homosexuality of the prospective juror, is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, charged with the crime of assault in the third degree, N.Y. Penal Law § 120.00, moved to disqualify a prospective juror for cause on the ground the juror was prejudiced in a case where the complaining witness was a homosexual.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with committing the crime of assault in the third degree. During an examination of a jury panel, the district attorney informed the panel that the complaining witness was a homosexual and asked if any other jurors would be prejudiced as a result of the witness's sexual orientation. Several jurors were excused. Upon learning that a prospective juror had practiced homosexuality, defendant moved to excuse him for cause, arguing that the juror might have an implied bias that should disqualify him. The court held that the facts of the case did not establish a relationship that warranted exclusion of a prospective juror. The fact of sexual orientation created only a mere potentiality for bias, and this was not enough to excuse a prospective juror. Accordingly, defendant's challenge for cause based upon homosexuality of the prospective juror was denied.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion was denied because the mere potentiality for bias was not enough to excuse the juror.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Voir Dire -- Challenge for Cause of Homosexual Juror

A prospective juror, who is a homosexual, may not be disqualified for cause by defendant from hearing defendant's assault trial in which the complaining witness is a homosexual and in which evidence may be heard at trial concerning a random attack on homosexuals, said juror having professed throughout the voir dire, without reservation, his ability to judge impartially, fairly and without prejudice, since the fact of the prospective juror's homosexuality creates only a "mere potentiality" for bias insufficient to warrant exclusion of a prospective juror ( CPL 270.20); in addition, excluding a group of otherwise qualified citizens from sitting as jurors merely because of their homosexuality is tantamount to a denial of equal protection under the Constitution.

 

1988 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Sheridan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Justice Court of New York, Town of Perinton, Monroe County


105 Misc. 2d 317; 432 N.Y.S.2d 70; 1980 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2482



September 22, 1980 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state of New York (the people) initiated a cause of action against defendant in the court when a traffic violation ticket was issued and placed upon defendant's car for a violation of a handicapped parking restriction.


OVERVIEW: The town's sign code did not have any requirements with reference to the nature and manner of handicapped parking areas. The contested sign was brown with a white logo of a wheelchair. The court held that the sign did not conform with the state's requirements that required a green legend with a white background or to the federal requirements that required a green border and a white symbol on a blue and white background. The court held that the power of the town was derived from the state and the state's statute provided that no ordinance, order, rule, or regulation made by any local authority would have become effective until signs or markings giving notice were posted, unless authorized in writing by the department of transportation. No written authorization from the department of transportation was presented to the lower court. The obligation was placed upon local authorities to place and maintain traffic control devices conforming to the state manual and specifications. The signs were not posted at the places ordinarily used to warn drivers of restricted parking, resulting in a trap to the unwary citizen.


OUTCOME: The proceeding against defendant was dismissed and the court ordered that upon proper application of writ of error coram nobis that the fines imposed by reason of violations at the shopping center be considered for refund.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Motor Vehicles -- Parking Restrictions -- Handicapped Parking Areas

A town ordinance, which provides that parking in spaces designated for handicapped persons is by permit only, and which may be enforced in private parking areas upon written request of the owner, may not be enforced against a driver who parked his vehicle in a private parking area in a space designated for handicapped parking since the signs indicating the restricted nature of the space do not conform to State standards and the written request of the owner to enforce handicapped parking constitutes an implicit consent to abide by State law as well as the town ordinance which the signs failed to do; furthermore, the nonconformity of the signs to those used by governmental authorities causes a failure to apprise citizens of the enforceability of the signs by fine or penalty, constituting a trap for the unwary; therefore, the proceeding against the driver must be dismissed.

 

1989 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Burton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Buffalo


105 Misc. 2d 469; 432 N.Y.S.2d 312; 1980 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2528



September 12, 1980 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved the court for an order dismissing the information charging her with a violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.37(2) on the grounds that the information was insufficient to constitute a violation of § 240.37(2) and that § 240.37(2) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant alleged that her prosecution for a violation of § 240.37(2) was unconstitutional because the overwhelming majority of those prosecuted for prostitution were women rather than men. The court found that, although the circumstances of defendant's arrest and the fact that almost all the people arrested in the city for prostitution-related offenses were women would seem to suggest that defendant and other women were victims of bias, a comprehensive view of the prosecutions on prostitution charges showed that there were good and sufficient reasons, mainly problems with manpower, to justify the police on concentrating on the arrest of female prostitutes. The court held that the information was sufficient because all the areas described were public places and the conduct otherwise fell within that which was proscribed under the applicable and statutory case law. The court found that the conduct proscribed was sufficiently delineated to put the public on notice, and thus the law was not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad. Finally, the court ruled that there was a valid public interest and purpose in prohibiting sex for money as opposed to sex that was offered freely.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motions.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Prostitution -- Discriminatory Enforcement against Women

Since the fact that almost all the persons arrested in Buffalo for prostitution-related offenses are women is based on good and sufficient reasons, mainly problems with police manpower justifying the police in concentrating on the arrest of female prostitutes rather than their male customers, defendant has failed in her burden of proving that her prosecution for loitering for the purpose of engaging in a prostitution offense (Penal Law, § 240.37, subd 2) violates her rights as a woman under the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment; the conduct proscribed by the statute is sufficiently delineated to put the public on notice and thus the law is not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad and there is also a valid public interest and purpose in prohibiting sex for money as opposed to sex which is offered freely.

 

1990 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Nelson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Westchester County


105 Misc. 2d 747; 433 N.Y.S.2d 314; 1980 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2580



September 5, 1980 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, respondent's motion for leave to file the demand for jury nunc pro tunc is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner executors filed a motion for summary judgment dismissing the objections of respondents, the decedent's children, in a contested accounting procedure. The decedent's children filed motions for summary judgment on their objections. The decedent's children also sought leave to file a jury demand nunc pro tunc.


OVERVIEW: In a contested accounting proceeding, executors filed motions for summary judgment dismissing the objections of the decedent's children, and the decedent's children filed motions for summary judgment on their objections. The decedent's children also sought leave to file a jury demand nunc pro tunc. Although the court denied summary judgment to each of the parties on most of the claims because triable issues of fact existed, it concluded that the validity of payments made to the executors was an issue of law that depended entirely on the constitutionality of N.Y. Surr. Ct. Proc. Act Law § 2307. The court determined that the statute, which directed a uniform rate of compensation for executors, was constitutional because it treated those similarly situated equally; thus, objections to the payments were dismissed. The court denied the request by the decedent's children for leave to file a jury demand nunc pro tunc because the proceeding was essentially equitable in nature and historically and traditionally no jury trial was available under any statute or constitution.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the objections of the decedent's children to the compensation paid to the executors and denied their motion for a jury trial nunc pro tunc. All other motions for summary judgment were denied.


HEADNOTES

Executors and Administrators -- Statutory Right to Commissions -- Constitutionality

1. SCPA 2307, which authorizes the payment of commissions from the principal of an estate to fiduciaries other than trustees, does not deprive beneficiaries of an estate of their property without due process of law or in violation of the equal protection clause since (1) fixed compensation for fiduciaries is a strong incentive to diligence, induces qualified persons to act as fiduciaries, and removes the temptation of pecuniary interest as an influence on the fiduciary; and (2) the statute treats those similarly situated equally; a statute which results in pecuniary injury does not contravene the due process or equal protection clauses unless it is arbitrary, unreasonable or discriminatory.

Accounts and Accounting -- Contestors' Right to Jury Trial

2. In a contested accounting proceeding in the Surrogate's Court where respondent beneficiaries are objecting to the administration, management and business judgment of the executors of an estate, respondents are not entitled to a jury [***2] trial since the objections are claims not at law but for equitable relief for which historically and traditionally no jury trial is available under statute or Constitution (SCPA 502).

 

1991 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Keystone Associates v. State


Claim No. 49592


Court of Claims of New York


107 Misc. 2d 169; 433 N.Y.S.2d 695; 1980 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2842



September 5, 1980 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Appeals (New York) remitted claimant lessee's takings lawsuit against defendant state for a determination of the proper amount of damages.


OVERVIEW: The lessee entered into an agreement with an opera association to lease the property occupied by an old opera house (house). Under the lease, the house was to be demolished and the lessee was going to construct an office building (office). A statute was passed to save the house, authorizing the superintendent of buildings to refuse to issue a demolition permit. The statute was declared unconstitutional, but the lessee was deprived of it's intended use of the premises for eight months. The appellate court determined that the lessee was entitled to the fair rental value of the property, as determined by what a willing lessee would pay and a willing lessor would accept for the use of the premises for the eight months of the taking. The court computed the fair rental value by estimating the operating expenses and the projected income using both the state's and the lessee's methods. The court used future projections of income in determining the fair rental value because the property was intended to be developed. Because damages were incurred continuously during the course of the taking period, interest was computed from the midpoint of the taking period.


OUTCOME: The court determined that proper amount of damages in the lessee's takings lawsuit against the state.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Eminent Domain -- Compensation -- Temporary De Facto Appropriation -- Fair Rental Value

The proper measure of damages for the eight-month de facto appropriation of claimant's interest in property located in midtown Manhattan, such taking in 1966 under an unconstitutional statute having prevented claimant lessee from demolishing the old Metropolitan Opera House and erecting a high-rise office building on the property, is the fair rental value of the property for the eight-month period claimant was delayed in developing the property and although both claimant and the State made significant factual errors in arriving at fair rental value, neither method is per se wrong as a matter of law and there is thus an adequate range from which a proper finding of value can be made; accordingly, based upon a 30-month construction period, the estimate of operating expenses and projected income, the fair market value of the subject property at and for the duration of the taking is $ 800,000 and since damages were incurred continuously during the course of the taking period, interest is computed from the "reasonable intermediate date" ( CPLR 5001, subd [b]) of September 16, 1966, the midpoint [***2] of the taking period.

 

1992 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re D.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


106 Misc. 2d 370; 431 N.Y.S.2d 936; 1980 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2721



September 5, 1980 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent mother sought dismissal of a petition for the termination of her parental rights on the grounds of her alleged mental illness. She contended that termination under N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 384-b based on the mental illness or mental retardation of the parent was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Termination of the mother's parental rights was ordered because of mental illness, and the court of appeals reversed, referring the case for a new trial. The mother filed a motion for dismissal challenging the constitutionality of N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 384-b, which permitted termination on grounds of mental illness or mental retardation. The court denied the motion for dismissal and held that § 384-b was constitutional. The court found that the classifications did not violate equal protection, that there was no presumption that the parent was unfit, and that the statute required a higher standard of proof of clear and convincing evidence that the parent was mentally ill. The court found further that the statute required a showing that termination was in the best interest of the child and that the fundamental rights of the parent ware clearly balanced against the child's fundamental rights.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion for dismissal. The court determined that after the appointment of a guardian ad litem for the mother, the proceeding would begin with a determination of the allegation of mental illness. If clear and convincing evidence of mental illness were found, a dispositional hearing would be scheduled to determine whether termination of parental rights would be in the best interests of the children.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Parent and Child -- Termination of Parental Rights -- Mental Illness

The fact that the statute authorizing the termination of parental rights because of clear and convincing evidence that the parent will be unable "to provide proper and adequate care" for the child in the foreseeable future by reason of mental illness (Social Services Law, § 384-b) does not specifically require a separate determination of the best interests of the child does not render the statute unconstitutional since, in every case where termination of parental rights may take place, the law requires that the court determine that the best interests of the child will be promoted by such termination; the mental illness grounds for termination of parental rights is not violative of due process or equal protection under both the strict scrutiny standard for suspect classifications and the standard of a rational relationship to a legitimate State purpose since the parent's fundamental rights to the child must be balanced against the child's fundamental right to a permanent home and, in recognition of the danger of unfairness to those whose very illness may hinder their defense, the statute requires that clear [***2] and convincing evidence be presented, a higher standard of proof than that required for other termination grounds.

 

1993 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Goding v. Goding


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


106 Misc. 2d 423; 431 N.Y.S.2d 797; 1980 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2760



September 3, 1980 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] A short-form order is being signed simultaneously herewith denying the plaintiff's motion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff wife brought a motion to amend her divorce complaint against defendant husband to allege a right to equitable distribution of the marital property or to permit a distributable award based upon the new amendments to N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 236.


OVERVIEW: The wife's divorce complaint was filed several months prior to the effective date of an amendment to § 236, which provided for equitable distribution of marital property. She sought to amend her complaint so that the new provisions of § 236 would apply, but the court denied her motion. The court held that the legislative history and the preamble to § 236 provided that the law effective on the date the complaint was filed applied to the action. The court held that the preamble was clear and concise, providing that § 236(A) was applicable to actions previously filed. The legislative intent was to create a new system for maintenance and distribution of assets only with respect to those actions commenced after the effective date of the statute. The only exception was for agreements the parties made between themselves so as to relieve them of the strict interpretation of the legislation. To hold that equitable distribution was meant to apply to actions commenced prior to the effective date of the statute would fly in the face of clear legislative intent and totally annul and destroy the weeks and months of effort to develop legislation acceptable to both houses of the legislature.


OUTCOME: The court denied the wife's motion for leave to amend her complaint.


HEADNOTES

Husband and Wife -- Equitable Distribution -- Retroactive Application

The statutory equitable distribution provisions for matrimonial actions, which establish a new concept of support and distribution of property by implementation of the terms "maintenance", "distributive award" and "marital property" (Domestic Relations Law, § 236), apply only to matrimonial actions commenced on or after the effective date of the statute of July 19, 1980 in view of the clear legislative intent that the principles of equitable distribution not be retroactively applied; allowing a party to discontinue an action commenced prior to July 19, 1980 and commence a new action so as to come under the equitable distribution principles would do violence to the legislative intent and would prejudice one of the parties.

 

1994 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


American Cyanamid Co. (Lederle Laboratories Div.) v. Public Service Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


106 Misc. 2d 275; 431 N.Y.S.2d 336; 1980 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2678



August 25, 1980 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff company sought a preliminary injunction against defendant, the Public Service Commission of the State of New York (commission), to prevent a certain commissioner from participating in the rate case proceedings.


OVERVIEW: The matter before the court concerned the rates of a public utility company. The company sought a declaratory judgment and a permanent injunction. The company objected to a certain commissioner's participation in the rate case proceedings. The commission contended the company's sole remedy was found in N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 and that the declaratory judgment action was so inappropriate as to warrant a dismissal. The commission also argued the action before the court was premature as it was brought before the determination of administrative proceedings in violation of the finality requirement of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 7801. The court determined that the commission was entitled to judgment dismissing the complaint for the reason that the complaint failed to state a cause of action. The court held the participation of the commissioner was statutorily authorized. The court concluded the company was not denied due process. The court held the company failed to make the showing of the requisite right to ultimate relief.


OUTCOME: The court held the company failed to establish it was entitled to a preliminary injunction to prevent a certain commissioner from participating in the rate case proceedings.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Administrative Law -- Participation of Commissioner in Hearing

1. A Commissioner of the Public Service Commission, who participated in an administrative hearing as to the fixing of utility rates to be charged by plaintiffs and coauthored a recommended decision in the matter, is not prohibited from further participation in the commission's review of the recommended decision and in the commission's final determination of the matter by the due process clause and the statute authorizing the commissioner's participation in the hearing process (Public Service Law, § 11) is not unconstitutional since the agency's procedures may not be equated with the judicial process; while a Trial Judge is possessed with the power to make binding determinations and thus must function independently from the appellate tribunals, an Administrative Law Judge makes no determinations but serves to aid the agency in fact finding and in determinations, a service which, by facilitating the development of a complete record, aiding in the identification of issues and the positions of the parties, actually enhances the potential for a fair resolution of a matter.

Administrative Law -- Participation  [***2] of Commissioner in Hearing -- Injunction

2. A motion for a preliminary injunction seeking to enjoin an agency commissioner, who has participated in an administrative hearing and coauthored a recommended decision, from further participating in the agency's review of the recommended decision and final determination of the matter must be denied since plaintiff has not shown a right to ultimate relief and since it has not shown that it would be irreparably damaged upon denial of the requested injunction.

 

1995 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cunningham


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, Kings County


106 Misc. 2d 326; 431 N.Y.S.2d 785; 1980 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2686



August 22, 1980 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion to dismiss is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants brought a motion to dismiss the renewal of criminal prosecutions against them by the restoration of an adjournment in contemplation of dismissal based upon their failure of restitution due to indigency.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were granted adjournments in contemplation of dismissal (ACD) upon their promises to make restitution. Upon failure of restitution, plaintiff had the matters restored. Defendants' dereliction was due to indigency occasioned by the loss of their jobs subsequent to the restitution agreement. Defendants brought a motion to dismiss the renewal of criminal prosecutions against them, arguing that the renewal of criminal prosecution by the restoration of an ACD because of the failure of restitution due to indigency in the six-month period between disposition and final dismissal was not permissible. The court ruled that restitution became inappropriate when defendants' financial condition was such that a civil judgment against them would be incapable of satisfaction by virtue of indigency. Further, the court held that indigency precluded revocation of probation for inability to pay a fine, it precluded incarceration for inability to pay court costs, and there was no distinction between the deprivation worked upon defendants when inability to make restitution payments triggered renewed prosecution.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Adjournment in Contemplation of Dismissal -- Restoration of Case to Calendar

The restoration of an adjournment in contemplation of dismissal to the Trial Calendar ( CPL 170.55, subd 2) on the basis of defendant's failure to make restitution to the victim, due to defendant's indigency, is improper and impermissible since restoration on these grounds will have an unconstitutional effect; once the prosecutor has determined that a matter merits dismissal, defendant's inability, due to indigency, to complete restitution payments is an illegitimate basis for reversing the decision since to mandate restoration of a case on this basis is to create a class of status offenders, namely the impoverished; there should be no distinction made between the deprivation worked upon a defendant when inability to make restitution payments triggers renewed prosecution and when inability to pay a fine causes revocation of probation, which is precluded by the indigent status of the defendant.

 

1996 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Walter


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


106 Misc. 2d 359; 431 N.Y.S.2d 776; 1980 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2718



August 18, 1980 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the defendant's motion to dismiss the summonses issued is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were summoned before the Criminal Court of the City of New York (New York) on charges of operating an unlicensed cabaret.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were issued summonses alleging that they operated an unlicensed cabaret in violation of the city administrative code. The people alleged that the establishment in question featured a five-piece band and that the patrons engaged in dancing on the premises where drinks were sold. Defendants filed a motion to dismiss all of the summonses on the grounds that they were unconstitutional as violative of U.S. Const. amend. I, XIV. The court held that the statute was not unconstitutional because it clearly banned dancing. The court noted that the ostensible purpose of the licensing ordinance was to give the Department of Consumer Affairs the power to regulate certain kinds of establishments where the health and safety of the people may need to be protected. The court further noted that the portion of the statute that allowed music by mechanical devices, while live music was subject to licensing was outdated. The court reasoned that because of technology advances, mechanical devices could now sound like an orchestra and that the distinction between live and mechanical music had become obsolete.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motion to dismiss the summonses for operating an unlicensed cabaret because the statute was constitutionally permissible.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Standing

1. A party may challenge the constitutionality of an ordinance which requires a license for the operation of a cabaret where he is present before the court facing prosecution for violating the ordinance since he is affected by its enforcement; furthermore, regardless of whether a party has ever applied for a license or could have obtained a license, that party may challenge the constitutionality of a licensing ordinance on its face.

Licenses -- Amusement License -- Cabaret

2. A portion of the Administrative Code of the City of New York (Administrative Code, §§ B32-297.0, B32-296.0, subd 3), which prohibits the operation of an unlicensed cabaret, is capable of a broad interpretation which the legislature may not have intended and the citizen who runs an establishment providing amusement not specifically prohibited by the statute is left at his peril, not knowing if he has violated a penal statute and, likewise, that portion of the statute which creates an exception to the licensing requirement could conceivably mandate a result not intended by the legislature in [***2] view of modern technology; accordingly, inasmuch as judicial self-restraint and due deference for the separation of powers doctrine dictate that a trial court's determination of invalidity be made only when such construction is inelectably inescapable, the ordinance need not be declared unconstitutional as applied to defendants where it is alleged that the establishment in question featured a five-piece band with brass and percussion instruments and that the patrons engaged in dancing on the premises where drinks were sold since the part of the statute that requires an establishment with dancing to obtain a cabaret license is clearly acceptable as the kind of activity that can be regulated.

 

1997 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Mirto v. Sadowski


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


106 Misc. 2d 245; 430 N.Y.S.2d 1017; 1980 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2672



August 15, 1980 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition of Peter G. Mirto is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner candidates filed an action against respondent board of election seeking to require that the candidates for two vacancies for the public office of judge of a civil court from a certain district to run together in a primary election for a certain political party, with the two receiving the greatest number of votes to become the nominees of such party.


OVERVIEW: When one of two civil judges assigned to a district retired, creating a vacancy for which a nomination had to be made at a primary election, the board of elections made available a list of all vacancies in the office of civil court judge, and designating petitions for such vacancy were circulated and filed for four candidates. Then, the remaining judge was removed, creating a vacancy seven days before the last day to circulate designating petitions and requiring a nomination at the primary election. Two candidates for the first vacancy sought to require the candidates for both vacancies to run together, with the two receiving the greatest number of votes being the nominees of the party. The court held that: (1) where the vacancy had not occurred within such time as to permit the designation of the vacancy upon the list required by N.Y. Elec. Law § 6-168(1), § 6-168(3), giving a nomination without participation in a primary election, was not effective; and (2) instead, § 6-168(4) applied, requiring that candidates for both vacancies be listed on the primary ballot without reference to the seat designated, and the two highest number of votes would be nominees for such office.


OUTCOME: The court granted the candidates' petition to the extent that the board of elections should arrange the ballot for the political party's primary election in such a manner as to reflect two vacancies for the public office, to place upon said ballot the names of all persons whose petitions had been validated for either vacancy, and to declare as the nominees those two candidates receiving the highest number of votes in said primary election.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Standing -- Judicial Nominations

1. A prospective candidate for the office of Judge of the Civil Court of the City of New York, who alleges that the statutorily prescribed method of gathering designating petitions is unconstitutional in that it does not permit for a fair opportunity to gather sufficient petitions for a primary election where a vacancy for said office arises more than seven days before the last day to circulate petitions, lacks standing to maintain the proceeding which he brought as an "aggrieved candidate", since he failed to tender any petitions in attempted compliance with the law.

Elections -- Primary Elections -- Judicial Nominations

2. Section 6-168 of the Election Law, which is a clear attempt by the Legislature to assist incumbent Judges seeking re-election by providing that not later than 14 days before the first day to circulate designating petitions for a primary election for the office of Judge of the Civil Court of the City of New York, a list of all vacancies for such offices shall be prepared and that such petitions shall indicate which office is being sought, thereby allowing [***2] aspiring jurists to run for only vacant offices rather than the incumbent's, must be construed strictly and in a manner as to not deny equal opportunity to appear on the primary ballot; therefore, where a vacancy arose subsequent to said 14-day period but prior to the last day to make the vacancy subject to the primary election process, the operative statutory language giving a nomination without participation in a primary is ineffective since the Legislature at no time considered a vacancy occurring at such a time, and the previously used nominating system, which lumped all candidates together and selected as candidates for the general election those equal to the number of offices available, receiving the greatest number of votes in the primary election without reference to the particular office sought, must be employed.

 

1998 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Golomb v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


106 Misc. 2d 264; 431 N.Y.S.2d 332; 1980 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2675



August 15, 1980 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner probationary teacher initiated a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding against respondents, the board of education and others, seeking back pay for the period of her discontinuance prior to her reinstatement by the chancellor.


OVERVIEW: The teacher returned to work at the beginning of a school year as a probationary teacher, but she was orally informed that her services were terminated. Hearings were conducted several months later, and the chancellor disagreed with the recommendation to terminate. The following school year the teacher returned to work. The teacher claimed that she was entitled to back pay for the year that she did not work. The court denied back pay to the teacher. The court stated that the teacher presented no evidence that the discontinuance or termination reviews, which were held pursuant to a collective bargaining agreement, were wrongful, illegal, or improper. Respondents acted completely within their discretionary authority when they discontinued the teacher's probationary employment. The decision to reinstate the teacher was also within respondents' discretionary authority and was not an admission of wrongdoing or an adjudication on the merits of the teacher's earlier discontinuance. Neither the state constitution nor laws gave the teacher the right to recover back pay for work that was not performed.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the teacher's petition.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- Teachers -- Probationary Employee

A probationary teacher, reinstated by the Chancellor after termination, is not entitled to back salary for the duration of her termination, since, pursuant to section 2573 of the Education Law, the school board may dismiss the probationary teacher at any time; she therefore has no property rights attaching to her employment; the board's discontinuance of the teacher's probationary employment and its subsequent reinstatement of the teacher were completely within its discretionary authority, and not an admission of wrongdoing or an adjudication on the merits of her discontinuance; accordingly, the teacher has no basis for her claim of back pay upon reinstatement, and since neither the Constitution nor New York law recognizes the right of a reinstated probationer to an award of back pay, such a payment would be an unconstitutional gift of funds.

 

1999 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Miller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


105 Misc. 2d 41; 430 N.Y.S.2d 1007; 1980 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2496



August 13, 1980 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent father sought an order to show cause, seeking to set aside an order of the court that terminated his parental rights on the ground that petitioner agency failed to notify him of the prior proceeding.


OVERVIEW: The agency instituted a proceeding under N.Y. Soc. Serv. Laws § 384-b to terminate the father's parental rights. The father was served by publication, a default inquest was held, and an order of termination was entered. By order to show cause, the father sought to set aside the order and have a hearing on the ground that he had received no notice of the proceeding. The father alleged that when the agency brought the action, he was in a state correctional institution and no research or diligent effort was made to contact him there to give him notice of the proceeding before jurisdiction was obtained by publication. He also said that he was on parole and had learned of the proceedings for the first time. The agency argued against reopening the hearing because the father's confinement at a state correctional facility for a felony conviction meant that his consent to an adoption was not required under N.Y. Dom. Rel. Laws § 111(2)(d). The court reopened the case. The court held that section 111(2)(d) as applied to parolees was unconstitutional because the lack of distinction between parents who were on parole and parents who were incarcerated made no rational sense.


OUTCOME: The court granted the father's request to reopen the hearing on the termination of his parental rights.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Parent and Child -- Termination of Parental Rights -- Abandonment

1. The default of a respondent father in a proceeding to terminate his parental rights on the ground of abandonment pursuant to section 384-b of the Social Services Law must be vacated and a hearing must be held to determine whether there are grounds for termination on the merits; the father, who was incarcerated in a State correctional institution at the time that the default occurred, was served by publication, and no inquiry was made to the New York State Department of Correctional Services, under whose jurisdiction he was held; therefore, his default must be vacated in the interests of justice.

Parent and Child -- Termination of Parental Rights -- Dismissal of Proceeding

2. The petition of a child welfare agency brought to terminate the father's parental rights on the ground of abandonment may not be dismissed since a dismissal would implicity determine that section 111 (subd 2, par [d]) of the Domestic Relations Law, pursuant to which the agency claims that the father, by operation of his incarceration in a State correctional institution and subsequent parole, is not entitled to a hearing on [***2] the merits of his fitness as a parent, is valid and, therefore, acts as a bar to a further proceeding; furthermore, dismissal is improper since it would prejudice the rights of the child who would necessarily remain in limbo while a new proceeding is begun; therefore, the validity of section 111 (subd 2, par [d]), as limited to the father's situation, must be determined.

Adoption -- Consent of Parolee Father

3. As applied to parolees, section 111 (subd 2, par [d]) of the Domestic Relations Law, which provides that the consent of a parent sentenced to a term of imprisonment is not required for the adoption of his child is unconstitutional since the lack of distinction between prisoners, who are being punished for their crimes, and parolees, who are considered worthy of rehabilitation, has no rational basis; the punishment aspect of dispensing with a parolee's consent to the adoption of his child makes successful rehabilitation more difficult.

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws

4. The strict scrutiny test must be applied in determining whether section 111 (subd 2, par [d]) of the Domestic Relations Law, which provides that the consent of a parent [***3] sentenced to a term of imprisonment is unnecessary for the adoption of the child, is violative of the equal protection clause, since the fundamental right of a parent to rear his child is involved; no compelling State interest exists to apply section 111 (subd 2, par [d]) to parolees since the parent is no longer incarcerated, may be beginning to rehabilitate himself and can play an active role in the rearing of his child; denying the father this right does not serve the compelling State interest of providing the child with a stable and permanent home.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law

5. A parolee father, whose parental rights are sought to be terminated without his consent on the basis of section 111 (subd 2, par [d]) of the Domestic Relations Law, is denied procedural due process since the presumption of unfitness, used solely, in this instance, because it is more convenient to presume than to prove, is insufficient to justify denying the father a hearing when the issue is the dismemberment of his family. Since both the interests of the parent and the child must be balanced, as in New York the best interests of the child standard may only be applied in extraordinary [***4] circumstances, the parolee father is entitled to a hearing to ascertain whether or not extraordinary circumstances are present.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law

6. A parolee father is denied procedural due process of law by operation of section 111 (subd 2, par [d]) of the Domestic Relations Law, which provides that the consent of a parent sentenced to a term of imprisonment is not required for the adoption of the child, since parole status is distinguishable from incarceration and application of the statute to a parolee father would deprive him of his parental rights simply because of his status as a felon parolee without reference to his fitness as a parent; accordingly, a hearing on the ground of abandonment, the ground on which the petition was originally instituted, is mandated.

 

2000 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Archer v. North Greenbush


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Rensselaer County


105 Misc. 2d 60; 431 N.Y.S.2d 644; 1980 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2498



August 12, 1980 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the court finds no legal basis for relief, and grants the respondents cross motion under CPLR 7804 (subd [f]) to dismiss the petition with prejudice because it fails, as a matter of law, to state a legally cognizable cause of action.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner town landowners brought a show cause proceeding to invalidate respondent town's North Greenbush, N.Y., Local Law No. 3 (1980), which made N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 458's "veterans exemption" subject to increase or decrease in the same proportion as the assessed valuation of any real property in the town. The court converted the landowner's N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding into a declaratory judgment proceeding.


OVERVIEW: Town landowners brought a show cause proceeding to invalidate the town's North Greenbush, N.Y., Local Law No. 3 (1980), which made N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 458's "veterans exemption" subject to increase or decrease in the same proportion as the assessed valuation of any real property in the town. The court converted the landowner's N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding into a declaratory judgment proceeding. The court dismissed the landowner's petition for failure to state a cognizable claim. The court held that the town ordinance was not subject to the permissive referendum requirements of N.Y. Mun. Home Rule Law § 24(2)(c ) and neither the ordinance nor N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 458 was violative of the constitutional equal protection requirements. The court reasoned that the ordinance did not change a provision of law relating to assessments of real property because it did not change the procedure or manner of assessment as did a change from "fractional" to "full value" assessments. An exemption statute only offered relief from the standard assessment to individuals who demonstrated they qualified as one of the persons in the class eligible for a reduction from the standard assessment.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the landowner's petition because it failed to state a cognizable claim.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Real Property Taxation -- Veterans' Exemption

1. Local Law No. 3, 1980 of the Town of North Greenbush which made the "veterans' exemption" of section 458 of the Real Property Tax Law subject to increase or decrease in the same proportion as the assessed valuation of "any real property" in the Town of North Greenbush is increased or decreased on the assessment roll to be finally completed and filed in 1980 by reason of real property being assessed at full value on such assessment roll, and was passed by the town board without a vote by the taxpayers, is not subject to the permissive referendum requirement of section 24 (subd 2, par c) of the Municipal Home Rule Law, since that requirement gives property owners the right to vote on local laws relating to the procedure or manner of assessment, but not on local laws which implement an exemption from taxes which is legally permissible under the Real Property Tax Law;  [***2] said local law does not change a provision of law relating to assessments of real property because it does not change the procedure or manner of assessment as does a change from "fractional" to "full value" assessments.

Taxation -- Real Property Taxation -- Veterans' Exemption

2. Neither Local Law No. 3, 1980 of the Town of North Greenbush, which made the "veterans' exemption" of section 458 of the Real Property Tax Law subject to increase or decrease in the same proportion as the assessed valuation of "any real property" in the Town of North Greenbush is increased or decreased on the assessment roll to be finally completed and filed in 1980 by reason of real property being assessed at full value on such assessment roll, nor section 458 of the Real Property Tax Law, nor the manner of their application, is violative of constitutional equal protection requirements. A statute is presumed constitutional unless the discrimination between different classes of citizens has no relevance to the purpose for which the classification is made; here, the tax benefit given veterans, presumably because they have offered some portion of their lives in service to the country, which is not [***3] accorded non-veterans, is not violative of equal protection.
